###1###
When forty winters shall besiege thy brow,
And dig deep trenches in thy beauty's field,
Thy youth's proud livery, so gazed on now,
Will be a tatter'd weed, of small worth held:
Then being ask'd where all thy beauty lies,
Where all the treasure of thy lusty days,
To say, within thine own deep-sunken eyes,
Were an all-eating shame and thriftless praise.
How much more praise deserv'd thy beauty's use,
If thou couldst answer "This fair child of mine
Shall sum my count and make my old excuse,"
Proving his beauty by succession thine!
   This were to be new made when thou art old,
   And see thy blood warm when thou feel's it cold.

###2###
My glass shall not persuade me I am old,
So long as youth and thou are of one date;
But when in thee time's furrows I behold,
Then look I for the lesson of my fate.
For all that beauty that doth cover thee,
Is but the seemly raiment of my heart,
Which in thy breast doth live, as thine in me:
How can I then be elder than thou art?
O, therefore, love, be of thyself so wary
As I, not for myself, but for thee will;
Bearing thy heart, which I will keep so chary
As tender nurse her babe from faring ill.
   Presume not on thy heart when mine is slain;
   Thou gav'st me thine, not to give back again.

###3###
As an unperfect actor on the stage
Who with his fear is put besides his part,
Or some fierce thing replete with too much rage,
Whose strength's abundance weakens his own heart,
So I, for fear of trust, forget to say
The perfect ceremony of love's rite,
And in mine own love's strength seem to decay,
O'ercharg'd with burden of mine own love's might.
O, let my looks be then the eloquence
And dumb presagers of my speaking breast,
Who plead for love and look for recompense
More than that tongue that more hath more express'd.
   O, learn to read what silent love hath writ:
   To hear with eyes belongs to love's fine wit.

###4###
The sun itself sees not till heaven clears,
Nor can the world find promise in the dawn
Till eastern clouds have wept their misty tears,
And night's dark cloak be from the day withdrawn.
So in the shadow of my doubt I stay,
And find no comfort in the coming light,
For thy displeasure turns the sun to grey,
And makes a dungeon of the morning bright.
Why dost thou wrong the world with such a face,
And veil the beams that should all sorrows mend?
Let not my sins thy clemency disgrace,
But bid this endless night of sorrow end.
   For if thy grace can make me live anew,
   The world's true morning I shall find in you.

###5###
When I do count the clock that tells the time,
And see the brave day sunk in hideous night;
When I behold the violet past prime,
And sable curls all silver'd o'er with white;
When lofty trees I see barren of leaves
Which erst from heat did canopy the herd,
And summer's green all girded up in sheaves
Borne on the bier with white and bristly beard,
Then of thy beauty do I question make,
That thou among the wastes of time must go,
Since sweets and beauties do themselves forsake
And die as fast as they see others grow;
   And nothing 'gainst Time's scythe can make defence
   Save breed, to brave him when he takes thee hence.

###6###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date:
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimm'd;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance or nature's changing course untrimm'd;
But thy eternal summer shall not fade
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st;
Nor shall Death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st:
   So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,
   So long lives this and this gives life to thee.

###7###
That time of year thou mayst in me behold
When yellow leaves, or none, or few, do hang
Upon those boughs which shake against the cold,
Bare ruin'd choirs, where late the sweet birds sang.
In me thou see'st the twilight of such day
As after sunset fadeth in the west,
Which by and by black night doth take away,
Death's second self, that seals up all in rest.
In me thou see'st the glowing of such fire
That on the ashes of his youth doth lie,
As the death-bed whereon it must expire,
Consum'd with that which it was nourisht by.
   This thou perceiv'st, which makes thy love more strong,
   To love that well which thou must leave ere long.

###8###
If the dull substance of my flesh were thought,
Injurious distance should not stop my way;
For then despite of space I would be brought,
From limits far remote, where thou dost stay.
No matter then although my foot did stand
Upon the farthest earth remov'd from thee;
For nimble thought can jump both sea and land
As soon as think the place where he would be.
But, ah! thought kills me that I am not thought,
To leap large lengths of miles when thou art gone,
But that so much of earth and water wrought,
I must attend time's leisure with my moan,
   Receiving naught by elements so slow
   But heavy tears, badges of either's woe.

###9###
What is your substance, whereof are you made,
That millions of strange shadows on you tend?
Since every one hath, every one, one shade,
And you, but one, can every shadow lend.
Describe Adonis, and the counterfeit
Is poorly imitated after you;
On Helen's cheek all art of beauty set,
And you in Grecian tires are painted new:
Speak of the spring and foison of the year;
The one doth shadow of your beauty show,
The other as your bounty doth appear;
And you in every blessed shape we know.
   In all external grace you have some part,
   But you like none, none you, for constant heart.

###10###
Then let not winter's ragged hand deface
In thee thy summer, ere thou be distill'd:
Make sweet some vial; treasure thou some place
With beauty's treasure, ere it be self-kill'd.
That use is not forbidden usury
Which happies those that pay the willing loan;
That's for thyself to breed another thee,
Or ten times happier, be it ten for one;
Ten times thyself were happier than thou art,
If ten of thine ten times refigur'd thee:
Then what could death do, if thou shouldst depart,
Leaving thee living in posterity?
   Be not self-will'd, for thou art much too fair
   To be death's conquest and make worms thine heir.

###11###
When in disgrace with fortune and men's eyes,
I all alone beweep my outcast state,
And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless cries,
And look upon myself and curse my fate,
Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,
Featur'd like him, like him with friends possess'd,
Desiring this man's art and that man's scope,
With what I most enjoy contented least;
Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,
Haply I think on thee, and then my state,
Like to the lark at break of day arising
From sullen earth, sings hymns at heaven's gate;
   For thy sweet love remember'd such wealth brings
   That then I scorn to change my state with kings.

###12###
My mistress' eyes are nothing like the sun;
Coral is far more red than her lips' red;
If snow be white, why then her breasts are dun;
If hairs be wires, black wires grow on her head.
I have seen roses damask'd, red and white,
But no such roses see I in her cheeks;
And in some perfumes is there more delight
Than in the breath that from my mistress reeks.
I love to hear her speak, yet well I know
That music hath a far more pleasing sound;
I grant I never saw a goddess go;
My mistress, when she walks, treads on the ground:
   And yet, by heaven, I think my love as rare
   As any she belied with false compare.

###13###
Some glory in their birth, some in their skill,
Some in their wealth, some in their bodies' force,
Some in their garments, though new-fangled ill,
Some in their hawks and hounds, some in their horse;
And every humour hath his adjunct pleasure,
Wherein it finds a joy above the rest:
But these particulars are not my measure;
All these I better in one general best.
Thy love is better than high birth to me,
Richer than wealth, prouder than garments' cost,
Of more delight than hawks or horses be;
And having thee, of all men's pride I boast:
   Wretched in this alone, that thou mayst take
   All this away and me most wretched make.

###14###
Let me not to the marriage of true minds
Admit impediments. Love is not love
Which alters when it alteration finds,
Or bends with the remover to remove:
O no! it is an ever-fixed mark
That looks on tempests and is never shaken;
It is the star to every wandering bark,
Whose worth's unknown, although his height be taken.
Love's not Time's fool, though rosy lips and cheeks
Within his bending sickle's compass come:
Love alters not with his brief hours and weeks,
But bears it out even to the edge of doom.
   If this be error and upon me proved,
   I never writ, nor no man ever loved.

###15###
Weary with toil, I haste me to my bed,
The dear repose for limbs with travel tired;
But then begins a journey in my head,
To work my mind, when body's work's expired:
For then my thoughts, from far where I abide,
Intend a zealous pilgrimage to thee,
And keep my drooping eyelids open wide,
Looking on darkness which the blind do see:
Save that my soul's imaginary sight
Presents thy shadow to my sightless view,
Which, like a jewel hung in ghastly night,
Makes black night beauteous and her old face new.
   Lo! thus, by day my limbs, by night my mind,
   For thee and for myself no quiet find.

###16###
O, how I faint when I of you do write,
Knowing a better spirit doth use your name,
And in the praise thereof spends all his might,
To make me tongue-tied, speaking of your fame!
But since your worth, wide as the ocean is,
The humble as the proudest sail doth bear,
My saucy bark inferior far to his
On your broad main doth wilfully appear.
Your shallowest help will hold me up afloat,
Whilst he upon your soundless deep doth ride;
Or, being wreck'd, I am a worthless boat,
He of tall building and of goodly pride:
   Then if he thrive and I be cast away,
   The worst was this; my love was my decay.

###17###
How like a winter hath my absence been
From thee, the pleasure of the fleeting year!
What freezings have I felt, what dark days seen!
What old December's bareness everywhere!
And yet this time removed was summer's time;
The teeming autumn, big with rich increase,
Bearing the wanton burden of the prime,
Like widow'd wombs after their lords' decease:
Yet this abundant issue seem'd to me
But hope of orphans and unfather'd fruit;
For summer and his pleasures wait on thee,
And, thou away, the very birds are mute;
   Or, if they sing, 'tis with so dull a cheer
   That leaves look pale, dreading the winter's near.

###18###
They that have power to hurt and will do none,
That do not do the thing they most do show,
Who, moving others, are themselves as stone,
Unmoved, cold, and to temptation slow;
They rightly do inherit heaven's graces
And husband nature's riches from expense;
They are the lords and owners of their faces,
Others but stewards of their excellence.
The summer's flower is to the summer sweet,
Though to itself it only live and die,
But if that flower with base infection meet,
The basest weed outbraves his dignity:
   For sweetest things turn sourest by their deeds;
   Lilies that fester smell far worse than weeds.

###19###
So shall I live, supposing thou art true,
Like a deceived husband; so love's face
May still seem love to me, though altered new;
Thy looks with me, thy heart in other place:
For there can live no hatred in thine eye,
Therefore in that I cannot know thy change.
In many's looks the false heart's history
Is writ in moods and frowns and wrinkles strange.
But heaven in thy creation did decree
That in thy face sweet love should ever dwell;
Whate'er thy thoughts or thy heart's workings be,
Thy looks should nothing thence but sweetness tell.
   How like Eve's apple doth thy beauty grow,
   If thy sweet virtue answer not thy show!

###20###
Two loves I have of comfort and despair,
Which like two spirits do suggest me still:
The better angel is a man right fair,
The worser spirit a woman colour'd ill.
To win me soon to hell, my female evil
Tempteth my better angel from my side,
And would corrupt my saint to be a devil,
Wooing his purity with her foul pride.
And whether that my angel be turn'd fiend
Suspect I may, yet not directly tell;
But being both from me, both to each friend,
I guess one angel in another's hell:
   Yet this shall I ne'er know, but live in doubt,
   Till my bad angel fire my good one out.

###161###
Whence comes the power that within thine eyes
Doth hold the sun, and keep the stars in fee?
For in their depths a truer heaven lies
Than any astronomer shall ever see.
What art is this, that on thy cheek doth bloom,
And lends thy breath a sweetness like the spring?
That in my heart creates a gilded room
Wherein thy praises I am bound to sing.
Yet this fair show, this form of mortal make,
Is but a shadow of the truth I find,
A lovely vessel for a lovelier sake,
The perfect casket for a constant mind.
   So let the world admire but what it sees,
   I love the soul that gives such forms their ease.

###162###
When I do count the sorrows of the world,
And see how Fortune plays a fickle game,
How kings are from their highborn stations hurled,
And humble men are put to open shame;
When I behold how beauty must decay,
Like to a rose that withers on the vine,
And how the strongest arms do waste away,
Losing the strength that they did once define;
Then do I think of thee, and in my thought,
A fortress strong against despair is made,
For all the evils that the world hath wrought
Are by the memory of thy love outweighed.
   So though my state be poor and fortune grim,
   Thy love's a cup filled ever to the brim.

###163###
Why dost thou chide my pen for being slow,
And say my verse thy virtue fails to tell?
Dost thou not see that words can never show
The sacred place wherein thy graces dwell?
Should I compare thee to a summer's day?
That simile is worn and over-spent.
Or say thy beauty steals my breath away?
Such praise is but a hollow compliment.
For when I strive thy wondrous worth to write,
My ink runs dry, my weary hand doth fail,
The fairest word shrinks from thy purer light,
And all my craft is of no true avail.
   So blame me not if silent I remain,
   For speech itself thy grace would but profane.

###164###
That time of year thou mayst in me behold,
When summer's lease hath all expired and run,
And anxious thoughts, like creeping winter's cold,
Do cloud the aspect of a weaker sun.
My youth's bright fire, that once did burn so hot,
Is now but ash, a dull and fading gleam,
Its former triumphs buried and forgot,
The fleeting substance of a waking dream.
Yet grieve not thou that this my glass runs fast,
Nor let thy heart with sorrow be oppressed,
For what is present shall outshine the past,
And in thy constancy my soul is blessed.
   So let age come, and let my vigour cease,
   Within thy love I find eternal peace.

###165###
O, how my heart doth wage a heavy war,
When envious thoughts conspire to steal my rest,
And show me others, happier by far,
Who in the sunshine of thy favour's blessed.
I see a rival whisper in thine ear,
And watch a smile that is not meant for me,
And in that moment, all I hold most dear
Seems but a prize another strives to plea.
Then jealousy, that monster green and foul,
Doth with its venom poison all my mind,
And makes my spirit like a tempest howl,
To reason deaf, to reassurance blind.
   But when thou turn'st to me, my fears take flight,
   And all the world is once again made right.

###166###
So oft I have rehearsed thy sacred name,
It hath become a prayer upon my tongue,
A charm against all sorrow, hurt, and shame,
A private hymn that in my soul is sung.
When bleak despair doth lay a siege to me,
And all my hopes like fearful cowards fly,
I speak thy name, and in its sound I see
A hopeful banner raised against the sky.
It is the key that opens memory's door
To rooms of joy where we have laughed and been;
It is the balm that makes a spirit, poor
In worldly goods, a monarch and a king.
   For in that single word, a world I find,
   That rules my heart and pacifies my mind.

###167###
The actor leaves the stage when play is done,
His borrowed robes and majesty laid by,
He is no more the king whose race is run,
But common man beneath a common sky.
So I, who in thy presence feel so great,
As if a prince of some divine estate,
Must, when thou partest, suffer that same fate,
And be again a thing disconsolate.
For thou art my sole audience and my crown,
Thy gentle judgment is my only fame;
Without thine eyes, my spirit tumbles down,
And all my glory turns to silent shame.
   So come again, and let me play my part,
   And be the king that reigns within thy heart.

###168###
Weary with toil, I haste me to my bed,
The dear repose for limbs with travel tired;
But then begins a journey in my head
To work my mind, when body's work's expired:
For then my thoughts, from far where I abide,
Intend a zealous pilgrimage to thee,
And keep my drooping eyelids open wide,
While shadows mock my sore capacity.
I see thy form within the dark of night,
A phantom vision, beautifully clear,
And reach for thee with all my spirit's might,
But find, alas, that thou art nowhere near.
   Thus, by day's toil and night's deceit I'm vexed,
   For thee, by thee, I am for ever flexed.

###169###
Is it thy beauty that enslaves me so?
Or is't the wit that from thy mind doth flow?
Is it thy voice, so musical and low,
That makes my frozen heart to melt and glow?
It is not one of these, but all combined,
A perfect union of the good and fair,
A noble spirit and a lovely mind,
A prize so rich it is beyond compare.
The fairest flower may have a scentless head,
The sweetest songbird be of dullest plume,
The richest cloth conceal a rotten thread,
And handsome tombs may hold a vacant room.
   But thou art truth, in whom all virtues meet,
   A flawless whole, in every part complete.

###170###
Look in thy glass, and tell the face thou viewest
Now is the time that face should form another;
Whose fresh repair if now thou not renewest,
Thou dost beguile the world, unbless some mother.
For where is she so fair whose uneared womb
Disdains the tillage of thy husbandry?
Or who is he so foolish as to tomb
Himself in death, and leave no legacy?
Thou art thy mother's glass, and she in thee
Calls back the lovely April of her prime:
So thou through windows of thine age shalt see,
Despite of wrinkles, this thy golden time.
   But if thou live, remembered not to be,
   Die single, and thine image dies with thee.

###1-71###
Let me not to the marriage of true minds
Admit impediments. Love is not love
Which alters when it alteration finds,
Or bends with the remover to remove:
O, no! it is an ever-fixèd mark,
That looks on tempests and is never shaken;
It is the star to every wandering bark,
Whose worth's unknown, although his height be taken.
Love's not Time's fool, though rosy lips and cheeks
Within his bending sickle's compass come;
Love alters not with his brief hours and weeks,
But bears it out even to the edge of doom.
   If this be error and upon me proved,
   I never writ, nor no man ever loved.

###172###
My love is as a fever, longing still
For that which longer nurseth the disease;
Feeding on that which doth preserve the ill,
The uncertain-sickly appetite to please.
My reason, the physician to my love,
Angry that his prescriptions are not kept,
Hath left me, and I desperate now approve
Desire is death, which physic did except.
Past cure I am, now reason is past care,
And frantic-mad with evermore unrest;
My thoughts and my discourse as madmen's are,
At random from the truth vainly expressed.
   For I have sworn thee fair and thought thee bright,
   Who art as black as hell, as dark as night.

###173###
Shall I compare thee to a winter's night,
When frosty air doth bite and winds do blow?
Thou art more constant and more truly bright,
Than any moon that shines on fallen snow.
Rough winds do shake the branches of the trees,
And winter's term is all too long and grey;
And often is the sun obscured with ease,
And cannot show his face for half the day.
But thy eternal virtue shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that grace thou ow'st,
Nor shall Despair e'er say thou art his shade,
When in these hopeful lines to time thou grow'st.
   So long as men can breathe, or eyes can see,
   So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###174###
Against that time, if ever that time come,
When I shall see thee frown on my defects,
When as thy love hath cast his utmost sum,
Called to that audit by advised respects;
Against that time when thou shalt strangely pass,
And scarcely greet me with that sun, thine eye,
When love, converted from the thing it was,
Shall reasons find for its own swift goodbye;
Against that time do I insconce me here
Within the knowledge of my own desert,
And this my hand against myself uprear,
To guard the lawful reasons on thy part:
   To leave poor me, thou hast the strength of laws,
   Since why to love, I can allege no cause.

###175###
Thou art the music to which my soul doth dance,
The silent melody that fills my brain;
A single note in thy sweet, passing glance
Can soothe my sorrow and can quell my pain.
But when thou'rt absent, all is out of tune,
A jarring discord grates within my breast,
The sun seems cold, a mockery the moon,
And in the world I can find no true rest.
For what is birdsong to a deafened ear?
Or what are colours to a man born blind?
Without thy presence, nothing can be dear,
And no contentment can my spirit find.
   So be the harmony that makes me whole,
   The perfect song that is my very soul.

###176###
Some glory in their birth, some in their skill,
Some in their wealth, some in their body's force,
Some in their garments, though new-fangled ill,
Some in their hawks and hounds, some in their horse;
And every humour hath his adjunct pleasure,
Wherein it finds a joy above the rest:
But these particulars are not my measure;
All these I better in one general best.
Thy love is richer than high birth to me,
Sweeter than wealth, prouder than garments' cost,
Of more delight than hawks or horses be;
And having thee, of all men's pride I boast:
   Wretched in this, that thou mayst take away,
   And make me poor on any given day.

###177###
The little moth, that circles round the flame,
Knows not the danger of his brief desire;
He sees the light, but cannot know the shame
Of being conquered by the burning fire.
So I, bewitched by thy most radiant eye,
Do flutter near the beauty I behold,
And hear not reason when it warns me fly
To safety's dark, and sanctuary's cold.
My foolish heart, a willing sacrifice,
Invites the wound that it should rightly dread,
And for a moment in thy warm paradise,
Would gladly number with the scorched and dead.
   For such a light is worth the price of pain,
   To burn in bliss, and burning, live again.

###178###
How can I then return in happy plight,
That am debarred the benefit of rest?
When day's oppression is not eased by night,
But night by night and day by day oppressed?
And each, though enemies to either's reign,
Do in consent shake hands to torture me;
The one by toil, the other to complain
How far I toil, still farther off from thee.
I tell the day, to please him thou art bright,
And dost him grace when clouds do blot the heaven:
So flatter I the swart-complexioned night,
When sparkling stars twire not though art my even.
   But day doth daily draw my sorrows longer,
   And night doth nightly make grief's strength seem stronger.

###179###
The world's a stage, and we are merely players,
Each with his entrance and his exit bound,
And in our time we suffer many cares,
And with our lines a hollow stage resound.
But thou art not an actor in this fray,
Who puts a mask on for a passing scene;
Thou art the truth at the conclusion of the play,
The thing itself which all the shadows mean.
The rest of us are false, and speak our lines
By rote, without true knowledge of the art;
But in thy face, a genuine spirit shines,
And every word comes truly from the heart.
   So let them play their parts and fret and bow,
   My only audience and my truth art thou.

###180###
O, learn to read what silent love hath writ:
To hear with eyes belongs to love's fine wit.
My bookish mind, which once took idle joy
In tales of heroes and the fall of Troy,
Now finds its study only in thy face,
Where every line a sweeter story tells,
A living poem of such perfect grace,
As in no ancient, dusty volume dwells.
Thy brow's the title page, thine eyes the text,
Wherein love's deepest philosophies I find;
No more by fabled histories perplexed,
Thy living truth illuminates my mind.
   So let me be thy scholar, and be taught
   The only lesson that my soul hath sought.

###61###
When thou dost speak, a music fills the air,
More sweet than lark that greets the morning light,
And casts a spell to banish all my care,
And puts the shadows of the world to flight.
The lutanist who plies his skillful art
Doth make a sound that cannot long enthrall
The captive senses of my listening heart,
For at thy voice, the proudest notes must fall.
Thy gentle words can soothe a savage breast,
Or raise a soul from depths of bleak despair;
In their soft cadence I do find my rest,
A balm for grief, a treasure beyond compare.
So let me hear that sound, and I shall be
Content to live, in listening but to thee.

###62###
Mine eyes forswear the heavens and the night,
And seek no counsel from the stars above,
For in thine eyes they find a truer light,
The twin-born constellations of my love.
Let astrologers their tedious charts unfold,
And in the spheres a doubtful fortune see;
A richer prophecy I may behold
When thy dear gaze doth turn its light on me.
Were those two suns to take the place of Mars,
The sailor, lost, would find his homeward way,
Forgetting all the old and lesser stars,
And steer his course by their more certain ray.
So am I lost when thou dost look aside;
Thine eyes alone my wandering soul must guide.

###63###
I see how Time with his relentless hand
Doth steal the bloom from summer's fairest rose,
And turns to dust the glories of the land,
As his swift, silent, cruel procession goes.
And so I fear the winter he will bring
To ravage the sweet garden of thy face,
When all the virtues that the poets sing
Are vanquished by his cold and grim embrace.
Yet in the black ink of this humble verse,
A timeless monument I strive to build,
That future ages may thy praise rehearse,
When Time's own hungry appetite is filled.
For here thy beauty shall forever dwell,
A living shield against his mortal spell.

###64###
Each weary step I take when thou art gone
Doth measure out a world of lesser worth,
For all the joy my spirit fed upon
Hath fled with thee and vanished from the earth.
The sun may shine, yet darkness clouds my eye,
The birds may sing, yet I their songs detest;
For in thy absence, nature seems to lie,
And in my soul, I find no peaceful rest.
I count the hours as some poor captive might,
Who in his cell awaits the turning key,
And longs for but a glimpse of blessed light,
Which liberty, and thy sweet face, would be.
For thou art all the world's delight to me,
And where thou art not, I can never be.

###65###
What tyrant taught thee such a cruel state,
To look on sorrow with a pleased disdain,
To make my constant love thy constant hate,
And build thy triumph on my growing pain?
My humble pleas, which for thy mercy call,
Are met with scorn and looks of cold device,
As if the tears that from my conscience fall
Were but the melting of some worthless ice.
So let thy heart, a fortress made of stone,
Resist the siege of my affection's plea;
One day thou'lt stand, imperious and alone,
And crave a love that will not come to thee.
Then shalt thou know the hell thou didst impart,
And feel the wound of a self-broken heart.

###66###
How can a visage of such gentle grace
Conceal a heart so pitiless and cold?
How can such beauty in a heavenly face
A purpose of such cruelty enfold?
It is a paradox that tears my mind,
A bait of sweetness on a bitter hook;
The more I look, the more I am made blind,
Betrayed and captured by a single look.
As summer's flower may hide a stinging bee,
Or fairest streams may run to bitter seas,
So is my love deceived and warned by thee,
Who offers poison with a show of ease.
And still, though knowing well the deadly art,
I cannot teach this lesson to my heart.

###67###
Tired with all these, for restful death I cry:
To see true merit by the false surpassed,
To see bright honour in the gutter lie,
And fickle judgment on the worthy cast;
To see plain truth dismissed as foolishness,
And gilded nonsense take the speaker's chair,
To see the strong맨 obey the rule of less,
And art made tongue-tied by a vacant stare.
Tired with all these, from these I would be gone,
Save that to die, I leave my love alone,
Who in this wicked world must carry on,
And make my absence her new cause to moan.
Ah, sweet my love, for thee I'll bear this strife,
And make thy presence my sole joy in life.

###68###
When drowsy sleep doth seal my worldly eyes,
And frees my soul to wander where it may,
Thy perfect form before my thoughts doth rise,
And turns the darkest midnight into day.
Thy shade appears, more radiant and more clear
Than when the sun affords thee to my sight,
Dispelling all the shadows of my fear,
A blessed phantom in the lonely night.
But when the dawn, that rude and churlish guest,
Doth chase the treasured vision from my head,
I wake to find my woe-filled heart oppressed,
And all the joy that filled my slumber, fled.
So must I praise the night, and curse the light,
That gives me thee, then steals thee from my sight.

###69###
My traitor eye doth sometimes find a flaw,
A fleeting frown, a word not wholly kind,
And by the light of reason's sober law,
A lesser grace within thy form I find.
My heart, enraged, cries out that this is lies,
And calls my eye a fool that cannot see
The constant sun that in your heaven lies,
But marks a cloud that passes fleetingly.
This civil war 'twixt heart and eye doth rage,
One swearing truth, the other beauty's part;
The eye would have me turn a wiser page,
The other bids me listen to my heart.
But in this war, the outcome is foreshown:
My heart will win, and claim thee for its own.

###70###
Let others praise the beauty of thy face,
The perfect arch of brow, the ruby lip,
And find within thy form a stately grace
No sculptor's hand could ever hope to strip.
But I, who look beyond the outward show,
Do see a treasure richer and more deep:
A constant mind where fairest virtues grow,
And in whose soil no seeds of malice sleep.
For fleeting Time, who makes the fairest foul,
And carves his lines in every lovely thing,
Hath not the power to touch thy noble soul,
Nor from thy mind its gentle wisdom wring.
So love I not what all the world can see,
But that immortal part which conquers thee.

###71###
My halting verse cannot contain thy worth,
Nor can my pen, with its poor, stumbling pace,
Describe the sum of heaven here on earth
That I behold when looking on thy face.
Should I compare thee to the budding rose?
Thou art more constant, and thy scent more true.
Should I extol the winter's gentle snows?
They are less pure, less perfect than are you.
And so I fail, in metaphor and rhyme,
To build a monument that's fit for thee,
To capture grace that stands outside of time,
For all the listening, future world to see.
And all my words are but a feeble sign
Of light too great for any art of mine.

###72###
When winter's breath encrusts the world with frost,
And barren branches shiver in the cold,
I think of all the vibrant green that's lost,
And how the year, like mortal man, grows old.
The cheerful birdsong is no longer heard,
The busy streams are captured in a trance,
And nature speaks a melancholy word
Of beauty's brief and sorry circumstance.
In thy sweet youth I see a summer's day,
A warmth that holds the coming chill at bay,
And in my mind I passionately pray
That thy fair season will not fade away.
But since it must, let this my verse remain,
To bring thy summer back to life again.

###73###
To me, my love, you are the world's one state,
The only kingdom where I wish to dwell,
And in your eyes I see my future fate,
My only heaven or my only hell.
Your smile is law, your frown a harsh decree
That banishes my soul to wretched lands;
Your favour is the only liberty
My heart desires or my mind understands.
Let other men for paltry honours strive,
Or seek for gold with ever-greedy hands;
The richest prize for which I'd be alive
Is but the love that your dear heart commands.
For in the compass of your soul I find
The only empire that can rule my mind.

###74###
That which you gave, you cannot take away,
Though you may scorn me now and turn aside;
For on my heart, it is impressed for aye,
The sacred image that does there abide.
It is a portrait that no hand did paint,
A likeness fashioned out of memory,
Which time cannot erase, nor sorrow taint,
A testament of what you were to me.
And though you change, and prove yourself unkind,
And be no more the creature I adored,
The first perfection of you in my mind
Shall in my verse forever be restored.
So you shall live, as I first found you true,
And my heart's love will make you ever new.

###75###
Why do my thoughts, like ever-circling doves,
Return to thee, no matter where they roam?
Why, amongst all the world's abundant loves,
Does my heart know thy presence as its home?
I send my thoughts to sail on other seas,
To seek new lands and find a different shore,
But they are turned by some unlooked-for breeze
That blows them back to where they were before.
They are but vassals to thy sovereign will,
And have no power but to think on thee;
Though I command them, they are rebels still,
And fly to serve thee, paying no heed to me.
Thus am I captive, and my thoughts my chain,
Bound by thy power in a sweet, glad pain.

###76###
I am ashamed of this my barren rhyme,
That cannot with more rich invention sing,
And but repeats, time after weary time,
The same poor praise for such a worthy thing.
My love is new, yet my old words are spent,
Forever telling of thy truth and grace,
As if my mind, on this one subject bent,
Could find no other theme, no other face.
Yet, as the sun is daily new and old,
And blesses us with one unchanging light,
So doth my tongue a single story hold:
Thy beauty makes the darkest corners bright.
Forgive my verse, that it no better be;
I cannot change my theme, for it is thee.

###77###
What is this life, if it be not with thee?
A tedious play upon a barren stage,
A ship that sails upon a shoreless sea,
A gilded prison or a barren cage.
The sweetest food to bitter ash doth turn,
The softest music grates upon mine ear;
For thee alone my senses truly yearn,
And find all things are dull when thou'rt not near.
Each day a burden, and each night a grief,
A weary torment that I must endure,
And from my sorrow I can find no leaf
Of healing herb, no hope of easy cure.
Save this one thought, which mitigates my dread:
That I may dream of you when I am dead.

###78###
My love is not so blind that it can't see
The little faults that others may decry;
I know thou art not from all error free,
And that some virtues in thee sleeping lie.
I see the flash of an impatient mood,
A stubborn will that will not quickly bend,
A mind that can be easily misunderstood,
A haste to judge a foe, or doubt a friend.
But these small specks, which others look upon
As blemishes that mar a perfect face,
Are, to my fond and partial eye, the dawn
Of some new, undiscovered, human grace.
For in thy faults, a truer self I view,
And for thy flaws, I find my love more true.

###79###
This world's a mask of painted, false delight,
Where every smile a hidden dagger keeps,
And fairest days can turn to darkest night,
While cunning Fortune in her cradle sleeps.
I see the proud man praised for his deceit,
The humble man ignored for being plain;
I see the wise man begging in the street,
While fools are showered with abundant gain.
And seeing this, my heart doth grow so cold,
So weary of the cheap and hollow show,
I would not stay to watch the scene unfold,
But for the single, honest truth I know.
That thou art good, and in thy soul doth shine
A light that makes this darkened world divine.

###80###
So let this be the record of my love,
A final will and testament to thee,
That future eyes my constancy may prove,
When I am dust for all the world to see.
Let no grand stone be raised above my head,
Nor gilded words my humble life commend;
Let it be said, when all of me is dead,
"His verse was true, his love was till the end."
For in these lines, my spirit shall survive,
A faithful witness to thy beauty's might,
And keep the memory of thy grace alive,
A burning candle in the endless night.
Thus, when my body to the earth is given,
My love for thee shall find its own new heaven.

###41###
When I behold that face of wondrous light,
Whose youthful blush by cruel time is made
To lose the lustre that was once so bright,
And in the glass of age is seen to fade;
Then do I think upon the summer's rose,
Whose tender petals must give way to strife,
As winter's unforgiving nature shows
The fragile hold that beauty hath on life.
Yet in the prison of this sable ink,
I make to thee a most eternal vow,
That on thy grace my constant soul shall think,
And keep thee evermore as fair as now.
So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###42###
Thy pretty words are poison to my heart,
Which, though it breaks, must love thee all the same;
My foolish mind plays a tormented part,
And in its sorrow whispers thy dear name.
Thou art the sun that blinds my weeping eyes,
That makes a shadow where my hope would stand;
My every prayer is met with thy disguise,
A gentle look from an ungentle hand.
What magic dost thou use to keep me weak?
What subtle potion in thy voice's fire?
My will is gone, I have no strength to speak,
And am consumed by my own vain desire.
Thus in thy thrall I must forever be,
For 'tis my love that gives thee power o'er me.

###43###
O lonely moon, pale watcher of my grief,
Why dost thou gaze upon my wretched state?
Thou see'st my pain, but bringest no relief,
And only mirror'st my unhappy fate.
Thy silver face, a stranger to the sun,
Hath looked upon a thousand lovers' tears,
And know'st my sorrow is not the first one
To haunt the darkness with a host of fears.
As thou art bound to follow through the night
The distant sun that gives thee all thy light,
So am I bound, a slave to beauty's might,
And find my day in what's beyond my sight.
So watch me, moon, for thou dost understand
The heavy heart that love hath in its hand.

###44###
My mind a stage is where you daily play,
And all my thoughts the audience to the scene;
A thousand times you enter in a day,
A thousand times the king, or else the queen.
One moment you are cruel and full of scorn,
And all my thoughts do sorrow and despair;
The next, you are as bright as summer's morn,
And banish thence all trace of worldly care.
And I, the playwright, have no power to change
The script that you yourself alone devise;
Your wit can make the common seem most strange,
And truth appear from out a web of lies.
So act thy part, and I will watch, content
To be the world o'er which thou'rt president.

###45###
That jealous fiend which plagues the lover's thought
Hath built its nest within my weary soul,
A monstrous shadow from a trifle wrought,
Which reason's light can nothing now control.
It feeds on whispers, glances cast in haste,
And turns a kindness to a wicked sign;
It lays the garden of my spirit waste,
And makes me doubt that thou art truly mine.
This green-ey'd monster, with its bitter sting,
Makes bitter all that once to me was sweet;
And to my heart no comfort can it bring,
But only prospects of my own defeat.
For love that's poisoned by a jealous mind
Hath its own death within itself defined.

###46###
I have seen rubies, and the purest gold,
And diamonds that mock the starry sphere;
A thousand tales of treasure have been told,
Of pearls and gems that princes hold most dear.
But when I think upon thy virtue's light,
All worldly riches seem a paltry thing;
Thy grace is fairer and thy soul more bright
Than all the jewels of a waiting king.
For gems are cold, and gold can be but dust,
And empires fall beneath the sweep of time;
But thy true worth, wherein I place my trust,
Is of a substance altogether more sublime.
So let them hoard their baubles, I'll not miss
A kingdom, having such a wealth as this.

###47###
Thy face a book of beauty doth appear,
Wherein are written words of gentle grace;
But in thy heart, a different tale, I fear,
Hath found a dark and secret hiding place.
Thy tongue can speak with such a silver sound,
As might persuade the angels to descend;
Yet in its speech, no true affection's found,
But a false promise from a falser friend.
I see a summer heaven in thine eye,
But feel the winter's chill within thy soul;
Thy smile a sun that shines upon a lie,
A perfect part that plays a wicked role.
And I, a fool, was taught too late to find
That fairest flowers oft have foulest mind.

###48###
When leagues of land between us two are set,
And cruel daylight keeps thee from my sight,
My mind, a painter, will not let me yet
Be wholly parted from my heart's delight.
For in the workshop of my memory,
Thy perfect form is rendered fresh and new,
A timeless portrait there for me to see,
In every feature, every colour, true.
My thoughts become the frame in which 'tis held,
My love the light by which its grace is shown,
And by this artistry I am compelled
To feel thee near, though I be all alone.
Thus distance is a tyrant overthrown,
When what the eye has lost, the mind can own.

###49###
I call this love a prison for my heart,
For in thy presence I no longer feel
That I am free to choose a different part,
Or from thy power my devotion steal.
And yet this prison is a paradise,
These chains are fashioned from the purest gold;
This bondage is a gift of wondrous price,
A happy story waiting to be told.
For being captive to thy gentle hand,
I find a freedom I had never known;
Thy sweet command is my soul's own command,
And in thy will I discover my own.
So let me be thy prisoner for aye,
Lest I, if freed, should lose myself straightway.

###50###
My humble pen can never hope to write
The sum of all the beauty I can see;
The brightest words would be as dark as night
If set against the light that shines from thee.
I could compare thine eyes to stars above,
Thy voice to music, and thy lips to wine,
But all such praise would fall far short of love,
And fail to capture what is so divine.
For earthly things are but a shadow cast
By that true form of fairness in thy face;
All metaphors and likenesses are passed,
For none can match thine inimitable grace.
So I'll be mute, and let my silence prove
What speech cannot: the measure of my love.

###51###
Old Time, that thief which steals our youthful years,
Hath set his mark upon my weary brow;
He pays his wage in sorrows and in fears,
And what was vibrant, he makes feeble now.
He plucks the rose from out the fairest cheek,
And silvers o'er the hair of shining gold;
He makes the strongest arm become most weak,
And turns the fire of passion into cold.
Against this foe no worldly might can stand,
No wall can stop his silent, creeping pace;
He takes the gift from out the giver's hand,
And leaves but ruin in its former place.
Yet in my verse his power I defy,
Wherein thy youth shall live, and never die.

###52###
If music be the food of love, as said,
Thy voice alone is a sufficient feast;
On every word my hungry soul is fed,
And all my sorrows are for a time released.
When thou dost speak, the birdsong holds its breath,
As if ashamed of its own simpler art;
Thy gentle tone can rescue me from death,
And mend the pieces of my broken heart.
Let others praise the lute or viol's sound,
Or anthems sung in some cathedral high;
A sweeter music in thy speech is found,
Beneath the arch of any common sky.
And when thou'rt silent, all the world seems wrong,
Awaiting but the comfort of thy song.

###53###
I go to sleep to find some small relief
From all the waking torments of the day;
But slumber is a traitor and a thief,
And steals my only moments of calm away.
For in my dreams, thy powerful image reigns,
And holds a court within my resting mind;
It reenacts my pleasures and my pains,
And leaves my weary spirit more confined.
Sometimes I dream thy kindness makes me bold,
And then I wake to find it is not so;
Sometimes thy cruelty, a story told,
Renews a grief that I had sought to let go.
So be it night or day, I find no peace,
For love of thee doth grant my heart no lease.

###54###
Though all the world may praise your perfect face,
And call you fair beyond all mortal measure,
I find a lack of some essential grace
If in your soul there lies no hidden treasure.
For beauty is a flower, quickly dead,
Whose painted petals wither and decay,
A fleeting show, a book that is unread,
If inner virtue does not light the way.
A lovely form may catch the roving eye,
But a kind heart is what can make it stay;
For outward fairness may contain a lie,
And like a shadow, fade and slip away.
So I prize most the beauty of the mind,
Which time cannot corrupt, nor fortune blind.

###55###
This world's a volume, writ by God's own hand,
Where every soul's a page, for good or ill;
And I, a reader, search throughout the land
For some great chapter that my heart can fill.
I've read the tales of heroes and of kings,
And seen the poetry of sun and star,
But all their glory, all that nature brings,
Cannot compare with what I know you are.
For in the book of your most gentle heart,
A tale of truth and beauty is engraved;
You are the masterpiece, the finest part,
The central text for which my spirit craved.
And in that story, I desire to be
A footnote, there for all the world to see.

###56###
My mirror shows a stranger to my youth,
A man whose brow is lined with doubts and fears,
And in his eyes, I see the solemn truth
Of all the damage done by passing years.
The spring is gone that once was in my stride,
The fire is dimmed that once did burn so bright;
My former confidence has turned to hide
From the cold scrutiny of morning's light.
This slow decay, this thief of what I was,
Doth make me question what I have to give;
Am I a ruin, fit only for the daws,
In whose sad shadow no new love can live?
And yet my verse, a braver man than I,
Against the march of aging dares to fly.

###57###
Thine eyes are windows to a world inside,
Through which I gaze to know what's in thy heart;
All of thy thoughts in them I see reside,
And learn in truth what thou dost feel in art.
They cannot lie, though false thy tongue may be,
For joy will make them like the stars to shine,
And sorrow clouds them for the world to see,
Revealing thus your soul's most true design.
When love for me within them makes its home,
A sacred light illuminates thy face;
But when to other thoughts they start to roam,
I see the shadow of an empty space.
So look on me, and let my spirit read
The silent promise of thy loving deed.

###58###
Thou art my star, which in the dark of night,
Doth shine upon me from a world away;
So distant is thy pure and constant light,
It cannot warm me with a single ray.
And yet I steer my ship by thy design,
Across the troubled ocean of my days;
Thy steady presence is a holy sign
That guides my spirit through confusing ways.
Though I may never reach thy lofty sphere,
Nor touch the fire that I so admire,
It is enough to know that thou art near,
To fuel the flame of my devout desire.
So shine, my star, and I shall not be lost,
Whatever pain my lonely voyage cost.

###59###
If thou must be so cruel, then be it so,
But grant me this one mercy in thy pride:
Do not with scorn upon my sorrow throw
A further weight for my poor heart to hide.
Thy cold disdain is punishment enough
For the great crime of loving thee too well;
My path is thorny and my journey rough,
My life a story that is sad to tell.
One gentle word, one look that is not sharp,
Would be a balm unto my wounded soul;
It would be music from an angel's harp,
To make my broken spirit feel half-whole.
But if you cannot grant this simple plea,
Then kill me with your eyes, and set me free.

###60###
So ends my song, my pen has run its course,
My weary muse now begs a final rest;
I have no more from sorrow to divorce,
Nor any passion left within my breast.
This fever, which so long has ruled my brain,
And made my hand to tremble as I write,
At last gives way to reason once again,
And leads me from the darkness to the light.
And yet, these lines, a monument to thee,
Shall stand when all my feeling has grown cold,
A testament for other eyes to see
A love more precious than a mine of gold.
For though my passion dies, my verse remains,
And in this ink, thy deathless beauty reigns.

###121###
When first I looked upon that wondrous sight,
Thy beauty did my very soul embrace;
And from thine eyes there shone a holy light,
Which left no room for any other face.
What sweet enchantments doth thy presence hold,
What silent sorcery thy glances art,
Whose pow'r by mortal tongue cannot be told,
And binds in chains my once-rebellious heart.
So let the world its lesser charms defy,
And kings their empty pageantry command;
I find a richer kingdom in thine eye,
And all my fortune in thy gentle hand.
So this I swear, and know my oath is true,
My world begins and finds its end in you.

###122###
When I behold that beauty on thy brow,
More precious than a miser's hidden gold,
I think how Time, that makes all creatures bow,
Doth plot to make this perfect image old.
His hurried minutes, on their thieving feet,
Will grant thy youth but a momentary stay,
And steal the bloom that makes the rose so sweet,
To bear its fragile elegance away.
Yet in my verse his cruel hand I'll fight,
And hold thy fairness from his hungry power;
Thy lasting essence shall these lines make bright,
When thou art withered in some future hour.
For what Time loses, so my pen divines,
Shall live for ever where thy beauty shines.

###123###
Since from my hopeful sight thou first art gone,

###21###
When sorrows map the country of thy face,
And in thy crystal eyes a grief I see,
I long to banish them with loving grace,
And mock the fates and their unkind decree.
For every worldly woe that plays a part
To make the lines upon thy forehead lie,
Doth wound with equal measure my own heart
And draw a tear of salt from out mine eye.
Yet if thy troubles must by thee be shown,
Let them not fall as some unholy pain,
But as a secret to thy lover known,
A gentle and a cleansing summer rain.
So let me in the book of thy soul read,
And answer with my love thy truest need.

###22###
That thievish Time with ever-grasping hand
Doth steal the lustre from thy youth's pure gold,
And makes the days like quickly falling sand,
A tragic tale by every mirror told.
It carves new furrows in thy perfect brow
And plants the silver in what once was fair,
To make thee but a memory of now,
And steals the sun from out thy glorious hair.
But let that villain do what he may take,
His cruel conquest cannot long hold stay;
For in my mind a monument I make
That shall outlive his most destructive day.
So age not, love, within these faithful lines,
Where thy eternal summer ever shines.

###23###
Whilst all the world doth in soft slumber keep,
A thousand phantoms in my mind arise,
And from mine own tired lids do banish sleep
To paint thy image for my waking eyes.
No peace doth find my agitated breast,
Which, starved of thee, on weary thought is fed;
My body begs for but an hour's rest,
And finds a battle-ground within my bed.
For in the darkness I must see thee bright,
A vision clearer than my sight may own,
A star that gives the blackest heavens light,
A beauty to the waking world unknown.
Thus night to day is turned when thou art near,
And day is night when thou art not held dear.

###24###
My clumsy verse can never speak so well
Of all the virtues that comprise thy heart;
It is a faulty and a cracked old bell
That cannot sound the whole of what thou art.
It strives to capture thy most perfect grace,
To paint in words a likeness that is true;
But finds a shadow where it sought a face,
And dullest grey for heaven's brightest hue.
And when my pen attempts to write thy name,
The very ink, it seems, itself grows dim,
As if it knows its own and my great shame
To sketch a seraph with a mortal limb.
For how can blackest ink presume to write
Of that which is create of purest light?

###25###
The world's a stage, as all the wise men say,
Wherein we strut and speak our given role,
And are but players for a fleeting day
Who bare the outward but conceal the soul.
I watch the lords and ladies act their part,
With painted smiles and courtesies so grand,
But all their passion is a memorized art,
A gesture learned and not a feeling scanned.
But when thou speak'st, no studied line I hear,
Thy every sigh is from a truer place,
The love that shines within thine eyes is clear,
And honesty is writ upon thy face.
So let them have their stage, their fame, their show,
My world's thy heart, the only truth I know.

###26###
If my poor love were but an idle thing,
A fancy born of some light summer's day,
Then with the winter it would soon take wing,
And in the bitter frost would fade away.
If it were built upon thy form so fair,
Then cruel Time, that makes all beauty strange,
Would turn my worship into cold despair,
And with thy fortune would my feelings change.
But no, my love is of a substance made
That winter's chill and Time's assault defies;
It is a sun that cannot sink to shade,
A constant star in ever-fixed skies.
So doubt me not when age thy youth hath crossed,
For what I love can never be unloved or lost.

###27###
My eye, a traitor to my mind's command,
Did first conspire to take thy beauty in,
And led it captive through this wretched land
To where my heart's true sorrows now begin.
For in that fortress it is now confined,
A willing prisoner in a gentle jail,
And rules the thoughts and workings of my mind,
Making my reason tremble and grow pale.
That first small glance, so innocent a thing,
Hath grown to be a king of all my days,
And from that look a thousand tyrants spring
That govern me in all my works and ways.
So am I conquered by a single sight,
And lose my freedom in my own delight.

###28###
I envy oft the glove upon thy hand,
The very air that doth thy beauty bless;
The humble ground on which thy feet do stand,
The silken fabric of thy chosen dress.
I envy too the cup that meets thy lips,
The sun that warms the fairness of thy cheek;
The roving wind that in thy tresses trips,
And hears the secrets that thou dost not speak.
For these dull things, that have no sense to know
The holy honour which is them assigned,
Are granted grace that I may not be showed,
And leave my jealous, wanting self behind.
Yet this thought mends my sorrow and my strife:
These things but touch thy form; I love thy life.

###29###
Dost thou not think it is a cruel deceit
To wear the visage of an honest soul,
When in thy heart all treasons do compete,
And falsehood over every promise stole?
Thy words are smoother than the smoothest oil,
Thy oaths as sacred as a holy book,
Yet in their sweetness lies a deadly coil,
A poisoned bait upon a silver hook.
I did believe that what was fair must be
As virtuous and true as it appeared,
But thy sweet face hath made a fool of me,
And taught me that my trusting heart hath erred.
Thus have I learned from thy most lovely art,
A perfect face may hide a broken heart.

###30###
When in the court of mine own silent thought,
I hold a session for the judging soul,
I find my worth is but a thing of naught,
A heavy debt upon a beggar's scroll.
My wit is poor, my station is but base,
My comeliness is nothing to commend;
I bear the marks of failure on my face,
A man whom fortune never called her friend.
But when despair doth almost win the field,
And shame would have my spirit fall and die,
The sudden thought of thee becomes my shield,
And all these hosts of inner demons fly.
For being loved by thee is such a state,
I would not trade it for a monarch's fate.

###31###
Why art thou silent for so long a space,
And send no word to ease my hungry ear?
Doth some new joy now hold thee in its place,
Or hast thou found another to hold dear?
This quiet is a more tormenting sound
Than angry shouts or sharpest words could be;
Upon this rack of stillness I am bound,
Awaiting some small sign of life from thee.
The postman passes, and my hopes do fall;
The sun doth set, and brings no news of grace;
My anxious heart doth make a mock of all,
And sees thy malice in this empty place.
Oh speak, my love, though thy words bring me pain,
For silence murders hope and keeps it slain.

###32###
Perchance when I am dust and thou dost live,
Thou'lt find this book of my poor, simple rhymes,
And in thy judgment, thou may'st half forgive
The many faults and errors of my times.
Here thou shalt read of jealousy and fear,
Of selfish thoughts and moments of disgrace,
And see my weaknesses inscribed so clear,
They bring a blush of shame upon my face.
But grant me this, when thou my sins dost see,
And count the sum of all my worthlessness,
That one small virtue may remembered be:
I loved thee more than my own words express.
So read my failings, but this truth retain:
My love was pure, though all the rest was stain.

###33###
Nature did strive when she created thee,
To make a pattern for all things divine,
A living testament for men to see
How grace and truth and beauty may combine.
The rose, abashed, doth hide its crimson head,
The morning sky doth seem a duller blue,
The lily's white a pallid thing and dead,
Compared with the fresh wonder of thy hue.
So all that once I held as fair and right
Now seems but practice for thy perfect form;
Mere shadows gathered to produce thy light,
A calm prelude before a glorious storm.
So blame me not if I see naught but you,
For all else is a copy, and less true.

###34###
A civil war doth rage within my breast
Between my reason and my loving heart,
And in this fight I can obtain no rest,
As each doth play a fierce and tyrant's part.
My reason tells me I am but a fool
To stake my peace upon a passing glance,
And bids my measured thoughts take up and rule
This wild and unpredictable romance.
But then my heart, with eloquence and fire,
Calls reason coward, cold, and slow to trust,
And says that life, without its one desire,
Is but a weary journey to the dust.
So stand I, torn between the two estates,
And know not which of them my soul awaits.

###35###
When I behold my face in faithful glass,
I see the work of Time's destructive quill,
Who writes the story of the years that pass
And bends my strength against my struggling will.
The brow that once was smooth is lined with care,
The eyes that shone now hold a dimmer cast;
Time's silver threads are woven in my hair,
And tells me that my summer cannot last.
And with this sight, a chilling fear takes hold,
That thy young heart, which now doth hold me dear,
May soon grow weary and thy love grow cold
When my life's autumn browns what now is sere.
So love me now, whilst I have youth to give,
Lest in my age, I have no love to live.

###36###
The world to me is like a stormy sea,
Where angry waves of fortune rise and fall,
And I, a sailor, am tossed helplessly,
Answering to the tempest's frantic call.
My ship is battered by the winds of spite,
My compass lost to envy's thickest fog,
And in the darkness of the endless night,
I cling for life to but a broken log.
But then the thought of thee, a distant shore,
Appears a harbour, safe and full of grace,
Where tempests cease and oceans cease to roar,
A sheltered and a peaceful landing place.
Thou art the star that guides me through the dark,
The single hope for my despairing barque.

###37###
A memory of thee from long ago,
When first my eyes upon thy beauty fell,
Doth haunt my present days and will not go,
But holds my captive heart within its spell.
Though I do see thee now, and find thee fair,
That first bright image will not be displaced;
It is a ghost that lingers in the air,
A perfect painting that cannot be erased.
Sometimes I think that ghost more real than thee,
A younger vision, untouched by despair,
And in my fondness for what used to be,
I do neglect the truth that is now there.
So am I cursed to love a moment past,
A fleeting glimpse too beautiful to last.

###38###
As barren men who have no child to bear
The honour of their name when they are gone,
I look upon my state with some despair,
To be forgot when my last day is done.
I have no son to keep my likeness true,
No daughter's grace my memory to save;
My worldly deeds are common and but few,
And will not live beyond my humble grave.
But these poor words, these children of my brain,
Conceived in love and born of lonely ink,
Shall be the heirs that after me remain,
And of their father may some scholar think.
So in this verse, my lineage may be,
That some small part of me shall not flee thee.

###39###
The cruel expense of all these weary miles
That lie between thy gentle soul and mine,
Doth mock my love and practise hateful wiles
To make my solitary spirit pine.
Each dawn I wake and find an empty space
Where thy warm presence ought by right to be,
And curse the journey and the tedious pace
That robs my hungry eyes of seeing thee.
The sun, the moon, the very stars above
Can look on both of us within one night,
And are not hindered in their path of love
By leagues that put my treasure from my sight.
So let these lines traverse where I cannot go,
And tell thy heart what it doth surely know.

###40###
If from my love no great reward should grow,
And all my service earns me no estate,
I ask but this, that thou wouldst pity show,
And not condemn me to a loveless fate.
I do not ask for jewels, nor for land,
Nor public honours for the world to see;
But for the simple touching of thy hand,
Or one kind word sent from thy heart to me.
For in the barren desert of my days,
A single drop of grace from thy sweet spring
Would be a feast, a cause for endless praise,
The greatest treasure that a king could bring.
So in my poverty, this much I plead:
Let thy compassion answer to my need.

###101###
When winter's breath doth glaze the barren bough,
And sullen clouds the sun's fair face obscure,
I think of thee, and make a silent vow,
Thy summer's grace shall in my heart endure.
The fairest rose must yield to time's decay,
The swiftest stream to frozen stillness yields,
And golden dawns must fade to sober gray,
As frost lays claim to autumn's faded fields.
But thy sweet aspect knows no season's wrong,
Nor can the wind steal thy most gentle light;
Thy beauty is the subject of my song,
That makes a constant morning of my night.
So let the world grow cold and skies grow dim,
Thou art the substance of my living hymn.

###102###
When I take up my pen to write thy praise,
A poverty of words doth vex my soul,
For how can ink describe thy sunlit days,
Or make a broken heart's affection whole?
I speak of stars, but thy two eyes shine more,
Of music sweet, but thy voice holds more art,
Of treasures found upon a distant shore,
But none so rich as is thy constant heart.
Yet let these words a lasting tribute be,
A fragile shield against oblivion's knife,
That future eyes thy wondrous form may see,
And grant thy shade a secondary life.
For though my skill is poor, my love is vast,
Within this verse, thy memory shall last.

###103###
Another dawn ascends the eastern sky,
And finds me here in this same lonely state,
To heave for thee a melancholy sigh,
And curse the hours that seal my bitter fate.
The birdsong mocks my soul's unquiet grief,
The sun's warm kiss upon the dew-kissed ground
Doth bring my heavy heart no sweet relief,
Wherein no trace of thy soft voice is found.
Each passing face, a torment to my sight,
For none is thine, which I so long to see;
The day becomes an echo of the night,
A world of people, yet a lack of thee.
So must I walk through days that have no sun,
Until our time of parting is outrun.

###104###
I see the world puts on a virtuous show,
And speaks of truth with a deceitful tongue,
While underneath, the seeds of malice grow,
And foulest deeds by highest praise are sung.
The greedy miser preaches charity,
The faithless judge doth speak of what is right,
The coward scorns with false severity,
And darkness sells itself as holy light.
This stage of fools, where every man doth play
A part assigned by fortune or by sin,
Doth wear my tired and weary soul away,
To see the rot that festers deep within.
Yet in this den of lies, I look to thee,
The only form of truth mine eyes can see.

###105###
What need have I of painter's skillful hand,
Or poet's verse that rings with borrowed grace?
When in thy face I see a fairer land,
And find all art perfected in that place.
The sculptor's marble, cold and without breath,
Can shape a form, but cannot make it live;
A pale reflection, triumphing o'er death,
Yet not the warmth thy living glances give.
Thine eyes are stars that teach the sky to shine,
Thy voice the tune that shames the poet's lyre;
No art can capture beauty so divine,
Or match the heat of thy sweet nature's fire.
So let them toil, their empty praise to learn;
From thy dear face, all beauty I shall learn.

###106###
The weary night her sable curtain draws,
To grant the world a respite from its care,
But to my soul she offers no such pause,
And plants a thousand worries thriving there.
For sleep, that balm to any troubled mind,
Doth flee my couch and mocks my tired request;
And in the dark a torment I do find,
With wakeful thoughts that give my heart no rest.
My fancy then doth paint thy form anew,
A sudden star within this endless black,
Thy virtues and thy graces I review,
And count the joys that I so sorely lack.
Yet e'en this pain a kind of sweetness brings,
To think of thee, from whom my sorrow springs.

###107###
Some say that in thy nature lies a pride,
A certain scorn that holds thy heart aloof,
A fault that thou art powerless to hide,
Which reason offers as a sober proof.
They see a shadow where I see the sun,
And call it coldness that I name as grace;
Their catalogue of charges, one by one,
Finds no such flaw when I behold thy face.
And if it be a fault, I count it dear,
This queenly air that common souls resent;
It holds thee from the vulgar world so clear,
A perfect flaw, for thy protection sent.
So let them talk and judge what they can't know,
Thy pride's the soil whereon my best thoughts grow.

###108###
That subtle thief, whose footstep makes no sound,
Doth steal the blush from beauty's tender cheek,
And casts the monarch's glory to the ground,
And makes the fortress of our bodies weak.
Old wrinkled Time, with his relentless pace,
Doth turn the fairest summer into frost;
He carves new lines upon the smoothest face,
And counts the treasures that our lives have lost.
But 'gainst thy memory he hath no might,
For in my mind thy youth shall stand so fast,
It shines, a constant and eternal light,
A sun-filled present, proof against the past.
Let him claim all, with his insatiate greed,
My love for thee shall still survive his deed.

###109###
When to the sessions of my silent thought
I summon up remembrance of thy grace,
All other joys and sorrows come to nought
Before the memory of thy sweet face.
The wrongs I suffered, and the tears I shed,
The winter seasons of a lonely heart,
The heavy words that bitter fortune said,
All seem to play a faint and distant part.
For thy dear image, a most blessed thing,
Doth banish all that made my soul despair,
A balm of peace thy very name can bring,
And cast out shadows that were nesting there.
Thus, rich in thee, I let the world go by,
And find my kingdom in thy memory.

###110###
To see thee weep is to see heavens rain,
Not with the fury of a winter squall,
But with a soft and purifying pain,
That makes the thirsty flowers grow more tall.
Each tear a pearl that doth thy cheek adorn,
A liquid gem of sorrow, pure and deep,
A sign that even beauty can be torn,
And that the gentlest heart hath cause to weep.
I would that I could be a silken cloth
To dry the evidence of that sweet grief,
And swear to thee a firm and sacred oath
My love would be thy sorrow's true relief.
Let not such precious drops for sadness fall,
Unless for me you mean to give them all.

###111###
My love is like a fever, hot and cold,
A burning hope, a chill and dark despair;
A story that can never be full told,
A heavy burden and a weightless air.
At times I soar upon an eagle's wing,
Convinced thy heart will answer to my own,
And in that fantasy, I am a king,
Who fears no threat to his beloved throne.
Then doubt, a traitor, whispers in my ear
That all my hopes are built on shifting sand,
And I am but a fool consumed by fear,
Who fails thy noble spirit to command.
So in this war of peace, and joy, and strife,
I live a hundred deaths, and but one life.

###112###
That green-eyed monster, which doth mock the meat
It feeds upon, hath made his nest in me,
And turns what was exquisitely sweet
To bitter gall and sharpest jealousy.
If I but see another praise thy name,
Or linger in the sunshine of thy gaze,
My heart is set upon by jealous flame,
And all my thoughts are lost in a dark maze.
It is a madness that my reason fights,
A tyrant that enslaves my better part,
That fills my days and haunts my sleepless nights,
And plants a dagger in my trusting heart.
Yet this my torment proves my love is true,
For I am nothing if not wanting you.

###113###
When first thy glance did intercept with mine,
A silent pact between our souls was made;
I saw a light I took to be a sign,
A sudden sun that banished every shade.
No word was spoken, not a single sound,
Yet in that quiet moment, worlds were said;
A truth was given and a reason found
To raise my hopeful heart from being dead.
The tongue can lie, and vows can be forsworn,
And written words may lose their power to bind,
But what is in a single moment born
Of two souls' meeting can no falsehood find.
So let them doubt who trust in what they hear;
My faith is in that look which holds thee dear.

###114###
My face, a mask, doth show a placid scene,
A calm reserve for all the world to see,
As if my heart were peaceful and serene,
From any torments of affection free.
My tongue speaks trifles, and my lips may smile,
And join the laughter of the passing crowd,
Appearing careless for a little while,
With no complaint or sorrow spoke aloud.
But none can see the tempest rage within,
The war of hope against a rising dread,
The secret penance for a silent sin,
The thousand things my heart has left unsaid.
This outward calm is but a careful lie
To hide the truth that for thy love I die.

###115###
The earth again her verdant cloak doth wear,
And newborn lambs do dot the greening hill;
A gentle breeze doth stir the warming air,
And wakes the dance of golden daffodil.
But springtime's joy doth bring no joy to me,
For all its beauty serves but to remind
Me of a fairer sight I cannot see,
A grace and softness I can no more find.
The sun may warm the soil and melt the frost,
And coax the buds to open to the light,
But cannot mend the happiness I've lost,
Or make my endless, lonely winter bright.
So let the seasons turn as they are meant,
Without thy presence, I am not content.

###116###
Sometimes I count my virtues, which are few,
And weigh them 'gainst the fortune I desire,
And wonder what great deed that I could do
To make me worthy of thy sacred fire.
My wit is plain, my station is not high,
My face no model for a sculptor's hand;
I have no lands that spread beneath the sky,
No worldly wealth that I can boast or brand.
And when I see the suitors at thy gate,
With golden words and gifts of greater price,
I fear that I am destined by my fate
To lose the hope of my soul's paradise.
Yet I have this, a love that's rich and true;
And in that love, I offer all to you.

###117###
I have no patience for the flattering friend,
Who shapes his tongue to please the listener's ear,
Whose protestations have a selfish end,
Whose praise is false, whose loyalty's unclear.
They are but merchants of a hollow phrase,
Who sell their breath for favour or for gold,
Who in your light will spend their sunniest days,
But in your darkness will grow distant, cold.
Give me the truth, though it may have a sting,
The honest counsel of a caring heart;
For in thy plainness, thou art more a king
Than those who play a false and flowery part.
Thy simple yea, thy unadorned nay,
Outweighs all flattery and sweet display.

###118###
Mine eye and heart are at a mortal war,
And each the other's argument denies;
Mine eye doth claim the image that it saw,
While my heart says in him the true worth lies.
My heart insists it knows thy inner grace,
A constant virtue that can never fade;
Mine eye, it answers, only knows thy face,
The perfect form that Nature's hand hath made.
And so this battle rages without end,
The outward beauty and the soul within,
On which of these my senses should depend,
Where does the substance of thy self begin?
But this dispute is foolish and is vain;
Both face and heart in my own heart remain.

###119###
What is a king with coffers full of gold,
Or merchants rich with treasures from the sea,
If in their lonely counting they grow old,
And have not known a love as I know thee?
This earthly wealth is but a passing game,
A heap of dust that time will sweep away;
A monarch's crown, a widely-honoured name,
Are but the fleeting players in a play.
But to be rich in having thy regard
Is to possess a mine of endless worth,
A prize against which all things else are marred,
A private heaven on this common earth.
So let them keep their silver and their land;
My greatest treasure is to hold thy hand.

###120###
Let not the fear of change assail thy mind,
Or let malicious rumour plant a doubt,
That my firm love could ever prove unkind,
Or that its constant flame could be put out.
Though other men may shift as doth the tide,
And follow fortune where she leads them on,
My steadfast heart shall in thy truth abide,
And love thee still when youth and bloom are gone.
For what I love is not a passing show,
A rosy cheek, a bright and sparkling eye,
But the true soul that I have come to know,
Whose virtue and whose grace can never die.
So trust my vow, which I do swear again,
My love shall be your shield against all pain.

###141###
When I upon thy wondrous visage gaze,
And see a world of beauty there compiled,
I lose my path within a sunlit maze,
By such a perfect map of heav'n beguiled.
Thy brow, a throne where noble thoughts do sit,
Thy lips, the gate whence courteous words proceed,
Thy cheeks, whereon the rose of virtue's writ,
Do plant in me a love-devoured creed.
Thine eyes, two stars that in the night do gleam,
And banish shadows that my soul oppress,
Do cast on me a soul-enchanting beam,
And bring my weary spirit sweet redress.
Yet all this outward grace that holds me thrall,
Is but a shadow of thy soul in all.

###142###
That thievish Time, with unrelenting hand,
Doth carve deep furrows on the smoothest face,
And steals the fairest treasures from the land,
To leave but ruin in their former place,
Makes my sad heart consider how thy bloom
Must yield to age's siege and winter's might,
And find at last a cold and silent tomb,
Wherein is banished all thy living light.
Yet in the ink that from my pen doth flow,
A timeless garden of thy youth shall be,
Where thy eternal summer still shall grow,
For future eyes in wonderment to see.
So let Time wreak his worst with cruel rage,
Thy beauty lives, immortal, on this page

###81###
When I reflect upon my barren state,
And count the blessings that have passed me by,
I deem myself abandoned by my fate,
And to the heavens send a woeful cry.
All that I have is but a paltry show,
A hollow triumph built on shifting sand,
And where my seeds of effort I did sow,
I find but weeds in a forgotten land.
But when I think of thee, my fortune turns,
And all my sorrow vanishes away;
A hopeful fire in my spirit burns,
That transforms dark December into May.
For having but one thought of thy dear face,
I would not trade with kings my lowly place.

###82###
How heavy is my soul's most sad estate,
To be so many weary miles away,
And count the hours that run so slow and late,
Which drag like winter through a summer's day.
My searching eyes are robbed of their true sight,
And breathe the heavy air of my despair,
For thou, who art my world's most glorious light,
Art absent from the common, sunlit air.
My weeping spirit gazes to the sky,
And feels a sharp and solitary pain,
To know the sun looks on thee from on high,
While only clouds do answer me with rain.
All that I am doth on this torment feed,
For in thy absence I am poor indeed.

###83###
Shall Time's relentless hand deface thy grace,
And in the earth thy faultless beauty lie?
Must wrinkles mar the map of that sweet face,
And all the praise it earned be forced to die?
Shall envious ages turn thy worth to dust,
And faith in fairness be a fool's false trust?
Shall prideful Death strike all this virtue down,
And steal from nature its most precious crown?
O, no! For in this consecrated verse,
Thy living virtues future times rehearse,
And in these lines, thy beauty shall shine bright,
A constant star against eternal night.
For this my verse a truer life shall give,
And in the minds of men thy beauty live.

###84###
When darkling curtains rob the world of light,
My weary limbs I give to wished-for rest,
But find no comfort in the lonely night,
For thoughts of thee do trouble my sad breast.
My mind, a stage where shadows pass me by,
Doth act and re-enact thy heavenly form,
And so my lids refuse to close the eye,
But keep their watch against a waking storm.
Why must thy image thus my slumber keep,
And banish peace that I so long to find?
To close my eyes is not to truly sleep,
When thou art ever present in my mind.
This truth the long and sleepless night doth prove:
To be without thy sight is not to love.

###85###
This barren season doth reflect my state,
Since thou, my summer, art from me removed;
The frozen earth and skies compassionate
The lonely sorrow of a heart unloved.
The naked trees against the biting wind
A woeful and a shivering aspect make,
And in their posture I my own self find,
A soul that for thy warm return doth ache.
The sun itself, so pale and wanting heat,
Gives forth a light that cannot pierce the grey;
The song of birds no longer sounds so sweet,
For all the world is winter with thee away.
So let thy spring return and melt this frost,
Or in this cold my very life is lost.

###86###
I thought thy love a star by which to steer,
A constant promise in a changing sky,
But find it is a wandering, faithless sphere
That with each turning of the world doth fly.
At first it burned with such a hopeful flame
It made all other, lesser lights seem poor,
But now I see the treason and the shame,
And find that I was foolish to adore.
So is it with the vows that once you swore,
Which like the wind have altered their design?
Was all the truth you showed me heretofore
A mask to hide a heart that was not mine?
Then let me learn this lesson from my pain:
To trust in shifting hearts is but in vain.

###87###
Thy beauty is a tyrant, and doth wield
A cruel power over my weak heart,
Which hath no weapon and which hath no shield
To fend against so sharp and swift a dart.
Thou art a conqueror who takes no prize
But the poor homage of a captive soul,
Who reads his sentence in thy judging eyes,
And yields his freedom to thy full control.
I do not ask for mercy from thy hand,
Nor for a pardon from this sweet despair;
I am content to serve in thy command,
And be a vassal to a queen so fair.
So let me live and die thy willing slave,
Since 'tis thy tyranny I truly crave.

###88###
Why dost thou use such cruelty on me,
