Whose only crime is that he loves thee well?
What pleasure canst thou find in what you see:
A faithful heart condemned to live in hell?
Thy cold disdain, a sharp and wintry frost,
Doth kill the buds of hope ere they can bloom,
And in this bitterness my soul is lost,
Consigned by thee to a most wretched doom.
If I have wronged thee, tell me of my fault,
That I may learn my error and repent,
But do not with thy silence thus assault
A spirit that thy beauty has unbent.
For if you speak no word, my case is thus:
You are unjust, and I am ruinous.

###89###
My heart, a ship, upon thy sea doth sail,
And takes thy love to be its guiding star,
But now it meets a most unwelcome gale,
And strays from port, uncertain where we are.
Thy fickle mood, a sudden, rising storm,
Doth drive my vessel onto rocks of doubt,
Where cold despair assumes a dreadful form,
And all my hopeful maps are rendered nought.
What wind has turned against me in thy breast?
What newfound current pulls thy love from me?
And where shall my poor, battered heart find rest,
If it is banished from thy calmer sea?
Then be my harbour, bid the tempest cease,
And guide my heart to thy safe shore of peace.

###90###
Let me not call it love, which alters so,
Or bends its course when fortune's winds do shift;
True love is firm, and all the world may know
It is a constant and a holy gift.
It is a sun that through the darkest cloud
Still sheds its warmth upon the earth below;
It is a vow that, spoken not aloud,
The faithful heart doth yet most surely know.
It fears not time, nor winter's chilling breath,
Nor all the petty quarrels lovers make;
It stands a victor over life and death,
And will not for a thousand kingdoms shake.
If this be error and my words untrue,
Then I have never known that love is you.

###91###
What is this war that in my breast I find,
A bitter battle fought 'twixt head and heart?
My reason tells me I should be unkind,
And from thy powerful orbit press to part.
It warns me that thy beauty is a snare,
That thy sweet words are but a siren's song,
It builds a case from logic and despair,
And proves with coldest argument me wrong.
But then my heart, a fool who will not learn,
Recalls one glance, one whisper, or one touch,
And all my reason's sober work doth burn,
For this poor foolish heart desires so much.
So let them fight, this battle strange and long;
My heart is weak, but oh, my love is strong.

###92###
When in the mirror I behold my face,
And see the work of time's ungentle hand,
I wonder what desert or what good grace
Should make thee in my humble presence stand.
The lines of care, the hair that turns to grey,
The weary look

###181###
When in the mirror of my mind, thy grace
Doth show a beauty past all painter's art,
I gaze upon the scripture of thy face
And learn by rote what I have lost by heart.
For in thine eyes a truer path I see
Than any star that guides the darkest night;
Where I am bound, there must my travel be,
Drawn onward by thy soul's celestial light.
And should I falter, or my course go stray,
Or lose the bearings of my constant mind,
One thought of thee recalls the righteous way,
A truer chart than any I could find.
Thus all my travels have one purpose true:
My only destination is to you.

###182###
Let not the tyrant Time make thy form's decay,
For in my verse thy beauty he must fear;
His theft of youth I count a petty crime,
Which shall not touch the likeness written here.
While marble turns to naught but common dust,
And brassy monuments are brought to shame,
My lines shall stand against consuming rust
To keep the living echo of thy name.
So when his scythe shall all the world's wealth see,
And make of fairest things a barren part,
The essence of thy soul shall captured be
Within the loving prison of my art.
For what I give, Time cannot take away,
And in my words, thy spring shall ever stay.

###183###
Since thou art gone, I live in such a state
As when a cloud obscures the morning sun;
My joy, which bloomed so richly of late,
Is by thy sudden absence quite undone.
The sweetest songs like hollow sorrows seem,
The fairest sights do but a falsehood lie,
For all my world is but a waking dream
From which the soul and colour take to fly.
What mirth can this abandoned bosom heart,
What comfort can these lonely hours bestow,
When every moment holds us more apart
And nurtures but the bitter root of woe?
There is no world for me to truly see,
Until my world again is filled with thee.

###184###
Thine outward shape, so wondrously fair,
Doth promise warmth that Winter could not hold,
A breath of kindness on the summer air,
Yet in thy deeds thy heart is bitter cold.
A perfect rose, adorned with heavenly grace,
That tempts the hand to touch, the soul to swoon,
Doth hide the thorn of scorn upon thy face,
And grants no favour, begs no gentle boon.
Why dost thou arm such beauty with such fire
Of cold disdain, to wound a worshipper?
And fuel the flames of unfulfilled desire
Only to be a frozen murderer?
So is thy beauty but a tyrant's art,
To build a throne upon a broken heart.

###185###
What right have I to think of peaceful sleep
When all the world is hushed and takes its light,
While shades of thee their constant vigil keep
And make a busy noontime of my night?
For when I shut my lids, thy pictures rise,
And march in proud procession through my head,
Debarring rest from my most weary eyes
And bidding me to leave my empty bed.
Thus darkness brings no comfort, but thy form,
Which haunts the chambers of my lonely mind;
My thoughts of thee do raise a gentle storm
Wherein no peace or quiet I can find.
And so I pray for the return of day,
To drive the thoughts of night, and thee, away.

###186###
When thou dost smile, I see the summer skies,
And all the world is dressed in hopeful green;
The source of spring is in thy loving eyes,
The fairest season that was ever seen.
But when thou frown'st, a sudden winter's chill
Doth freeze the very courage from the ground;
The birdsong in the forest groweth still,
And not a trace of warmth is to be found.
So by thy brow my weather is decreed,
Without respect for what the year doth bring;
Thy look alone can make my spirit bleed,
Or teach my mournful, silent heart to sing.
So heaven's grace on thy poor subject shines,
Or plunges him in sorrow's deepest mines.

###187###
My foolish heart and my more foolish eye
Do wage a constant and a bitter war;
The eye doth claim thy faults it can descry,
The heart doth swear thou hast no flaw, nor scar.
The eye reports a pride that mars thy sway,
A sharp-edged word, a glance that cuts and scorns;
The heart replies thou dost but brightly play,
And's blinded by the beauty he adores.
This civil strife my state hath overthrown,
My mind, the judge, can give no firm decree;
For reason from his rightful seat is thrown,
And passion's argument persuades but me.
So let them fight, for I must take thy part,
And be the willing captive of my heart.

###188###
I shall not liken thee to a red rose,
For fear that cruel winter makes it bright,
Nor to the sun, whose golden glory goes
When clouds conspire to steal away his light.
Why should I praise the lark whose tune doth spring
From earthly throat, when thy voice is more deep?
Or any treasure that the world can bring,
Which jealous Time doth not for his own keep?
All these are but poor copies of thy art,
Faint echoes of a music truly grace'd;
They play a fleeting and a lesser part
Within the boundless beauty of thy space.
No, nature borrows from thy truer score,
And having thee, what need I wish for more?

###189###
I hear the world, with its well-practised tongue,
Speak gilded words that glitter and deceive;
In courts of kings are false praises sung,
A hollow web that flatterers do weave.
Their friendships shift, their promises are sand,
Built on the hope of what a man might be;
They give with one, and take with the other hand,
And swear that truth which is but vanity.
But from thy lips, one simple, honest word
Doth banish all that counterfeit and plain;
A truer music I have never heard,
That cleanses all the world's ambitious stain.
And so I turn from all their loud untruth,
To find my only verity in you.

###190###
That envious sickness which on fancy feeds,
And makes a monster of a passing thought,
Hath planted in my heart its bitter seeds,
And with a subtle poison, all is wrought.
It whispers doubts when I am most secure,
And turns thy kindness to a cunning trust;
It makes my steadfast certainty unsure,
And grinds my happy confidence to dust.
O, wretched fiend that doth my soul devour,
And makes a prison of my loving mind,
Why dost thou grow in strength with every hour,
And seek a fault where there is none to find?
So let me kill this viper with my love,
Or by its venom shall my own love die.

###191###
When my own glass doth show me to my face
The coming winter etched upon my brow,
And cruel Time with his unhurried pace
Doth steal the strength that I possessed but now,
When I perceive my youth is but a lease
That nears its term with every passing year,
My weary heart can find no true release
From that sad knowledge which I hold so dear.
But then I think of thee, whose constant truth
Doth mock the seasons of my slow decay,
And in thy love I find a second youth,
A sun that chases all the frost away.
So let my body feel Time's winter crime,
Thy love's my summer, conquering all time.

###192###
Some men do boast of how they may be free,
And wander where their own desire remains;
But I, more happy, am enslaved by thee,
And find my only freedom in thy chains.
My prison is the space where thou dost rest,
My cell, the circle of thine arms' embrace;
My sentence, to be ever by thee pressed;
My keeper, the dear tyrant of thy face.
And shouldst thou grant me leave to take my flight,
And break the bonds that hold me to thy sweet will,
I would not stray into the lonely night,
But at thy feet would be thy captive still.
For what is freedom but a bitter thrall,
If from thy loving service I should fall?

###193###
My humble pen, a vassal to my heart,
Doth lose its courage when it tries to write,
And cannot play its designated part
To show the world the glory of thy light.
It stammers, fails, and puts my words to shame,
Though I possess the will, I lack the skill
To build a fitting temple for thy name,
Or bend my language to my loving will.
For what mere ink can capture thy soul's grace,
Or what poor rhyme can hope to find
The perfect measure of thy perfect face,
Or paint the boundless kingdom of thy mind?
So I must fail, and leave the work undone,
For words are shadows, and thou art the sun.

###194###
In that dark theatre of silent sleep,
Thou cam'st to me, a vision sweet and real,
A sacred promise that my soul could keep,
A phantom joy my waking sense could feel.
I saw thee smile, I heard thy gentle speech,
And for a moment, heaven was so near,
My dreaming hand thy phantom hand could reach,
And all was well, and banished was all fear.
But then the mournful, grey-eyed morning's break
Did steal the precious substance of my joy,
Commanding my sad, heavy lids to wake
To a true world that doth but truth destroy.
And so I learned that joy in darkness lies,
And sorrow comes with opening the eyes.

###195###
Thy voice is music to my weary soul,
A sweeter sound than any lute's soft art,
That makes my broken, wounded spirit whole,
And stills the frantic beating of my heart.
When shadows fall, and sorrows I do hear
Debating how they might my peace undo,
One whispered word from thee into mine ear
Doth arm my heart, and makes it strong and new.
Let others praise the choir that sings and brings
The soul to heaven on a silver note;
A greater choir within my memory sings
When I recall the sounds from thy dear throat.
So speak, my love, and let thy music be
The only cure for all that aileth me.

###196###
I am afraid my love is made a sin,
A kind of worship that I should not pray,
For I have built a temple deep within
Wherein I honour thee throughout the day.
My every thought is a devoted hymn,
My every glance, a supplicant's request;
I have forgotten my creator's whim
To make thy heart the haven for my rest.
This blasphemy, I fear, my soul will take,
And for this sweet transgression I shall fall;
And yet, what other choice am I to make
When thou art my creator, and my all?
So if for this idolatry I'm spurned,
In heaven's fire, 'tis by thy light I've burned.

###197###
My heart, which once did dwell within my breast,
Hath made a journey and is now called thine;
And in its place, to give my body rest,
Thy own dear heart hath taken roost in mine.
This fair exchange, a bargain of such grace,
I would not alter for a world of gold;
Thy heart in me hath found a worthy place,
And mine in thee a fortress to be bold.
So now two hearts are housed where there was one,
And in my chest a double pulse is done,
Both for myself and thee a race to run,
And by this trade a truer self is won.
For I am more myself in being you,
And in this union, one is made of two.

###198###
That silent thief, whose pace none can outrun,
Doth make a mockery of beauty's cheek;
The work of youth, so lovingly begun,
He plunders daily, whilst the strong grow weak.
He steals the gold from hair, the rose from skin,
And in their place leaves silver and sad shade;
The light that once did brightly burn within,
Doth by his cold and constant siege fade.
But though his might can ruin what I see,
And turn to ruin all that I hold fast,
He hath no power o'er the memory
That holds thine image, safe within the past.
For what the eye has lost, my mind can find,
And keep thy beauty, timeless, in my mind.

###199###
I have no value in this world of worth,
No store of riches, nor a noble praise,
A common tenant of the common earth,
Who wastes in fruitless searching all his days.
I see how men of state and high renown
Are fawned upon for what they have and hold;
My poverty invites the world to scorn,
My story is a tale that is not told.
But should thy gaze but rest on me a while,
And bless my name by speaking it aloud,
My state is richer by thy passing smile
Than he who is the master of a crowd.
For in thy grace, my fortune is so paid,
That by thy love, a king of me is made.

###200###
Though monuments of brass and quarried stone
Are raised to keep in mind the deeds of state,
Time grinds to dust the king's ambitious throne,
And makes a trifle of the worldly great.
For what is empire, but a passing dream,
And what is power, but a puff of air?
The glory of the world is but a gleam
That fades and leaves the landscape stark and bare.
But this my vow shall suffer no such wrong,
Nor be to ruin and decay a friend;
My love for thee, enshrined within this song,
Shall stand when all these prouder things must end.
So let them build, their works are brief and base,
My love's the only thing that time cannot erase.

###241###
When I do look upon thy comely face,
Wherein the map of all true beauty art,
I see the lines of such a wondrous grace
As paints a legend for the wanting heart.
Thine azure eyes, like to the morning skies,
A tale of constant faith and honour tell;
And from their depths such gentle spirits rise,
Wherein no thought of treachery can dwell.
Thy noble brow doth make a silent vow
To shun the words that flatter and beseech;
By its own truth, it shows the spirit how
To live beyond what wicked tongues can teach.
So let me gaze, and by this study find
The perfect model of a virtuous mind.

###242###
That churlish thief, old Time, with thieving hand,
Doth steal the rose from out thy beauty's glass,
And turns thy youthful gold to silver sand,
Making the fairest of all glories pass.
He carves his lines upon thy smoothest brow,
And makes a mock of nature's best decree;
The very grace that doth adorn thee now,
He vows in dusty annals shall not be.
Yet shall he fail in all his cruel spite,
And find his triumphs are not half so strong,
For I shall prison thy eternal light
Within the lasting fortress of my song.
For what is writ in this my loving rhyme
Shall stand a shield against the wars of Time.

###243###
My tongue is made a bankrupt and a fool,
That cannot pay the debt thy praises ask,
For when it might thy virtues best extol,
It finds its store unequal to the task.
My mind conceives a wealth of golden thought,
A kingdom shining with thy every deed,
But by my lips this treasure comes to naught,
A barren harvest from a fertile seed.
So must my pen attempt what speech denies,
And labour hard to sketch what I behold,
To give a shape to what my heart decries
As riches far too great to be enrolled.
Yet if these lines but one true grace impart,
Know they're but shadows of a loving heart.

###244###
When in the lonely congress of the night,
My thoughts, like restless spirits, walk abroad,
They seek no comfort but thine own dear sight,
By all the world's distractions overawed.
The day's loud toil, its business and its care,
Resolved to nothing, fade and disappear,
And in their place, thy sainted image there,
Doth banish every doubt and every fear.
Thou art the sun that my dark musings chase,
The star by which my wandering soul is led;
The memory of thy sweet and loving face
Is the soft pillow for my weary head.
So sleeps my grief, and so my joys arise,
Waking or dreaming, fixed upon thine eyes.

###245###
Why dost thou wear that veil of cold disdain,
As though my constant love were cause for shame?
Each scornful glance renews a bitter pain,
And pours cold water on a hopeful flame.
If I have erred in loving thee too well,
Then is my fault a virtue in disguise,
For in what heaven could my spirit dwell,
If not the one reflected in thine eyes?
Oh, teach me not this lesson of despair,
Nor let my pure devotion be thy foe;
It is a tyrant's art to seem unfair
To one who hath no other place to go.
Then look with kindness, lest in truth you prove
That you can murder with a lack of love.

###246###
The winter wind, a bitter, cruel guest,
Doth strip the trees of all their summer dress,
And sends the shivering songbird from its nest,
To leave the forest hushed and comfortless.
The frozen streams no longer laugh and run,
But lie in silent bondage, hard and grey,
And pale and distant is the cheerless sun
That loans a meagre portion to the day.
So is my world when thou art far from me,
A landscape stark, of all its warmth bereft;
No song, no light, no living thing I see,
With but the memory of summer left.
Only thy swift return can break the spell,
And from this frost my captive heart release from hell.

###247###
If I should count the reasons for my love,
I should be counting till the end of days;
I could as well the sands and heavens move,
Or find a limit to my endless praise.
It is not for thy cheek of rosy hue,
Though nature's triumph there is manifest;
Nor for thine eyes of that celestial blue,
Wherein the thoughts of angels seem to rest.
It is not for thy voice, whose music sweet
Could tame a savage breast, or calm the sea;
These are but parts that make the whole complete,
But not the substance of my love for thee.
I love thee for a cause I cannot show;
'Tis for thy soul, which my soul knows is so.

###248###
My jealous mind, a self-tormenting spy,
Doth conjure phantoms from the empty air,
And sees a rival in each passer-by
Who casts a glance upon thy beauty rare.
Each gentle word to another thou dost speak,
Sounds as a thunder-clap within my ear;
My own foundation I believe so weak,
It must be shattered by a budding fear.
This wretched fever, born of my own doubt,
Makes me a traitor to my truest friend,
Besieging that dear heart from all about,
Which to my own its fortress ought to lend.
Forgive this madness, born of love's excess,
Whose only crime's a fear of loving less.

###249###
When in the chronicle of ages past,
I read of ladies praised and knights so bold,
Of beauties whose fair fame was made to last,
In verses penned on parchments rich with gold;
I think their poets, with prophetic art,
Did see a vision of thy form and face,
And tried to capture, with a hopeful heart,
Some portion of thine yet-unfashioned grace.
All of their praise, of Helen or of Jane,
Were but the prophecies of what would be;
Their ink but sketched in shadow and in rain
The sun-kissed perfection I behold in thee.
For in thy being, all their virtues meet,
Making the ancient eulogies complete.

###250###
The miser, with his hoard of hidden gold,
Doth live in constant terror of the thief,
And in his counting, finds his joys grow cold,
So great his fear, so brittle his belief.
And I, who hold a treasure far more dear,
Thy priceless love, which is my sole estate,
Am wracked by that same ague-shaken fear,
And count my blessings with a trembling fate.
For what if Fortune, with a fickle frown,
Should steal away the prize I cherish most?
And cast my spirit from its high renown
To be a beggar on a barren coast?
Thus rich in love, I'm poor in my content,
Lest what you gave, was only what was lent.

###251###
Let those who will, take pride in worldly things,
In noble blood, in lands, or heaps of coin;
My heart a far more simple measure sings,
And in a different company doth join.
For what is pomp, but care in rich attire?
And what is wealth, but gilded servitude?
And what is rank, but fuel for the fire
Of envy in the thankless multitude?
My chiefest treasure, and my proudest boast,
Is one sweet look from thy beloved eye;
Of all the world, 'tis this I value most,
And for its lack, all other gains would die.
So let them have their castles and their lands;
My world is held within thy loving hands.

###252###
This heavy ink, a servant to my will,
Moves slow across the page in humble black,
And strives with its poor, unaspiring skill
To show the light it cannot but lack.
It wishes it were made of morning's gold,
Or of the silver from a moonlit stream,
That it a worthier story might unfold,
And better serve its high and holy theme.
For when it writes the letters of thy name,
It feels its common nature, and is shamed,
A flickering candle to a burning flame,
A stammering voice by which the stars are named.
Yet read it with thy heart, and thou shalt find
The soul it serves is of a brighter kind.

###253###
As an unskillful actor on the stage,
Forgetting all the lines he should recite,
Doth stand in silence, or in foolish rage,
And turns his finest moment into night;
So I, in thy magnificent presence, find
My ready wit and practised words take flight,
And all the stored-up treasures of my mind
Are struck to silence by thy beauty's light.
My heart prepares a speech of wondrous art,
With reason, passion, and with compliment,
But when I see thee, it forgets its part,
And love's own advocate is impotent.
Oh, let my books, these lines, then speak for me,
Who am tongue-tied when I am touching thee.

###254###
Canst thou not see the torment in my gaze,
This silent plea my fearful lips conceal?
My love for thee, through all my nights and days,
Is the one truth my spirit doth feel.
Like to a pilgrim at a holy shrine,
Who dares not touch the saint he kneels before,
So do I worship that sweet grace of thine,
And in my silence, I but love thee more.
But faith requires a sign, however small,
A whispered word, a look, a fleeting smile,
Lest this devoted heart should faint and fall,
Finding its long devotion but a guile.
Then break thy silence, and with mercy speak,
Lest hope should die, and my poor heartstrings break.

###255###
The forward violet, in early spring,
Dares show its purple head to chilly skies,
And though the winds a bitter message bring,
In its own brief and fragrant beauty lies.
It does not ask to be the garden's rose,
Nor with the stately lily to compare;
It takes the little time that fate bestows,
And breathes its gentle perfume on the air.
So is my love, which dares to show its face,
Though of thy high perfection all unworthy;
It seeks not to usurp a prouder place,
But in thy sight would simply live and be.
And if its purple pleases thee awhile,
It asks no more than one approving smile.

###256###
My weary soul, much like a battered ship,
That storms have tossed upon a cruel sea,
Finds its safe harbour on thy gentle lip,
And in thine arms, its calm security.
The howling gales of fortune and of spite,
The treacherous currents of the world's deceit,
Do lose their power in thy calming light;
Here is my anchor, and my journey's feat.
Let others seek new continents to find,
Or chase the fleeting whale across the foam;
I have no wish to leave my port behind,
For in thy steadfast heart, I am at home.
Why should I sail, when all I'd wish to win,
Is this dear haven that I'm anchored in?

###257###
Thy voice is music, yet no common sound
That from a lute or well-tuned viol rings;
In its sweet cadence, something more is found
Than all the art a practiced minstrel brings.
For when thou speak'st, the words are so arrayed,
With such a grace of reason and of thought,
That wisdom's self seems like a lowly maid,
Next to the simple truths thy speech has taught.
Let others praise the lark's song at the dawn,
Or the sad nightingale that wails by night;
These are but notes on morning's air withdrawn,
Whilst thy words lend the very soul its light.
And when thou'rt silent, I in memory hear
A music that makes all other noises drear.

###258###
To be away from thee is to be dead,
A walking ghost in a forgotten play,
With hollow words by rote and custom said,
To mark the passing of an empty day.
I see the sun, but feel no warmth or fire;
I see the crowds, but feel myself alone;
Each sight and sound becomes a torment dire,
A sharp reminder of the joy that's flown.
My life is but a clock, whose hands do creep,
Marking the hours until I see thee next;
My waking thoughts are but a kind of sleep,
By dreary dreams of thine own absence vexed.
For thou art life, and when thou art not here,
My world's a tomb, and I thy mourner dear.

###259###
So oft I have compared thee to the sun,
Or to a star that guides the lost at sea,
That all my metaphors have overrun,
And seem but poor, worn garments now for thee.
To say thine eyes are bright, or voice is sweet,
Is but to state what every fool can see;
These common praises, I no more repeat,
Lest they should cheapen what thou art to me.
So I will silent be, and let my gaze
Become the poet that my tongue is not,
And in the quiet worship of my days,
Let a more profound and truer verse be got.
For words may fail, and comparisons grow stale,
But my true love shall never, ever fail.

###260###
If this be magic, that thy presence brings,
Then let me live enchanted evermore,
Forswearing all the truth of common things,
To dwell a captive on thy mystic shore.
For when thou'rt near, the world is born anew,
The very air with brighter colours glows,
Each blade of grass is jewelled with the dew,
And from the thorn erupts a perfect rose.
This strange alchemy I cannot understand,
How from thy glance such transformations spring,
As if a god had touched this barren land,
And made the rocks and silent deserts sing.
Call it what you will, a madness or a grace;
My only heaven is thy wondrous face.

###221###
What king commands such undisputed might
As doth reside within thy sovereign state?
Thy two bright eyes, a world-consuming light,
Do rule a heart that once was proud and great.
I see no standard in the fields of war,
Nor hear the sound of any harsh decree,
That holds my soul in captive awe far more
Than when thy gentle aspect falls on me.
For in that gaze a treasure I behold,
More precious than a man could ever find,
Which makes my tongue, that once was speaking bold,
A silent servant to thy matchless mind.
  So let me live forever in thy thrall,
  For in thy prison, I possess my all.

###222###
The world doth show to me a withered grace,
When I am absent from thy loving eye;
A frown appears on Nature's cheerful face,
And clouds obscure the glory of the sky.
The sweetest music sounds but harsh and dull,
The poet's verse no longer rings as true,
My senses find life's cup is rendered null,
And have no object for their gaze but you.
But when your presence doth a new day start,
And breaks upon me where all late was dark,
A sudden, hopeful song within my heart
Doth rise as joyful as the morning's lark.
  From this I learn all glories that e'er shine,
  Are but reflections of that soul of thine.

###223###
Against the tyranny of Time's swift hand,
Which brings all worldly treasure to decay,
I take my pen, and by its strength you stand,
When monuments of stone are swept away.
Though proud-built towers must crumble into dust,
And fairest creatures are but born to die,
In these poor lines you may securely trust,
That they shall cheat the world and never lie.
So let Oblivion wage its wasteful strife,
And seek to bury your most precious name;
This verse shall grant to you a second life,
And build a living temple for your fame.
  For what Time steals, my verse will hold here still,
  And guard your worth against its cruel will.

###224###
When I thy much-desired voice do hear,
It is a music sweeter than the lute;
And every word you speak is held so dear,
That all the world's loud chatter becomes mute.
No singer hath such captivating art,
To make my humbled spirit bend so low;
Thy quiet speech doth captivate my heart,
From which such streams of gentle wisdom flow.
The very air is hallowed by the sound,
A gift that only heaven's grace could bring;
It makes a palace of the common ground,
And makes of me a subject, you a king.
  So speak again, for I have made my choice:
  To find my only kingdom in thy voice.

###225###
It is a torment for mine eyes to see
Your gracious smile on some slight fool cast down,
When all that light should rightly fall on me,
Who for your sake would spurn a monarch's crown.
A casual glance, a word of little weight,
Becomes a jewel when it comes from you,
And I, who watch and curse my wretched fate,
Am starved of that which is my only due.
Why must you spend that treasure of your heart
On those who know not what they freely share,
And leave to me the most unhappy part,
To feed upon the scraps of your spent care?
  Yet even in this, you hold a sovereign

###301###
When I in idle thought do call to mind
The swift and silent passing of the years,
A bitter truth in memory I find,
Which summons forth a host of foolish fears.
For beauty fades as doth a summer rose,
And strength of youth must wither and decay,
As winter's frost upon the garden grows,
And steals the fairest blossoms quite away.
But thy fair form, a constant, fixed star,
Shall not be subject to such cruel decline;
My verse shall keep thee ever as thou art,
And make thy virtues through the ages shine.
  So time may rage and wreak its wicked will,
  Within these lines, thy youth shall flourish still.

###302###
My hungry eyes, that feast upon thy grace,
Find in thy presence such a sweet repast,
That when thou art removed from this same place,
A wretched famine on my soul is cast.
No other sight can my starved sense content,
No earthly beauty can my want supply;
The world's a void, its colours falsely spent,
When the bright sun of thy face is not nigh.
So do I pine, and think upon that hour
When fate shall grant my vision its reward,
And to my sight restore that blessed flower
Whose absence makes my state of life abhorred.
  Until that time, I live in darkest night,
  And blind man's-like, do grope for missing light.

###303###
What cunning artisan did frame thine eyes,
To hold the world so captive in their gaze?
What stars forsook their station in the skies
To light thy face and set my heart ablaze?
They are not glass, but portals to a soul
Wherein all virtues have their dwelling-place,
Which take my own poor spirit in control,
And hold it subject by their gentle grace.
And when a tear that precious orb doth fill,
It seems a pearl in liquid silver set,
That makes the world around it stand quite still,
And all my former sorrows I forget.
  Let me not live if I'm denied their view,
  For in their light, my world is born anew.

###304###
That which I feel, my tongue can never tell,
For words are weak and common things, and base,
Which in their clumsy effort to compel
Thy praise, succeed but in thy form's disgrace.
I could compare thee to a day in June,
Or to the wonder of the rising sun,
But every verse and every hopeful tune
Falls short of all the glories thou hast won.
My pen is but a fool, my ink is faint,
My wit too dull to capture what I see;
How can a mortal hand a goddess paint,
Or show the world what thou dost mean to me?
  So I'll be mute, and in my silence prove,
  What speech would ruin: my unending love.

###305###
Why must this love, which brings so sweet a prize,
Be yet attended by such bitter pain?
Why do such clouds of doubt and fear arise
To bring a downpour of tormenting rain?
One moment I am lifted to the height
Of all the bliss a mortal heart can know;
The next I'm cast into the darkest night,
And left to wander in a world of woe.
This strange affliction, this confusing state,
Doth make a battleground within my breast,
Where warring thoughts of love and jealous hate
Do rob my weary, aching soul of rest.
  And yet to lose this war I would not choose,
  For in this fight, my very life I lose.

###306###
My love is not a fair and gentle thing,
With rosy cheeks and hair of golden thread;
No, from a darker, earthier soil doth spring
The sharp desire on which my soul is fed.
Her eyes are not like stars, but shadowed pools
Wherein a stark and knowing truth I find;
She scoffs at flattering and sighing fools,
And with her wit, can be most cruelly kind.
And yet this flawed and worldly heart I prize
Above the bland perfection poets praise;
A truer fire is burning in her eyes
Than in the sun's most celebrated rays.
  Let others love the calm and sunlit shore,
  I love the storm, and her I'll e'er adore.

###307###
How many nights have I lain wide awake,
And watched the slow moon trace her path on high,
My heavy heart possessed by such an ache,
It can but shape itself into a sigh.
For thoughts of thee do banish gentle sleep,
And in its place a host of visions bring:
I see thy face, and in my spirit weep
That winter holds me from my promised spring.
The darkness is no comfort to my soul,
But serves to make my solitude more deep;
Thy memory doth take a heavy toll,
While all the world around is fast asleep.
  So night by night, for thee I lie and burn,
  And pray for day, and for thy swift return.

###308###
This world's a stage, where every man must play
A part assigned to him by cruel-writ fate,
And I, it seems, am destined to obey
A role of love in a most piteous state.
The scenes are set with sorrow and with doubt,
My lines are spoken only to the air,
For she who is the plot I turn about
Knows not the weight of my concealed despair.
Another takes the stage and wins her heart,
With bolder speech and more becoming grace,
While I, a fool, must act this silent part,
And watch his triumph from a hidden place.
  When the play's done and all the actors cease,
  May death's dark curtain bring my soul some peace.

###309###
As some poor miser counts his treasured gold,
And fears a thief will steal his hoard away,
So in my jealous heart thy worth I hold,
And live in constant terror and dismay.
Each glance another on thy beauty spends
Is like a dagger to my worried mind;
Each pleasant word to one of thy fair friends
I must construe as faithless and unkind.
This love hath made a prison of my soul,
Wherein suspicion is the gaoler fell,
Who keeps my thoughts in his severe control,
And turns my hopeful heaven to a hell.
  O, cure this madness, with one look assure
  My heart that its most precious prize is secure.

###310###
When in the chronicle of ages past
I read of knights and ladies fair of face,
Of loves that were too beautiful to last,
And beauty subject to time's slow disgrace,
I think that Nature, in those former days,
Was but practicing her creative skill,
Preparing for the triumph of thy praise,
Her ancient patterns better to fulfill.
All of their grace, their virtue, and their truth,
Were but a shadow of the thing to be,
A prophecy of thy perfected youth,
Which ancient poets had no eyes to see.
  So in thy form, the best of old we find,
  The sum of all that came before, combined.

###311###
How canst thou see my sorrow and my pain
And yet remain so placid and so still?
Doth not my grief, that falls like winter's rain,
Affect thy heart with some responsive chill?
The hardest stone by water is worn down,
The proudest cliff is beaten by the sea,
But thy resolve, it seems, doth wear a crown,
And scorns the constant tears that fall from me.
My sighs are gales that should a tempest make,
My love a fire that should consume all doubt,
But thou, unmoved, for thy sweet pity's sake,
Dost let the storm and flame just rage without.
  So be it then, if thou must be so strong;
  But where's the right in such a lovely wrong?

###312###
If music be the very food of love,
Then thy sweet voice is a celestial feast,
Sent from the choir of seraphim above,
At which the greatest king and poorest priest
Would find a solace for their worldly cares,
A balm to soothe the most afflicted heart;
For when thou speakst, my soul forgets its snares,
And from all troubled, earthly thoughts doth part.
The lark's high song, the viol's rich lament,
Are but a discord, harsh and out of tune,
Compared with those soft accents heaven-sent,
Beneath the gaze of the approving moon.
  So speak again, and let me ever be
  Entranced by thy voice's harmony.

###313###
Weary with toil, I haste me to my bed,
The dear repose for limbs with travel tired;
But then begins a journey in my head
When body's work is blessedly expired.
For then my thoughts, from far where I abide,
Intend a zealous pilgrimage to thee,
And keep my drooping eyelids open wide,
While looking on a darkness which I see.
Save that my soul's imaginary sight
Presents thy shadow to my sleepless view,
Which, like a jewel hung in ghastly night,
Makes black night beauteous, and her old face new.
  Lo, thus, by day my limbs, by night my mind,
  For thee, and for myself, no quiet find.

###314###
The sun doth rise and chase the night away,
But brings no comfort to my sunless state;
For what care I for the most brilliant day,
When on my heart there sits so dull a weight?
My friends all chide me for my solemn mood,
And bid me join them in their life of mirth;
They cannot know my spirit is subdued
By thoughts of thee, the rarest thing on earth.
Your absence is a winter in my soul,
Wherein no flower of happiness can grow;
Your memory exerts a full control,
And lays my every cheerful prospect low.
  One smile from you could make my summer bloom,
  And banish all this self-consuming gloom.

###315###
Shall I compare my love to Fortune's grace,
Which shines on some, and others passes by?
Nay, for her gifts she often will misplace,
And on the least deserving let them lie.
Shall I compare her to a kingdom's crown,
For which great men do sacrifice their all?
Nay, for a crown brings with it ill-renown,
And those who wear it live in fear to fall.
My love is not a prize to be so won,
Nor some rich treasure that a man might keep;
She is the light of a far truer sun,
Whose sacred mystery is wide and deep.
  She is herself, beyond all such compare,
  A thing of wonder, and beyond all share.

###316###
O, how my wit is humbled and made poor
When I take up my pen to write thy praise!
I find my muse hath shut and barred her door,
And left me stranded in a verbal maze.
The very birds that sing upon the bough
Seem to reproach me with their artless song;
Their simple notes are truer, I'll allow,
Than my attempts to right my loving wrong.
For words are but a net to catch the wind,
When set against the truth of what you are;
The best of them fall short, and leave behind
The brighter glory of my distant star.
  So let this verse, in its own failure, show
  The boundless debt my silent gazes owe.

###317###
As an unthrift, who having spent his worth,
Doth then reflect upon his foolish ways,
So I, in counting all the joys of earth,
Lament the squandering of my former days.
I chased false shadows, sought for gilded toys,
And gave my heart to what was not worthwhile,
Mistaking fleeting, superficial joys
For the true substance of a loving smile.
But now I know, since I have looked on thee,
What treasure is, and what is worthless dross;
Thy virtue is a world's whole wealth to me,
And all I had before I count as loss.
  In finding you, my starved soul has been fed,
  And from my bankrupt past, is newly bred.

###318###
When forty winters shall besiege thy brow,
And dig deep trenches in thy beauty's field,
Thy youth's proud livery, so gazed on now,
Will be a tattered weed, of little held.
Then being asked where all thy beauty lies,
Where all the treasure of thy lusty days,
To say, within thine own deep-sunken eyes,
Were an all-eating shame and thriftless praise.
How much more praise deserved thy beauty's use,
If thou couldst answer "This fair child of mine
Shall sum my count and make my old excuse,"
Proving his beauty by succession thine!
  This were to be new made when thou art old,
  And see thy blood warm when thou feel'st it cold.

###319###
Against that time when thou shalt strangely see
My love's true fire for but a dying spark,
And all my vows shall seem but perjury,
My sun of faith a candle in the dark;
Against that time when distance, doubt, or art
Shall steal thy heart and turn its love away,
And I, a player with a broken part,
Must watch the ending of our hopeful play;
Against that time, I fortify my mind
With this defence, which shall for me contend:
That my poor verse thy lasting worth shall find,
And prove my love was true unto the end.
  So when all else shall fail, this mayst thou read,
  And know my faith outlived the faithless deed.

###320###
So oft have I invoked thee for my Muse,
And found such fair assistance in thy name,
That every alien pen hath got my use
And under thee their poesy would frame.
Thine eyes, that taught the dumb on high to sing
And heavy ignorance aloft to fly,
Have added feathers to the learned's wing
And given grace a double majesty.
Yet be most proud of that which I compile,
Whose influence is thine and born of thee:
In others' works thou dost but mend the style,
And arts with thy sweet graces graced be.
  But thou art all my art, and dost advance
  As high as learning my rude ignorance.

###201###
When envious Time shall steal thy youth away,
And golden locks are turned to silver-grey,
When that which makes thee lovely must decay,
And beauty's treasure can no longer stay;
When in thy glass a stranger's face appears,
A wrinkled map of sorrows and of fears,
The sad account of all thy passing years,
That pays its heavy debt with silent tears:
Then take this book, and in its pages find
The perfect image of thy former grace,
The lasting portrait of a constant mind,
The truth that time itself cannot erase.
   So in my verse, thy youth shall ever live,
   And have that life which nature cannot give.

###202###
My heart, a ship upon a stormy sea,
Doth lose its course when thou art not in sight;
My hopeful thoughts, which ever sail to thee,
Are lost to darkness in a starless night.
Thou art the sun that should my journey guide,
The single star by which my soul might steer,
Yet in the clouds of thine own scornful pride,
Thou dost abandon me to doubt and fear.
What tempest of despair must I endure?
What rocky shore of sorrow must I meet?
Without thy light, my voyage is unsure,
And every bitter wave tastes of defeat.
   So shine thou forth, and calm this inner strife,
   Or let me wreck upon the shoals of life.

###203###
I see thee praised by every common tongue,
And hear thy virtues spoken of with glee;
Thy perfect grace by every poet sung,
And yet this praise brings little joy to me.
For what they praise is but the outward part,
A lovely face for all the world to see,
They cannot know the kingdom of thy heart,
Which I had foolishly reserved for me.
This jealousy, a poison in my soul,
Doth make me question that which I hold dear;
It takes my reason far beyond control,
And turns my worship to a bitter fear.
   For though thy light on all the world may shine,
   I would it were a light for me and mine.

###204###
When I am weary of the world's loud noise,
And all its empty triumph and its pain,
I turn my thought from its ephemeral joys,
And in my mind I see thy face again.
Thou art a shelter from the driving storm,
A quiet harbour from a troubled sea;
A constant fire to keep my spirit warm,
A place of refuge where my soul is free.
For in the haven of thy loving eyes,
All of the cares that did my heart oppress
Depart, as darkness from the morning skies,
And leave but peace and gentle happiness.
   Let other men seek fortune, fame, or art;
   I seek but this: a place within thy heart.

###205###
I lay awake while all the world doth sleep,
And hold a silent council with my grief;
Into the darkest hours, my thoughts do creep,
To find in misery a sad relief.
My closed eyes, which should be blind to light,
Behold thine image, steadfast and so clear,
That it doth make a daytime of the night,
And brings thy very distant shadow near.
But then my soul, remembering the cause,
Doth chide my heart for its own foolishness,
That it doth break all of nature's laws,
To think on one who brings it such distress.
   And so the night in thinking I consume,
   And greet the dawn with sorrow for my doom.

###206###
Thy beauty is a sickness in my blood,
A fever that I neither wish to cure,
Nor can I quell; it is a rising flood
That my poor heart no longer can endure.
One moment's sight of thee, one passing glance,
Is all the cause of this my fevered state;
I am a player in a deadly dance,
And by thy simple presence sealed my fate.
The leech may counsel me to stay away,
And wise men warn me of the coming cost,
But what they speak, my ears will not obey,
For in thy gaze, my reason is quite lost.
   Thus must I burn, and in this fire pine,
   For health itself is no life, if not thine.

###207###
How like a knave thou dost my love deceive,
With promises as fair as they are false;
What web of charming lies thy tongue doth weave,
To make my reason in its purpose halts.
Thou show'st a face of such an honest kind,
As if no treason harboured in thy breast,
That I, by my own adoration blind,
Do take thy poison for a welcome guest.
Thy vows are but as words writ on the sand,
Which the next tide of fancy washes clear;
Thou hold'st my trusting heart within thy hand,
And dost not see the ruin thou mak'st here.
   So play thy part, and wear thy sweet disguise;
   I love thee still, though I believe thy lies.

###208###
As the green shoot doth press toward the sky,
Then blooms in glory ere its petals fall,
So doth our life in hurried seasons fly,
And pay its final duty unto all.
The Spring of youth, a promise fresh and bold,
Gives way to Summer's strong and steady grace;
Then Autumn comes, with stories to be told,
And leaves its golden lines on time's worn face.
At last, the cold of Winter's endless white,
Which claims the leaf and lays the branch all bare,
Doth rob the weary landscape of its light,
And buries all beneath a sheet of care.
   Yet in thy love, a constant Spring I find,
   That drives the chill of winter from my mind.

###209###
Look in thy glass, and tell the face thou see'st,
That it is cruel to be so fair and proud;
Thou art the host of beauty's richest feast,
Yet starve the world by living in a cloud.
What right hast thou to hoard such wondrous light,
And keep that treasure only for thine own?
The sun itself doth not withhold its sight,
But shines on all from its celestial throne.
If thou shouldst die without a child to bear
Thy living image to a future day,
The world will weep that one so passing fair
Did take such beauty with her to the clay.
   So leave a copy of thyself behind,
   That grace may live, when thou art unconfined.

###210###
Doubt not the stars, for they are fire indeed,
Nor doubt the sun, that it will ever move;
Doubt not that truth itself can spring from seed,
But never for a moment doubt my love.
For it is truer than the ground I tread,
More constant than the turning of the sphere,
The first and last thing in my heart and head,
The only cure for all my worldly fear.
Though all the world should prove to be a stage,
And every friend I have, a faithless part,
My love for thee, upon this mortal page,
Is the one truth residing in my heart.
   So if I seem to fail, or go astray,
   My love is true; 'tis but my heart gives way.

###211###
We are but players on a painted stage,
Who strut and fret our hour for all to see,
Then take our exit, as is writ on page,
And slip into what we are meant to be.
We learn our lines of sorrow and of joy,
We wear the mask of lover or of fool,
We treat the soul as if it were a toy,
And make our passion subject to a rule.
But what of thee, whose part is to be fair?
And what of me, whose part is but to gaze?
Is thy perfection but a practised air?
Is my devotion but a passing craze?
   Nay, if this world's a play, our love's the theme,
   And all the rest is but a waking dream.

###212###
The moon doth shine with a respected light,
And all the stars in heaven are adored;
The world is filled with many a wondrous sight
Where nature's boundless beauty is outpoured.
The rose in bloom, the eagle in his flight,
The ancient mountain, sovereign and proud,
The sun itself, in its majestic might,
That burns its way through every clinging cloud.
All these are fair, and worthy of my praise,
And for a moment captivate my mind;
I look on them with wonder and amaze,
And think what greater beauty could I find?
   And then I think of thee, and know it's true,
   That all their beauty is but serving you.

###213###
To be away from thee is to be dead,
A walking ghost with no true life within;
My senses fail, my reason is misled,
And every day a new despair must spin.
The food I eat hath lost its former taste,
The songs I hear are but a hollow sound;
My hours are spent in melancholy waste,
Where not a moment of true joy is found.
My friends do ask why I am pale and wan,
And why my speech is now so brief and cold;
I cannot tell them that my sun is gone,
A truth too dear and painful to be told.
   For thou art all my world, my life, my breath,
   And in thine absence, there is only death.

###214###
Let them who have no beauty of their own
Rely on powders, paints, and costly dress
To build a charm upon a plainer stone,
And feign a grace they never did possess.
Their artifice may fool an unwise eye,
And win a fleeting praise from simple minds,
But truth, like sun, will burn away the lie,
And show the plain reality it finds.
But thou, my love, need'st no such false defense;
Thy beauty comes from nature, not from art.
It is the outward sign of innocence,
The true reflection of a gentle heart.
   And so thy grace shall never know decay,
   When all their painted charms have washed away.

###215###
This world, they say, is but a vale of tears,
A place of sorrow, suffering, and strife,
Where every man is plagued by doubts and fears,
And death's cold shadow haunts the whole of life.
I have found cause to think this saying true,
For I have known despair and bitter loss;
My sunniest days have been both dark and few,
And every comfort bore a heavy cross.
But that was in the time ere I met thee,
When all my world was winter, cold and grey;
Thy love was like the sun that set me free,
And chased the darkness of my soul away.
   Now let them call this world a place of pain;
   To me, 'tis paradise, where thou dost reign.

###216###
When I am old and all my strength is gone,
And sit alone to count my fading days,
My memories of thee I'll feed upon,
And warm my soul in their forgotten rays.
I will remember how thy voice did sound,
And how thy laughter filled the summer air;
The holy space where first our love was found,
The faultless shape of thy most perfect hair.
I will recall the softness of thy hand,
The steady light that shone within thine eye,
A truth my aged heart can understand,
A beauty that shall live and never die.
   And in these thoughts, I shall not be alone,
   But hold thee still, and keep thee for my own.

###217###
What bitter war is this between us two,
That turns our former kindness into strife?
What careless word has made my heart untrue
To the one comfort of my weary life?
The pleasant garden where our love once grew
Is now a field where angry armies meet;
The gentle words that once between us flew
Are turned to weapons, poisoned and un-sweet.
Oh, let us call a truce to this sad fight,
And lay our foolish, hurtful banners down,
Let us not spend another precious night
In the cold kingdom of a weary frown.
   For life's too short for such a lengthy cost;
   Let's mend our love, before it is all lost.

###218###
The placid surface of a summer's lake
May hide the dark and hungry depths below,
And so my face, for gentle pity's sake,
A calm and mild contentment seeks to show.
No frown or furrow on my brow is seen,
My voice is steady and my smile is free;
I play my part upon this worldly scene,
For none to guess the war inside of me.
But in the silent theatre of my breast,
A frantic tempest rages without end;
My heart, by sorrow's tyranny oppressed,
Can find no peace, and knows no trusted friend.
   And none who see my face can guess the sin,
   That I do smile, while sorrow reigns within.

###219###
Thy

###381###
My every waking thought is wrought of thee,
And occupies the passing of the day;
So that no other form my mind can see,
Nor from thy picture do my fancies stray.
And when the dark doth veil the world from sight,
And weary souls seek solace in the night,
Thy constant image is my only light,
A vivid dream that makes the shadows bright.
What sorcery is this, what potent art,
That holds my reason so in thy command?
To play so sovereign a ruling part,
And take the government of heart and hand.
  Yet I'll not strive to break this sweet decree,
  For in thy bondage I would ever be.

###382###
Let tyrants boast the power of their gaze,
Whose single word can make a kingdom groan;
My world is measured only in the days
I see thy face, and call that sight my own.
What is a king's severe and high command,
Or all the force held in a mailed hand,
Compared to that which I cannot withstand,
The silent law thy gentle eyes demand?
Then let them rule with sceptre and with sword,
And in their might and majesty take pride;
One tender look from thee, one loving word,
Doth all their strength and glory set aside.
  So am I conquered by thy beauty's might,
  And find my freedom in thy captured sight.

###383###
When ruthless winter strips the shivering tree,
And lays a sheet of white upon the ground,
In that bleak barrenness, thy lack I see,
Where warmth and life and colour are not found.
The very air, so bitter and so cold,
Doth tell a tale my grieving heart holds dear;
A story of a love grown faint and old,
And all the sorrow of a sunless year.
And though the sun, a stranger to the sky,
But briefly shows his pale and cheerless face,
His weak farewell is like thy last goodbye,
Which left my world a cold and empty place.
  Yet shouldst thou but return, and love renew,
  The spring would bloom in spite of winter's view.

###384###
My troubled soul, a vessel tempest-tossed,
Doth sail upon a sea of black despair;
My course is gone, my guiding compass lost,
And angry waves conspire to keep me there.
No friendly shore, no harbour can I find,
To grant my weary spirit safe abode;
And every hopeful thought I leave behind,
Bears but the weight of sorrow's heavy load.
But if through stormy clouds and endless night,
One glimpse of thy dear countenance should shine,
Thou art that constant star, that polar light,
To steer my path with influence divine.
  So by thy beauty is my journey charted,
  To lead me home, no longer broken-hearted.

###385###
That Nature drew thee with her finest grace
Is truth acknowledged by each soul that sees;
For she did strive to perfect in thy face
A pattern for all men in time to please.
But of this gift, why art thou such a miser,
To keep this treasure only for thine own?
Be not of thy sweet self the sole deviser,
Lest with thy passing, beauty be o'erthrown.
Thy youth is but a mirror, a frail glass,
Which time will crack, and all its glory break;
Let not thy likeness from this cold world pass,
But for the future's sake, a copy make.
  So shall thy beauty live when thou art gone,
  And in thy child, thy springtime carry on.

###386###
A mortal war my heart and eye do wage,
On how to share the prize they hold in thee;
My eye doth claim thy form upon life's stage,
My heart, thy love's invisible decree.
My heart asserts that in his tender thought,
Thy truest image is most truly held;
My eye replies, 'tis by thy form I'm caught,
By which all other beauties are dispelled.
So to a jury of my thoughts they plead,
To judge the merit of their rival right;
And I, the judge, must weigh their selfish need,
And give my verdict in the endless fight.
  But this the sentence that I must impart:
  Thine eyes have thine, my love, and thou my heart.

###387###
That villain Time, with swift and silent pace,
Commits his crime against the fairest things,
To steal the bloom from off thy lovely face,
And dull the light that thy sweet beauty brings.
He is a thief that pilfers precious youth,
A creeping shadow turning all to grey,
Who scoffs at vows and mocks eternal truth,
And bears the spoils of summer to decay.
But I against his cruel and wasteful rage
Shall build a fortress with my humble pen,
So that thy beauty, safe within this page,
May live anew for eyes of other men.
  For what his ruin seeks, my verse shall save,
  And cheat the silence of a thankless grave.

###388###
If these poor words that I for thee devise
Seem weak expressions of my love's true state,
Blame not my pen, but thy soul-charming eyes,
Whose boundless worth no poem can relate.
For when I strive to capture thy desert,
My labouring quill doth falter and fall short;
No metaphor can furnish half thy worth,
Nor render justice in this rhyming sport.
What poet's tongue could tell of such a grace?
What mortal song could hold a light so fine?
The richest verse grows poor before thy face,
And all description proves itself malign.
  So if my verse seems wanting in its art,
  'Tis thy perfection that undoes my heart.

###389###
I envy much the free and golden sun,
That he may kiss thy cheek without a thought,
And on thy brow his daily journey run,
Which is a bliss for which I've vainly fought.
I am jealous of the bold and wanton wind,
That he may dally with thy flowing hair,
And in thy tresses, sanctuary find,
A freedom that to me he will not spare.
He whispers secrets to thy listening ear,
Whilst I must keep my distance and my place;
The air that holds thy breath I hold most dear,
Yet am denied thy presence and thy grace.
  Thus sun and wind are blessed in their degree,
  To touch thy form, which is forbid to me.

###390###
Thou art a feast unto my starved desire,
A banquet rich with all things good and sweet,
At which my soul, consumed by loving fire,
Doth find its only sustenance to eat.
All other fare, that once did please my taste,
Now seems but coarse and common to my sense;
Compared to thee, 'tis but a barren waste,
Devoid of flavour or of excellence.
My hungry eye, which on thy beauty feeds,
Finds other forms but empty, plain, and poor;
Thy voice alone my raptured hearing needs,
And having thee, my heart can ask no more.
  For thou art all my world's variety,
  And naught is good that is not found in thee.

###391###
When thou art absent, all the world's bright hues
Do lose their lustre and their cheerful guise;
The blushing rose its vibrant colour loses,
And sullen clouds possess the morning skies.
The green of fields, the heavens' deepest blue,
Become a sad and melancholy grey;
There is no joy, nor anything of new,
To lift the gloom of such a weary day.
The world, a stage, when thou art far from me,
Is but a play of sorrows and of sighs;
Each object that my mournful eye doth see,
Reflects the tearful grief that in me lies.
  So doth thy presence all the world sustain,
  And with its lack, brings darkness and great pain.

###392###
The sweetest music that a lute can sound,
When played by hands of most celestial skill,
Is but a harsh and melancholy round
Compared to thy dear voice, that holds me still.
Though symphonies may make the heavens ring,
And sacred choirs may lift the soul to grace,
Their finest art no greater joy can bring
Than simple words spoken within that place
Where thy soft breath gives life to melody,
A music that no instrument can make;
It is a perfect, living harmony
That bids my soul from its deep slumber wake.
  So let musicians put their craft aside;
  Thy voice alone as music shall abide.

###393###
My constant love for thee is as a fever,
That holds my senses in a sickly sway,
From which my weary mind can find no lever,
To put the burning torment of the day.
I am afflicted with a strange disease,
For which the world can offer me no cure;
No balm, no potion gives my spirit ease,
'Tis but a woeful state I must endure.
The learned doctors, with their knowing looks,
Do speak of humours and anatomy,
But find no remedy within their books,
For this strange malady that prisons me.
  My only hope of health remains in thee,
  For thy neglect is what makes sick of me.

###394###
When to the sessions of my life's account,
I summon up the deeds of what is past,
I find that losses grievously surmount
The paltry gains that fortune may have cast.
For wasted youth and opportunity,
For friendships lost and labours spent in vain,
I pay the price with sorrow's currency,
And in the present feel the old loss' pain.
But in this audit of my soul's estate,
Where every entry shows a sorry debt,
I think of thee, and straight my wealth is great,
And all the losses I with ease forget.
  For having thee makes all my balance right,
  And turns my ledger's darkness into light.

###395###
Nature, intending that the world should see
What perfect form her craft was capable of,
Did take her finest elements for thee,
And made thee as a testament of love.
All other beauties that mine eye hath met
Are but poor copies, faulty in design,
Dim shadows of the true form she hath set
As her great masterwork in making thine.
For in thy face, the pattern is complete,
From which all lesser fairness must be judged;
Where grace and truth in lovely union meet,
A standard from which envy is not budged.
  So all that's fair but seeks to be like you,
  And in that trial, proves itself less true.

###396###
When I in weary bed do seek my sleep,
The rest that should my tired body mend,
A company of dreams begins to creep,
And to my thoughts thy lively image send.
My heavy lids, which shut away the light,
Can find no barrier to keep thee out;
Thy form doth pierce the darkness of the night,
And puts my hopes and my desires to rout.
For in that vision thou dost seem so near,
And yet so far, beyond my loving reach,
Which doth awake a fresh and painful tear,
As silent slumber learns from grief to speech.
  Thus day by thought, and night by dream of thee,
  I am possessed, and never can be free.

###397###
A crawling doubt, that venom of the mind,
Doth oft suggest that thy sweet love is feigned,
And that in time, I shall but sorrow find
Where once a paradise of joy hath reigned.
This jealous thought, a traitor in my breast,
Doth whisper that thy vows are light as air,
That thy affections cannot stand the test,
And are no more than false as they are fair.
It poisons all the pleasure I should take,
And turns my trust to anguish and to fear,
So that my very soul begins to ache
Whenever some imagined sign is near.
  Then by some deed, this wretched doubt remove,
  And with one truth, the truth of all thy love.

###398###
Thy cruel frown is like a winter's storm,
That casts a chill upon the hopeful land,
To rob the gentle flower of its form,
With icy breath and a destructive hand.
And thy displeasure, like a freezing wind,
Does make my soul disconsolate and bleak;
No sign of summer's comfort can I find,
Nor hear the soothing words I long to seek.
But shouldst thou change thy humour, and bestow
One simple smile, one look of gentle grace,
The frozen landscape of my heart would know
A sudden spring, and sun upon its face.
  So in thy power lies the changing year;
  Thy smile brings summer, and thy frown brings fear.

###399###
As some devout and faithful pilgrim might
Endure the pains of a long, weary way,
To see the object of his soul's delight,
And at its feet his adoration pay;
So have I journeyed through my lonely days,
With thy dear image as my guiding star,
Ignoring worldly censure or its praise,
To worship at the saintly shrine you are.
I seek no pardon, nor celestial prize,
But that my loyal service you shall see;
The only heaven that is in my eyes
Is but the grace you might bestow on me.
  So let this pilgrim, at his journey's end,
  Find in his goddess a most welcome friend.

###400###
Now with this verse let all my labours cease,
And let this be the last word I shall write
Of thy great worth, and of my soul's sweet peace,
Which finds its being only in thy sight.
For I have spent my wit, my time, my art,
To build for thee a monument of rhyme,
To show the world the treasure of my heart,
And guard thy beauty from the wreck of time.
Let future ages read and come to know
The truth of love that in these lines I prove;
How one true heart, in this world here below,
Did consecrate itself to one true love.
  So ends my song, but not the love I bear;
  That has no end, but lives beyond compare.

###361###
When Phoebus doth his golden banner spread,
And casts his eye on all the world below,
All life is from his warming presence fed,
And by his grace the tender blossoms grow.
The lonely moon, a thief of borrow'd grace,
Reflects a fire that is not her own,
And holds in heav'n a secondary place,
To rule a kingdom from a silver throne.
But when thy face doth turn its light on me,
The sun and moon are trifles to my mind,
For in thine eyes a universe I see,
More bright than any that the spheres can find.
   So let them boast of their celestial sphere,
   Thy beauty makes a constant daylight here.

###362###
Each hour that holds me from thy gentle side
Doth stretch itself into a weary day,
Wherein my heavy-footed sorrows stride,
And hope itself doth falter and decay.
I look for thee in ev'ry stranger's face,
And think I hear thy voice in every sound,
But find no comfort in this lonely place,
Where naught but echoes of my grief abound.
My only solace is a waking dream,
Wherein thy form to memory appears,
A fleeting, unsubstantial, ghostly gleam,
That cannot dry my unavailing tears.
   So Time, be kind, and learn to run with haste,
   Lest all my youth in sorrow be laid waste.

###363###
When wasteful Time brings beauty to decay,
And carves deep furrows in the smoothest brow,
When golden locks are turned to silver-grey,
And youthful vigour must to weakness bow;
Then shall my verse, against this cruel rage,
Preserve the truth of how thou dost appear,
And trap thy summer in a winter's age,
To hold thy living essence ever dear.
This monument of ink my hand doth raise
Shall stand when marble statues turn to dust,
And speak thy beauty to succeeding days,
A sacred record, a most holy trust.
   So, though thy form to common earth must go,
   Thy truer self in these black lines shall grow.

###364###
Mine eye, a painter, doth thy likeness paint,
Upon the living canvas of my soul,
And though its colours be imperfect, faint,
It strives to capture and possess the whole.
Its studio is the chamber of my heart,
Where memory doth hang thy lovely frame,
And for its labour asks for no reward,
Save but to whisper thy most blessed name.
No brush it hath but longing, and no hue
Save that which from thy truest beauty springs;
No other subject can it hold as true,
Nor joy in any other shape it brings.
   Yet this poor image which mine eye hath made,
   Doth cast all other worldly forms in shade.

###365###
This world's a stage of shadows and of seeming,
Where men do act their farces and their fears,
And waste their breath in idle, hollow dreaming,
To be forgot with the succeeding years.
Each struts and frets his hour in borrowed guise,
And speaks his lines with unsubstantial sound;
He wears a mask of falsehood and of lies,
Wherein no speck of honest truth is found.
But thou, my love, when stepping on the scene,
Dost make the painted backdrop feel as true,
Thy honest feeling shames the player queen,
And shows what artifice the rest all do.
   So let them play their roles and take their bow,
   Thou art the only truth mine eyes allow.

###366###
When all the world in silent slumber lies,
And darkness holds the weary earth in keep,
No gentle sleep will visit my poor eyes,
Nor in his peaceful arms my senses steep.
For then my thoughts, a restless, urgent flock,
Do fly to thee, as to their only star,
And pay no heed to the slow, ticking clock,
That measures how from me thou art so far.
They build thy image in the lonely night,
And conjure up the memory of thy face,
A phantom joy, a soul-consuming light,
That fills with thee this dark and empty space.
   And so the night, which should my cares allay,
   Is by thy shadow turned to brighter day.

###367###
What paradox is this strange thing called love,
That brings such pleasure and such bitter pain?
It is a torment sent from heav'n above,
A perfect drought, and a life-giving rain.
Sometimes it is a sea both calm and deep,
Wherein my trusting soul may safely lie;
Then, next, a frantic storm that will not sleep,
Beneath a wrathful and a sunless sky.
One moment, I am king of all I see,
Possessing riches that can have no price;
The next, a beggar, brought to poverty,
By one cold glance or one unkind device.
   Yet rich or poor, in tempest or in peace,
   My love for thee shall never know decrease.

###368###
Thou art a thief, and I have known it long,
Though by thy beauty I am held in thrall;
Thou stol'st my heart, and think'st thou didst no wrong,
And in its place hast left no heart at all.
Thou took'st my peace, and gav'st me reeling doubt,
Thou stol'st my reason with thy subtle art,
My former self is vanquished in a rout,
And of his will, thou hast the greater part.
Thou stol'st my sleep, and left me wakeful hours,
To count my losses in the lonely dark;
Thou art a frost that kills the hopeful flowers,
And for thy crime, thou bear'st no guilty mark.
   Yet I'll not charge thee, for the case is this:
   One stolen kiss would be my only bliss.

###369###
My tongue is bankrupt when it speaks of thee,
My store of words a poor and paltry thing;
For what I feel, and what mine own eyes see,
No poet's verse could e'er have skill to sing.
I might compare thine eyes to stars that shine,
But stars are cold, and have a distant fire;
Or say thy peerless form is half-divine,
But this would make the zealous gods conspire.
I might declare thy cheek a blushing rose,
But soon the rose must wither and decay;
I might in verse thy golden hair enclose,
But gold itself to tarnish finds a way.
   So words must fail, and my poor ink run dry;
   My love is best expressed within mine eye.

###370###
When I in memory do count the cost
Of all my follies and my youthful pride,
And think of all the faithless friends I lost,
And chances that have turned and passed aside;
When I recall the harsh and foolish word
That wounded one I should have held most dear,
And see the bitter consequence I stirred,
My soul is drowned within a sea of fear.
But as despair prepares its final blow,
And all my former self I come to hate,
A single thought of thee doth start to grow,
And clears my mind of its repentant state.
   For having thy sweet love as my reward,
   All past accounts I gladly disregard.

###371###
As when a ship, long-tossed by wind and storm,
That hath for months the angry ocean roamed,
At last discerns a well-remembered form
Of that dear coast where it was wont to be homed;
So doth my soul, long battered by despair,
And driven far by fortune's cruel tide,
Which found no comfort, rest, nor solace where
The winds of sorrow did my journey guide,
Now sight thy face, my harbour and my bay,
The only port where I would be at peace.
At once the clouds of grief all blow away,
And all the torments of the voyage cease.
   So let me in thine arms at anchor lie,
   And in that haven live, and in it die.

###372###
Mine eye and heart are in a mortal fight,
And make their case how they possess thee most;
Mine eye would claim the pleasure of thy sight,
And of its window makes a jealous boast.
My heart replies that in its deepest part
Thy truest image is securely set,
Beyond the fleeting treachery of art,
Where changing light can never be a threat.
The eye doth claim that it did first perceive
The outer form that gave the heart its prize;
The heart doth say, what senses can't conceive,
Within its own devoted chamber lies.
   But I shall end this war and their sad plight,
   Thy form to eye, thy love to heart, by right.

###373###
Now barren Winter lays his frosty hand
On every branch where green leaves used to grow;
A frozen silence lies upon the land,
And all the world is cloaked in patient snow.
The birds are fled that sang in summer's choir,
The chattering brook is fettered in the ice,
And we do huddle 'round a meager fire,
To guard us from the season's cruel device.
This cheerless scene a mirror is to me,
Of how my world did turn to bleak and cold,
The very moment I was kept from thee,
A sorrowful and heavy tale twice-told.
   But thought of thee is my eternal spring,
   Which makes my heart, e'en in this winter, sing.

###374###
That I am deaf to music, some do say,
That pleasing chords possess no charm for me,
That I do turn from choir and roundelay,
And have no care for skillful harmony.
'Tis true that when the cunning minstrel plays,
His lute or viol falls on careless ears;
I find no magic in his practiced lays,
No hidden joy, no cause for sudden tears.
But let them know the reason for this lack:
My soul is tuned to a more perfect sound,
From which, once heard, it cannot travel back,
Nor on a meaner, baser level's found.
   For since I heard thy voice, all other art
   Is but a noise that cannot touch my heart.

###375###
This green-eyed monster, Jealousy, is blind,
And feeds on whispers and on idle air;
It builds a prison in the troubled mind,
And for its keeper places grim Despair.
It turns a loving glance into a lie,
And finds a treason in a simple word;
It teaches the suspicious heart to spy
On things unknown and fantasies absurd.
O, let this madness never seize my soul,
Nor poison what is truest and most pure;
Let trust in thee maintain its full control,
And make my constant, faithful love secure.
   For I should count myself a fool indeed,
   To plant a weed where such a flower should seed.

###376###
In sleep's dark theatre, I saw thee plain,
Not as a shade but as a living thing;
I felt no distance, and I knew no pain,
And heard the very words my soul would sing.
Thou didst appear, and with a gentle hand,
Didst touch my cheek and bid my grief adieu;
I was a king in that enchanted land,
Where all my fondest wishes had come true.
But then the dawn, that cruel and jealous light,
Did tear the curtain of the night apart,
And stole away the treasure of my sight,
And left a waking sorrow in my heart.
   Yet for the joy that such a dream did give,
   I'd choose to sleep, and never truly live.

###377###
The world doth see no blemish in thy face,
And calls thee perfect in both form and mind;
They say that thou art blessed with ev'ry grace,
And not a single, lonely fault can find.
But I, who watch thee with a lover's eye,
Have found a flaw that others cannot see;
A single vice that I cannot deny,
A cruel streak of bitter tyranny.
For thou dost take no pity on the heart
That for thy sake in constant torment lives;
Thou play'st a proud and a disdainful part,
And no relief to his quiet pleading gives.
   And yet this flaw I love more than the rest,
   For it alone puts my devotion to the test.

###378###
My heart, one day, did fly from out my breast,
And to thy own for sanctuary fled;
It thought to find a place of peaceful rest,
And in thy bosom make its lasting bed.
Thy heart, surprised, then fled its rightful place,
And seeking refuge, came to live in me;
And now it looks from out my mournful face,
To see itself, where it desires to be.
So we are crossed, and each of us doth bear
A heart that is not ours, and is misplaced;
Mine lives with thee, a willing prisoner there,
While thine in me with sorrow is encased.
   Return my heart, or keep it, as you choose,
   For in this trade, I find I cannot lose.

###379###
When others praise thee with a honeyed tongue,
And to thine ear their flattering verses bring,
Believe them not, for from false hearts are sprung
The empty words that such false poets sing.
They call thee sun and moon, and rose, and star,
And with their praise they make a hollow sound;
They know not what thy truest virtues are,
Nor on what soil their roots may best be found.
But I, who love in silence from afar,
And rarely dare to speak my full devotion,
Do know thee better than these praisers are,
And measure thee with a more just emotion.
   Their praise is breath, but my love is a deed,
   On which thy truth, if thou shouldst look, may read.

###380###
So here I stand, at the conclusion drawn
Of twenty arguments my ink hath penned;
From silent grief to the most hopeful dawn,
My love for thee hath been their only end.
I have compared thee to all things most fair,
And railed at Time that seeks to do thee wrong;
I have enshrined thee from the world's despair,
And made thy name the subject of my song.
I have spent wit, and plundered my poor brain,
And vexed my Muse to make her labours shine;
I have done all to make my purpose plain,
And prove my love more worthy to be thine.
   Now, words are done; what more is there to say?
   Bestow thy love, or bid me go away.

###261###
When forty winters shall besiege thy brow,
And dig deep trenches in thy beauty’s field,
Thy youth’s proud livery, so gazed on now,
Will be a tattered weed, of little worth held.
Then being asked where all thy beauty lies,
Where all the treasure of thy lusty days,
To say within thine own deep-sunken eyes,
Were an all-eating shame and thriftless praise.
How much more praise deserved thy beauty’s use,
If thou couldst answer, ‘This fair child of mine
Shall sum my count and make my old excuse,’
Proving his beauty by succession thine.
  This were to be new made when thou art old,
  And see thy blood warm when thou feel'st it cold.

###262###
When in the chronicle of wasted time
I see descriptions of the fairest wights,
And beauty making beautiful old rhyme
In praise of ladies dead, and lovely knights;
Then in the blazon of sweet beauty’s best,
Of hand, of foot, of lip, of eye, of brow,
I see their antique pen would have expressed
Even such a beauty as you master now.
So all their praises are but prophecies
Of this our time, all you prefiguring,
And for they looked but with divining eyes,
They had not skill enough your worth to sing.
  For we which now behold these present days,
  Have eyes to wonder, but lack tongues to praise.

###263###
My love is strengthened, though more weak in seeming;
I love not less, though I appear so cold;
That love is merchandised whose rich esteeming
The owner’s tongue doth publish everywhere.
Our love was new, and then but in the spring,
When I was wont to greet it with my lays,
As Philomel in summer’s front doth sing,
And stops her pipe in growth of riper days.
Not that the summer is less pleasant now
Than when her mournful hymns did hush the night,
But that wild music burdens every bough,
And sweets grown common lose their dear delight.
  Therefore like her, I sometime hold my tongue,
  Because I would not dull you with my song.

###264###
Against that time, if ever that time come,
When I shall see thee frown on my defects,
When as thy love hath cast his utmost sum,
Called to that audit by advised respects;
Against that time when thou shalt strangely pass,
