And scarcely greet me with that sun, thine eye,
When love converted from the thing it was
Shall reasons find of settled gravity;
Against that time do I insconce me here
Within the knowledge of mine own desert,
And this my hand against myself uprear,
To guard the lawful reasons on thy part.
  To leave poor me, thou hast the strength of laws,
  Since why to love, I can allege no cause.

###265###
Shall I compare thee to a summer’s day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate.
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer’s lease hath all too short a date.
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimmed;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance, or nature’s changing course untrimmed.
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow’st,
Nor shall death brag thou wander’st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow’st.
  So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,
  So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###266###
So is it not with me as with that Muse,
Stirred by a painted beauty to his verse,
Who heaven itself for ornament doth use,
And every fair with his fair doth rehearse,
Making a couplement of proud compare
With sun and moon, with earth and sea’s rich gems,
With April’s first-born flowers, and all things rare
That heaven’s air in this huge rondure hems.
O let me, true in love, but truly write,
And then believe me, my love is as fair
As any mother’s child, though not so bright
As those gold candles fixed in heaven’s air.
  Let them say more that like of hearsay well;
  I will not praise, that purpose not to sell.

###267###
Let those who are in favour with their stars
Of public honour and proud titles boast,
Whilst I, whom fortune of such triumph bars,
Unlooked for joy in that I honour most.
Great princes’ favourites their fair leaves spread
But as the marigold at the sun’s eye,
And in themselves their pride lies buried,
For at a frown they in their glory die.
The painful warrior famousèd for fight,
After a thousand victories once foiled,
Is from the book of honour razèd quite,
And all the rest forgot for which he toiled.
  Then happy I that love and am beloved
  Where I may not remove nor be removed.

###268###
Weary with toil, I haste me to my bed,
The dear repose for limbs with travel tired,
But then begins a journey in my head
To work my mind, when body’s work’s expired.
For then my thoughts, from far where I abide,
Intend a zealous pilgrimage to thee,
And keep my drooping eyelids open wide,
Looking on darkness which the blind do see.
Save that my soul’s imaginary sight
Presents thy shadow to my sightless view,
Which like a jewel hung in ghastly night,
Makes black night beauteous and her old face new.
  Lo thus, by day my limbs, by night my mind,
  For thee, and for myself, no quiet find.

###269###
How can I then return in happy plight,
That am debarred the benefit of rest?
When day’s oppression is not eased by night,
But day by night and night by day oppressed,
And each, though enemies to either’s reign,
Do in consent shake hands to torture me,
The one by toil, the other to complain
How far I toil, still farther off from thee.
I tell the day, to please him thou art bright,
And dost him grace when clouds do blot the heaven:
So flatter I the swart-complexioned night,
When sparkling stars twire not thou gild’st the even.
  But day doth daily draw my sorrows longer,
  And night doth nightly make grief’s strength seem stronger.

###270###
When in disgrace with fortune and men’s eyes,
I all alone beweep my outcast state,
And trouble deaf heaven with my bootless cries,
And look upon myself and curse my fate,
Wishing me like to one more rich in hope,
Featured like him, like him with friends possessed,
Desiring this man’s art, and that man’s scope,
With what I most enjoy contented least;
Yet in these thoughts myself almost despising,
Haply I think on thee, and then my state,
Like to the lark at break of day arising
From sullen earth, sings hymns at heaven’s gate.
  For thy sweet love remembered such wealth brings,
  That then I scorn to change my state with kings.

###271###
Being your slave, what should I do but tend
Upon the hours and times of your desire?
I have no precious time at all to spend,
Nor services to do till you require.
Nor dare I chide the world-without-end hour,
Whilst I, my sovereign, watch the clock for you,
Nor think the bitterness of absence sour,
When you have bid your servant once adieu.
Nor dare I question with my jealous thought,
Where you may be, or your affairs suppose,
But like a sad slave stay and think of naught
Save where you are, how happy you make those.
  So true a fool is love, that in your will,
  Though you do anything, he thinks no ill.

###272###
My glass shall not persuade me I am old,
So long as youth and thou are of one date,
But when in thee time’s furrows I behold,
Then look I death my days should expiate.
For all that beauty that doth cover thee,
Is but the seemly raiment of my heart,
Which in thy breast doth live, as thine in me;
How can I then be elder than thou art?
O therefore, love, be of thyself so wary
As I not for myself, but for thee will,
Bearing thy heart, which I will keep so chary
As tender nurse her babe from faring ill.
  Presume not on thy heart when mine is slain;
  Thou gav’st me thine not to give back again.

###273###
So are you to my thoughts as food to life,
Or as sweet-seasoned showers are to the ground;
And for the peace of you I hold such strife
As 'twixt a miser and his wealth is found:
Now proud as an enjoyer, and anon
Doubting the filching age will steal his treasure;
Now counting best to be with you alone,
Then bettered that the world may see my pleasure;
Sometime all full with feasting on your sight,
And by and by clean starvèd for a look;
Possessing or pursuing no delight
Save what is had or must from you be took.
  Thus do I pine and surfeit day by day,
  Or gluttoning on all, or all away.

###274###
Mine eye hath played the painter and hath stelled
Thy beauty’s form in table of my heart;
My body is the frame wherein ’tis held,
And perspective it is best painter’s art.
For through the painter must you see his skill
To find where your true image pictured lies,
Which in my bosom’s shop is hanging still,
That hath his windows glazèd with thine eyes.
Now see what good turns eyes for eyes have done:
Mine eyes have drawn thy shape, and thine for me
Are windows to my breast, where-through the sun
Delights to peep, to gaze therein on thee.
  Yet eyes this cunning want to grace their art,
  They draw but what they see, know not the heart.

###275###
Thy bosom is endearèd with all hearts,
Which I by lacking have supposed dead,
And there reigns Love, and all Love’s loving parts,
And all those friends which I thought burièd.
How many a holy and obsequious tear
Hath dear religious love stol’n from mine eye,
As interest of the dead, which now appear
But things removed that hidden in thee lie.
Thou art the grave where buried love doth live,
Hung with the trophies of my lovers gone,
Who all their parts of me to thee did give;
That due of many now is thine alone.
  Their images I loved I view in thee,
  And thou, all they, hast all the all of me.

###276###
From you have I been absent in the spring,
When proud-pied April, dressed in all his trim,
Hath put a spirit of youth in everything,
That heavy Saturn laughed and leapt with him.
Yet nor the lays of birds, nor the sweet smell
Of different flowers in odour and in hue,
Could make me any summer’s story tell,
Or from their proud lap pluck them where they grew.
Nor did I wonder at the lily’s white,
Nor praise the deep vermilion in the rose;
They were but sweet, but figures of delight,
Drawn after you, you pattern of all those.
  Yet seemed it winter still, and you away,
  As with your shadow I with these did play.

###277###
They that have power to hurt, and will do none,
That do not do the thing they most do show,
Who moving others, are themselves as stone,
Unmovèd, cold, and to temptation slow:
They rightly do inherit heaven’s graces,
And husband nature’s riches from expense;
They are the lords and owners of their faces,
Others but stewards of their excellence.
The summer’s flower is to the summer sweet,
Though to itself it only live and die;
But if that flower with base infection meet,
The basest weed outbraves his dignity.
  For sweetest things turn sourest by their deeds;
  Lilies that fester smell far worse than weeds.

###278###
That time of year thou mayst in me behold
When yellow leaves, or none, or few do hang
Upon those boughs which shake against the cold,
Bare ruined choirs, where late the sweet birds sang.
In me thou seest the twilight of such day
As after sunset fadeth in the west,
Which by and by black night doth take away,
Death’s second self, that seals up all in rest.
In me thou seest the glowing of such fire
That on the ashes of his youth doth lie,
As the death-bed whereon it must expire,
Consumed with that which it was nourished by.
  This thou perceiv’st, which makes thy love more strong,
  To love that well which thou must leave ere long.

###279###
No longer mourn for me when I am dead
Than you shall hear the surly sullen bell
Give warning to the world that I am fled
From this vile world with vilest worms to dwell.
Nay, if you read this line, remember not
The hand that writ it, for I love you so,
That I in your sweet thoughts would be forgot,
If thinking on me then should make you woe.
O if, I say, you look upon this verse,
When I perhaps compounded am with clay,
Do not so much as my poor name rehearse,
But let your love even with my life decay;
  Lest the wise world should look into your moan,
  And mock you with me after I am gone.

###280###
Let me not to the marriage of true minds
Admit impediments. Love is not love
Which alters when it alteration finds,
Or bends with the remover to remove.
O no, it is an ever-fixèd mark
That looks on tempests and is never shaken;
It is the star to every wandering barque,
Whose worth’s unknown, although his height be taken.
Love’s not Time’s fool, though rosy lips and cheeks
Within his bending sickle’s compass come;
Love alters not with his brief hours and weeks,
But bears it out even to the edge of doom.
  If this be error and upon me proved,
  I never writ, nor no man ever loved.

###341###
My love for thee hath overthrown my state,
A willing captive to thy conquering eye,
And sealed my soul to a most cursèd fate,
Where freedom’s hope can only wish to die.
Thou hast by some strange magic seized my heart,
And planted there the standard of thy grace,
So reason yields and gives no help to art
To paint a world that shows no other face.
Thus in this prison I am bound to see
The very source of all my sweet demise,
And what I should most desperately flee, must be
The sole reward of my life’s enterprise.
  Yet grant me this, and I'll not curse thy power,
  That I might serve thee for one fleeting hour.

###342###
When I consider how relentless Time
Doth with his cruel and ever-wasting hand
Make of our youth a momentary prime,
A fleeting bloom that cannot long command;
Then in my thoughts I look upon thy face,
Where Nature's finest work is set so fair,
And see no sign of age's coming trace,
Nor any fault that time cannot repair.
But in my mind, a wiser sorrow plays,
To know this beauty time shall do it wrong;
Yet thy perfection through these coming days
Shall live immortal in my humble song.
  Let age then come, and steal thy beauty's part,
  It cannot touch the image in my heart.

###343###
Dost thou behold the pale and changeful moon,
That borrows from the sun her silver light?
She waxes full, but wanes again too soon,
And leaves a greater darkness in the night.
So is thy love, a fair and shifting face,
That promises it will forever stay,
But in a moment withdraws all its grace
And turns from my poor heart to steal away.
I thought thy constant heart was all my own,
And swore by every oath and sacred vow,
But thy firm faith was never truly known,
For all thy promises are broken now.
  Thus have I learned thy love is just as false
  As the pale moon, whose borrowed splendour falls.

###344###
Within the pages of my book, a strife
Is waged, where two great forces take the field:
Fell Time, that seeks to end thy beauty's life,
And thy sweet form, that vows it shall not yield.
He brings his armies of relentless war,
His winter frosts and age's pale-faced might;
But thou hast champions that are greater far,
My words, that for thy cause do bravely fight.
And in my verse I shall so sing thy praise,
That future ages, reading, shall all see
How thy perfection lit my humble days,
And grant my pen a famous victory.
  For though Time mars, within these living lines
  Thy constant essence, ever-youthful, shines.

###345###
Since thou, my joy, wert forced to go away,
The world hath lost its bright and vibrant hue;
The sun itself, that rules the weary day,
Seems but a phantom, hollow and untrue.
The fairest flowers that I chance to see
Now lack the lustre of their former light;
For how can beauty have a right to be
When thy dear absence turns my day to night?
No painter's skill, no poet's knowing art
Can make these barren, empty hours seem
Aught but a wound upon my grieving heart,
A frightful vision in a fever-dream.
  For all the world is dark when thou art gone,
  A sky that waits in vain to see its sun.

###346###
That green-eyed monster, Jealousy of mind,
Doth plant within my soul a bitter seed,
Which in my doubtful fancies I do find
Upon my own tormented thoughts to feed.
It takes a word, a glance, a passing thought,
And gives it soil and makes it take deep root,
Till from this nothing, something foul is wrought,
Which bears a most unwholesome, poisoned fruit.
So am I sickened in my truest part,
As this dark weed with choking tendrils grows,
And winds itself about my trusting heart,
Until no peace my weary spirit knows.
  If thou prove false, then let this sickness turn
  My very being, and my soul to burn.

###347###
When I am given o'er to gentle sleep,
And darkness holds the waking world in night,
Thy precious image in my mind I keep,
A private sun that gives my darkness light.
Then in my dreams I feel thee standing near,
A perfect vision, wondrous to be shown,
And all my sorrow turns to joy, my dear,
Before the truth of morning makes it flown.
For that short time I am most truly blest,
But wake to find a cruel and empty ache;
The happy phantom leaves my soul no rest,
For what can morning give for what it takes?
  So do I curse the coming of the day,
  Which brings the sun, but steals my love away.

###348###
I have heard music of the rarest kind,
From trembling lute-strings and from silver flute,
And lofty songs that captivate the mind,
Whose sweet persuasion makes the critic mute.
Yet all these harmonies that so astound
Are but a discord to my longing ear,
An empty, frail, and unimportant sound,
When thy soft voice I am not blessed to hear.
Thy speech is music of a higher make,
A perfect cadence, flowing, rich, and deep,
Whose every note can cause my heart to break,
Or soothe its sorrows and its passions sleep.
  Let others praise the master's skilled accord,
  Thy softest whisper is my true reward.

###349###
As some poor miser, having found by chance
A hidden jewel of a worth unknown,
Dares not to show it with a boastful glance,
But keeps the treasure for his eyes alone;
So I, who found thee shining in the dark,
A perfect diamond in a world of stone,
Did seek to hide the bright, celestial spark,
And treasure up thy beauty as my own.
But such a fire cannot be held unseen,
For thy great virtues set the world alight;
Thy grace, thy wit, thy fair and noble mien
Shine forth and put all lesser gems to flight.
  Now all men gaze, and I, poor fool, must share
  The public sun, who kept thee as my air.

###350###
Thy heart is fashioned from the coldest stone,
And in thy veins doth icy water run;
Thou tak'st a cruel pleasure to be shown
The woeful sorrow that thy pride hath won.
Though I do praise thee with my every breath,
And make thy name a consecrated thing,
Thou art content to sentence me to death,
And heed no tribute that my verses bring.
What glory is there in a conquest made
O'er one who yields and lays his weapons down?
What honour in the lover's heart betrayed,
To wear his suffering as a victor's crown?
  Be not so proud of this poor victory,
  For in my ruin, thou art shamed with me.

###351###
My love is like a fever in the blood,
That makes me burn with anguish and desire;
It is a famine and a bounteous flood,
A chilling torment and a holy fire.
One moment I am raised to heaven's height,
Believing I am favoured by thy grace;
The next I am cast down to endless night,
When some dark frown appears upon thy face.
This constant turning 'twixt two sharp extremes
Doth vex my spirit and consume my health;
My life is but a war of troubled dreams
Concerning my poor heart's uncertain wealth.
  So is my state, which thou alone canst mend:
  A pleasing sickness that I pray won't end.

###352###
Behold the winter, in his grim array,
Doth strip the forest of its summer green,
And with his icy breath drive far away
The cheerful warmth that in the world was seen.
The brook is captured in a silent frost,
The birds are fled, the flowers turned to dust;
All that was fair and vibrant now is lost,
Betrayed by Nature's cold and cruelest trust.
So art thou, love, a winter to my soul,
Since thy fair heart to me hath frozen grown;
Thy cold disdain doth take a heavy toll,
And makes my life a landscape bare and lone.
  Yet in my heart a foolish springtime lies,
  And waits for sunshine from thy absent eyes.

###353###
My foolish eye and my more foolish heart
Do now engage in a most bitter war,
For each doth claim to own the greater part
Of that dear sight for which I did thee star.
My eye denies my heart thy picture's right,
And says thy image in its gaze doth dwell;
My heart replies it holds thee day and night,
A secret captive in its loving cell.
So to decide this civil, inward plea,
A quest of thoughts is summoned in my breast;
And their true verdict must henceforth agree
That in my body thou canst have no rest.
  For eye and heart, they share thy form so true:
  My eye thy shape, my heart the love of you.

###354###
As one who stands upon the fading shore
And watches as a ship puts out to sea,
To see the sails grow smaller and no more,
And feel the weight of what is not to be,
So do I stand and watch thee drift away,
A vessel laden with my fondest hope;
I track thy course throughout the lonely day
Until my sight can no more with thee cope.
Thou art bound for some far and happy land,
While I am left with sorrow for my guide,
Upon this bleak and ever-shifting sand,
To be washed over by a mournful tide.
  Yet send some word, if e'er thy voyage ends,
  That my poor heart may try to make amends.

###355###
My worth is nothing, measured by itself,
A barren field where no good thing can grow,
A dusty book left on a forgotten shelf,
A worthless coin that no man would bestow.
My wit is dull, my tongue is slow to speak,
My verse is poor and stammers in its art,
My spirit is uncertain, faint, and weak,
And melancholy claims my heavy heart.
But when the sun of thy dear favour shines
Upon this wretched and forsaken ground,
A sudden, wondrous miracle designs
That grace and beauty everywhere abound.
  Thus I am made by what thy love doth give,
  And in thy sight alone, I truly live.

###356###
When present sorrows press upon my soul,
And fortune's spite doth threaten to undo
The little joy that makes my spirit whole,
I find my solace in the thought of you.
I do not need to see thy living face,
Or hear the music of thy spoken word;
I need but summon from its secret place
A memory, the sweetest ever heard.
And in that thought, thy very form appears,
As bright as when I saw thee first and best,
And with its light, it dries my falling tears
And gives my weary, troubled spirit rest.
  So let grief come, and bring its dark despair,
  One thought of thee is armour I can wear.

###357###
My love, some say that in thy face they find
A fault or two where beauty should not stray,
Or that thy humour is not always kind,
And thy sun shines not on them every day.
Let them find spots upon the summer moon,
Or chide the rose because its thorn doth prick;
Their judgment is delivered far too soon,
Their eyes by envy or by folly sick.
For in my sight, these things they call a flaw
Are but the marks that make thy form divine,
Subjecting thee to Nature's gentle law,
Which proves thy very substance to be mine.
  Those little faults which critics harsh decry
  Are but the reasons that I love thee; I.

###358###
If all my love were made a kingdom vast,
With soaring mountains and with fertile plains,
From some grey dawn unto a future last,
My heart would be the country where thou reign'st.
Thy every wish would be a royal decree,
Thy passing frown a threat of bloody war,
Thy smile a peace for all the realm to see,
And thy dear self my kingdom's guiding star.
My thoughts would be thy faithful subjects all,
Devoted to thy service and thy will,
Prepared to rise, or ready for to fall,
As thy commanding moods would have them still.
  So rule me, sweet, for in this state I'm free:
  A willing subject to thy monarchy.

###359###
Speak, love, I pray thee, break this silent pact
That holds thy tongue in such a cruel repose;
This quiet is a torment, and in fact
A harsher sentence than a storm of blows.
For were you angry, and your words were spears,
My heart would bleed, but know for what it bled;
But this long silence conjures up my fears
And leaves the truest reasons all unsaid.
Dost thou forbear from pity or from hate?
Is this a trial of my constant soul?
Or is my love condemned by silent fate
To guess at that which makes it less than whole?
  Say but one word, be it of fire or frost,
  For in thy silence, all my world is lost.

###360###
If ever pity in thy heart held sway,
Or gentle mercy found a home with thee,
Then look upon my sorrow-eaten day,
And grant a crumb of comfort unto me.
I ask not for the treasure of thy love,
Which is a prize I am not fit to win;
I do not ask for blessings from above,
To cleanse my soul of its devoted sin.
I ask but for a look, a passing sigh,
A brief acknowledgement that I exist,
Lest in this state of misery I die,
A thing by all, and even by thee, missed.
  Then in thy pity, let this much be shown:
  You saw my grief, though you have left me lone.

###321###
When silent night doth draw her sable veil,
My thoughts to thee on restless wings take flight,
And tell my soul a far more wondrous tale
Than all the stars that ornament the night.
Thy memory, a constant, burning fire,
Doth banish shadows that about me creep,
And fills my waking dreams with sweet desire,
While all the weary, troubled world doth sleep.
But when the dawn doth steal the dark away,
And golden light upon the land is shed,
I find no comfort in the coming day,
For thoughts of thee still occupy my head.
   So night and day are one, I must confess,
   Held captive by thy face and loveliness.

###322###
I mark the rose in summer's gentle reign,
Whose crimson pride a season's grace doth see,
Yet know that frost shall come with cold disdain
And end what youth and ardour made to be.
The sturdy oak that doth the storm defy
Must yield its leafy crown when autumn's near,
And 'neath a cold and unforgiving sky
Lament the passing of the fruitful year.
But thy fair beauty knows no such decay,
No winter's breath can mar thy perfect hue;
It burns more brightly with each passing day,
Forever constant, and forever new.
   So let time spoil the blossoms on the bough,
   It hath no power to change what is thee now.

###323###
That face, a map of heaven's own design,
Wherein each feature doth with grace conspire,
Doth yet conceal a heart of such incline
As burns with falsehood and with cold desire.
The fairest apple, blushing on the tree,
May hide a worm that gnaws its core within,
And so thy virtue, as it seems to me,
Is but a painted mask to hide thy sin.
I thought thee angel, sent from spheres above,
A balm to heal my soul's long-festering wound,
But found a demon in the guise of love
On which no truth nor honesty is found.
   For beauty's craft can treacherously disguise
   A spirit filled with naught but hateful lies.

###324###
When tyrant Time with his unpitying hand
Shall turn to dust thy youth and perfect form,
And thou in age's winter bleak shalt stand,
Bereft of shelter from life's cruel storm;
When those bright eyes, which are my suns by day,
Grow dim and lose the fire they now possess,
And all thy flowing hair is turned to grey,
A mirror to the world's own emptiness;
Yet in these lines thy beauty shall not fade,
But live anew for ages yet to come,
A lasting monument my love hath made,
To strike the envious tongues of critics dumb.
   So thou shalt thrive when marble turns to nought,
   Preserv'd immortal in my loving thought.

###325###
Since I am made to be so far away
And lose the blessed comfort of thy sight,
Each waking hour seems longer than a day,
And every lonely moment is a night.
My books and tasks no consolation find,
For when I look I see thy vacant place;
Thy perfect image governs all my mind,
I see in every stranger's glance thy face.
What miseries doth absence teach the heart,
To count the cost of every unheard plea;
To know what sorrows rend a soul apart,
And make each passing minute misery.
   But this sad journey's end will comfort be,
   When its long road doth lead me back to thee.

###326###
Upon this earth we strut our little stage,
And each man plays his melancholy part;
We fret through youth and then complain in age,
And learn our lines with an unwilling heart.
Some play the king, some play the fool, I see,
And some the lover, signing with unrest;
But all the world's false roles do not agree
With the true grief that lodges in my breast.
For this sad character forbids me play
The happy wit, or speak a joyful line;
My only hope is for that blessed day
When I may drop this mask and call thee mine.
   And so my tragic scene I must play on,
   Until the final curtain falls, and I am gone.

###327###
My foolish eye, how canst thou tell this lie,
And swear to me thy outward form is true,
When my poor heart, whose judgment cannot die,
Doth find in thee a spirit false and new?
Thine eyes a tale of honesty do hold,
But my sad heart a different truth doth know;
A warning by my reason hath been told
That fairest looks are oft a hollow show.
My sight, bewitched, still argues I am wrong,
And for thy case a thousand times doth plead;
This fond delusion is so passing strong,
It fashions virtue where there's none to read.
   So must I live, by this division rent,
   By sight beguiled, by my own heart condemned.

###328###
Let others praise the beauty of the rose,
Or lips whose redness doth the cherry stain;
A higher love my constant spirit knows,
Which holds such earthly treasures in disdain.
It is the beauty of thy noble mind
That holds my soul in willing servitude,
A truer wealth than any I might find
In fleeting charms by hungry eyes pursued.
For physical perfection will decay,
And passion's fire will turn to embers cold,
But thy sweet reason holds eternal sway,
A thing of worth that never can grow old.
   This sacred bond no earthly power can break,
   'Tis for thy soul I love thee, for its sake.

###329###
My soul, a ship upon a sea of doubt,
Hath lost the stars by which it used to steer;
The winds of sorrow toss it all about,
And every wave is partnered with a tear.
I see no harbour where I might find rest,
No friendly shore to welcome my distress,
The chart of hope is stolen from my breast,
And all my course is utter aimlessness.
But when despair would have me sink and drown,
And yield my fortunes to the ocean's might,
I see thy love, a light from heaven's crown,
A single star that banishes the night.
   Thou art my guide, my compass true and sole,
   Who saves my vessel and directs my soul.

###330###
My glass doth show the work of winter's frost,
A web of lines where youth did once reside;
It tells me of the vigour I have lost,
And wounds the flimsy battlements of pride.
This face, which once could front the world with joy,
Is now a ruin for the world to see;
Time, that doth all created things destroy,
Hath spent his cruelest injuries on me.
How then can I, so weathered and so worn,
Present this faded gift for thee to take?
My love's a burden, of my weakness born,
Which for thy own sweet grace thou shouldst forsake.
   Yet if thou wilt but love this poor decay,
   My youth shall be renewed in thee this day.

###331###
Two loves I have, a comfort and despair,
Which like two spirits do suggest me still;
The one an angel, virtuous and fair,
The other tempts my better part to ill.
My good angel, a man of saintly hue,
Doth counsel me to keep my honour bright;
My bad, a woman, coloured like the night,
Doth lure my soul from all that's good and true.
And now I fear my angel is undone,
His virtue captured by her wicked snare;
I see them whisp'ring, and my friend is won
To fall from grace and languish in her care.
   So I have lost them both, it seems to me,
   My friend to her, and her to misery.

###332###
Let fortune's wheel turn howsoe'er it will,
And cast me down from some once-lofty state;
Let slanderous tongues their venomed poison spill,
And turn my former glories into hate.
Let envious rivals steal my worldly prize,
And fickle crowds forget my very name;
Let poverty look from my hollowed eyes,
And clothe my weary body in its shame.
These are but trifles, sorrows of a day,
The shifting sands of life's uncertain shore;
They have no power to take thy love away,
Nor make my true devotion any poor.
   For having thee, a treasure I possess,
   Which makes all worldly loss seem happiness.

###333###
Her voice is music, but of such a kind
As makes the lute and viol sound but coarse;
The sweetest airs that craftsmen ever find
Seem but a noise when measured by its force.
When she doth speak, the birds in envy hush,
Ashamed to find their simple songs outdone,
And on her words the listening angels rush,
To learn a beauty that they had not known.
I need no choir, no organ's solemn sound
To lift my spirit to some holy place;
A greater, purer worship I have found
Within the soft enchantment of her grace.
   Her every word's a note in heaven's song,
   And where she is, is where my ears belong.

###334###
I sit me down to write, and find no words
That can contain the measure of thy worth;
My barren fancy, like a flightless bird,
Remains forever chained unto the earth.
My clumsy hand, which strives to serve thee well,
Doth make a blot of what was pure and bright;
My mind, a prisoner in its earthly cell,
Can shed no beam of intellectual light.
And so these lines must fail, as all attempts,
To build in verse a home for thy renown;
My ink is poor, my sorry wit exempts
Itself from earning thee a proper crown.
   Yet read my love in what my pen doth miss,
   For where words fail, my silence tells thee this.

###335###
When I behold the state of this vile world,
Where vice is robed and virtue goes in rags,
Where truth's fair banner is kept tightly furled,
And greedy falsehood of its triumph brags;
When I see justice bought and basely sold,
And simple goodness treated with contempt,
And purest love exchanged for cursed gold,
And not a soul from this decay exempt;
Then in my heart a sickness takes its root,
And I desire to quit this mortal fray,
To see this tree of evil bear no fruit,
And from this dunghill steal myself away.
   Yet thinking on thy goodness makes me stay,
   Lest leaving thee, I leave my only day.

###336###
That bounty nature gave thee to possess,
That store of beauty granted to thy face,
Is not thy own to hoard in idleness,
But is a loan to honour time and place.
Thou art its steward, and must give account
Of how this treasure has been put to use;
For from thy spring a living stream should mount,
Lest beauty's line should suffer this abuse.
Then make a copy of thy perfect form,
A child to bear thy likeness and thy name,
To keep thy summer safe from winter's storm,
And lend new fuel to thy fading flame.
   For beauty held, and not passed on, shall die,
   And with the holder in the cold earth lie.

###337###
Forgive the faults that in my nature dwell,
The hasty word, the thoughtless, selfish deed;
My better angel struggles in this hell,
And strives to plant a more virtuous seed.
For I am prone to jealousy's sharp sting,
And quick to doubt where I should wholly trust;
A melancholy humour makes me cling
To idle fears that crumble into dust.
But these are shadows, not the substance true
Of that devotion which I hold for thee;
My soul's compass still points its north to you,
Across the waves of my infirmity.
   Then chide my weakness, but do not condemn,
   For my worst self, my love shall conquer them.

###338###
What paradox is this, that brings me pain,
Yet in that pain I find my sweetest joy?
What sacred thirst is quenched by bitter rain,
What strange new cure doth poison's art employ?
To be thy captive is to be most free,
To lose thy favour is a kind of death;
Thy harshest frown is gentleness to me,
And every sigh of thine is my life's breath.
This fever, which would make another weak,
Doth fill my veins with unaccustomed might;
This wound, from which a dying man would shriek,
Is now the source of all my spirit's light.
   So let me suffer, and in strife rejoice,
   Since all my sorrow's born of thy sweet voice.

###339###
The poet's praise of Helen's fatal grace,
Or Cleopatra's wiles on Egypt's shore,
But prophesied the beauty of thy face,
And dreamt of that which I do now adore.
They saw in shadow what I see in sun,
And guessed the pattern of a perfect thing;
Their ancient verses, though so finely spun,
Did of an unknown, future glory sing.
Their fairest subject was a cloudy glass
Wherein a vision of thy form appeared;
They were but heralds, whom thou dost surpass
As truth surpasses that which is but feared.
   For they described but what their dreams could find,
   And thou art the reality behind.

###340###
Let this last verse a solemn contract be,
Sealed with the ink that writes my love for thee,
A testament for all the world to see,
That thou art all of hope and life to me.
No change of fortune, nor the creeping blight
Of cankered age, which withers all things fair,
Shall have the power to dim this holy light,
Or turn my constant vow into despair.
So when my body to the dust is sent,
And my poor name forgotten by the throng,
Let this one poem be my monument,
And prove my love was true, and pure, and strong.
   This much I swear, and what I write is true,
   My first and final love belongs to you.

###281###
When in thy presence all my senses gaze,
I find a torment in a thing so bright,
And wander lost within a sunlit maze,
As if sweet day had turned to darkest night.
Thy gentle voice is music that I hear,
A sound to make a raging tempest still;
It banishes all sorrow and all ill,
And makes the world a place no longer dear.
Yet when thou art away, a diff'rent breast
Doth house a heart where only sorrows grow,
And puts my soul's endurance to the test,
That I the depths of misery may know.
  So is thy pow'r a strange and potent art,
  To mend, and in the mending, break my heart.

###282###
That cruel thief, old Time, which steals the face
Of youth and beauty with a heavy hand,
Will leave no remnant of its former grace,
But turn to dust what seems too fair to stand.
The rose's blush, the lily's purest bloom,
Must yield their sweetness to his cold decree,
And kings themselves must find a narrow tomb,
For from his grasp no living thing is free.
But thy perfection shall not so decay,
Nor shall thy summer know a winter's line;
For in this verse thou shalt not pass away,
But in eternal characters shalt shine.
  While eyes can read and hearts have cause to be,
  This poem lives, and this gives life to thee.

###283###
Mine own self-doubt, a most unwelcome thought,
Hath conjured phantoms that before my eyes
Do dance and mock what thy true love hath wrought,
And whisper to my soul deceiving lies.
Each kindly word that thou dost softly speak
A double meaning to my mind conveys,
It finds me strong, and yet it leaves me weak,
To be so lost in such a jealous haze.
This green-eyed sickness makes my reason frail,
And turns to poison all that was so fair;
It bids my once-unshakable trust to fail,
And builds a prison from the empty air.
  So let me kill this monster in my mind,
  Or in its madness no relief I'll find.

###284###
When I am but a memory and dust,
And this poor body to the earth gives breath,
My love for thee, a consecrated trust,
Shall live beyond the silent rule of Death.
For on this page thy beauty I shall hold,
Against the wreckage of consuming age,
Thy story in these characters enrolled,
Shall be forever on a living stage.
And those that come when we are laid to rest
Shall read these lines and marvel at thy praise,
And say that you of all were truly blessed
To be the sun of those forgotten days.
  So fear not time, nor envy's wicked art,
  For thou art graven here upon my heart.

###285###
Now winter's hand upon the world is ground,
And all the trees are barren, stark, and grey;
No bird is heard to make a cheerful sound,
To welcome in the pale and shortened day.
A brittle frost upon the window lies,
The sun itself doth seem to have grown old;
A sheet of white across the landscape flies,
And in my soul I feel a matching cold.
For thou, my summer, art too far away,
Thy warmth and light are but a fading dream,
And in this gloom my heavy thoughts do stray,
Like stones that lie within a frozen stream.
  Let but thy presence, like the sun, return,
  And for this winter my poor heart shall burn.

###286###
This world's a stage where all must play their part,
And we are actors for our fleeting plays,
Who learn our lines to speak them with our art,
And strut and fret for our allotted days.
Some play the king, in purple robes they seem,
And some the fool, who jests to hide a part
Of sorrow buried deep within a dream,
While some as lovers break a tender heart.
But when the final curtain starts to fall,
And all our feeble pageantry is wrong,
The king and fool must answer to the call,
And exit with the great and silent throng.
  So what is all our triumph and our lie?
  A momentary fiction, born to die.

###287###
My love is like a hot and burning fire,
Which thy cold heart, a thing of marble stone,
Receives, and yet it feels not my desire,
But leaves my heated spirit all alone.
I send thee sighs, and yet thou dost not see;
I speak thy beauty, praise thy holy name,
Yet all my words return again to me,
As echoes of a self-consuming flame.
Why must my passion fall on barren ground?
Why must my pleas be met with such a cold
And silent look, where no relief is found,
A sad reply to make my spirit old?
  If I must love thee, though my love's in vain,
  Then let me burn, and ask for naught but pain.

###288###
When to the sessions of my silent mind
I summon up the ghosts of seasons past,
A specter of thy countenance I find,
A lovely shadow that was built to last.
And in that court of memory, thy face
Holds sway o'er all the sorrows I have known,
A single, perfect, and redeeming grace
That I may look upon and call my own.
Though thou art gone, and I am left to weep
For all the joy that I no longer meet,
Thy image visits me in fitful sleep,
And makes the taste of bitterness more sweet.
  Thus, in my thoughts, our love remains as true
  As on the day I gave my heart to you.

###289###
I see a fault in thee, and yet my eye,
Which should by reason find thy beauty less,
Doth see that flaw and cannot pass it by,
But with that blemish loves thy loveliness.
Thy sharpest word, which strikes me to the ill,
Doth seem a lesson that my soul must learn;
Thou bend'st my heart against its very will,
And from thy coldness I am made to burn.
Should I not curse this imperfection's shame,
And seek a love more constant and more kind?
And yet I find I must adore thy name,
And be to all thy imperfections blind.
  This foolish love such contradictions prove,
  That I must love the very flaw I love.

###290###
The world is practiced in a smiling show,
To hide the canker lurking in the heart,
That none but God or devil well may know
The player's meaning from the player's art.
The face may speak of friendship and of guile,
The tongue may flatter where it means to wound,
And for a season, or a little while,
A loyal servant in a foe is found.
So how may I discern the honest truth,
When all about me are but painted lies,
And wicked age wears the disguise of youth,
Reflected in a pair of hollow eyes?
  If all be false, then let me be deceived,
  For in a lie I may be less aggrieved.

###291###
When dark despair doth cloud my weary sight,
And I can find no comfort or my way,
I look to thee, a single point of light,
A distant promise of a coming day.
Like to a sailor on a sunless sea,
Who sees one star that glimmers in the far,
And knows not if that star will set him free,
But trusts his course to it, as I to your star.
Thy memory, a beacon, stays my soul,
And bids my ship of sorrows not to sink;
It is the hope that makes my spirit whole,
When I am stood upon destruction's brink.
  So shine, my star, and let thy distant grace
  Suffice to guide me through this wretched place.

###292###
What is a king, who wears a golden crown,
If fortune's wheel should turn against his state?
His power and his glory are cast down,
And he becomes a servant unto fate.
What worth is gold, if it cannot buy breath,
Or hold the creeping sickness from the cold
And final kingdom ruled by hungry Death,
Who claims the young as well as claims the old?
The proudest name upon a marble stone
Is worn away by wind and bitter frost;
Our deeds are writ on water, and are gone,
Their memory to future ages lost.
  So all is vanity that man has made,
  For we are grass, and like the grass we fade.

###293###
The deepest red that in the summer rose
Doth paint its tender petals, here but shows
A shadow of thy lips; the sky so blue,
A pale reflection of thine eyes so true.
The sweetest bird that in the forest sings
Can't match the music that thy laughter brings;
The softest breeze that whispers in the air,
A poor attempt thy gentleness to share.
All things of beauty in this world I find
Are but poor copies that remind my one
True love for thee that occupies my mind,
Beneath the circling of the moon and sun.
  For nature strives, with all its mimic art,
  To paint the image graven on my heart.

###294###
My troubled mind is like a ship at sea,
Assailed by winds of sorrow and of storm,
Where anxious thoughts do make a wreck of me,
And darkest doubts take on a dreadful form.
My reason is a captain in a rage,
Who cannot steer against the rising sky;
My heart, a broken and a fearful cage,
From which my hopes like frighten'd birds do fly.
No anchor holds me, and no harbor brings
A moment's respite from this weary fraught;
Each wave a new and fresh despair upflings,
Upon the broken vessel of my thought.
  Only thy voice, a calm and steady bell,
  Can guide me from this self-created hell.

###295###
My muse is fled, and I am left to write
These barren lines when inspiration's flown;
I stare upon the page in blackest night,
And find I am most wretchedly alone.
No clever phrase, no well-constructed word
Comes to my aid, my inkwell has run dry;
All my past songs seem foolish and absurd,
Beneath a cold and unsympathetic sky.
My weary hand can scarce the quill command,
My thoughts are scattered, broken, and in vain;
This noble work I cannot understand,
And what was pleasure has become a pain.
  Without thy face to be my verse's heart,
  I have no subject, and I have no art.

###296###
Let all the kingdoms of the world know fears,
And let the mountains to the sea all change,
My constant love, throughout the coming years,
Shall look on ruin and not find it strange.
Though seasons turn and beauty starts to fade,
And friends prove false, and fortunes tend to decay,
The vow I gave, the promises I made,
Shall be the one thing not to pass away.
My love is not a ship that wind canst throw
From its true course, nor is it built on sand,
But is a star that all the lost may know,
A fixed point within a changing land.
  So let time do its worst, we shall hold fast,
  For what is true was always meant to last.

###297###
If my harsh words have caused thy heart some wrong,
Or if some careless and unthinking plea
Has stayed within thy memory too long,
I beg thee now, grant pardon unto me.
For in my haste, I spake what was not meant,
A bitter deed my soul doth now regret;
Let my true sorrow be the sweet repent
That pays in full my lamentable debt.
Wipe from thy mind the injury and pain,
And see me as I was and long to be:
Thy faithful servant, who would fain regain
The gentle grace you once bestowed on me.
  My world is nothing, if we be apart;
  Pray, give me back my place within thy heart.

###298###
He bears the outward semblance of a grace,
And speaks in tones so wondrously true,
That one would think no falsehood in that face,
Nor deem his friendship anything but new.
But in his heart a nest of vipers sung,
Where envy, malice, and contempt took life;
His praise, a poison waiting on his tongue,
His handshake, the concealing of a knife.
Oh, how could I have been so slow to trust
My own misgivings, when I felt the wound?
My faith in him is scattered now to dust,
And his deceit in every corner's found.
  Let all be warned who look upon a friend,
  A smiling start may have a bitter end.

###299###
Sweet sleep, why dost thou fly from my request,
And leave my mind to its own company?
All other creatures find their needful rest,
But no such comfort is there left for me.
The hours of the long and lonely night
Are populated by a ghostly host
Of fears and sorrows that avoid the light,
Each one more dreadful than the one that's lost.
My weary eyes, like suns that will not set,
Do burn and ache within my troubled head,
And in the darkness, I can but regret
The foolish words that I have left unsaid.
  For in the night, my only thoughts I see
  Are thoughts of sorrow, and of losing thee.

###300###
And so we come to the last words to say,
Before thee on thine own sad path must go;
The sun shines down upon this parting day,
And mocks the heart that only feels its woe.
Let me not weep, nor with a heavy look
Add to the burden which you now must shore,
But in my memory, that sacred book,
I'll keep thy face, and ask for nothing more.
Be well, my love, and think of me in part,
As I shall think of you where'er I be;
Though distance sets a gulf between each heart,
My thoughts shall fly across the widest sea.
  This is the end, a sorrow to be past;
  Go now, and let this kiss be not our last.

###401###
When I upon the starry heavens gaze,
And watch the moon in her slow course pass by,
I lose my way within a wondrous maze,
And think no greater beauty climbs so high.
But then I call to mind a dearer light,
The peerless wonder of thy lovely grace,
Which doth outshine the most bejewell'd night,
And steal all glory from that empty space.
For what is Phoebus' fire or Cynthia's art,
Compared to what within thine eyes I see?
They play a far more captivating part,
And hold my captive thoughts for all to be.
   So let the planets in their courses shine,
   Their light is dim before this love of thine.

###402###
Since thou art gone, all joy is banished hence,
And every hour brings but a fresher woe;
My heavy heart, which gave thee no offence,
Doth break to think that thou didst have to go.
The sun that shines brings me no pleasant day,
My mind is dark, possessed by fiendish thought;
All happiness with thee was borne away,
And all my present comfort comes to naught.
How can the world continue in its part,
Whilst I so great a treasured jewel keep
Locked only in the coffer of my heart,
And find no solace, waking or in sleep?
   No peace nor rest my troubled soul can find,
   For thou hast taken all, and left thy shade behind.

###403###
When ruthless Time shall steal thy beauty's bloom,
And silver threads amongst the gold appear,
When youthful pride is sent unto its tomb,
And what you are is no longer held so dear,
Think not that all thy worth shall turn to dust,
Or thy sweet memory forgotten be,
For in my verse, protected from all rust,
The world for evermore thy soul shall see.
These humble lines thy monument shall be,
Wherein thy grace eternally shall live,
A testament for all the world to see,
The truest praise that my poor pen can give.
   So let age come, and do its worst to thee,
   Thy beauty in my verse shall never die, but be.

###404###
That which I love, I see with my mind's eye,
A phantom shape that haunts my weary head,
A whispered sound when all the world doth lie
In silent slumber on a midnight bed.
Thy form, though distant, is for ever near,
A constant presence that I cannot shun;
Thy voice, though silent, is what I do hear,
From morning's rise until the day is done.
But this sweet curse, this torment of delight,
Doth make the truest things seem all untrue;
The world itself is blacker than the night,
When set against the memory of you.
   Thus in my thoughts, I am a king in state,
   But waking, I am poor and desolate.

###405###
As some poor actor, thrust upon the stage,
Forgets the lines he knew so well before,
And in his fear, on his own self doth rage,
So I, in thy grand presence, can no more.
My tongue, so nimble when I am alone,
Which fashions speeches worthy of a king,
Before thy gaze is turned to silent stone,
And cannot speak, or any tribute bring.
I have prepared such words of sweet design,
And practiced well the vows I mean to swear,
But they do flee this foolish head of mine,
And leave my heart in eloquent despair.
   So let mine eyes my clumsy speech amend,
   And read in them the love I can't pretend.

###406###
When winter's hand lays all the meadows bare,
And frozen silence holds the barren field,
The summer's warmth is but a dream to share,
To which the present cold refuses yield.
So is my heart, now in your absence' frost,
It cannot feel the sun that once it knew;
All former joys are in this winter lost,
And every thought is of a frozen hue.
But as the spring doth promise to return,
And wake the flowers from their earthly sleep,
For thy sweet company my soul doth yearn,
A vow of future warmth for it to keep.
   Come thou, my sun, and end this winter's day,
   And with thy light, chase all the cold away.

###407###
My jealous mind, a tyrant in its might,
Doth conjure phantoms from the empty air,
And turns the comfort of a peaceful night
Into a court of judgement and despair.
It questions every smile you do bestow,
And finds a treason in a simple glance;
It plants a seed of doubt and makes it grow
Into a thorny hedge of circumstance.
This madness, born of love's untamed excess,
Doth make a prison of my own poor heart,
Where every thought is witness to distress,
And plays a cruel and accusatory part.
   Forgive the thoughts that do thy faith offend;
   My love's too great, and fears to finds its end.

###408###
Why doth this world, so full of crafted things,
Not hold a mirror worthy of thy face?
No artist's hand, no poet's verse that sings,
Can truly capture thy celestial grace.
The painter's brush can show a crimson lip,
The sculptor give a form of perfect stone,
But from their art, the spirit deep doth slip,
The living light, which is in thee alone.
They can but show a shadow of the truth,
A pale reflection, cold and incomplete,
Of that fair wonder of thy glorious youth,
Which makes my verse and all my wit retreat.
   For art is but a copy, pale and faint;
   Thou art the truth, which no mere hand can paint.

###409###
As a lost ship upon a boundless sea,
That hath no chart nor guiding star to find,
So is my reason when it thinks on thee,
A storm-tossed vessel in my troubled mind.
My passion is the wind that fills the sail,
And drives me onward to an unknown shore;
My sense of right, a voice that can but wail
Against the tempest's deafening, mighty roar.
At times a calm may settle on my soul,
And I may steer a course both straight and true,
But then thy memory takes its heavy toll,
And I am lost in seas of love for you.
   So I must drift, where'er this passion blows,
   And what my fate may be, God only knows.

###410###
Last night in slumber, thou didst come to me,
And with thy presence, made my dream so bright
That waking life seemed but a misery,
A gloomy shadow in the morning's light.
Thou spok'st such words as I do long to hear,
And on my lips didst place a tender kiss,
Which banished every doubt and every fear,
And filled my dreaming soul with perfect bliss.
But when the dawn did break upon my room,
And I did wake to find an empty place,
My joy was turned to sorrow and to gloom,
Without the comfort of thy sleeping face.
   Thus I would rather sleep and dream of thee,
   Than wake to face a harsh reality.

###411###
Thou art the music that my soul doth hear,
A secret song that no one else can know,
A perfect chord that calms my every fear,
And makes a garden in my heart to grow.
The lutes and viols of this common world
Produce a noise that grates upon my ear,
Their shallow tunes, like banners false unfurled,
Cannot compare to what I hold so dear.
For in thy voice, a sweeter sound is found,
Than Orpheus with his lyre could e'er command,
It holds my senses captive and spell-bound,
The finest music in this mortal land.
   So let them play their songs of fleeting art,
   Thy melody is written on my heart.

###412###
Like to a miser with his hoard of gold,
Who counts his treasure in a secret place,
I keep thy image, precious to behold,
The priceless memory of thy perfect face.
He fears the thief who in the night might creep,
To steal the source of his tormented joy;
And so my heart, thy sacred picture keep,
Lest prying eyes its sanctity destroy.
This rich possession makes me poor indeed,
For I am starved of thy true company,
And on a painted, silent face I feed,
Embracing thoughts for sweet reality.
   Thus I am rich in thought, but poor in fact,
   Cursed by the very treasure that I lacked.

###413###
Some say that love is but a foolish game,
A childish fancy, or a passing whim,
A fevered moment, a consuming flame,
That lives a while, and then grows cold and dim.
They speak with reason, cold and analytical,
And weigh the heart's dear impulse on a scale;
To every passion they are sharp and critical,
And say that sense and wisdom must prevail.
But what know they of this transcendent state,
Who have not stood within thy presence' light?
They cannot judge, nor can they estimate
The sun's great power, who live in endless night.
   Let them speak on; their words are empty air;
   I know my truth, and find my heaven there.

###414###
When I do count the catalogue of woes
That plague mankind throughout his short-lived day,
The sickness, loss, and pain that no one knows
How to avoid, or wish them far away,
I think on war, on famine, and on strife,
On friendship's breach, and on the slanderer's tongue,
On all the grief that burdens every life,
And how the good do often die so young.
This grim account would make my spirit sink,
And curse the fate that gave me mortal breath,
Did I not have a sweeter thing to think,
A saving grace that rescues me from death.
   For when I think on thee, all sorrows cease,
   And in thy love, my heart finds lasting peace.

###415###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's rose?
Nay, for the rose must wither and decay,
And its sweet scent upon the wild wind blows,
And all its beauty vanishes away.
Shall I compare thee to a precious gem?
Nay, for a stone is cold and lacks a heart,
Though it be set in a king's diadem,
It cannot play a true and loving part.
All earthly things that men do hold as fair
Are flawed by time, or lack a spirit true;
There is no object that I can compare
With any justice, my dear love, to you.
   For thou art both the beauty and the soul,
   A living wonder that doth make me whole.

###416###
My weary pen, which hath so often tried
To map the country of thy wondrous face,
Hath found its skill and artistry defied,
And lost its way in that enchanted place.
It writes of eyes like stars in darkest night,
Of cheeks that shame the dawn's own rosy hue,
But these are words that fail to capture light,
And cannot show the soul that shines in you.
This ink is but a shadow, dark and thin,
My paper, but a field of barren white,
Compared to all the beauty held within
The book of thee, which is my heart's delight.
   So I must fail, and lay my pen to rest,
   For what's in thee can't be by words expressed.

###417###
How many times hath this poor heart of mine
Been offered up upon thy beauty's shrine?
How many vows, and protestations grand,
Have been dismissed by one wave of thy hand?
I bring thee tears, where others bring thee smiles,
I bring thee truth amongst a world of wiles,
I bring a love so constant and so deep,
While other, lighter loves their promise sleep.
Yet for all this, I have no prized reward,
No gentle glance, no single tender word,
But am cast out, a beggar at thy gate,
To mourn my love and my unhappy fate.
   So be it; yet my love shall not be done,
   Though I may lose, and you have nothing won.

###418###
O, how my eyes do envy my own thought,
Which can in freedom fly straightway to thee,
And in an instant, quick and unbesought,
Can be where my poor body cannot be.
For when I'm chained by duty to a place,
My thought breaks free, and on a swift-winged course,
It flies to gaze upon thy lovely face,
And drink of beauty at its very source.
I bid my thoughts to bring a true report
Of how thou dost, and what is in thy sight,
But they, as traitors, make but cruel sport,
And show me but my own unhappy plight.
   They tell me thou art well, and I undone,
   So close in thought, but by hard distance, gone.

###419###
The time has come when I must seem but old,
My youthful haste by careful years replaced,
My story's better portion hath been told,
And lines of care are on my forehead traced.
Thou art in spring, where all is fresh and green,
And I in autumn's season must now dwell,
Where fading colours everywhere are seen,
And soon must come the winter's frozen spell.
How can thy May find comfort in my fall?
What union can there be 'twixt sun and shade?
It is not right that my love's heavy pall
Upon thy blooming innocence be laid.
   Yet love, not seeing years, doth still aspire,
   And frosty age can burn with youthful fire.

###420###
Let this be then the last word I shall write,
If all my pleading doth but prove in vain,
The final flicker of a dying light,
The last confession of a lover's pain.
My store of words is spent, my hope is low,
My trembling hand can scarce the quill command;
There are no further arguments to show,
No other ways to make you understand.
If all this verse, which holds my heart so true,
Hath failed to stir one feeling in thy breast,
Then there is nothing more that I can do,
But let my weary, loving spirit rest.
   Yet know, as I descend to silent dust,
   My love was real, and my devotion, just.

###441###
When I do look upon that face of thine,
Which mocks the clumsy artistry of man,
I see a beauty that is half divine,
Too rich to measure by a mortal span.
For in thine eyes a brighter sun doth rise
Than that which gilds the vapours of the air,
And from thy lips a sweeter music flies
Than any song that mortal tongues can share.
Yet this perfection is a lease from Time,
Who with his theft a-grieving makes my heart,
To steal the subject of my hopeful rhyme,
And of thy youth to take the better part.
   But what he steals, my verse shall hold in store,
   That thou mayst live when thou art seen no more.

###442###
What bitter draught is this that I must drink,
What poison courtesy that thou dost show?
Upon the precipice of doubt I sink,
And watch the seeds of rank mistrust to grow.
For in a smile, I read a coming frown,
And in a touch, a cold and distant thought;
Thy protestations cannot cast it down,
This jealousy thy careless hand hath wrought.
For every vow that thou dost swear to me,
Another, silent, to thyself is made,
A promise kept where I may never see,
A light that casts my constant love to shade.
   And so I live, a king in gilded chain,
   Who fears the truth would bring a greater pain.

###443###
When all the world is in a slumber deep,
And silence holds its reign o’er field and town,
My thoughts of thee their restless vigil keep,
And on my pillow will not lay them down.
The curtains of the night I draw aside,
In hope the moon might bring my soul some ease,
But in her face, thy cruel beauties ride,
And in the wind, I hear thy sweet decrees.
So all the night I wage a private war,
With phantoms of thee fashioned in my head,
More brilliant than the most resplendent star,
From which my weary, weeping eyes have fled.
   Thus night to me no gentle darkness gives,
   But is the stage whereon thy torment lives.

###444###
I have seen lilies, when their leaves unfold,
And watched the rose in her most proud attire;
I have seen silver streams and burnished gold,
And felt the warmth of summer’s gentlest fire.
The lark’s high song at morning have I heard,
The softest whisper of a southern breeze,
Yet every sight and every pleasant word
Doth but my sense a little while appease.
For when they stand against thy form and face,
Their boasts of beauty are but shadows thrown;
They have some portion, but thou all the grace,
A wonder that the world hath never known.
   So I confess all nature’s art is less
   Than that which doth thy single self possess.

###445###
So thou art gone, and I am left to mourn
The heavy space that 'twixt our bodies lies,
A lonely wretch, of all his comfort shorn,
Who for a glimpse of thy dear vision cries.
Though reason tells me it is but a day,
My heart doth count each moment as a year,
And every hour that thou art kept away
Doth cost my soul a thousand kinds of fear.
I chart the sun’s slow journey through the sky
And curse the sluggish circle that he makes,
For till he sets and doth his journey ply
Once more to dawn, my woeful spirit aches.
   Thus distance is a tyrant to my mind,
   That robs my eyes, but leaves my love behind.

###446###
Look how the world doth with a painted lie
Applaud the rich and praise the powerful man,
Though in his soul a nest of vipers lie,
And all his deeds defy a virtuous plan.
For worth is measured not by what is true,
But by the glitter of a golden coat,
And honesty is of a sober hue,
On which the common eye will seldom dote.
So vice in robes may pass for purest grace,
While ragged virtue is a thing of scorn,
A hidden shame writ on a humble face,
To bear the harsh opinions of the born.
   But thou, my love, in thy clear soul dost see
   The simple truth the world denies to me.

###447###
Thine eyes are not like windows to thy soul,
But armouries from whence love’s war is fought;
The glances that thou canst so well control
Are sharpened arrows with my sorrow fraught.
One look from thee can raise me from despair,
Another cast me to the darkest hell;
One fleeting glimpse, when thou art unaware,
Holds more of magic than a wizard’s spell.
For in their depths a sovereign power resides,
To which my heart must willingly submit,
It is the star by which my fortune glides,
The law by which my every act is writ.
   So let thy gaze be gentle, I implore,
   Or with thy look, condemn me evermore.

###448###
When sleep should come to grant my mind its peace,
The ghosts of former days begin their raid;
The memory of joy that now doth cease
Makes present grief a darker, deeper shade.
I see thy smile, I hear thy voice again,
I feel thy hand as it was wont in mine,
Which makes the knowledge of my current pain
A bitter cup of discontented wine.
So memory, which ought to be a friend,
Doth act the part of a tormenting foe,
And to my nights no peaceful comfort send,
But keeps the fresh account of all my woe.
   For what is past is but a dream now lost,
   And waking proves the greatness of the cost.

###449###
My halting verse, that strives to speak thy worth,
Doth shame the thought from which it first was born;
It cannot capture all thy heav'n on earth,
And leaves my truest feeling half forsworn.
My tongue feels thick, my wit is rendered slow,
When charged to build a monument for thee,
For where a tide of eloquence should flow,
A shallow stream is all the world can see.
The learned poets, with their golden quills,
May paint the world with words of richer dye,
And summon Muses from the sacred hills,
But still to match thy beauty they must try.
   Yet know this truth, though my poor art be small,
   The love that writes it is the best of all.

###450###
I see the pride of summer’s green array
Give way to autumn’s melancholy gold,
And hear the bleak wind of a winter’s day,
That tells a tale of how the year grows old.
The hopeful bud that springtime did release
Is now a ghost upon a frozen bough,
Its season of sweet thriving now at peace,
A fragile wreck of what it once was now.
And in this turning of the world I find
The map of man’s own brief and fleeting state,
From youth’s warm sun to the decaying wind,
That blows us all towards a common fate.
   But in thy face, a spring that knows no end
   Doth time’s most cruel offences well defend.

###451###
Thou dost protest thy love is still as strong
As when our hearts first learnt a single beat,
That in thy breast there is no hint of wrong,
Nor any falsehood in thy words so sweet.
Thy constant vows fall eas'ly from thy tongue,
And thou dost wear the very face of truth,
A perfect tale by a siren sung,
To steal away the wisdom of my youth.
But in thine eyes a subtle shadow plays,
A guarded look that I have learn'd to read,
That tells of secret walks and hidden ways
Wherein thy heart hath planted traitor's seed.
   So tell thy lies, and I will feign belief,
   And in this play, we'll both find sweet relief.

###452###
This world’s a stage, where all men have their part,
And each must play the role to him assigned;
Some have a crown, and some a broken heart,
And to their fortune, they are all confined.
We fret our hour, we speak our borrowed lines,
And strut in costumes that are not our own,
We seek applause and favourable signs,
Then in the wings, we vanish, all alone.
And I, it seems, am cast to play the fool,
Who loves too much and is not loved again,
Made subject to a faithless lover’s rule,
And for my payment, I receive but pain.
   But when this weary, tragic act is done,
   I'll rest at last, my final curtain won.

###453###
If I were deaf, my love for thee would grow,
Fed only by the beauty of thy sight;
If I were blind, my heart would surely know
Thy presence by a feeling of pure light.
But being blessed with senses to perceive
The many ways thy grace is manifest,
My ear doth most unwillingly believe
That it is held more captive than the rest.
For when thou speak’st, a hush falls on my soul,
The world outside no longer can intrude,
Thy voice alone assumes a full control,
And fills with music all my solitude.
   So speak, my love, and let thy sweet words be
   The only sound the world shall grant to me.

###454###
I do not need to seek a hollow stone
Nor bow my head in some cathedral vast,
For I have made thy loving heart my own,
And in that temple is my anchor cast.
Thy body is the shrine where I do pray,
Thine eyes the candles that my soul doth light,
Thy sacred voice doth guide me on my way
And makes the darkest of my sorrows bright.
I need no saint to plead my case above,
No chanted psalm, no priest in solemn dress,
For all the faith I have is in thy love,
Thy sweet approval is my holiness.
   So in this church of just us two, I find
   A truer heav’n than that which priests designed.

###455###
The barren branch against the winter sky,
The frozen earth where flowers used to grow,
The mournful wind that utters its low sigh,
Are but the outward signs of my own woe.
For since thy warmth from me hath been withdrawn,
A private winter on my heart descends;
I see no promise of a coming dawn,
But feel a chill that my own spirit rends.
The sun itself seems cold and far away,
Its golden light a mockery to me,
Whilst I remember that one blissful day
When all my summer was possessed by thee.
   So let the world its changing seasons prove,
   'Tis always winter, absent from thy love.

###456###
Why dost thou arm thy beauty with such scorn,
And make thine eyes the agents of my pain?
Was such a cruel spirit in thee born,
Or dost thou pleasure in my sorrow gain?
My constant heart, which offers thee no fight,
But yields itself a captive to thy will,
Thou strik'st with words of cold and sharp delight,
And with thy silence, thou dost seek to kill.
If thou but knew the torment I endure
When thy great pride doth turn its face from me,
Thou wouldst, perhaps, find in thy soul a cure,
And grant my loyal love some small decree.
   Then pity me, who for thy mercy cries,
   And slay me not with daggers from thine eyes.

###457###
Thou hast a face that treason cannot stain,
A look of truth, an aspect of pure grace,
That he who sees it must believe thy reign
Is held within a true and honest place.
Thy words are coins of such a fine alloy
They seem new-minted from the purest thought,
Designed to bring a faithful lover joy,
In whom no seed of doubting can be wrought.
And yet I find, behind this fair disguise,
A mind that plots and keeps its counsel deep,
A second face that watches from thine eyes,
While all thy vows of constancy do sleep.
   Thus art thou like a book with gilded trim,
   Whose fairest outside hides a story grim.

###458###
These brittle lines, which strive to make thee fast
Against the sure erasure of the years,
Shall, like the pyramids, themselves be cast
To dust, and be the subject of men’s tears.
Nor brass, nor stone, nor this my loving rhyme,
Can build a fort to stand against decay;
There is no art can beat relentless time,
Nor bid the night to give back yesterday.
But that which holds us in its sacred chain,
That timeless bond, that marriage of the soul,
When all the monuments of earth are vain,
Shall of our spirits keep the perfect whole.
   For though our forms and this my verse decline,
   My love is endless, as is yours for mine.

###459###
When I do count the hours I have lost
In idle sport and unproductive quest,
I see how great a debt, and what a cost,
Hath been incurred by youth’s unwise behest.
Those days that should have been to knowledge lent,
Or used to build a work of lasting might,
Were like a careless spendthrift’s fortune spent,
And purchased nothing but a brief delight.
And now that age with his slow craft appears
To mark my brow and steal my vital flame,
I look upon my hoard of misspent years,
And feel them burn my very soul with shame.
   But thinking on thy love, I am repaid,
   And all my loss a worthy bargain made.

###460###
Let others seek the laurel for their brow,
And chase a name that echoes after death;
I do not make so arrogant a vow,
Nor tie my fame to my departing breath.
My only art is to reflect thy light,
My single skill, to hold thee ever dear;
Thou art the sun that makes my darkness bright,
And banishes the cause of all my fear.
So let this verse, and all that I shall write,
Be but a mirror where the world can see
A faint and faulty image of that sight
Which thy perfection hath bestowed on me.
   For all the credit of my work is thine,
   Whose matchless worth inspires every line.

###461###
When winter’s breath upon the glass doth creep,
And all the world in frozen slumber lies,
My thoughts of thee a summer warmth do keep,
Reflected in the hope within mine eyes.
Though barren branches claw against the grey,
And mournful winds do tell a bitter tale,
I conjure forth a bright and distant May,
Whose promised sweetness never shall grow stale.
For in the storehouse of my constant mind,
Thy-visage blooms, untouched by frost or snow,
A sun-kissed rose of a most gentle kind,
That in my soul eternally doth grow.
  So let the seasons turn as they are bid,
  My love for thee in winter is not hid.

###462###
I look upon the stars that stud the night,
And think them but a shadow of thine eyes,
For they possess a cold and borrowed light,
Wherein no true or living passion lies.
The moon, a silver coin in heavens thrown,
Doth wax and wane, a fickle, faithless thing;
It cannot match the beauty thou hast shown,
Nor half the joy thy constant presence bring.
Why then do poets praise these orbs above,
And waste their ink on such inconstant fire?
They have not felt the power of my love,
Nor been consumed by such a pure desire.
  All light in heaven is a paltry art,
  Compared to that which shines within thy heart.

###463###
Full many a time I take my pen in hand
To write the litany of all thy grace,
But words, like traitors in a foreign land,
Refuse to serve and flee from thy fair face.
My mind conceives a wealth of loving praise,
My heart dictates a volume yet untold,
But when my clumsy quill the ink doth raise,
The fire of my invention turneth cold.
What skill can capture how thy laughter rings?
What verse can hold the kindness in thy soul?
My speech is bankrupt when of thee it sings,
For parts cannot describe the perfect whole.
  So I fall silent, knowing wit is vain,
  For what is perfect, silence must contain.

###464###
Doth thou not see what mirrors show to thee?
A miracle of form, a matchless sight,
Whose great perfection puts a tax on me
To find new words to praise thee day and night.
But envious Time, with his unpitying rage,
Doth carve new lines upon that smoothest brow,
And turns with haste life’s fair and hopeful page
To what it was, instead of what is now.
Then in this battle, let my verse be shield,
To guard thy beauty from his cruel decay,
And make the essence that thy features yield
Immortal, though the substance wastes away.
  Thy outward form may fade as all things must,
  But these black lines shall save thee from the dust.

###465###
As some poor actor, fretful on the stage,
Forgets the lines he knew so well by heart,
And in his fear, turns to a silent rage,
Unable to perform his simple part;
So I, in thy magnificent presence, stand,
And all my well-rehearsed composure flies,
My clever tongue cannot obey command,
Struck dumb beneath the judgment of thine eyes.
My love is great, though my expression weak,
A treasure hidden in a clumsy chest,
My heart is full of things it longs to speak,
But by thy beauty is its voice oppressed.
  Then let my looks my silent plea convey,
  And read what my poor tongue can never say.

###466###
The sun, arising, puts the dark to flight,
And wakes the world with his imperial gaze,
Yet to my soul he brings no true delight,
For thou art absent through these weary days.
The birds may sing their sweet, melodious tune,
The flowers open to the morning air,
But all of joy beneath the sun and moon
Is turned to sorrow when thou art not there.
My food is tasteless, and my wine is sour,
My work a burden and my rest a fight,
I count each slow and melancholy hour,
And curse the day, and pray for endless night.
  For day is night when thy face I can’t see,
  And only thy return brings day to me.

###467###
My guilty conscience holds a court within,
Wherein my deeds are brought to stand their trial,
And every thought is judged a heavy sin,
Each broken promise and each small denial.
I stand accused of squandering my days,
Of giving not what my poor heart could give,
Of walking down self-serving, crooked ways,
And not for thee, but for myself to live.
The verdict’s cast, no pardon can I find,
The sentence given is a deep despair,
To be tormented by a restless mind
That finds thy goodness and my faults laid bare.
  Yet thy forgiveness, if it could be won,
  Would make me feel as if new-born, my sun.

###468###
An age I deem each moment we’re apart,
A century, each day that I’m alone,
And cruelest distance lays siege to my heart,
To hear it make its solitary moan.
I send my thoughts as messengers to thee,
And bid them fly on wings of swift desire,
To ask if thou, perchance, dost think of me,
And if thy love still burns with equal fire.
But they return, and bring no word but this:
That leagues of earth and water lie between,
That I am robbed of a most sacred bliss,
And of thy face no likeness can be seen.
  Thus am I prisoner to this time and place,
  Until once more I look upon thy face.

###469###
So great a bounty nature gave thy form,
That all who see thee must perforce admire,
A steady anchor in life’s brutal storm,
The final goal of every soul’s desire.
Thy voice, a music banishing all strife,
Thine eyes, the stars by which my ship is steered,
Thou art the very substance of my life,
More sacred, and more loved, and more endeared.
But in this praise, a danger I confess,
A sin of worship that I should not make,
For in adoring thee, I love God less,
And risk my soul for thy dear beauty’s sake.
  So art thou both my heaven and my hell,
  A blessed curse under whose spell I dwell.

###470###
Why didst thou show me such a lovely face,
And let me hear the sweetness of thy tongue,
If only to withdraw that saving grace,
And leave my heart by misery unstrung?
‘Twas like a feast spread for a starving man,
Who, ere he tastes, sees it all snatched away,
Or like a glimpse of heaven’s perfect plan,
Shown to a soul that hell is bound to claim.
Better it were to never have been blessed,
Than for a moment hold a thing so dear,
And then to lose it, and find no more rest,
But live in want, and in perpetual fear.
  Thy memory is a wound that will not heal,
  A phantom pain of joy I cannot feel.

###471###
I marvel not that other eyes do gaze
Upon the perfect garden of thy cheek,
And lose themselves within the sunlit maze
Of thy bright hair, and find their tongues too weak.
I chafe not that their praise doth fill the air,
And poets make thy name their only theme,
For such a beauty is beyond compare,
The waking substance of a perfect dream.
I am not jealous of this common view,
For they see only what is on the skin,
A painted surface, exquisite and new,
But cannot know the loyal heart within.
  They see the casket, richly wrought and fine,
  But I alone possess the gem divine.

###472###
When I am dead and in the earth am laid,
And all the world has quite forgotten me,
Let this one comfort my poor ghost persuade:
My love shall live, immortalized in thee.
For thou art made of such a constant stuff,
So true, so fair, so gracious, and so kind,
My verse, which sings thy praise, is not enough
To show the boundless riches of thy mind.
Let not the thought of my decay bring pain,
Nor my departure cause thy heart to bleed;
Think that my spirit doth in thine remain,
A part of every good and honest deed.
  So weep not, for my essence shall not die,
  But in thy virtue, find eternity.

###473###
That mouth, from which such gentle words proceed,
Hath other uses which I call to mind,
Wherein it plants a sweet and silent seed
Of bliss, to which all speech is deaf and blind.
Those lips, that argue with such perfect grace,
And common subjects into gold can turn,
Can press upon another time and place
A lesson that my soul is quick to learn.
This double power thy sweet mouth doth hold:
To charm the ear with its judicious sound,
And with a kiss, a story to unfold
More profound than in any book is found.
  So speak or kiss, I am in either blessed,
  But in thy kiss, my heart finds sweetest rest.

###474###
Thou art the sun, whose coming makes my day,
The moon, whose absence makes my night so long,
The very air I breathe along the way,
The silent music in my soul’s best song.
Thou art the book wherein I long to read,
The steadfast anchor in a turning tide,
The wholesome fruit on which my spirit feed,
My truest counsel and my safest guide.
Since thou art all of this, and so much more,
How can I live when thou art far from me?
I am a ship wrecked on a barren shore,
A tree uprooted, for the world to see.
