  Without thee, I am but an empty frame,
  A hollow echo of a whispered name.

###475###
Against the lies that envious tongues will spread,
Against the doubt that in my heart may grow,
Against the fear that fills my soul with dread,
I have one shield to guard me from the blow.
It is the memory of thy honest eyes,
That look on me with such unfeigned love,
In which no hint of artifice doth rise,
Sent as my solace from the heavens above.
Let others whisper, let their malice bite,
And spin their webs of cynical deceit;
Their feeble shadows flee before the light
Of thy true faith, which makes my life complete.
  For trusting thee, I arm myself so well,
  I am made safe from all the crafts of hell.

###476###
As a dull scribe, I copy what I see,
And strive to make the image clear and fast,
So thy perfection is writ down by me,
In lines of ink intended long to last.
Each virtue is a letter I must trace,
Each smile a word, each look a lovely phrase,
The noble story of thy mind and face
Is all the work that occupies my days.
But I am poor in skill, my hand unsure,
And blot the page where beauty should appear;
The portrait of thy soul, so fine and pure,
Is rendered coarse, and flawed, and insincere.
  Yet if one word of truth in this appears,
  ‘Tis that I love thee, far beyond my fears.

###477###
The world’s a stage, and we are players all,
As wise men say, and I believe it true,
And I have watched the greatest monarchs fall,
And seen fair triumphs bid a swift adieu.
All things are subject to the shifting scene,
And what today is hailed with loud acclaim,
Tomorrow is as if it had not been,
A forgotten title or an empty name.
But thy dear love is no such fleeting part,
Played for an hour to please a passing crowd;
It is the very fabric of my heart,
My constant thought, in silence or aloud.
  So let the world its pageant roll away,
  Thy love’s the truth that shall forever stay.

###478###
Was it my fancy, or did thine eye rest
A moment longer than it should on me?
And did a blush, a feeling unconfessed,
Colour thy cheek for all the world to see?
Or does my hope, that ever-eager fool,
Invent the signs for which it longs to find,
And break discretion’s sober, cautious rule,
With silly fictions of a lovesick mind?
I am afraid to think it might be so,
For such a joy my heart could not contain;
And yet I am afraid to let it go,
For fear ‘twill never grace my sight again.
  This hope and doubt a constant war do wage,
  And make my heart their bitter battle-stage.

###479###
The cunning painter with his brush may seek
To steal the rosy blush that tints thy skin,
Or capture how the light upon thy cheek
Doth show the gentle soul that dwells within.
The sculptor may in cold and lifeless stone
Attempt to carve thy form for all to praise,
And make thy perfect likeness to be known
As beauty’s standard for all future days.
But these are idols, dead and lacking breath,
Their art a mockery of living grace;
They cannot save thy substance from its death,
Nor keep the sweet expression on thy face.
  Only my verse, with love’s own fire penned,
  Shall make thy living spirit transcend time’s end.

###480###
My love is not a garment to be worn,
Subject to fashion and the changing year,
To be put on at whim on some bright morn,
And then cast off when it no more seems dear.
Nor is it like a house built on the sand,
To be washed out by fortune’s angry tide,
A fragile structure, foolishly unplanned,
With no safe place for constancy to hide.
Nay, it is forged of something hard and deep,
A diamond, proof against the sharpest blow,
A secret that my faithful heart doth keep,
Which, under pressure, only brighter grows.
  This love is all I am, and all I'll be,
  Through life, through death, and through eternity.

###421###
When wasteful Time destroys a kingly state,
And steals the living fire from the eye,
And sets for all a grim and certain date
Whence mortal flesh must wither, droop, and die;
When thy sweet face, which is my world's delight,
Shall bear the lines of sorrow and of years,
And thy fair day descend to sullen night,
Assailed by doubts and tributary tears;
Then shall this verse, a fortress for thy name,
Against the siege of ages make a stand,
And hold in custody thy lasting fame,
Escaping death from my unworthy hand.
So ages hence, when I am naught to see,
This ink shall keep the truest part of thee.

###422###
My weary thoughts do travel far away,
To where thou art, beyond my longing sight,
And curse the coming of each empty day,
That brings no comfort, only endless night.
I am a prisoner in a golden chain,
For thought of thee doth bind me, yet I weep
To know such thoughts but magnify my pain,
And from all restfulness my senses keep.
And yet, what else but of thy form to think?
What other vision could my mind embrace?
Upon thy memory my soul must drink,
For want of looking on thy living face.
So is my greatest wealth the thought of thee,
And my great poverty, where I must be.

###423###
Let others praise the lute's enchanting sound,
Or find in harmony a curious art;
Upon such trifles is their pleasure found,
Which have no power to captivate my heart.
A sweeter music never met mine ear,
Than when thy tongue some gentle word doth say;
The very angels hold their breath to hear,
And bid the choirs of heaven cease to play.
What care I for the viol's trembling strings,
Or what contrived melody is sweet,
When thy discourse a greater pleasure brings,
And makes my joy in listening complete?
So let them have their notes; I have my choice:
To find all music captured in thy voice.

###424###
I watch the path of thy unconstant eye,
And in its travels read a bitter thought,
That somewhere else thy secret loves do lie,
With sweeter bait than mine thy heart is caught.
I see a stranger win from thee a glance,
A simple kindness that thou giv'st for free,
And in that look I see a happy chance
For him to steal what is reserved for me.
My reason tells me that my fear denies
The constant faith that I should hold in you;
Yet this same love, which lives within mine eyes,
Makes every rival lovely to my view.
So in the prison of my heart and mind,
No peace from this self-torment can I find.

###425###
The sun retires, and winter's pallid hand
Upon the world a heavy sadness lays;
The cheerless birds have fled this frozen land,
And shortened are the miserable days.
The naked branch, which summer did adorn,
Now weeps with ice and shivers in the blast,
A wretched shape, of all its beauty shorn,
Its time of glory now forever past.
So is my soul, when thou art not in sight,
A barren country, desolate and grey,
Deprived of warmth, of comfort, and of light,
And subject to perpetual decay.
For thou my summer art, and in thy grace
Is all my life, my fortune, and my place.

###426###
Some say that beauty's standard must be bright,
With hair of gold, and skin as pale as snow;
But such a truth is fashioned in the light,
And in the shadows has no strength to grow.
I say thy

###501###
When I behold how wondrous thou art fair,
What lesser beauties can the world e'er show?
The golden sun that plays within thy hair
Doth lose its fire to thine eyes' bright glow.
Let cruel Time his sharpened sickle wield,
And bring to dust the monuments of kings;
Thy youthful grace shall never to him yield,
But soar above on my poor fancy's wings.
For in these lines thy spirit shall reside,
A living ghost to mock at death's decay,
And where my humble verses shall abide,
Thou shalt not suffer Time to have his way.
   So live in this, and this shall give thee breath,
   To triumph over age and conquer death.

###502###
Each weary step that takes me far away
Is but a wound that bleeds my heart full dry;
The sun itself seems sorrowful this day,
And finds no reason to adorn the sky.
My thoughts, like pilgrims, to thy shrine do fly,
And in the temple of my constant mind
I keep thine image, lest my memory die,
Though naught but shadows of thy form I find.
And so I chide the hours for their slow pace,
And curse the journey that holds me from thee,
Imagining the sorrow in thy face,
Which looks for my return, but finds not me.
   Thus in my exile, every joy is fled,
   And all my living comfort lieth dead.

###503###
I take my pen to write of thy sweet grace,
But find my words are beggars, poor and weak;
They cannot hope to capture thy fair face,
Nor of thy virtues adequately speak.
The finest ink, the smoothest poet's phrase,
Doth turn to lead when set to such a task,
For how can mortal art describe the rays
Of light that from thine inner beauty bask?
My stumbling verse doth thee a disservice do,
And every line a failed attempt appears
To paint the soul that shineth out of you,
A truth that grows more potent with the years.
   So I must fail, and in this failure, prove,
   Some beauty lives too far for words to move.

###504###
Why doth that other poet sing thy praise
With such false gold and gaudy rhetoric?
He steals the worth of thy most perfect days
To make his vulgar, common verses quick.
His sugared tongue doth flatter and deceive,
His meter marches with a hollow sound;
He bids the world his fantasies believe,
Wherein no grain of thy true self is found.
His art is but a mirror, cracked and old,
That shows a warped and twisted effigy;
A story by a frantic player told,
Devoid of truth, and full of vanity.
   But my plain lines a truer love impart,
   For they are written with my very heart.

###505###
When clouds of doubt upon thy brow do sit,
And thy displeasure turns thy face from me,
The world itself for me hath lost its wit,
And all things fair no longer seem to be.
The sun may shine, but I perceive no light;
The birds may sing, but I can hear no song;
Thy frown hath cast o'er all an endless night,
And in that darkness, every hope goes wrong.
O, speak but one kind word to clear the air,
And banish this self-inflicted storm of mine;
For in thy grace, all things again are fair,
And in thy smile, all heavenly lights combine.
   Without thy favour, I am but a shade;
   From thy sweet look, my very world is made.

###506###
When gentle sleep doth close my weary eyes,
My soul escapes its prison of the day,
And to thy presence in an instant flies,
To be with thee, though thou art far away.
There do I see thee in a perfect light,
More real than all my waking senses know,
A brilliant star that conquers my long night,
And on my soul a sacred peace bestow.
But then the dawn, that tyrant, breaks the spell,
And with its cruel and unrelenting hand,
It pulls me back to my own waking hell,
An exile in a bleak and barren land.
   And so I live for night, and dread the sun,
   For only in my dreams our souls are one.

###507###
If music be the food of love, they say,
Then thy dear voice is a celestial feast;
It drives all worldly, common sounds away,
And holds my heart a willing captive beast.
The lute's soft cry, the viol's deep lament,
Are but a discord when compared to thee;
In thy speech, all sweet harmonies are blent,
A perfect, soul-entrancing melody.
Let others seek the choir's soaring height,
Or find their pleasure in an actor's art;
I need but hear thee speak, and my delight
Is full, and lodges deeply in my heart.
   Thy every word a golden note doth seem,
   The substance of a poet's fondest dream.

###508###
Thou speak'st of friendship with a silver tongue,
And proffer'st me a hand that seems so true,
Yet in thy heart, a serpent's nest is sprung,
With venomed thoughts and mischief ever new.
Thy face is like a book of pious prayer,
Wherein no guile or treachery is writ,
But he who reads thy soul will find despair,
And fall into a dark and endless pit.
I once did think thee honest, fair, and kind,
A constant star in this inconstant sphere,
But now I see thy love was but a blind,
To hide the truth I held so very dear.
   So keep thy smiles for those thou wouldst betray;
   I have thy measure, and will walk away.

###509###
The barren branch, the earth as hard as stone,
The bitter wind that bites with frosty breath;
All this harsh winter I must bear alone,
And think upon a coldness worse than death.
For thy remove hath stolen all my heat,
And in my breast a frozen silence grows;
The seasons turn, their purpose to repeat,
But in my soul, no gentle springtime shows.
This outward chill is but a mirror for
The icy waste that is my heart's domain;
Since thou, my sun, hast shut thy golden door,
I am condemned to this perpetual reign.
   Of biting frost and dreary, sunless skies,
   Until the summer returns to thine eyes.

###510###
The marble tombs of princes turn to dust,
And bronze, though strong, by cankering time is stained;
The sword of mighty conquerors will rust,
And of their glory, nothing is remained.
But thy fair form, which I in verse enclose,
Shall not be subject to such base decay;
Here, like a bud of an eternal rose,
Thou shalt not fade, nor wilt, nor pass away.
When all the proudest works of man are gone,
And ages new have buried old renown,
Thy beauty still shall in these lines live on,
And wear its youth as an immortal crown.
   For this my verse a monument shall be,
   Where Time itself will come to worship thee.

###511###
I envy so the ground on which you walk,
The very air that takes your gentle breath,
The flowers that attend your pleasant talk,
And live their short sweet lives before their death.
I envy so the sun that sees your face,
And the kind mirror that reflects your look;
I envy every object in the place
That is thy world, and thy sweet presence took.
For they are near thee, and are blessed thereby,
Whilst I am banished to a distant state,
And can but send my longing through the sky,
And curse the tyranny of this my fate.
   To be a glove upon that hand were bliss,
   And live or die upon a single kiss.

###512###
I see the rose that blossoms for a day,
And mark how swift its crimson beauty flies;
I see the pride of youth in its brief sway,
And how it withers before our very eyes.
This fleeting show of things that cannot last
Doth make me ponder on thy own sweet prime,
And think how soon thy summer will be past,
A spoil for the devouring jaws of Time.
But in the archive of my faithful mind,
Thy grace is captured and protected well;
There, age cannot its cruel advantage find,
Nor can the thief of years thy beauty quell.
   So though thy spring must yield to winter's hand,
   Within my love, thou shalt forever stand.

###513###
From whence do all my simple verses spring?
What power gives my untaught fancy flight?
It is thy love that makes my spirit sing,
And fills my barren darkness with its light.
Thou art the only subject of my art,
The sun and moon of my poetic sky;
All other themes are strangers to my heart,
For at the mention of them, my words die.
If I should write of war or of a king,
My pen would stray and write thy name instead;
If I of sorrow or of grief should sing,
My thoughts would be of thee, and tears I'd shed.
   For thou art all my work, my soul, my muse,
   The only argument my lines can use.

###514###
I love and hate, and in this war I bleed,
A willing captive shackled to my foe;
Thy beauty plants a sweet but bitter seed
That blossoms into both my joy and woe.
One look from thee can raise me to the height
Of heaven's bliss, a king upon his throne;
Thy scorn can cast me to the darkest night,
Where I must make my solitary moan.
How can one soul such contradictions hold?
How can one heart both burn and freeze within?
This paradox, a story to be told,
Is where my torment and my hopes begin.
   I am thy prisoner, and hold no key,
   But find my sweetest freedom serving thee.

###515###
I ask no riches from the world's great store,
Nor crave the fame that politics can bring;
I do not seek what other men adore,
A title from the favour of a king.
My whole ambition, all in this is bound:
That thou shouldst turn thy face and look on me;
That in thine eyes some pity might be found
For him who serves with such idolatry.
One fleeting glance, a nod of thy dear head,
Would be a treasure richer than all gold,
And on that morsel I would be well fed,
A greater bounty than my heart could hold.
   So grant this boon, and make my fortune high,
   Lest for the want of it, I pine and die.

###516###
This world's a stage, and we are players all,
Each with his entrance and his exit planned;
We fret and strut until the curtains fall,
And our brief acts are writ in shifting sand.
Some play the king, and some the weeping clown,
Some are the heroes, some the villains base;
Each seeks his glory and his small renown
Within this frantic and disordered place.
But when thou tread'st the boards, all action stays,
The other players halt and stand aghast,
Their own poor parts forgotten as they gaze
Upon the perfect mould in which thou'rt cast.
   For thou art not a player, but the play,
   The truth and beauty of our fleeting day.

###517###
Dost thou remember when our eyes first met?
A careless moment in a crowded room;
My soul at once did know it was in debt,
And sealed its fate and its most happy doom.
I can recall the light, the air, the sound,
The very pattern of the floor we trod;
It seemed to me 'twas consecrated ground
Where I had chanced to see a walking god.
The world moved on, the chatter rose and fell,
But in that space, a silent vow was made;
My heart was caught, and fell beneath thy spell,
A willing tribute, joyfully unpaid.
   That single glance, a lifetime did contain,
   And in my memory, it doth still remain.

###518###
My muse is barren and my spirits low,
My verse, once proud, now limps in sorry shame;
The words of praise I formerly did know
Refuse to come when I invoke thy name.
For I am so unworthy of thy grace,
A shadow creeping where the sun doth shine,
How can my pen presume to limn thy face?
How can my love be worthy to be thine?
This weight of doubt doth crush my best intent,
And turns my inspiration into lead;
The poet that thy beauty might have lent
Is by my own self-scornful thinking, dead.
   And yet I write, from habit or from need,
   To plant in barren ground a hopeless seed.

###519###
Thou art a ship with sails of silver bright,
That catches all the winds of my desire;
And I, the sea, that lives within thy sight,
Reflecting back thy beauty's holy fire.
When thou art calm, my waters lie at peace,
A glassy mirror for thy tranquil form;
But when thy passions rise and find release,
My very depths are stirred into a storm.
I follow thee where'er thy course may be,
Upon my surface all thy fortunes rest;
My constant tides are governed all by thee,
My only purpose, serving thy behest.
   So sail thou on, my ocean and my guide,
   And I shall be the world on which you ride.

###520###
Let this my final, humble verse declare
The sum of all that I have writ before:
That thou art wise and virtuous as fair,
And being so, I love thee evermore.
Let critics carp and cynics mock my truth,
Let jealous rivals slander and defame;
My love is rooted in thy constant youth
And the eternal music of thy name.
Time cannot change it, absence makes it grow,
And fortune's malice cannot do it harm;
This is the only certain thing I know,
Protected by thy soul's own potent charm.
   So take these lines, the best that I can give,
   And in their love, let my own spirit live.

###541###
When cruel Time, with his unpitying hand,
Doth etch deep furrows in a lovely face,
And beauty's flower, throughout this weeping land,
Must yield its bloom and lose its youthful grace;
When raven locks are turned to winter's snow,
And eyes that shone like stars begin to dim,
As envious years in their procession go,
To fill life's cup with sorrow to the brim;
Then in the fortress of my verse, thy youth,
Which envious age shall never dare assail,
Shall live immortal in its perfect truth,
And in these lines, its beauty shall prevail.
   So let time do its worst, it cannot slay
   The memory of thy sweet and golden day.

###542###
The world, to me, is but a barren stage,
When from my sight thy presence is removed,
A book of sorrow where on every page,
The tragedy of absence may be proved.
The sun itself doth seem to hang its head,
The birds conceal their music in the trees,
And all the blooming roses appear dead,
To my sad heart that cannot find its ease.
But when thou dost return, the world is new,
The very air with joyful music rings,
The sun puts on a far more golden hue,
And my poor heart, in exultation, sings.
   So is my state dependent on thy view,
   With thee, all's fair; without thee, nothing true.

###543###
If I should count the reasons for my love,
I'd need more numbers than the stars in sky,
Or all the grains of sand the waters move,
Or all the tears that weeping eyes can cry.
I love thee for the wisdom in thy speech,
But for thy silence, I do love thee more;
For all the hidden lessons thou dost teach,
And for the gentle soul I so adore.
I love the way a shadow from thy frown
Can make my hopeful day as dark as night,
And how a smile can lift me from the ground,
And fill my world with unalloyed delight.
   Yet summing all, this truth I must impart:
   I love thee not for reasons, but with heart.

###544###
My weary eyes, that long for restful sleep,
Do find no comfort in the dark of night,
For in my mind, thy constant image deep
Doth make a mock of slumber, shining bright.
My thoughts, like pilgrims, to thy beauty stray,
And in that worship, they can find no rest;
They journey through the night until the day,
Afflicted by this love within my breast.
And when the morning comes, my state is worse,
For other men's affairs do claim my sight,
And I am subject to the daily curse
Of being parted from my soul's delight.
   So by the day my duties make me pine,
   And by the night, my sleepless thoughts are thine.

###545###
Let those who hunger for a monarch's crown
Endure the treason of a fickle court,
And fear the envy that would pull them down,
To make their brief and wretched reign more short.
Let merchants risk their fortunes on the sea,
In hope of treasure from a foreign shore,
And brave the storm's destructive tyranny,
To gain a wealth that leaves them wanting more.
My kingdom is the space that thou dost fill,
My only treasure, thy most sacred trust;
To have thy love is my ambitious will,
The rest I count as little more than dust.
   For having thee, I am a greater king
   Than any who can make the world's praise ring.

###546###
My foolish heart, a bird that cannot learn,
Doth fly too near the sun that is thine eye,
And though its singèd feathers make it burn,
It will not from that dangerous beauty fly.
It sees the warning in my reasoned thought,
And hears the counsel of a prudent mind,
Yet all this wisdom counts for less than naught,
When it may leave all earthly care behind.
For in thy presence, it forgets the pain,
And thinks the burning is a sweet desire,
Prepared to be consumed by thee again,
And find its heaven in that holy fire.
   So chide me not if I to thee am drawn,
   Like the poor moth that flies into the dawn.

###547###
When winter lays his siege to field and wood,
And all the world in frozen white is clad,
The memory of summer, understood,
Makes even cold-nipped nature seem less sad.
When storms do rage and angry thunders roll,
And darkness holds the heavens in its sway,
The thought of dawn gives comfort to the soul,
And promises the coming of the day.
So in the winter of my deep despair,
When fortune frowns and sorrow is my guide,
I think of thee, and all the world is fair,
And feel a warmth and comfort deep inside.
   Thy love's remembrance is my summer's sun,
   That shines for me when all to frost has run.

###548###
I look upon the moon, so cold and bright,
And think she watches me with silver eye,
A lonely sentinel throughout the night,
Who sees my lonely, sleepless moments by.
She hath no warmth to give, no gentle word,
To soothe the ache that sorrows in my breast;
The plaintive song of my sad soul, unheard,
Disturbs the silence of her chilling rest.
Yet she is constant in her borrowed grace,
And keeps her vigil in the heavens above,
And gazes down upon this woeful place,
As if she understood the pains of love.
   Perhaps she too loves one she cannot near,
   And that is why she sheds a月光 tear.

###549###
Is it a fault in me that I should find
More truth in thee than in a holy book?
Or that thy voice can calm my troubled mind
More than a prayer? Forgive this view I took.
The world may preach of virtues writ in stone,
And promise solace for a soul in strife,
But I have found my comfort in thee alone,
For thou art both my anchor and my life.
Men seek for grace in chapels cold and dim,
And look to distant heavens for a sign;
I find my faith, my vesper and my hymn,
In the religion of thy hand in mine.
   If this be heresy, then I shall be
   Most gladly damned for my true love of thee.

###550###
My tongue, a traitor to my heart's command,
Doth often fail when I would speak thy praise;
The words I fashion are as shifting sand,
Unfit to last beyond these fleeting days.
I wish to build a monument of verse,
To tell the world how fair and good thou art,
But all my crafted lines do but rehearse
A fraction of the feeling in my heart.
My finest phrases seem but dull and weak,
My metaphors like shadows on the wall;
The truth of thy perfection I would speak,
My clumsy efforts make it seem too small.
   So read my love not in the words I write,
   But in the silence where my soul takes flight.

###551###
To me, thy face is like a perfect map,
Wherein I read the contours of my soul;
I see my joy, and my own sorrow's trap,
The parts of me that make my spirit whole.
A smile from thee is like a sunlit plain,
Where all my hopeful thoughts may freely roam;
A frown from thee is like a bitter rain,
That makes my wandering heart wish for its home.
Thy glance, a river where my thoughts may drift,
Thy speech, a forest where my mind may lose
Itself in wonder at so great a gift,
Whose winding paths I would forever choose.
   Thus in thy geography my world I see,
   And find my truest self in loving thee.

###552###
Like some poor actor on a bare-wood stage,
Who frets his hour with unconvincing art,
My life doth seem a poorly written page,
Wherein I play a sad and foolish part.
I speak my lines of sorrow and of doubt,
To an uncaring and distracted crowd,
And wait for cues that never are called out,
And bow my head, in solitude enshroud.
But when thou dost appear, my light, my cue,
The scene is changed, the play is given grace;
My lines have meaning, and my heart is true,
And purpose shines upon my humble place.
   For thou art both my audience and my sun,
   My play is for thy heart, and it is won.

###553###
The rose, though praised for its most crimson hue,
Can boast no colour that compares with thine;
The violet, though admired for its deep blue,
Before the lustre of thine eyes must pine.
The lily's white, which poets hold so dear,
Is but a shadow next to thy fair skin,
And music's sweetest sound upon the ear
Is harsh, when thy soft-spoken words begin.
All beauty that in nature we can find,
From mountain peak to ocean's endless roll,
Is but a copy, of a lesser kind,
To the perfected beauty of thy soul.
   So let them praise the world, I'll praise thy part,
   For thou art nature's finest work of art.

###554###
Oft in my dreams, a kinder fate I see,
Wherein no distance holds us two apart,
And fortune's cruel and unjust decree
Hath not the power to possess my heart.
In dreams, thy hand in mine I gently hold,
And watch the easy smile upon thy face,
A treasure richer far than gems or gold,
A perfect moment in a perfect place.
But then the dawn, that thief of fantasies,
Doth steal this vision with its morning light,
And I awake to life's realities,
And to the sorrow of the endless night.
   Thus I prefer my sleep, with dreams of you,
   To waking life, where all my grief is true.

###555###
My love is not a new-sprung, greening thing,
That with the changing season comes and goes,
To flourish in the sunshine of the spring,
And die beneath the winter's falling snows.
It is no bud that fears a passing frost,
Nor summer flower that with the heat will fade;
It is a tree whose worth cannot be lost,
Whose roots run deeper than a sorrow's shade.
Time's angry axe may strike and leave a scar,
The winds of chance may tear a bough away,
But still it stands beneath a hopeful star,
And waits the dawning of a brighter day.
   So though the storms of life may rage and cease,
   My love for thee shall know a lasting peace.

###556###
If all my thoughts were writ for men to see,
Upon a scroll that all the world might read,
They'd find no word that was not born of thee,
For thou art root and flower of my creed.
My morning thought is of thy waking grace,
My noonday thought, how I might see thee soon,
My evening prayer is for thy resting-place,
Beneath the watch of the approving moon.
Each memory, each hope that I possess,
Is but a thread within a tapestry,
Whose central image, I must needs confess,
Is but the fair and perfect shape of thee.
   So if my mind seems ever far away,
   'Tis with thy spirit that my thoughts do stray.

###557###
Why dost thou spend thy breath to chide my verse,
And say my praise is much too great a gift?
To speak less of thy beauty were a curse,
A truth my honest pen could never shift.
As well to ask the river not to flow,
Or tell the faithful sun he shines too bright;
As well to bid the summer wind not blow,
Or rob the heavens of their starry light.
My praise is but a mirror, clear and true,
That shows the world what I can plainly see;
It is no flattery, but what is due
To the great wonder that resides in thee.
   So blame me not if I thy virtues sing;
   The fault's in thee, for being such a thing.

###558###
I made a vow to hold my tongue in chains,
And keep my heart's affection out of sight,
To spare thee from my sorrow's bitter rains,
And lock my love within an endless night.
I swore my eyes no longer would betray
The foolish worship that they hold for thee,
And from thy presence I would turn away,
Lest in my gaze my secret you should see.
But vows are straw, and oaths are dust, I find,
When thy sweet image comes before my eye;
They cannot bind the passion of the mind,
Nor quell the soul that utters its soft cry.
   My will is weak, my promises are air;
   I see thy face, and I am in despair.

###559###
The cunning painter with his skillful hand
Can steal a likeness from a sitter's face,
And make a painted shadow seem to stand
With captured beauty and with copied grace.
He mimics well the colour of the eye,
The gentle curve of lip, the fall of hair,
But his poor art can never truly spy
The living spirit that doth linger there.
He cannot paint the music of thy voice,
Nor show the kindness shining from thy soul;
He cannot capture that which makes my choice,
The inner light that makes my being whole.
   His art is but a mask, a clever lie;
   Thy true self lives but in my heart's fond eye.

###560###
So let this be the last, my final word,
Though not the final thought I have of thee;
Let this poor sonnet be the last one heard,
To tell the tale of thy effect on me.
This book is closed, the weary ink is dry,
The poet's voice now falls to silent rest;
No more shall my fond, foolish verses try
To paint the burning torment in my breast.
But though the songs may cease, the music stays,
A constant echo in my memory;
The sun may set, but still its golden rays
Will light the path my heart takes back to thee.
   And in that silence, may my love be known,
   More than in all the seeds of praise I've sown.

###521###
My love, thy beauty is a constant sun,
Whose holy light no passing cloud can stain,
Whereas the day's bright course is quickly run,
And yields too soon to night's obscuring reign.
The fairest rose that in the garden grows
Must bow its head when summer's lease is spent,
And all that lives, the way of nature knows,
To fading age by Time's decree is sent.
But in the aspect of thy lovely face,
A timeless spring doth hold eternal sway,
And age's winter finds therein no place
To steal one jot of beauty's light away.
  So let the world to its own ruin fall,
  Thy grace alone is ample store for all.

###522###
Each weary mile that takes me far away
Doth work a treason on my own true heart,
And ev'ry passing moment of the day
I feel a life from which I am apart.
In busy crowds no company I find,
But think on thee, and so become more sad,
For thou art all that occupies my mind,
And robb'st me of what little joy I had.
The sweetest sights a lesser pleasure seem,
The brightest thoughts are to a sorrow grown,
I live my life as in a waking dream,
And in the midst of many, feel alone.
  For what is all the world, and all I see,
  When its best part is absent far from me?

###523###
Shall I not fight against the tyrant Time,
And save thy beauty from a mortal state,
Who thinks to make thy fading a sad crime,
And give thy graces an forgotten date?
No, he shall fail, though monuments of brass
He turns to ruin and to swift decay,
And makes the glory of the world to pass,
As if its beauty were but for a day.
Yet in the black ink of this humble pen,
Thy living likeness shall for ever shine,
To be admired again by future men,
And prove thy beauty truly is divine.
  So shall my love a second being give,
  And in my verse thy better part shall live.

###524###
Thou hast established such a cruel reign,
That all my thoughts must thy commands obey,
And in my heart I feel a constant pain,
From which my soul can never get away.
Thou art a tyrant, beautiful and proud,
Who with a glance can make my spirit fall,
And speak my sentence to the listening crowd,
To hold my very being in thy thrall.
Why must I love the source of all my grief,
And praise the hand that deals so harsh a blow,
And in my torment find no sweet relief,
But from thy power, nowhere else can go?
  For by thy law my fate is overthrown,
  And thou dost rule from beauty's fatal throne.

###525###
This world's a stage, and all of us poor fools
Are taught our lines by some unseen design;
We play at being kings, and break the rules,
And think the wit we speak is truly thine or mine.
We strut and fret, and make a petty show
Of all our triumphs and our miseries,
Until the prompter, death, doth let us know
Our part is done, and all our clamour is.
But thou, my love, art no such actor here;
Thy truth is real, thy grace is not a part,
Thou art the audience that I hold most dear,
And the sole critic of my fumbling art.
  So let me play my role as best I can,
  To win thy praise is all the plot and plan.

###526###
When all the world is to its slumber gone,
And silent darkness holds its gentle sway,
I lie awake, and watch until the dawn,
And in my thoughts I waste the night away.
My mind becomes a court of sad debate,
Where arguments of thee rise and contend,
I count my losses and my poor estate,
And find in sorrow I have lost a friend.
My heavy eyes, which can no closure find,
Stare at the shadows which about me creep,
And form thine image in my troubled mind,
So that I see thee, though I cannot sleep.
  For day's a toil, and night denies me rest,
  While with thy phantom I am so possessed.

###527###
My heart, which once was mine and mine alone,
Hath fled its home within my troubled breast,
And to thy sweeter service it hath flown,
There to have found a more contented rest.
And thy fond heart, in fair and just exchange,
Hath left its lodging in thy gentle side,
And in my bosom, finds it nothing strange
To have a new, but faithful, place to bide.
So am I thine, and so art thou made mine,
A happy bargain that we both have made,
Thy truth in me, and my faith in thee shine,
A debt of love that's willingly been paid.
  Thus two are one, and one is doubly blessed,
  For in each other's heart, we are possessed.

###528###
When I behold the barren, frozen field,
And hear the wind's cold and lamenting cry,
I see the face of winter is revealed,
Beneath the grey and melancholy sky.
The trees, like skeletons, against the light
Do stretch their empty arms as if to pray;
The world is wrapped in an untimely night,
And all the life of summer's fled away.
So is my soul, when thou art not in sight,
A cold and desolate and empty place,
Devoid of comfort, warmth, and all delight,
Until I look again upon thy face.
  For thou art summer to my winter's cold,
  And in thy presence, all things turn to gold.

###529###
What pow'r did Nature for thy making use,
When she designed thy strange and wondrous frame?
For in thy face she did at once produce
A thing that puts her other works to shame.
The rose's blush that on thy cheek doth lie,
The morning's gold that in thy hair is spun,
The starry brightness of thy piercing eye,
Hath all her simple patterns over-run.
She broke the mould when she had finished thee,
Lest she might try to make thy like again,
And in her pride, she did at once decree
That all her 'prentice work was poor and vain.
  Thus art thou proof, in living form expressed,
  That Nature's best can rival all the rest.

###530###
My heart and eye are at a mortal war,
And each the other's verdict doth deny;
My eye doth swear you are the brightest star,
My heart affirms that this is all a lie.
My heart declares your soul is full of scorn,
That pride and cruelty within you dwell;
My eye, by your sweet outward look, is sworn
That from your face no evil thing can well.
And so these two, my senses and my soul,
Are locked in combat for the truth of you;
My reason cannot take its due control,
To judge between the witness of the two.
  Then I, the judge, must end this bitter strife,
  And take thee as thou art, my death-in-life.

###531###
When fortune's spite and men's contemptuous scorn
Have left me outcast in my wretched state,
And I, all friendless, weary, and forlorn,
Bewail my lot and curse my bitter fate,
And wish I were like one with greater hope,
Or rich in friends, or blessed with noble skill,
And with myself in discontent do cope,
And all my thoughts with useless sorrow fill;
Yet in these moments, should I think on thee,
My soul, which lately was so near the ground,
Takes wing like to the lark, and flies with glee,
And in thy memory is such treasure found.
  That then I scorn to change my state with kings,
  For thy sweet love such wealthy comfort brings.

###532###
I do not love thee for a cheek of red,
Nor for a lip that shames the coral's hue,
Nor for the golden wires upon thy head;
There's nothing in thy face that men call true.
Thine eyes are not like suns, they are but eyes;
Thy voice is speech, and not an angel's song;
And from thy breath no sweet perfume doth rise;
In thy plain form, the world finds much is wrong.
And yet there is a magic in thy look,
A subtle grace that reason cannot name,
A secret chapter in a hidden book,
That sets my very intellect aflame.
  Let others praise the beauty they can see;
  I love the soul that thou hast shown to me.

###533###
How canst thou seem so fair and be so false?
How can such truth upon thy brow be writ,
While in thy heart, thy loyalty defaults,
And for a stranger's love, thou quittest it?
Thy words are sworn with such a saintly grace,
As if each vow were taken on thy soul,
And yet they vanish, leaving not a trace,
As soon as some new fancy takes control.
I see the beauty, and I am undone,
I hear the promise, and I dare to trust,
And ere the setting of another sun,
I find my hopes are scattered into dust.
  So is thy face a lovely, fatal snare,
  To make wise men believe what is not there.

###534###
If not with love, then look on me with hate,
But do not greet me with indifference;
To know my doom

###481###
My love is not like seasons, which must change,
It follows not the sun's appointed way;
It finds the fields of winter nothing strange,
Nor thinks the longest night a lesser day.
For what it loves, it loves beyond the eye,
And sees no fault when youthful blossoms fade;
It is a star that lives in beauty's sky,
By which my constant, truest course is made.
So let Time steal the colour from thy cheek,
And draw sad lines where laughter used to be;
My heart for thee a truer tongue shall speak,
Than what the fading light of day can see.
   So though by age thy outward form is crossed,
   No jot of my firm love for thee is lost.

###482###
When I do hear the lute's most pleasant sound,
And watch how skilled a player's fingers play,
My soul, though raised, feels chained unto the ground,
And wishes that the music drift away.
For in my mind a sweeter music sings,
Which from thy throat doth make a heavenly note,
A joy that shames the fairest tune of strings,
Whose lightest word on silent air doth float.
Yea, all the world's composers I may hear,
As they conspire to make a harmony divine,
But all their craft is worthless to mine ear,
Compared to one sweet, simple word of thine.
   So let them cease, their efforts are but bare;
   Thy voice alone is music past compare.

###483###
When I account my poor and wretched state,
And send my worthless cries to heavens high,
Then do I curse the darkness of my fate,
And get from heedless heaven no reply;
Despising what I am, I look with scorn
On other men, with far more cause for shame,
And wish that I had not to this been born,
And bear what feels a miserable name.
Yet from this pit of absolute despair,
My soul on sudden, hopeful wings can rise,
And breathe again a less tormented air,
When thy dear image comes before mine eyes.
   For with that thought such sudden wealth is brought,
   I scorn my state, and triumph in that thought.

###484###
That thief, old Time, upon thy lovely brow,
Doth seek to carve the place where wrinkles hold;
What is but beauty's blossom, even now,
He means to spoil, and turn its grace from gold.
He is a tyrant, stealing precious youth,
And gives no quarter for the theft he'll make;
He scoffs at pleas and disregards all truth,
And all that's fair he promises to take.
But in my verse thy yet-unblemished grace
Shall find a haven, safe from his decree,
Where future ages may still see thy face,
And know how fair thou once didst use to be.
   So by my pen thy youth shall ever stay,
   And mock the powers of ruin and decay.

###485###
When this world's glories one by one must fade,
And all that lives, we in its dying see,
When beauty's light is cast into the shade,
And what is fair, no longer fair shall be;
When monuments of brass are turned to dust,
And all the truths of old are proved a lie,
And even strongest iron yields to rust,
And all that ever breathed is born to die;
Yet in the haven of these faithful lines,
Thy beauty shall not suffer such decay,
A second sun, thy lasting image shines,
And drives the shadows of the tomb away.
   For here thou livest, captured by my art,
   And hast a second life within my heart.

###486###
My foolish eye pretends it doth not see
The common flaws that play a mortal's part;
It paints a saint, where mortal cannot be,
And sends this false report unto my heart.
My heart accepts this comfortable lie
Fed by the treason of my doting eye,
And overlooks the lack of inner grace
For painted beauty on a lovely face.
So what my captive senses now do hold
Is but a fiction, rimmed about with gold,
And what is real, and what does only seem,
Are lost within the fever of a dream.
   And thus enthralled, I am by my own will blind,
   And worship faults I do refuse to find.

###487###
To be from thee is to be not myself,
A harsher exile heart has never known;
The world's great treasure and its gathered pelf
Is but a straw, when I am left alone.
The sun may shine with all his summer might,
The birds may sing their songs upon the bough,
But all is darkness to my longing sight,
And all their music is but discord now.
For thou art both the measure and the cause
Of all that's good, or beautiful, or true;
Without thy presence, Nature shows her flaws,
And loses all her once-resplendent hue.
   So every hour that holds us two apart
   Is a long winter's age within my heart.

###488###
That green-eyed monster, Jealousy, doth feed
Upon the scraps my worried mind lets fall;
It takes a harmless word, a friendly deed,
And builds a towering sorrow from them all.
It poisons truth and makes a virtue seem
The veiled disguise of some unkind deceit;
It turns my waking hours to a bad dream,
And makes what once was wholly sweet, un-sweet.
O, teach my heart to quell this needless strife,
And trust the promise that thy love has made,
For this suspicion wounds my very life,
And casts my constant faith into the shade.
   If thou art true, then I am twice a fool,
   To let a baseless fear my reason rule.

###489###
Some praise the diamond for its peerless light,
Some say the ruby's fire is most divine,
Some claim the sapphire, blue as darkest night,
Doth all the lesser colored stones outshine.
But were I tasked to find a gem so rare
As might with thy own worthiness compare,
I'd scorn the earth, for nothing in its ground
Could ever hold the likeness of thee found.
Thy constancy's a diamond, clear and true,
Thy wit a sapphire of the deepest thought,
Thy love a ruby of the richest hue,
In whom all virtues are together wrought.
   So these are but the parts, and thou the whole,
   A living jewel that captivates the soul.

###490###
Love makes me strong, that I can bear the scorn
Of all the world, and count it little loss;
I feel a king, though wretchedly I'm born,
And cheerfully can take my heavy cross.
Love makes me weak, that at a single word
From thy dear lips, my courage melts away;
My firmest resolution is unheard,
And all my strength doth suddenly decay.
So am I in a strange and doubtful state,
Both slave and master, in a single breath;
Lifted to heaven by a blessed fate,
And in a moment plunged to near a death.
   This is love's power, this his mystic art,
   To rule with warring kings my tender heart.

###491###
My love's two eyes are nothing like the sun,
Her skin is dun, her hair like wiry black;
No roseate hue upon her cheek doth run,
And for a goddess' grace she hath a lack.
Her voice, when she doth speak, I will confess,
Doth not hold music's soul-enchanting sound;
She treads the earth with mortal heaviness,
And is no angel gliding o'er the ground.
And yet, by heaven, I think my love as rare,
And full of worth, as any she alive,
That is puffed up by false and vain compare,
On which such poets' hollow praises thrive.
   For she is true, and being true and mine,
   She wants no praise to make her light divine.

###492###
This world's a stage, and all men players be,
Who strut and fret their hour and are forgot;
Each has his part, his speech, his role's decree,
And plays his scene within a passing plot.
I have watched kings and lovers, fools and knaves,
Each mouth his lines with practiced, hollow art,
I have seen heroes marching to their graves,
As if they knew by heart their tragic part.
But when thou comest upon this wooden scene,
All other actors fade and hold their breath;
For truth itself attends thy modest mien,
And honesty in every word thou saith.
   So let them play their parts and be undone,
   My world's whole stage belongs to thee alone.

###493###
My mind and heart a dreadful war do wage,
And both use thee as their contested prize;
My mind doth claim thou art a gilded cage,
And warns my heart against thy lovely eyes.
It brings forth proofs and reasons, cold and stark,
Of pains that from such beauty oft do grow;
It bids me flee the inevitable dark
That waits for all who in love's garden go.
But then my heart, that foolish, tender thing,
Makes its reply, and speaks of thy dear face;
It claims in thee, all goodness has its spring,
And finds in thy defects a kind of grace.
   So stand I, torn between this fatal strife,
   Led by my heart to risk my mind's own life.

###494###
Now bitter winter holds the land in frost,
And chokes the field where summer's green did thrive;
The trees stand bare, their leafy pride all lost,
And scarce a living thing appears alive.
The sun, a stranger, shows a pallid face,
And yields his kingdom to the biting wind;
There is no comfort in this barren place,
No warmth or shelter for the eye to find.
Yet while this desolation rules the year,
And all the world is captured by the cold,
I feel no chill when thou, my love, art near,
But in thy presence, summer I behold.
   For in thy heart the truest

###581###
When I inscribe thy name upon the page,
I think how poor a thing this ink must be
To capture thee, and battle with the age
That seeks to steal thy memory from me.
This brittle paper and this fading script
Can not contain the sun that is thy face,
From which all grace and beauty have been sipped,
And where true virtue finds its dwelling place.
Yet since all worldly things to ruin haste,
And brass and stone by uncouth time are rent,
My loving words shall this brief tribute taste,
Upon this fragile monument intent.
   So when all else to dust and death is brought,
   Thy life in this, my verse, shall still be sought.

###582###
The winter wind, with breath of bitter frost,
Doth steal the leaves that summer held so dear,
And in this loss, I count my own sad cost,
For my life's summer fled when thou wert here.
Now every branch, a skeleton so stark,
Doth claw against a sky of endless grey,
And every bird that sang, from dawn to dark,
Hath taken wing and likewise flown away.
So is my heart, a landscape cold and bare,
Devoid of warmth, and stripped of all delight,
A frozen realm of sorrow and despair,
A sunless day that turns to starless night.
   Yet thought of thee, a distant, hopeful spring,
   Makes promise of the future joy 'twill bring.

###583###
Why dost thou spend thy beauty on the air,
And give to careless eyes what should be mine?
Why let the common crowd behold thee fair,
And on the undeserving world to shine?
The sun gives not his gold to endless night,
The rose keeps not her scent for desert stone,
Nor does the star bestow its silver light
On realms where its soft glory is unknown.
Then keep thy treasures from the plund'ring gaze
That knows not how to value such a prize,
And save the wonder of thy gracious ways
For one who sees a heaven in thine eyes.
   Lest thou, too generous, give thy all away,
   And have no riches for a later day.

###584###
My weary eyes, that long for restful sleep,
Do find no peace when darkness doth descend,
For in my mind, thy constant share they keep,
My waking thoughts upon thy form attend.
And when at last to slumber I am brought,
My dreams present thee to my spirit's sight,
A phantom shape with thy perfection wrought,
That gives false solace to the lonely night.
So by the day, thy absence is my grief,
And by the night, my dreams do mock me so,
That from my sorrow I find no relief,
And from this torment, nowhere can I go.
   Thus day and night a constant war they make,
   For one my peace, the other, joy doth take.

###585###
Let those who trust in princes and in might
Bestow their praise on pow'r and high estate;
Let merchants track the gold that shines so bright,
And scholars on their learned books await.
Let soldiers seek their glory in the fray,
And builders raise their monuments of stone;
All these shall find their triumphs must decay,
The honors that they fought for, overthrown.
But I, who have no care for worldly fame,
Nor wish to hold what fickle time can seize,
Do find my world encompassed in thy name,
And draw from thee my comfort and my ease.
   For what is wealth or pow'r or high renown,
   When in thy love, I wear a greater crown?

###586###
As some poor miser, counting o'er his gold,
Doth fear the thief who comes in dark of night,
So I, within my fearful heart, do hold
The precious treasure of thy beauty's light.
Each glance another gives, I deem a theft,
Each word of praise, a raid upon my store,
Until I feel of all my wealth bereft,
And think myself unfortunate and poor.
Is this not madness, so to guard a thing
That by its nature cannot be possessed?
For beauty, like a bird upon the wing,
Can never in one single place find rest.
   Yet in this madness, there's a certain joy,
   To love a wealth that no one can destroy.

###587###
When forty winters shall besiege thy brow,
And dig deep trenches in thy beauty's field,
Thy youth's proud livery, so boasted now,
Will be a tattered weed, of little held.
Then being asked where all thy beauty lies,
Where all the treasure of thy lusty days,
To say, within thine own deep sunken eyes,
Were an all-eating shame and thriftless praise.
How much more praise deserved thy beauty's use,
If thou couldst answer, "This fair child of mine
Shall sum my count and make my old excuse,"
Proving his beauty by succession thine.
   This were to be new made when thou art old,
   And see thy blood warm when thou feel'st it cold.

###588###
Must I compare thee to a summer's day?
That tired conceit hath worn itself so thin;
Thy beauty shines in a more constant way
Than any season that the year brings in.
The sun may hide its face behind a cloud,
The roughest winds may spoil the buds of May,
The fairest flower is in a funeral shroud
Ere long, as its brief glory fades away.
But thou, a light that holds no changing phase,
Dost shame the sun and mock the fleeting rose;
Thy virtue shines through all thy mortal days,
A steady grace that only stronger grows.
   So let the poets praise the summer's lease,
   Thy endless summer shall know no decrease.

###589###
Against that time, if ever that time come,
When I shall see thy love has met its end,
When thy sweet words to me are cold and numb,
And as a stranger, not as a dear friend,
Thou look'st on me; against that bitter day
I arm my heart with forethought of the pain,
That I might find some fortitude, and say
I knew that mortal love could not remain.
For I have seen the strongest oaths undone,
And fealty turn to discord and to strife,
And seen the race that loyalty had run
Cut short by the unkindly, cruel knife.
   Yet for all this, if thou shouldst prove untrue,
   My well-armed heart would break itself in two.

###590###
My tongue-tied muse, in silence bound so long,
Hath seen thy worth extolled by other quills,
And heard thy praises in a lesser song,
A tribute that thy greater glory stills.
Should I then speak, and with my meagre art
Attempt to paint what is beyond all praise?
Should I describe the kindness of thy heart,
Or the sweet wonder of thy gentle ways?
No, let them write who can with finer skill
Give voice to what is plain for all to see;
Let my part be to love thee and be still,
Lest my poor words should do some wrong to thee.
   For where true beauty doth itself commend,
   The greatest verse must in mute wonder end.

###591###
What potion hath this wicked world contrived
That makes me see thy faults as if they're fair?
From what strange madness is this thought derived,
That I find virtue in what others dare
To name as vice? If thou art proud or vain,
My foolish heart doth call it kingly grace;
If thy sharp tongue doth cause another pain,
I see but wit and truth writ on thy face.
This blind devotion, making wrong seem right,
Doth poison judgment and corrupt the mind,
So that I love the darkness, not the light,
And to thy truest nature am unkind.
   For in my praise of flaws, I do thee wrong,
   And make my love a weak and foolish song.

###592###
Was it the echo of some half-heard word
That made thee turn thy noble face from me?
Or some false slander that thy spirit stirred
To doubt the faith I keep alone for thee?
If so, then cruelest fortune is my foe,
To let a whisper tear our love apart,
And with a poisoned, unsubstantial blow,
Strike at the very centre of my heart.
But if on purpose thou hast sought this change,
To free thy fancy for some other quest,
Then is thy nature truly new and strange,
And all thy former kindness but a jest.
   Yet tell me plainly, that I may not doubt,
   If thy love's candle be but newly out.

###593###
This world is but a shadow, scant and pale,
Of that true world that lives within thy soul,
A fleeting story and a half-told tale
That thy perfection maketh sound and whole.
The colours here are but a faded wash
Compared to hues that in thy spirit dwell;
The glories of the earth are poor and rash
Next to the grace of which no tongue can tell.
Why then should I seek beauty in a rose,
Or look for comfort in the sun's frail light,
When all fulfillment my affection knows
Is found entirely in thy blessed sight?
   Thou art the substance, all the rest is dream,
   The living fountain, not the shallow stream.

###594###
Like to a ship that, having lost its guide,
Is tossed and battered by the angry sea,
So is my soul, since thou art not beside
To be the constant northern star for me.
The winds of doubt, the waves of deep despair,
Do drive my hopeless vessel to and fro;
I have no chart, no comfort anywhere,
And to what distant harbour, I know not.
My sails are rent, my weary rudder's gone,
My hopes are drowned within the ocean's grey,
And I am left to face the storm alone,
And for a saving glimpse of thee to pray.
   Without thy love, I am but sure to wreck,
   So put thy hand upon my fortune's check.

###595###
The forward violet thus did I chide:
"Sweet thief, whence didst thou steal thy sweet that smells,
If not from my love's breath?" The white lily replied,
"Fie on thee, thief, that in thy pride excels
To steal thy whiteness from my love's fair hand."
The marjoram had stolen thy dark hair;
The roses fearfully on thorns did stand,
One blushing shame, another white despair;
A third, nor red nor white, had stolen of both,
And to his robbery had annexed thy breath;
But for his theft, in pride of all this growth,
A vengeful canker eats him up to death.
   More flowers I noted, yet I none could see,
   But sweet or colour it had stol'n from thee.

###596###
That I am thine, the record is on high,
A truth that angels in their books have writ;
Nor can the turning spheres that wheel the sky
Persuade my constant heart to question it.
Let others build their love on shifting sand,
To be washed out when fortune's tide runs low;
My love is built upon a firmer land,
Where storms may beat, but it can never go.
For it is rooted in thy very being,
A part of thee, as thou art part of me,
And with a clear and spiritual seeing,
I know our souls are one for enternity.
   So let this world do what it has to do,
   My love is changeless, as my love is you.

###597###
So oft have I invoked thee for my Muse,
And found thy beauty fitter for my theme
Than any spirit which the poets use
To grace their fictions of a waking dream.
And in the process, I have made so bare
My endless love, so public and so known,
That every idle tongue can now declare
The deep devotion I for thee have shown.
Have I not cheapened what should be most prized,
And made a common ballad of our love?
Is my true feeling scorned and now despised,
A tale for gossips to make judgment of?
   No, let them look, my verse shall make them see
   That such a love gives fame to them, and me.

###598###
How can I say I love thee, when my deeds
So poorly show the passion in my breast?
My heart is like a field of barren weeds,
Wherein no flower of service finds its rest.
I make great vows, but they are only breath;
I promise gifts beyond the scope of kings,
Then give thee but a love that courts its death
Through small neglects and inconsiderate things.
My tongue can speak of fire, my hands are cold;
My mind can build a temple to thy name,
But then my lazy spirit, growing old,
Forgets to light the altar with a flame.
   Forgive the man whose art is not so strong
   As is the love that has possessed him long.

###599###
Tired of all these, for restful death I cry,
As, to behold desert a beggar born,
And needy nothing trimmed in jollity,
And purest faith unhappily forsworn,
And gilded honour shamefully misplaced,
And maiden virtue rudely strumpeted,
And right perfection wrongfully disgraced,
And strength by limping sway disabled,
And art made tongue-tied by authority,
And folly, doctor-like, controlling skill,
And simple truth miscalled simplicity,
And captive good attending captain ill:
   Tired of all these, from these would I be gone,
   Save that, to die, I leave my love alone.

###600###
When in the chronicle of wasted time
I see descriptions of the fairest wights,
And beauty making beautiful old rhyme
In praise of ladies dead and lovely knights,
Then, in the blazon of sweet beauty's best,
Of hand, of foot, of lip, of eye, of brow,
I see their antique pen would have expressed
But a dim prophecy of what thou art now.
So all their praises are but prophecies
Of this our time, all you prefiguring;
And for they looked but with divining eyes,
They had not skill enough thy worth to sing.
   For we, which now behold these present days,
   Have eyes to wonder, but lack tongues to praise.

###561###
When I behold thy beauty, fresh and fair,
Which puts to shame the glory of the day,
Time's cruel hand shall seek its own repair,
And o'er thy youth hold unresisted sway.
For that which now appears so pure and bright,
The world's admiring eye can scarce behold,
Must yield at last unto eternal night,
And in Death's ledger be accounted old.
But in the living record of my art,
Wherein thy truest essence I can see,
I shall preserve the treasure of thy heart,
That future ages may give praise to thee.
   So shall thy grace defy consuming time,
   And live forever in this humble rhyme.

###562###
Since cruel distance holds me far away,
No comfort can I find within my heart,
For thou, the sun that glorifies my day,
Didst from thy humble servant so depart.
I look for thee, but get an empty sight,
And for a solace, find a bitter tear;
The weary hours of the lonely night
Do make thy want a hundred times more dear.
So in my solitude I make my moan,
For every league that takes thee from my heart;
My wretched soul doth feel itself alone,
And bears of misery the greater part.
   Yet thought can fly where bodies may not be,
   And in a moment bring my love to me.

###563###
Thy words, which once did seem so passing sweet,
Are but

###601###
When in thine eyes I dare to cast my gaze,
I lose the path of reason's sober light,
And stand before thy beauty in a maze,
Like some poor traveller lost within the night.
For what are stars that poets long to see,
Compared to orbs that hold such wondrous art?
By heaven's own hand it was a firm decree,
That from their spell no mortal should depart.
Yet should I try with Phoebus to make fair
A lesser gleam, or praise a meaner grace,
My words would fail, for naught can I compare
To the celestial glory of thy face.
So let me dumbly in thy presence shine,
And own no light but that which is all thine.

###602###
When wasteful Time with his destructive hand
Doth carve deep furrows in a youthful brow,
And makes a ruin of the fairest land,
What once was then, is but a shadow now.
His cruel scythe, more sharp than any steel,
Doth seek to mar thy much admired face,
And make thy heart his cold dominion feel,
To steal away thy beauty and thy grace.
But let him rage and turn the passing year,
And bring all worldly glories to decay;
His power cannot touch what I hold dear,
Nor cause thy truest self to waste away.
For in my verse, thy spirit liveth true,
And conquers Time, which cannot conquer you.

###603###
When monuments

###681###
When winter’s frost a silver wood doth show
Upon the glass, a world of brittle hue,
Its fragile ferns and forests swiftly go
When morning sun presents his face to view.
So other beauties in their prime delight
The watch-ful eye with but a moment’s grace,
And having shone, they vanish from the sight,
And leave behind no memory of their face.
But thy fair form, which holds my heart in fee,
Hath no such kinship with a fading scene;
It is a sun that time shall never see
Descend, nor make its constant light less keen.
For though the frost must yield unto the day,
Thy summer beauty shall not melt away.

###682###
To be from thee is to be sunk in night,
Though sun may burnish all the world with gold;
My pensive soul, deprived of thy sweet light,
Doth in a dungeon dark its sorrows hold.
Each hour a year, each minute a long day,
My thoughts, like pilgrims, to thy image roam;
They find thee there, and there they wish to stay,
And curse the flesh that keeps them from their home.
So is my spirit with thy spirit blessed,
While this dull earth, my body, must endure
The heavy weight of being sore oppressed
By want of that which is my only cure.
And thus two parts of my poor self I find:
One with thee lives, and one is left behind.

###6-8-3###
I see the work of Time’s most cruel hand,
Which carves its lines upon a youthful face,
And makes the strongest tower no longer stand,
And robs each monument of ancient grace.
That hungry thief, with ever-stealing pace,
Doth turn to dust what once was built so strong,
And what was fair, he labours to deface,
And proves our worldly triumphs are not long.
When I this universal wreck behold,
And see how all things must at last decline,
This bitter thought maketh my courage cold:
That same proud thief shall steal that form of thine.
Yet in my verse thy beauty shall contend
With wasteful Time, and never see an end.

###6-8-4###
When brass and stone are by the years worn down,
And gates of steel to rust and ruin fall,
When kings and princes lose their high renown,
And silence answers to their empty call;
How shall thy beauty, fragile as a flower,
Expect to fare when such strong things decay,
Or hope to stand against that bitter power
Which turns the proudest works of man to clay?
O, in this battle, thou shalt not be lost,
For this my verse shall be thy monument,
And shall endure whatever be the cost,
When all the world to its own doom is sent.
So while men breathe, and mortal eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###685###
My heavy eyes, when I am laid to sleep,
Do not find rest, but find a stranger art,
For in my dreams thy shadow they do keep,
And bring thy vision closer to my heart.
Then darkness turns to day, and I can trace
The arching brow, the lip, the searching eye;
And for a moment, in that sacred space,
The pains of absence and of distance die.
But when the dawn, that jealous enemy,
Doth with its light my fantasy dispel,
I wake to find my sorrow stay with me,
And taste again the bitterness of hell.
Thus day by sorrow, night by dreams I’m tried,
And find no comfort, waking or aside.

###686###
The world admires what is but outward show,
And praises most what carries finest gloss,
And beneath gilded surfaces, we know,
Lies often hid a soul of utter dross.
A face of virtue may a villain wear,
A tongue of honey speak a poisoned thought,
And what seems wholesome, pure, and passing fair
May be with cunning and deceit enwrought.
But thou, my love, in thee all truths unite,
Thy outward fairness and thy soul are one;
Thy virtue is as radiant as the light,
Thy inner grace eclipsed by naught under sun.
Let others feign what they would wish to be;
Thy truth is beauty, plain for all to see.

###687###
Why dost thou build thy fortress out of stone,
And arm thy heart with armour of disdain?
Canst thou not hear thy loyal servant’s groan,
Or see the tribute of my falling rain?
If in thine eyes a judgment I might find,
’Twould be a sentence kinder than this chill,
For this deep silence grieveth more my mind
Than any cruel word or stated ill.
O, speak but once, and let thy verdict be
To kill my hope or give it leave to grow;
But in this war of doubt, what victory
Canst thou proclaim when I am brought so low?
Break up thy siege of cold and cruel pride,
Lest in its shadow, all my love hath died.

###688###
When autumn strips the branches of their gold,
And chilling winds usurp the summer’s breeze,
The weary world, grown suddenly more old,
Laments the loss of its late verdant ease.
Then winter comes, in robes of starkest white,
To bind the fields in chains of ice and frost,
And banish from the skies all cheerful light,
Till we do think the sun itself is lost.
Yet spring returns to make the meadows new,
And bids the frozen streams to run again;
The world revives with every shade and hue,
And life returns to banish winter’s pain.
So may my love, though in a winter now,
See thy sweet spring, and to its warmth allow.

###689###
My foolish heart, which knows not wrong from right,
Doth look on thee and finds no fault at all;
It is a subject yielding to thy might,
And at thy feet it willingly doth fall.
But then my eye, more skillful to perceive,
Doth spy a flaw, a shadow, or a stain,
And sends a message that I should not give
My whole devotion, lest it be in vain.
The heart replies that love is ever blind,
And true affection pardons every sin;
The eye insists a truer course to find,
And bids me look at what is foul within.
Yet heart o’er eye doth win this great debate;
I see the fault, but still I love my fate.

###690###
What heavy thoughts do murder wholesome sleep,
And make the night a council of despair,
When all the wakeful world doth silence keep,
And I alone must with my sorrows bear?
My mind, a restless pilgrim, travels far,
And in the darkness fashions thy sweet face;
Thou art my sun, my moon, my morning star,
The single light in this benighted place.
But then the thought that thou art far away
Doth quench that light, and I am left to mourn
The coming of another empty day,
Of all my hope and all my comfort shorn.
So night brings dreams, and dreams bring waking grief,
And from this torment there is no relief.

###691###
This world’s a stage, where every man must play
His given role before the judging crowd;
Some have a part of joy for one short day,
While others in a tragic garment shroud.
My part, it seems, is but to stand and weep,
To speak of loss and unrequited love,
And in my breast a constant sorrow keep,
Sent from some cruel director from above.
I act my grief, but ah, it is not feigned,
For every sigh is from my spirit drawn,
And every tear upon my cheek is stained
With true despair that greets each lonely dawn.
And my reward, when all my scenes are done,
Is but the knowing that my play is won.

###692###
When memory doth hold a solemn court,
And calls to stand the ghosts of things long past,
I summon up, for my most sweet support,
The vision of the day I saw thee last.
I see again the sunshine in thy hair,
The sudden smile that did my soul ensnare,
A moment captured, perfect and so rare,
A jewel brighter than I could compare.
But this same joy a present sorrow makes,
And this sweet ghost a bitter torment is,
For now my heart with desolation aches
To think that I no longer have that bliss.
So what was once a balm for all my pain,
Is now a wound that opens up again.

###693###
I do begrudge the very air you breathe,
And envy how it may embrace you so;
The ground on which your graceful footsteps cleave,
I curse that I cannot so near you go.
I am jealous of the sun’s most holy light,
That it may kiss your cheek where I may not;
The moon that watches o’er you in the night
Doth mock my soul with its more happy lot.
Yea, every trifle that your hand doth grace,
Each simple gown that doth your form enfold,
Hath by your presence such a blessed place
That its high fortune makes my own seem cold.
If things inanimate such joy can find,
What hope is there for my tormented mind?

###694###
They say that love’s a poison in the blood,
Which reason’s art can find no power to tame;
A fever that, misunderstood,
Doth set the heart and spirit all aflame.
And this I find, for since I looked on thee,
A strange disease hath laid a siege to me;
My thoughts are disordered, I cannot be free
From this sweet sickness and its tyranny.
Yet if this be a poison, it is one
That I would drink though I were sure to die,
For in this fever, all my joys are spun,
And in this sickness, all my comforts lie.
So let it be a poison or a cure,
Thy love’s the only ailment I’ll endure.

###695###
When in a crowd I hear another speak
Thy sacred name, which is my soul’s delight,
My heart doth leap, my falt'ring senses weak,
As though you had appeared before my sight.
That simple sound, upon a stranger’s tongue,
Becomes a hymn, a music to my ear;
And for a moment, all my sorrows, stung
By sudden joy, do wholly disappear.
But then I turn, and see thou art not there,
And that sweet music turns to a sad note;
The joy dissolves into the common air,
And on a sea of grief I am afloat.
Thus does thy name, in pleasure and in pain,
Both build me up and break me down again.

###696###
As some lost traveller in darkest night
Doth spy a star he thinks will be his guide,
And follows with new hope its distant light,
With naught but faith to walk with at his side;
So have I followed Hope, that treach'rous star,
Which shone so brightly in my lonely sky;
It promised joy, and bid me look afar,
And swore my days of sorrow would go by.
But that false gleam, which made my journey bold,
Was but a vapour of my own desire,
A fleeting fire that left me in the cold,
To mock the heart that it had set on fire.
And now I find, my guiding star being gone,
I am more lost than when I first set on.

###697###
Let not the poet praise the distant stars,
Nor call the moon the mistress of the night;
Diana’s beauty and the fire of Mars
Are but as shadows to thy greater light.
For in thine eyes two brighter suns do dwell,
Whose gentle beams can make a summer’s day,
And from their depths such holy truths do tell
As chase the clouds of my despair away.
They are the guide by which my life is steered,
The constant light in sorrow’s endless sea;
A firmament by which I am not feared
Of any storm that may descend on me.
So let the heavens their own glories keep;
My world’s whole sky is in thine eyes so deep.

###698###
My reason pleads with my rebellious will,
And shows the folly of this hopeless state,
It argues that my heart should now be still,
And learn to choose a more deserving fate.
It proves with logic, sharp and cold and clear,
That all my sighs are but a waste of breath,
That to hold one so high who is not near
Is to embrace a shadow, and a death.
But then my will, a tyrant in my breast,
Doth scorn such counsel, and doth praise thy name,
Declaring that in serving thee is rest,
And in thy worship, glory, not a shame.
So reason yields, and passion wins the fight,
For what is reason in love’s blinding light?

###699###
When in my glass I see my own face shown,
I mark the lines that sorrow hath writ there,
The youth that once I proudly called my own,
Now steals away and leaves my brow with care.
The black that once upon my head did grow
Doth yield its place to threads of silver-grey,
A sad reminder that time’s steady flow
Bears all our summer glories to decay.
How then can I, so weathered and so worn,
Present this ruin as a worthy prize?
My worthlessness doth make me feel forlorn
When I imagine I am in thine eyes.
Yet think of this, my love is not so old,
And in my verse, my faith is turned to gold.

###700###
Let fortune change, and let the world grow cold,
And let my friends my very name forget;
My constant love shall not be bought or sold,
Nor on its truth shall any price be set.
Though I be scorned and left in poor estate,
And all my hopes be scattered by the wind,
I shall not curse my miserable fate,
For still thy image lives within my mind.
This love a kingdom is, where I am king,
O’er which mischance can have no pow'r at all;
And from this fortress, I can safely sing
When other, prouder battlements do fall.
So let them take all else that they may find;
They cannot steal the treasure of my mind.

###641###
When I behold the wonder of your face,
And in your eyes, two suns, my fortune read,
The world transforms into a holy place,
Where every common stone's a prayer bead.
Your voice, a music that the angels sing,
Doth silence all the world's discordant sound;
No sweeter note could any season bring,
Nor richer treasure on this earth be found.
But this fair beauty, time shall take in fee,
And what I gaze on, he will steal away;
The rose which blooms today shall cease to be,
And fall to ruin and unseen decay.
Yet in this verse your truer self shall live,
And have that life which my poor pen can give.

###642###
When wasteful Time with his corrupting hand
Shall carve deep furrows in thy lovely brow,
And steal the fairest treasures of thy land,
To make thee other than what thou art now;
When his sharp cruelties, like biting steel,
Shall wound the blossom of thy youthful bloom,
And thou the tyrant's victory must feel,
As all of beauty finds a common tomb;
Yet shall this verse against his slow decay
Make stand, and chase his gathering shades away,
For in my ink thy worth shall live for men,
A fortress built by my defensive pen.
So in these lines a truer life you'll live,
Than fleeting Time could ever hope to give.

###643###
While others, blessed, their

###621###
When other stars do hide their timid light,
And shadows deep conspire to make a tomb,
Thou art the single jewel of the night,
Which doth alone dispel the world's dark gloom.
The weary sailor, lost upon the sea,
Whose chart is rent, whose guide has been misplaced,
Doth mend his course by looking upon thee,
A polestar fixed, of such unchanging grace.
Yet whilst I praise thy firm and constant fire,
My own poor state to sorrow I confess;
I am but drawn by unappeas'd desire
And gain no comfort in my wretchedness.
   For thou art heav'n, and I am bound to earth,
   And curse the day that gave my sorrow birth.

###622###
That cruel thief, old Time, with stealthy pace,
Doth steal the blush from ev'ry youthful cheek,
And carves deep furrows in the fairest face,
And leaves the strong, in their own winter, weak.
He takes the gold from out the finest hair,
And dims the fire that in the eye doth burn,
And bids the heart to couple with despair,
From whose cold kingdom none shall e'er return.
But 'gainst his pow'r thy beauty shall not fail,
For in this verse it finds a sanctuary,
And shall ride out Time's most destructive gale,
Safe from the spoils of his own tyranny.
   So let him rage, and do what fell deeds he must,
   Thy name shall live when his great pow'rs are dust.

###623###
When marble tombs and monuments of brass
By wasteful years to ruin are consign'd,
And all their glories like a dream doth pass,
A truer record shall the future find.
Not in the stone where envious mosses creep,
Nor in the frame that hungry worms shall fret,
Shall thy sweet form its lasting likeness keep,
Or pay to slow Oblivion his dark debt.
For I have built for thee a truer room
Within the structure of this careful rhyme,
To stand against the darkness of the tomb
And mock the swift, destructive hand of Time.
   So eyes unborn shall read of thee so fair,
   And find thy soul still breathing in this air.

###624###
What is the sun when I not see thy face?
Or what is music when thou art not near?
The world becomes a dark and empty place,
And ev'ry pleasing sound I cannot hear.
Each waking hour, a century of pain,
Doth stretch itself upon the rack of day,
Until my thoughts might have thee back again,
To chase the shadows of my soul away.
But thought is poor, it cannot hold thee fast,
It is a ghost that mocks my wanting eye,
A fleeting dream of happiness that's past,
A wat'ry image and a cheating lie.
   So come, my love, and make my darkness bright,
   And with thy presence, grant my world its light.

###625###
Thine eyes, that hold the world's most precious light,
Do make a mockery of the darkest night,
For in their depths a treasure shineth bright,
And blind the sun with their more potent sight.
To gaze on them is to forget all grace
That ever did adorn a mortal face,
For heav'n itself hath found its dwelling place
And holds its court in that most sacred space.
But from those spheres a double war is sent,
A fire to kindle hope, a frost to kill;
On this despair my sad resolve is bent,
And by that hope I am tormented still.
   Thus in thy glance my life and death I find,
   Both raised by thee, and by thee undermined.

###626###
Let idle tongues, which feed on bitter spite,
Conspire to do my constant passion wrong;
Let them declare my day is truly night,
And make my truth the subject of their song.
Let envy forge a thousand artful lies
To paint my loyal faith as counterfeit,
A truth I know your own heart must despise,
And see the trap that for our love is set.
Your fair opinion is the only prize
