For which I strive and hold my honour dear;
Your judgment is the court that makes me wise,
And in your sight my conscience becomes clear.
   So let them talk; their words can have no part
   In the true kingdom founded on thy heart.

###627###
As winter's frost doth silence ev'ry bird,
And locks the stream in crystal prison fast,
So by thy cold and unrelenting word
A sudden chill upon my soul is cast.
The laughing fields where summer lately played,
Now stand all barren, sorrowful, and grey;
So too my hopes, in brightest thoughts arrayed,
Before thy frozen temper fall away.
But as the sun returns to warm the land
And from the earth commands new life to spring,
I wait for some small gesture of thy hand,
That my poor heart may once again but sing.
   One gentle glance from thy returning sun
   Can end the winter that thy scorn begun.

###628###
Some love the beauty that the eye can see,
And praise the curve of lip or line of brow;
Though you have these in full sufficiency,
To such a shallow faith I'll not allow
My thoughts to sink, for I have looked more deep
And found a treasure of a greater kind,
A constant mind whose promise it can keep,
Wherein the soul's true riches I do find.
For outward form is but a flow'r of May,
That with the season's turn will fade and fall;
But the fair garden of thy mind will stay,
And grow more lovely, holding me in thrall.
   So let time steal the rose from off thy cheek,
   It is the wisdom of thy soul I seek.

###629###
Last night thou cam'st to me in gentle sleep,
A pleasing phantom to my soul's dark night;
And all the vows that I had sworn to keep
Were newly sworn in that false, happy light.
I felt the warmth of thy most tender hand,
I saw the love that in thy glance did burn,
And thought my sorrows banished from the land,
Ne'er to my waking senses to return.
But when the dawn did break the lovely spell,
And I did reach for what was but a dream,
I woke to know my solitary hell,
Where things are not as blessed as they seem.
   Thus am I cursed to find my only joy,
   In sleeping shades that waking hours destroy.

###630###
This world is filled with such a false pretence,
Where seeming friends have daggers in their smiles,
Where truth is sold for shillings and for pence,
And innocence is caught in cunning wiles.
Each man doth wear a mask upon his face,
And speaks in tones that are not truly his;
He'll vow his love, then in some other place
Will say how false his own devotion is.
But thou, my love, art of a diff'rent kind,
A store of truth in this deceitful age,
In whose clear soul no artifice I find,
But read my faith on an unblotted page.
   And in this play of shadows and of art,
   Thou art the only truth that holds my heart.

###631###
Let others praise what shifts and can be mov'd,
Or set their course by some new-risen star;
My own devotion cannot be reprov'd,
Though from my sight you travel ne'er so far.
For I have made thy constant soul my guide,
Which like the northern star doth not descend,
But holds its place whatever may betide,
A faithful point on which my thoughts attend.
The fickle moon may wax and then may wane,
And wand'ring planets from their courses bend,
But my true faith shall evermore remain,
And on thy virtue only shall depend.
   No change of time or place shall alter me,
   For my soul's compass points its north to thee.

###632###
I saw a rose in perfect, crimson hue,
That proudly stood to greet the morning's gaze,
Its velvet petals fresh with morning dew,
The fairest thing in summer's sunlit maze.
But ere the sun had reached his western bed,
I saw that flow'r, its glory overthrown,
Its petals scattered and its colour fled,
Its brief perfection to the winds all thrown.
And in that ruin I could not but see
The fragile state of all that we call fair;
It spoke of my own frail mortality
And bade my hopeful, foolish heart beware.
   For life and youth and beauty have their day,
   And like the rose, must wither and decay.

###633###
Thy voice is music, but of such a kind
As puts to shame the lute's most artful sound;
A sweeter harmony I cannot find
Though I should search the whole world's verdant ground.
When sorrow holds my heavy heart in thrall,
And gloomy thoughts my senses apprehend,
I need but hear thy gentle accent fall,
And all my griefs must straightway have an end.
The rarest songs that captive birds can sing,
The softest whispers of a summer stream,
No lasting comfort to my soul can bring,
Compared to this, thy voice's sacred theme.
   It is the chord to which my soul is set,
   Which, heard but once, I never can forget.

###634###
Thou art a thief, for thou hast stol'n from me
The very heart that beat within my breast;
I sleep no more for dreaming but of thee,
And in my waking hours can find no rest.
What right hadst thou to take what was not thine,
And leave me here so poor and so bereft?
I beg thee, give me back that heart of mine,
Or give me thine, for I have nothing left.
But thou art rich in hearts, and mine so small,
Thou wilt not miss its loss, nor feel its need,
Whilst I, who now have given thee my all,
Am poor indeed, and do for pity plead.
   Restore my heart, or else by this decree,
   Thy own is forfeit, and belongs to me.

###6...35###
The world's a stage, as weary sages write,
Whereon men play their parts of joy and rage;
My life hath been a play of endless night,
With sorrow's script writ on its ev'ry page.
I have seen kings in tragic ruin cast,
And watched as lovers sigh and knaves deceive,
And thought the part allotted me at last
Was but to stand in solitude and grieve.
But thy appearance on this dismal scene
Did change the plot and make the play divine;
Thou art its star, its glorious, ruling queen,
And all the worthiest speeches now are thine.
   For now my role is but to gaze on thee,
   And in thy light, applaud what I do see.

###636###
The jealous moon, that governs the pale night,
Once saw thy face as through thy pane it shone,
And from that time, hath paled her silver light,
And with a lesser grace now holds her throne.
For she, who once of all her beams was proud,
And mistress of the dark and silent hours,
Now draws about her face a mournful cloud,
And yields her claim to thy superior powers.
She sees thy cheeks out-blush the rosy dawn,
Thy starry eyes out-gleam her own faint fire,
And from the contest, humbled and withdrawn,
She bids her lesser, borrow'd lamps retire.
   Why needs the heav'n its fainter light to lend,
   When thy rich beauty doth the night attend?

###637###
The

###661###
When I am barr'd from thy most sacred sight,
And cruel distance doth our souls divide,
My world is plunged in an eternal night,
Wherein no gleam of comfort may abide.
The sun, a stranger to my heavy heart,
Doth cast his beams upon a world of grey;
The birds whose music used to play a part
In my soul's joy, have nothing left to say.
For what is beauty if thou art not near?
And what is music if it be not thee?
Each passing moment breeds a newer fear
That I thy face no more again shall see.
  So let this verse my faithful message prove:
  My world is nothing, absent of thy love.

###662###
My hungry eye, which on thy form doth feed,
And finds its only solace in thy grace,
Doth starve itself upon a bankrupt's creed
When thou art gone from this forsaken place.
My heart, in concert with that famish'd eye,
A solemn fast from all delight doth keep;
In silent chambers, it can only sigh,
And in the darkness of its sorrow weep.
So deep a famine hath thy absence made,
No worldly feast can my poor soul sustain;
All other beauties in thy light do fade,
And looking on them brings but further pain.
  Until thy face my senses doth restore,
  I look on naught, and wish to feed no more.

###663###
Shall I condemn the thief of passing Time,
Who steals the blush from off thy perfect cheek?
Nay, for such theft would be a lesser crime
Than my own silence, should I fail to speak.
For while his hand doth work to bring decay
And turn the gold of thy dear youth to dust,
My words shall build a fort against his sway,
And hold thy beauty in eternal trust.
His fleeting triumph in the world of sense
Shall find its match in this my loving art;
My verse shall be thy fortress and defence
When all that's mortal must from thee depart.
  So in this ink thy truer self shall live,
  And have that life which nature cannot give.

###664###
That envious poison, Jealousy, hath crept
Into the chambers of my trusting mind,
And murders all the peaceful thoughts that slept
In that sweet faith I once was proud to find.
Now every smile thou dost on others spend,
And every word that is not meant for me,
Doth seem a sign my happiness must end,
And proof of some untold conspiracy.
This sickly fever, born of my own fear,
Doth paint thee false when all the world says true,
And makes a monster of what is most dear,
Distorting all thy blessed virtues new.
  Yet is the fault not in thy heart, but mine,
  Which by its own disease cannot see thine.

###665###
Why doth the world commend the sun's great fire,
Or praise the moon that governs o'er the sea?
For I have found a light that is far higher,
And all its blessed glory shines from thee.
Thy two bright eyes, from which such radiance flows,
Outshine the stars in all their cold array;
And in their depths a truer wisdom grows
Than all the sages of the world can say.
So let them worship planets in the sky,
And chart the course of every distant sphere;
I need no guide by which to travel by,
Save for the two bright stars I hold so dear.
  For to my world, all other light is dim;
  Thy eyes my sun, my guiding cherubim.

###666###
When I do see how men with gilded tongue
Will praise a stone, if it be set in gold,
And how the ugliest of songs are sung
If by a prince the worthless tale is told,
My spirit wearies of the false report
That flatt'ry gives to undeserving things,
And flees the confines of the crowded court
Where vice is robed in the attire of kings.
But when I turn my tired eyes on thee,
In whom all truth and beauty sweetly join,
My faith in what is good again I see,
A priceless treasure of a purer coin.
  So let the world its hollow idols keep,
  While in thy truth my soul its comfort deep.

###667###
As weary night doth claim the waking day,
And lead my senses to the realm of sleep,
My soul to thee doth swiftly find its way,
From prisoned flesh its secret flight to keep.
In that dear dream, I hold thy hand in mine,
And gaze upon thy face without a tear;
Thy voice, a music perfectly divine,
Doth banish from my spirit all its fear.
But then the dawn, that traitor to my rest,
Doth pull me back to this most barren land,
To find my pillow by no dear head pressed,
With naught to hold but my own empty hand.
  Thus is my joy but for a fleeting night,
  And my long sorrow for the endless light.

###668###
What cause have I to envy crowns of kings,
Or crave the wealth that merchants toil to find?
My love for thee a richer treasure brings
Than all the gold that they have left behind.
Let others boast of land and high estate,
Of painted halls and garments finely wrought;
I hold a prize of a far greater weight,
A thing their worldly riches could not have bought.
Thy constant heart, thy spirit pure and free,
These are the jewels that I call my own;
And in the perfect kingdom of thy lee,
I find a comfort that exceeds a throne.
  For what is pow'r, and what is worldly art?
  Naught but a toy, next to thy faithful heart.

###669###
The war betwixt my heart and eye doth rage,
On how to share the prize they see in thee;
My eye, thy form would keep upon a page,
My heart, thy inner soul would hold in fee.
My eye doth claim it first thy shape did find,
A perfect image for the world to view;
My heart replies that it perceives thy mind,
A thing to which the outward eye is new.
And so they argue in a great debate,
Each pleading for a right to claim thee whole;
The eye for beauty, which it saw of late,
The heart for love, which occupies the soul.
  But then my thoughts a verdict wise decree:
  The eye thy form, the heart hath all of thee.

###670###
My love's two eyes are nothing like the sun;
In them, no golden fire doth brightly burn.
Her lips' soft red, by coral is outdone;
A lesson that my senses oft must learn.
I grant her voice no music doth possess
To rival that of larks in summer skies;
Nor would I say that she is a goddess,
For in her mortal tread some weakness lies.
And yet, by heaven, I think my love as rare
As any she that's praised with false compare,
And in her faults a greater truth I see
Than in the praise that rings with poetry.
  For while they love a thing of air and art,
  I love the very woman in my heart.

###671###
When in the glass I see my features change,
And note the lines that sorrow there hath writ,
I think it not unnatural or strange
That you some fault in my poor self admit.
My youth, that once some little praise could earn,
Hath paid its debt to unrelenting years;
The lessons that a harder life must learn
Are told in all my countenance appears.
So if thy gaze should ever turn away,
To find a face where summer still doth dwell,
I could not blame thee, nor would bid thee stay,
Though in thy leaving I should find my hell.
  Yet in this ruin, this one thing is true,
  My faithful heart doth keep its youth for you.

###672###
If it be sin to hold thy love so dear,
Then am I to my soul the greatest sinner;
For every thought I have, both far and near,
Doth find in thee its end and its beginner.
I have no wish that doth not thee involve,
No dream at night in which you have no part;
My every doubt, thy presence can resolve,
Thou art the compass of my wand'ring heart.
Some say that such devotion is a crime,
To make a heaven of a mortal face;
But I see in thy soul a truth sublime,
A glimpse of some divine and holy place.
  So if this love a wicked sin must be,
  I'll gladly sin for all eternity.

###673###
The heavy burden of a great misdeed
Doth press upon my conscience and my soul;
The bitter fruit of that one foolish seed,
Doth make me less than I would fain be whole.
When memory doth hold its court at night,
And calls upon me to defend the past,
I have no plea to offer in the light
Of that dark shadow which my shame hath cast.
And most I fear that you, whose gentle mind
Thinks ill of none, should come the truth to see,
And in my flawed and guilty nature find
A thing unworthy to be loved by thee.
  Yet for thy grace, my humbled spirit prays,
  To wash the stain from all my yesterdays.

###674###
This world's a stage of shallow masquerade,
Where every man in some disguise doth walk;
The vows of friendship are too lightly made,
And love is but the subject of loose talk.
The face they show you in the light of day
Is not the same that they in darkness wear;
Their truest thoughts they carefully hide away,
Beneath a mask of what seems kind and fair.
But thou, my love, art of a diff'rent kind,
Thy face and heart in perfect peace agree;
In thee, a plain and honest truth I find,
From

###701###
When I but think upon thy wondrous face,
My humble words like fearful servants hide,
No art of man can capture such a grace,
Nor speak the love that in my heart doth ride.
For in thine eyes two morning stars do gleam,
And on thy cheek a blushing rose is set,
To gaze on thee is like a waking dream,
A golden prize my soul can ne'er forget.
So how shall I, with ink and feathered quill,
Presume to draw what nature made so fair?
My hand doth shake, my labouring mind stands still,
To paint the light that shineth in thy hair.
Yet this poor verse I offer to thine ear,
That thou mightst know thy beauty held so dear.
###702###
I see the monuments of kings decay,
And brazen statues crumble into dust,
How short the triumph of a summer's day,
How soon the sword of mighty warriors rust.
Time, that swift thief, with silent, creeping pace,
Doth steal the bloom from off the fairest cheek,
And carves deep lines upon a youthful face,
And makes the strong in their own dotage weak.
What hope have we against this cruel foe,
Whose endless appetite shall all devour?
He lays the proudest of our glories low,
And reaps the harvest of our final hour.
Yet in these lines, thy youth shall ever live,
And 'gainst all time, a timeless beauty give.

###741###
When I thy youthful countenance behold,
And see the summer captured in thy grace,
My heavy heart cannot but then grow cold,
To think what winter shall invade that face.
For ruthless Time, which lets no beauty pass,
Doth with his scythe the fairest flower slay,
And shows within his truth-revealing glass,
How all that lives is destined to decay.
Yet let not this grim thought bring thee to art
A sorrow that might cloud thy present cheer;
I'll build a fortress for thee in my heart,
And hold thy perfect summer ever dear.
   Thus what is lost to Time, my verse shall lend,
   And make thy beauty's life to have no end.

###742###
Your cruel words, that give my soul such scorn,
Are answered with a dart from your cold eye;
And thus my hopeful heart is daily torn,
For to my loving sighs you grant no reply.
What use is there to make a lover's plea
To one whose ear is deaf, whose heart is stone?
You take a dark and perverse joy in me,
And pleasure in my lamentable groan.
My breast doth burn with unrequited fire,
My voice, in begging, makes a hollow sound;
I am consumed by my own fond desire,
Whose fruitless prayers fall on the barren ground.
   Yet in this pain my constancy I'll prove,
   For it is my own fate to die for love.

###743###
I have beheld the stars in midnight's sky,
And marked the moon when she is full and bright;
But neither can with thy two eyes stand high,
Which hold a clearer and a dearer light.
The reddest rose that in the garden shows
A crimson face to the admiring dew,
Cannot compare unto the blush that glows
Upon thy cheek, forever fresh and new.
The nightingale may sing a tune so sweet
That all the silent woods attend his thing,
But when thy voice and my glad ears do meet,
I hear an angel, not a bird, that sings.
   For Nature's best is but a mimic's art,
   Whilst thou of perfect beauty art the heart.

###744###
When I should take my rest in darkest night,
And close my weary eyes against the world,
Thy image comes and brings a burning light,
A banner in my sleepless mind unfurled.
So all the hours that others give to sleep,
Must I instead in watchful torment keep,
And in my soul a sad procession mind
Of all the joys that I can never find.
My thoughts of thee do banish gentle rest,
And make my bed a rack of inward strife;
My pining heart, with sorrow thus possessed,
Doth know no peace in this tormented life.
   For day is pain 'til thee again I see,
   And night brings no relief, but dreams of thee.

###745###
That I do love thee is no wondrous pride,
For who could see thy face and not adore?
But that I love thy faults, I cannot hide,
Which makes my strange affection all the more.
I love the haughty and unkind disdain
That thou dost show to those who seek thy grace;
I find a curious pleasure in the pain
That comes from judgment written on thy face.
Thy vanity, which others would decry,
Doth seem to me a jewel shining bright;
Thy sharpest word, thy most dismissive sigh,
Are but the shadows that define thy light.
   So let the world thy imperfections chide,
   My steadfast love will see them cast aside.

###746###
How is it that my love can be so sweet,
And yet the cause of such a bitter wrong?
Whene'er my thoughts and thy perfection meet,
My heart doth sing a lamentable song.
It is a feast at which I cannot eat,
A prize I see but never can possess;
It is a wound that never will retreat,
A state of more that makes me ever less.
This paradox doth govern all my fire,
To burn in hope and freeze in cold despair;
My soul is torn 'twixt torment and desire,
A heavy richness that I cannot bear.
   So love, in giving all, doth take my ease,
   And in its greatest torment, finds a peace.

###747###
This world's a stage, and we are players all,
Who strut and fret our hour and then are gone;
We each must play our part, as it may fall,
Until the final, weary curtain's drawn.
Some play the king, with pomp and hollow seem,
And some the fool, who jests to hide his tears;
The lover sighs within a waking dream,
The villain schemes and feeds on others' fears.
But what of me, who hath no role to look
Except the constant part of loving thee?
My lines are writ in passion's tear-stained book,
For all the world, and yet for none, to see.
   So let them play their parts of false and else,
   My only role is to be thy true self's.

###748###
Each mile that bears my body far from thee
Doth add a thousand to my spirit's woe;
For where thou art not, I can never be,
And in my breast the seeds of sorrow grow.
What joy is there in any sunlit place
If thy bright sun is not there to be seen?
The world's a tomb, devoid of all thy grace,
A winter landscape that can ne'er be green.
At every step I heave a heavy moan,
And think how happy are the stones I tread,
For they remain, where I must walk alone,
With thoughts of thee my only daily bread.
   No distance can my captive heart set free,
   Which, though I leave, remains behind with thee.

###749###
I cannot bear the sight when others see
The sun-like warmth that radiates from thee,
For in their eyes, a lesser love may be
Reflected where my own alone should be.
When they do praise thy wit or matchless grace,
A jealous rage doth seize my troubled heart;
I feel a thief has come to take my place,
To learn my lines and steal my given part.
Why should another's gaze be half so bold
As to presume to rest on what is mine?
Why should another's praise of thee be told,
When only my poor verse should make thee shine?
   Let them admire, but let them never prove
   A rival claim upon my sovereign love.

###750###
Though thou dost swear thy heart is true and fair,
And vow thy faith with every honeyed word,
I see a shadow that thy sweet oaths bear,
A second meaning that my soul has heard.
Thy beauty seems a mask for something false,
A painted curtain for a hollow show;
Thy virtue tastes of sweetened, bitter salts,
A lovely flower under which snakes go.
Why must a face so blessed with heavenly lie
Conceal a heart that practises deceit?
Why must the truth be absent from thine eye,
While from thy tongue fall phrases ever sweet?
   And yet, though I may know thy fickle art,
   I have no power to reclaim my heart.

###751###
Let greedy kings raise monuments of stone,
And trust to brass to keep their names from dust;
Their proudest works shall one day be o'erthrown,
And time shall turn their glory into rust.
But I for thee a better tomb shall time,
Stronger than marble, brighter than pure gold:
Thy memory, enshrined within my rhyme,
Shall keep thy beauty safe when thou art old.
For when the wars of ages lay to waste
The mightiest tower and the strongest hand,
The words of love that on this page are placed
Against the wreck of ages still shall stand.
   So let Death boast; he cannot make thee die,
   While in this verse thy truer self shall lie.

###752###
When I consider my own lowly state,
And all the wants by which my name is shamed,
I wonder how, by what strange, happy fate,
Thy perfect love for my poor self was framed.
I have no wealth, no title to commend,
No store of honours that the world holds dear;
My only riches, my beginning and my end,
Are that I may in thy sweet sight appear.
How can a thing so base deserve such grace?
How can the ground look up and claim the sky?
What merit is there in my humble face
That it should draw so excellent an eye?
   I have no answer, but must thankful be
   That heaven resides in what thou seest in me.

###753###
My mistress' eyes are not as sapphires bright,
Her hair not spun of gold, but raven's night.
No roses red and white her cheeks compare,
But dusk and shadow make their dwelling there.
I have heard music from a silver throat,
But her voice sings a far more common note.
I've seen the lilies in their fields of sun;
Her skin's a shade that might be called but dun.
And yet, by heaven, I think my love as rare
As any she, belied with false compare,
Whose beauty, praised by every poet's art,
Hath not her power to captivate a heart.
   Let others praise what's easy to be seen,
   I love the shadows where her soul has been.

###754###
My mind, a mirror to the turning year,
Doth show the summer when thy love is green;
But when thy favour is no longer here,
A barren, wintry landscape can be seen.
Thy absence is the first and killing frost
That strips the branches of their hopeful dress;
My heart, in thy departure, being lost,
Doth feel the chill of utter loneliness.
The barren boughs make music of their grief,
And cry aloud to cold and empty air;
The world doth seem one sad and fallen leaf,
A testament to sorrow and despair.
   So come, my sun, and let this winter end,
   And to my soul an early springtime send.

###755###
Why must swift Time, that villain, ever steal
The fairest treasures that our world can show?
His cruel and relentless hand doth seal
The fate of all the lovely things that grow.
He takes the rose's blush, the youth's bright gift,
The strength of kings, the lover's passing sigh;
His pace is sure, his punishment is swift,
And 'neath his gaze all mortal things must die.
He is a thief that comes by silent wrong,
And makes a spoil of all that we hold due;
He makes the day too short, the night too long,
And turns the constant heart to something new.
   Yet in my verse thy beauty I shall keep,
   To cheat the thief while all the world's asleep.

###756###
If my rash tongue hath done thy patience wrong,
Or my sad humours have seemed too unkind,
Let this poor verse be my repentant song,
And censure not what sorrow left behind.
My love for thee, a vast and stormy sea,
Doth sometimes cast up wreckage on the shore;
Forgive the faults that it brings forth from me,
And think upon the treasure that it bore.
My jealousies are but a twisted art
To show the depth of what I fear to lose;
My silence born of an o'erflowing heart
That knows not what poor, simple words to use.
   So chide me not for what I do amiss,
   But heal my trespass with a gentle kiss.

###757###
Let carping critics of the worldling say
My love's a folly and my praise untrue;
My constant heart shall not be led astray
By any judgment that is not of you.
What do they know, who measure love by scorn,
Or weigh affection on a scale of gain?
My love for thee was not for fashion born,
Nor does it fear the world's unjust disdain.
Let them be wise in what they think they know,
And praise the things that glitter for a day;
My heart has found where truer riches grow,
And from that treasure will not turn away.
   Their words are wind, their knowledge is but small;
   Thy love is my whole world, and that is all.

###758###
My heart and eye are at a mortal war,
For what one loves, the other doth deny;
My eye doth feast on all that you now are,
My heart sees truths on which it can't rely.
The eye is pleased with your external part,
And bids my soul to be contented there;
The heart doth look within, and finds an art
Of sweet deception, beautiful and rare.
My eye, a fool, is captured by the sight;
My heart, a slave, is bound to what it feels;
Thy outward truth is my poor eye's delight,
Thy inner doubt my loving heart reveals.
   So I am torn, and know not which is true:
   The saint my eye sees, or the flawed soul, you.

###759###
Let them have lands who heap up worldly rich,
And count their fortunes up in sums of gold;
My greater treasure is the love of which
Not even half my verse has ever told.
Let others climb to find a higher state
And bow to princes for a moment's grace;
I envy not their false and fickle fate,
For all my kingdom is in thy dear face.
They have but dross, which moth and rust devour,
A brittle wealth that vanishes to air;
My love is my own fortress and my tower,
A prize beyond what any king could bear.
   So let them have their crowns; I'll not despise
   My richer state, found only in thine eyes.

###760###
When this my hand is dust, and tongue shall end
Its long account of what you are to me,
Let this last verse my constant soul commend,
As true in death as it in life shall be.
Look not upon the ruin of the past,
Nor think my love could with my body die;
The mortal form away is quickly cast,
But my firm spirit shall not from thee fly.
The world may turn, and seasons rearrange,
And suns may cool, and stars may lose their day,
But this one thing is not subject to change,
Nor can the hand of ruin sweep away.
   For this my vow outlives my final breath:
   My love for thee knows no such thing as death.

###721###
When in the mind a treasured thought doth fade,
And memory, a ghost, doth slip away,
By time's relentless, silent hand unmade,
Lost to the light of a forgotten day;
Then what the heart did once so clearly see,
Becomes a doubt, a trick of fancy's eye,
And what was truth may seem a falsity,
A whispered word that taught the soul to lie.
But what in this, my verse, in solemn ink,
Is writ, shall stand when recollections fail,
And give thee cause for future days to think
Upon a love that never shall grow pale.
   So in these lines, thy truest form shall live,
   And to all time, thy lasting beauty give.

###722###
The stars that spangle the dark vault of night,
And lend their fire to heaven's distant face,
Do but reflect a far more holy light,
That finds its home within thy perfect grace.
Were thy two eyes set in that upper sphere,
No mariner would for the North Star yearn,
For they would make the cloudy heavens clear,
And guide all lost souls to a safe return.
The jealous moon would pale before their beam,
And draw her silvered veil, her face to hide,
Whilst dreaming birds would waken from a dream,
Thinking thy light the new-risen sun did guide.
   For in thy gaze all light and guidance art,
   The sun and compass of my captive heart.

###761###
I look upon the stars that grace the night,
And think their silver less than thy sweet face,
A thousand tapers lending feeble light,
Compared unto thy soul's more wondrous grace.
The summer's rose, in all its crimson pride,
Doth borrow from thy cheek its blushing hue,
And in its fragrant petals seems to hide
A shadow of the beauty found in you.
The flowing stream that sings a gentle song,
Doth learn its music from thy soft-spoke word,
And finds its purpose all the season long,
A lesser echo of the voice I heard.
So let all nature boast of its own art,
For it is but a copy of thy heart.

###762###
The sun departs and leaves the world in grey,
And so my world doth darken when thou'rt gone;
My heavy heart must count each passing day,
And wait until thou bring'st another dawn.
The lonely nightingale doth sing her pain
To empty boughs where late her lover sat;
So do my verses mournfully complain
Of this bleak state, this joyless habitat.
Each hour a year, each minute a long age,
Since last I saw the comfort of thine eyes;
My soul is captive on a barren stage,
And for thy presence, it unceasing cries.
Yet this one thought doth mitigate my strife:
Thy sweet return shall give my spirit life.

###763###
When thou dost swear that all my verse is fair,
And praise the wit that I can scarce pretend,
I know thou liest, weaving nets of air,
Yet love the sweet untruths that thou dost send.
Thy silver tongue doth paint a comely man,
A poet blessed with some celestial fire;
Though truth would show a far more humble plan,
I gladly burn upon thy false desire.
For in thy lies a kinder world is made,
Where my poor faults are hidden from the light,
And in this pleasing fantasy I've stayed,
A willing fool, enchanted by the night.
Thus let us both in sweet deceit agree,
I'll credit thee, and thou shalt flatter me.

###764###
That cruel thief, old Time, with stealthy pace,
Doth steal the blush from beauty's tender rose,
And carves deep lines upon the fairest face,
Wherein no lasting gift he e'er bestows.
The monuments of kings he turns to dust,
And mighty empires crumble at his will;
In his cold grasp all worldly treasures rust,
And e'en the soaring mountain he makes still.
Yet in the boundless kingdom of my mind,
Thy youthful form shall never know decay,
A truer record there the world may find
Than brass or stone that Time can wear away.
For what is stored within a loving breast
Shall by oblivious Time be unpossessed.

###765###
Mine eye and heart are at a mortal war
For how to share the conquest of thy sight;
Mine eye, thy outward form would keep in store,
My heart, thy inner spirit's holy light.
My greedy eye doth feast upon thy look,
And bars my heart from its entitled share,
For every glance is by the heart mistook
As if that were the only treasure there.
But then my heart, in its own silent plea,
Doth claim thy love, thy thought, thy gentle mind,
And scorns the eye for what it cannot see,
The truer substance it is sworn to find.
A pact is made: mine eyes thy form shall keep,
My heart shall hold thy love when I'm asleep.

###766###
This world's a stage where all must play their part,
We have our entrances and our last goodbyes;
One plays the fool, one acts a broken heart,
And masks conceal the truth within our eyes.
The king who struts with sceptre and with crown
Is but a player for a passing scene;
Tomorrow's plot may cast the monarch down
To be no more than what he hath erst been.
And I, for my part, play the lover true,
Whose every line is but to speak thy praise,
My constant role is to attend on you
Throughout the brief performance of my days.
But when the final curtain starts to fall,
My love for thee shall be the end of all.

###767###
Wherefore, my Muse, art thou so dull of late
That on my page no worthy verse appears?
Why dost thou leave my spirit desolate,
A prey to silence and to fruitless fears?
Hath some new poet stolen all thy grace
And drained the wellspring of thy sacred art?
Dost thou now serve him in some other place
And leave me with a mute and heavy heart?
I look for words and find but empty sound,
I seek a thought and grasp but at the air;
My barren wit lies fallow on the ground,
O'ergrown with weeds of sorrow and despair.
Then look but on my love, and thou shalt see
The only subject that can inspire thee.

###768###
Thou art the storm that overturns my peace,
Thy temper like the wind, thy tears the rain;
Thou bid'st the quiet of my reason cease
And bring'st a flood of pleasure and of pain.
One moment, sunny smiles do warm my soul,
The next, a frown brings winter's chilling frost;
Thy passions have me in their wild control,
Wherein my ship of sense is wrecked and lost.
Yet from this tempest I would not be free,
For in its rage my heart is made to live;
A dead calm sea holds no such life for me
As the fierce elements thy humours give.
So let thy winds of fury blow their worst,
For want of thee, my heart would die of thirst.

###769###
I see thee grant a smile to some new friend
And feel a pang I labour to conceal;
My troubled thoughts to dark conclusions tend
And all my secret miseries reveal.
Doth his discourse hold more delight for thee?
Is his small wit more pleasing to thine ear?
Must I now face the painful certainty
That my fond reign will end within a tear?
This green-eyed monster, jealousy, doth feed
Upon the scraps of doubt and idle fear;
It plants in fertile minds a bitter seed
And makes a pleasant garden dark and drear.
Yet banish him I cannot, for he's part
Of the great price I pay to hold thy heart.

###770###
If thou would'st know how fair thou dost remain
When passing years have dimmed thy looking-glass,
Peruse the tribute that these lines contain
And see a beauty that shall never pass.
For here thy virtues are embalmed in rhyme,
Thine eyes' bright stars, thy voice's silver sound,
Preserved against the injury of time,
Wherein no flaw nor blemish can be found.
This paper is a truer glass for thee
Than any crystal wrought by cunning hand;
It shows the soul that others cannot see,
The noblest heart in all this weary land.
So when in doubt of what you were of old,
Look in this verse, and let thy worth be told.

###771###
My heart, a willing captive to thy will,
Doth wear its chains as 'twere a golden prize;
It hath no wish to roam beyond the hill
That is the sweet horizon of thine eyes.
Some men seek freedom in a distant land
Or on the seas where wanton winds do blow,
But all the freedom I can understand
Is in the bounded space thy glances show.
This prison is a kingdom, vast and great,
This servitude, a monarch's high command;
To be thy thrall is to command my fate,
The richest subject in this happy land.
For in my bondage I am truly free,
Since I desire no liberty from thee.

###772###
When in the silent sessions of the night
I call to mind the days that are no more,
I see thy form, a ghost in pale moonlight,
That stands and waits beside a sealed door.
Thy voice I hear, a whisper on the breeze
That speaks of vows that time hath rendered vain;
It rustles through the sad and weeping trees
And brings a bittersweet and pleasing pain.
This phantom love, more real than living men,
Doth haunt the lonely chambers of my soul,
And with a spectral touch I feel again
The love that once did make my spirit whole.
So let me sleep and in that sleep be brave,
To find in dreams the love I could not save.

###773###
Let others scorn my name and my estate
And with contemptuous finger point me out;
I care not for their malice or their hate,
Nor for the slanders that they bruit about.
Let them decry my verse as poor and weak
And say my wit is but a shallow thing;
The harsh opinions that these critics speak
Have not the power to make my spirit sting.
For in the court of my own truest thought,
Where thou alone dost sit in judgment's seat,
Their idle censure counts for less than naught
Once thy approval makes my world complete.
The whole world's praise is but an empty sound
If in thy single voice no praise is found.

###774###
Why dost thou paint thy cheek with such false art
And borrow from a box thy rosy red?
Dost thou believe it can a grace impart
That nature hath not on thy beauty spread?
The loveliest lily needs no other white,
The blushing rose requires no crimson dye;
Why shouldst thou

###781###
When Luna shows her face in skies of night,
And casts a silver sheen upon the ground,
I find in her no measure of delight,
For in thy brighter beauty I am bound.
The stars that wheel about her ancient throne,
As loyal subjects to their distant queen,
Do seem to me but scattered seeds, wind-blown,
Compared to that which in thine eyes is seen.
Her borrowed glow, a pale and ghostly art,
Can never with thy living warmth compete,
For thou dost bear a sun within thy heart,
Whose rays make every shadowed thought retreat.
So let the moon rule o'er the lonely sea,
My world is lightened and is ruled by thee.

###782###
That cruel thief, old Time, with unseen hand,
Doth steal the roseate blush from beauty's cheek,
And ploughs deep furrows in a once-fair land,
To make the strong feel powerless and weak.
He wastes the gold of youth's untroubled hair,
And dims the fire that in the eye did burn;
He makes what was most lovely, past compare,
A ruin from which memory must turn.
His scythe is sharp, his progress without stay,
And turns the brightest morning to decay,
He takes no heed of riches or of might,
To usher in an everlasting night.
Yet in my verse thy beauty shall not die,
But live untouched by his swift cruelty.

###783###
Why dost thou turn

###821###
Thy visage is a map of cunning art,
Wherein all beauteous regions find their grace,
And I, a pilgrim, know it by my heart,
More than I know my own world-wearied face.
From thy two eyes what wonders may I find,
Which as twin stars do banish sullen night,
To navigate the oceans of my mind,
And steer my course by their most holy light.
Yet in this land such subtle ways are shown,
I fear to wander on a chartless sea,
A willing captive, on thy mercy thrown,
Lest I should lose myself in loving thee.
So let thy constant love my compass guide,
That in thy soul's safe harbour I'll abide.

###822###
When monuments of brass and carven stone
Are by the wasteful arm of Time laid low,
And gilded thrones are cruelly overthrown,
And all the seeds of empire cease to grow;
When all the triumphs that the proud amass
Do fade like whispers on a winter's wind,
And beauty, gazing in a faithful glass,
Sees but the ruin that it soon shall find;
Then shall this verse, a fortress for thy name,
Stand strong against oblivion's dark decree,
And hold thy virtue in a living frame,
For all the eyes of future worlds to see.
So fear not age, nor fortune's sharpest sting,
Thy praise eternal shall my tribute sing.

###823###
Since thou, my sun, art banished far away,
The birdsong doth a mournful sorrow seem,
And shadows lengthen in the brightest day,
As in the throes of some unhappy dream.
The sweetest music that mine ears can hear
Is but a discord, wanting thy sweet part;
The world's diversions are no longer dear,
For thou hast ta'en with thee my captive heart.
All that was fair is clouded from my sight,
And winter's chill usurps the summer's song;
The very stars refuse to lend their light,
And every pleasant thing to me is wrong.
So must this joyless state of being be,
Until thou dost return thy light to me.

###824###
Within thine eyes a double war I trace,
Wherein both haughty pride and pity lie;
One look grants hope, the other scorns my case,
And by their conflict makes my spirit die.
What strange contention doth thy soul embrace,
To wound the heart that holds thy form so dear?
To wear a seraph's smile upon thy face,
Yet breed within my breast a constant fear?
So are my days in contradictions bound,
By hope uplifted, by despair brought low,
Thy voice an angel's, yet a dreadful sound
That bids my well-sown seeds of love not grow.
Why dost thou both the cure and sickness give?
To kill with scorn, and with a glance bid live?

###825###
Old Time, that filching and most subtle thief,
Who makes the fairest things of summer fade,
Doth bring to youthful joy a bitter grief,
And casts all glory in an endless shade.
He carves deep lines where once was smoothest youth,
And steals the raven hair, to leave but dust;
He scoffs at vows and mocks love's simple truth,
Betraying every hope and mortal trust.
But from thy substance he can take no prize,
Which in the fortress of my mind I keep;
Thy beauty lives immortal in my eyes,
And visits me when all the world's asleep.
So let him rage and do his worst, for still
My love holds thee, and so forever will.

###826###
I am a miser of thy company,
And when another doth thy presence claim,
A wretched, green-eyed jealousy in me
Doth burn my very reason in its flame.
The sun itself, that looks on thee all day,
The wind that dares to whisper in thine ear,
Do steal a portion of my soul away,
And fill my thoughts with discontent and fear.
To every eye that on thy beauty looks,
To every voice that speaks thy precious name,
I write a curse within my secret books,
And wish them banished by my sorrow's shame.
Forgive this madness, born of love so true,
That cannot bear to share the world with you.

###827###
Thy voice is music, but of such a kind
As makes the lark ashamed to greet the dawn,
And leaves the nightingale no song to find,
Her envious notes by thy sweet sound withdrawn.
When thou dost speak, the air, a blessed thing,
Doth hold its breath to capture every tone,
And I, poor fool, can neither weep nor sing,
But stand enthralled, a captive made of stone.
Let others praise the viol or the lute,
And find their solace in a well-played part;
When thou art speaking, all those sounds are mute,
Mere empty noise before the bleeding heart.
Then speak again, and I will ask no more,
'Tis all the heaven I am longing for.

###828###
When weary night puts on her cloak of black,
And draws the curtains on the sleeping world,
My thoughts to thee begin their restless track,
Like frantic sails on stormy seas unfurled.
My heavy eyes, which can no slumber find,
Do paint thy picture on the dark's broad screen;
They have become the servants of my mind,
And show me what by day cannot be seen.
But oh, this image is a cruel friend,
That shows thy beauty but denies thy touch;
A sweet illusion that must shortly end,
For which I pay in sorrow overmuch.
And so I watch the wretched night away,
And curse the dark, and yet I dread the day.

###829###
The world's a stage, as all the wise men write,
Where men and women play their tedious roles,
Full of false passion and of borrowed light,
And petty wars within their little souls.
I too have played my part, and learned my lines,
And worn the mask of joy, the cloak of woe,
Observing all the customary signs,
And letting my true feelings seldom show.
But since I saw thee, all the rest seems vain,
A foolish play put on for idle eyes;
Thou art the only truth in all this feign,
The single star in these fictitious skies.
So let them act their scenes of love or hate,
My world begins and ends within thy state.

###830###
My verse is poor, my wit is far too weak
To capture all the grace I see in thee;
What paltry words my stammering tongue can speak
To show the wonder thou hast wrought in me?
I am no scholar, versed in ancient lore,
To find comparisons with gods of old;
My humble pen cannot invent much more
Than simple truths that have been often told.
And yet, if but my love could find a voice
As pure and constant as the love I feel,
The heavens themselves would listen and rejoice,
And learn a passion they could never steal.
So take the tribute, though the art be small;
The love is true, and that must be my all.

###831###
They call this love a prison, and they say
That I am bound in chains I cannot see;
They point to how I waste my every day
In thrall to what thy beauty makes of me.
It is a prison, I confess it's true,
Wherein my heart is sentenced to remain,
But from my cell I have a perfect view
Of thee, which banishes all sense of pain.
My gaoler's eyes hold all the light I need,
My only want is to receive thy grace;
On thy sweet kindness does my spirit feed,
And finds its freedom in this captive's place.
Then lock the door, and throw away the key,
If in this prison I may live with thee.

###832###
Thou art the spring for which the winter yearns,
The sudden bloom that conquers frost and snow;
At thy approach, the frozen earth returns
To life, and bids her sleeping blossoms grow.
Before I knew thee, all my world was grey,
A barren landscape, desolate and chill,
Where sullen clouds obscured the light of day,
And all the songs of summer had grown still.
But with thy presence came a gentle rain,
And then a sunbeam, glorious and bright,
To wake the dormant senses from their pain,
And fill my lonely soul with pure delight.
So let me be the soil where thou dost thrive,
And in thy season keep my heart alive.

###833###
This poem is a mirror I have made,
Not of bright silver, nor of polished glass,
Wherein thy form in cold reflection's laid,
To be forgot as soon as thou dost pass.
Nay, this is forged of spirit and of fire,
And polished with the tears that fall for thee,
To show the truth of what my thoughts admire,
Which thy own modesty forbids thee see.
Look here and find not just the outward part,
The rose and lily battling in thy cheek,
But the true beauty of thy constant heart,
Whose perfect virtue makes my words so weak.
This glass will not grow dim or fade with age,
But keep thee ever fair upon this page.

###834###
Of what proud substance art thou fashioned, say,
That thou canst look on sorrow with a smile?
That when my heart in anguish wastes away,
Thy cruel heart feels nothing all the while?
Art thou some statue, carved from marble cold,
That cannot comprehend a lover's plea?
A tale of tyranny by tyrants told,
Who take delight in others' misery?
For in thy brow such majesty is set,
And in thy form such heavenly design,
That nature's pattern seems entirely met,
Making thy coldness all the more a crime.
Have pity, then, and let thy actions be
As fair as what the wondering world doth see.

###835###
My heart, once mine, now finds its home in thee,
And thine, I trust, hath made its home in me;
A blessed bargain for eternity,
That neither's heart can ever more be free.
And yet this trade hath brought a strange new state,
For when thou sighest, it is my heart that breaks,
And when I weep, thy sweet eyes, by some fate,
Do feel the sorrow that my fortune makes.
So closely are our very beings joined,
So intertwined are both our joy and pain,
That by this fair exchange we two are coined
As one new soul that shall for aye remain.
Then guard my heart which in thy breast doth lie,
As I'll guard thine, until the day I die.

###836###
Last night in sleep, my soul did find its way
To that sweet court where thou dost hold thy reign,
And for a moment, banished was the day,
With all its longing and its bitter pain.
I touched thy hand, I looked into thine eyes,
And heard thee speak my name in accents low;
My spirit soared to blissful paradise,
And felt a peace it ne'er on earth can know.
But then the dawn, that envious, pale spy,
Did steal into my room and break the spell,
And I awoke to hear my own sad cry,
And found my heaven had become my hell.
For what is worse than tasting perfect grace,
Then waking to an empty, lonely place?

###837###
My foolish heart and my more foolish mind
Do wage a ceaseless and a dreadful war;
My mind declares that I am deaf and blind
To see such faults in what I most adore.
My reason shows me every proud defect,
Thy cold disdain, thy vanity, thy art,
And bids my soul this passion to reject,
And from thy powerful enchantment part.
But then my heart, a traitor to the cause,
Replies that these are virtues in disguise,
And, scorning reason's miserable laws,
Sees only heaven reflected in thine eyes.
So, torn between the two, I live in strife,
And lose my senses while I lose my life.

###838###
Let others praise the beauty of thy face,
Thy coral lip, thy cheek of rosy hue,
The outward form of captivating grace
Which holds the gaze of all who look on you.
This is a flower that must fade with years,
A fragile bloom for sickness to betray,
A fleeting prize for Time's remorseless shears,
Which cut the thread of every perfect day.
But I do love that which can have no end,
The constant spirit and the loyal thought,
The gentle soul that I have found my friend,
Whose worth by earthly measure is as naught.
For when thy summer's bravest days are done,
Thy truest beauty will have just begun.

###839###
The ink with which I write these words for thee
Is not a compound made by common skill;
'Tis brewed of sorrow and of misery,
And drawn from tears that from mine eyes do spill.
My pen, a feather plucked from sorrow's wing,
Doth move across the page with heavy pace,
And of thy cruelty it needs must sing,
Though it would rather write upon thy grace.
Each letter is a monument to pain,
Each line a record of a hope deferred,
A futile prayer, whispered all in vain,
A wretched cry that goes forever unheard.
Yet if this ink could make thee understand,
I'd weep a flood to fill my waiting hand.

###840###
If all this treasure of my love for thee
Is squandered on a heart that will not pay,
And if my verse, for all the world to see,
Cannot persuade thy coldness to give way;
If all my sighs, which freight the summer air,
And all my tears, which salt the winter rain,
Are but a tax I pay to my despair,
A fruitless tribute to thy proud disdain;
Then let this be the last that I shall write,
The final plea my weary soul shall send;
I'll turn my face away from thy bright light,
And let this long and foolish torment end.
Yet if thou call'st me, I will turn again,
And prove my former wisdom was but vain.

###801###
When I in idle moments think on grace,
My thoughts do paint no image but of thee;
For heav'n itself is written in thy face,
And nature's best in thy clear eyes I see.
The blushing rose that in the garden stands
Doth seem but pale when measured by thy cheek;
The whitest lily, brought from foreign lands,
Beside thy hand a lesser white doth seek.
So all that men account as sweet and fair
Is but a shadow of thy truer form,
A fleeting joy, a whisper in the air,
That cannot shield thee from life's coming storm.
   Yet in my verse thy beauty shall not cease,
   But find therein a timeless, lasting peace.

###802###
Since cruel distance holds me from thy side,
Each passing hour a tedious age doth seem;
My lonely thoughts on seas of sorrow ride,
And find their port but in a waking dream.
I look upon the sun's most glorious light,
And yet its gold to me seems dull and gray;
The moon that rules the kingdom of the night,
Hath not the pow'r to chase my gloom away.
For what is day, if I see not thy face?
And what is night, if I not dream of you?
The world itself is but an empty place,
Where ev'ry joy takes on a somber hue.
   Thus in this absence, I can only find
   The constant image of thee in my mind.

###803###
When ruthless Time with his destructive hand
Shall turn to dust the monuments of kings,
And make a ruin of this teeming land,
And silence all the joyful song it sings,
Thy beauty shall, against his cruel rage,
Find sanctuary from his endless war;
For thou shalt live upon this faithful page,
More lasting than a high and distant star.
Though marble crack and brass itself decay,
And all the works of proud men be forgot,
These words shall keep oblivion at bay,
And grant a life that time can injure not.
   So in my ink, thy youth shall ever bloom,
   And mock the silence of the lightless tomb.

###804###
Why dost thou spend thy smiles on all thou seest,
And grant a portion of thy gracious light
To ev'ry stranger at a common feast,
Which should by right be my own soul's delight?
That gentle word, that easy, pleasant glance
Which thou bestow'st on some unworthy one,
Doth pierce my heart as with a sudden lance,
And makes me feel impoverished and undone.
Oh, be more frugal with thy beauty's treasure,
And keep it locked from undeserving eyes;
Do not so freely measure out thy pleasure,
Which I above all other fortunes prize.
   For though thy kindness unto all be sweet,
   'Tis for my heart alone a food most meet.

###805###
Thy face was made to look like virtue's own,
Thy voice to sound as if it spoke the truth;
Upon thy brow sincerity is shown,
And in thine eyes, the innocence of youth.
But this fair show is but a painted screen,
A cunning trap to catch the trusting heart,
For all the good that on the outside's seen
Conceals a cold and a deceiving art.
Like a sweet flow'r that bears a poison deep,
Or gilded book that holds a wretched tale,
Thy beauty lulls the watchful soul to sleep,
Then lets the storms of misery prevail.
   So I, betrayed by what I thought divine,
   Now curse the day I wished to make thee mine.

###806###
That youthful beauty which thou hold'st so dear
Is but a tenant in thy mortal frame;
It cannot stay the swiftly passing year,
Nor still the tongue that whispers time's harsh name.
The looking-glass that shows thee now so fair
Will one day show the work of age's knife;
The lines of sorrow and the threads of care
Will write the weary story of thy life.
For summer's bloom must yield to winter's frost,
And smoothest brows by furrows be possessed;
The vibrant hue of crimson shall be lost,
And all thy pride be humbled and oppressed.
   So love me now, whilst thou art in thy prime,
   And make one conquest in thy war with time.

###807###
When peaceful night brings slumber to the world,
And weary bodies find their needed rest,
My soul upon a sea of thought is hurled,
And by a thousand waking woes oppressed.
No gentle sleep doth visit my sad eyes,
But in the darkness they do stare in vain;
And with the dawn my heavy sorrows rise,
To walk the world and know the self-same pain.
Thy constant image doth before me float,
An inward sun that banishes all night,
And makes my bed a ship, a lonely boat,
That sails toward thee, but never comes in sight.
   So is my state, by day or in the deep,
   A constant watch that love forbids to sleep.

###808###
When from my sight thou art compelled to go,
The very sun seems robbed of all its fire;
The winds that once did cheerfully to blow,
Now seem to mourn and with my soul conspire.
The songs of birds, which I was wont to praise,
Do sound no more like music to my ear;
And all the glory of the summer's days
Is but a sad and colorless affair.
The world's great stage, whereon all creatures play,
Becomes a void, a dull and empty space,
For thou, the light that turnèd dark to day,
Hast taken with thee every earthly grace.
   Thus all is nought until thou dost return,
   And for that hour my patient heart must yearn.

###809###
Let not the poet praise the morning sun,
Nor laud the stars that in the heavens glow;
For by thine eyes their race is far outrun,
And from thy light, a brighter light doth flow.
The sun may hide its face behind a cloud,
The fickle moon may choose to wane and fade,
The stars may stand aloof, remote, and proud,
And leave the world in darkness and in shade.
But thy two eyes, a more consistent pair,
Do shine undimmed by any passing storm;
They make a southern climate in the air,
And keep my soul eternally as warm.
   So let me worship at no other shrine,
   But find my light in those two suns of thine.

###810###
If in thine heart there be a place for grace,
Then show it now, and let thy pity speak;
For sorrow's lines are written on my face,
And all my strength is rendered poor and weak.
Thou hast the power with a single word
To raise me up from depths of black despair,
Or with a frown, like a descending sword,
To cut the thread of life I feebly bear.
Do not in sport a killing power use,
Nor take delight in being counted cruel;
Let thy sweet nature this harsh course refuse,
And add more lustre to thy brightest jewel.
   For mercy shown doth beautify the fair,
   And makes a tyrant's heart beyond compare.

###811###
I am a fool, a wretched, witless thing,
Made so by thee and thy bewitching glance;
I have no reason, and I cannot sing
Of aught but thee, who leads my soul this dance.
The wise man sees the folly of my way,
And bids me break the chains that hold me fast;
But what he says, I can no more obey
Than call again the moment that is past.
My will is slave to thy supreme command,
My mind rejects what my own eyes do see;
I am a captive in a foreign land,
Who cherishes his own captivity.
   Thus reason's dead, and I am ruled by heart,
   And play the fool, and thank thee for the part.

###812###
As winter's breath encrusts the world with white,
And stills the brook that once did freely run,
So thy cold heart denies my soul its light,
And freezes all the good it had begun.
The barren trees that stretch against the gray,
And mourn the loss of all their summer leaves,
Do show the state of my soul's sad decay,
Which for thy warmth in silent sorrow grieves.
I feel the frost of thy unkind disdain,
A bitter chill that settles in my breast;
And all my pleas for comfort are in vain,
For in thy coldness I can find no rest.
   Yet spring may come and melt the frozen ground;
   Will such a thaw in thy hard heart be found?

###813###
My glass doth show a man of measured years,
Whose youth has fled, and left behind its trace;
It tells a tale of sorrows and of fears,
And writes that story on my weary face.
But when I look on thy unblemished brow,
Where time's cruel hand hath left no mark nor sign,
I see a beauty flourishing, and how
My fading autumn's nothing to thy shine.
Thou art the spring, in all its hopeful green,
And I the winter, desolate and cold;
In thy fresh features is my comfort seen,
Which makes me feel less weary and less old.
   So in thy youth my own seems to revive,
   And in thy sight, I keep my heart alive.

###814###
Mine eye and heart are at a mortal war,
And each the other's argument denies;
Mine eye doth claim that thou art what I saw,
While my poor heart says 'tis not so, and cries.
My heart asserts that thou art kind and true,
And built within of goodness and of grace;
Mine eye replies that this is not its view,
Remembering a coldness in thy face.
So which of these two judges shall I trust,
This outward gaze, or that which feels within?
Must I believe that what seems fair is just,
Or that a lovely form can house a sin?
   The case is tried, but no result I find;
   The eye is blind, and yet the heart's not blind.

###815###
I hear the words that others speak of thee,
And mark the way they flatter and they fawn;
They praise thine eyes, which they can scarcely see,
And swear thy cheek is fairer than the dawn.
They lack the skill to tell of what thou art,
Their clumsy praise doth but profane thy name;
They cannot know the kingdom of thy heart,
Nor feel the fire of thy spirit's flame.
They see the cover of a wondrous book,
And judge its worth by the artisan's design;
But on the written contents never look,
Which store a knowledge that is near divine.
   So let them speak; their words are but as air,
   For what thou art, they never can declare.

###816###
Last night, as I in troubled slumber lay,
My dreams did conjure what my days deny;
And for a moment, sorrow fled away,
As thy dear shade did softly press me nigh.
Thy voice, it seemed, did whisper in mine ear,
And thy sweet hand did rest upon my brow;
I felt a joy that banished every fear,
And made a holy and a silent vow.
But then the dawn, that thief of all delight,
Did steal across the threshold of my room;
It banished thee, and with thee, all was bright,
And left me waking to my former gloom.
   Thus joy by night makes sorrow worse by day,
   To have thee close, then have thee snatched away.

###817###
Let those who will, the world's great riches prize,
The miser's gold, the monarch's mighty throne;
A greater treasure lies in thy dear eyes
Than all the gems to any king are known.
What good is land, if one must walk alone?
What use is pow'r, if it cannot command
A loyal heart to be its very own,
Or feel the comfort of a gentle hand?
Let others seek for glory and for state,
And spend their days in toil for empty fame;
I hold myself in far more high estate,
If I may but have leave to speak thy name.
   For having thee, my world is made complete,
   And every bitter thing is rendered sweet.

###818###
My heart, which once did dwell within my breast,
Hath found its home and lodging-place in thine;
And in its stead, to give my soul some rest,
Thy loving heart hath taken up with mine.
And so a wondrous bargain hath been made,
A fair exchange that none can take away;
I am by thy sweet charity repaid,
And gladly shall this happy tax to pay.
Thy heart in me doth teach me how to feel,
My heart in thee reflects upon thy grace;
This blessed union doth to both reveal
A love that time and fortune can't erase.
   So we are one, though we be two in sight,
   Bound by a love that makes both spirits light.

###819###
This love is both a torment and a balm,
A bitter draught, a wine of wondrous taste;
It brings a tempest, then a sudden calm,
It makes a garden of a barren waste.
One moment, I ascend to heaven's height,
Borne up by hope on wings of pure desire;
The next, I fall into the blackest night,
And feel my soul consumed by sorrow's fire.
What is this pow'r that gives such contrary,
That holds my life in such a puzzling sway,
That makes a pleasure of my misery,
And brings the darkest midnight to my day?
   It is a riddle that I cannot read,
   On which my heart must both rejoice and bleed.

###820###
Whatever worth these humble lines possess,
Or any grace my labouring pen can show,
It is not mine, I freely must confess,
But from thy beauty all these verses flow.
Thou art the subject and the only cause,
The sun that makes this simple blossom grow;
Thou art the source of nature's fairest laws,
The very font from which my thoughts I know.
My art is but a mirror, dull and poor,
That tries to catch the light that from thee streams;
My words are pilgrims knocking at thy door,
To speak the substance of my waking dreams.
   So if men praise what my poor hand hath writ,
   The praise is thine, for thou didst fashion it.

###861###
When I compare the sun's most golden light
Unto the living marvel of thy hue,
The sun itself is vanquished by the night,
Whilst thy fair form makes all old things seem new.
The painter's hand, with all his careful grace,
Doth strive to catch the truth his eye doth start,
But Nature, gazing on thy wondrous face,
Hath broke the mould that first did shape thy heart.
To what on earth shall I then thee compare?
What jewel's fire could my poor Muse's eye see?
For all that men account as rich or rare
Doth borrow all its boasted worth from thee.
   So let them praise the stars, or gems that shine;
   My world hath light and riches that are thine.

###862###
When cruelest distance holds thee far away,
And I no longer thy sweet face may see,
A heavy darkness falleth on my day,
And joy's bright sun doth cease to shine for me.
Each moment absent is a torment wrought,
Wherein my restless thoughts no comfort find,
Until some blessed dream of thee is brought,
To grant a brief reprieve unto my mind.
But waking, I with greater sorrow mourn,
As leaden hours pass me, dull and slow,
My soul from thy soul's better half is torn
And made to drink the cup of lonely woe.
   Thus languish I when we are far apart,
   For thou dost keep the key that turns my heart.

###863###
When I behold Time's thieving, ruthless hand
Pluck youth and beauty's blossom from the tree,
I fear his march, which none may countermand,
And what shall be his final, grim decree.
He carves deep furrows in the fairest brows
And turns the gold of summer into rust;
He breaks the strongest and the truest vows
And grinds all mortal glory into dust.
Yet in this verse, preserved by humble ink,
Thy gracious beauty shall not ever fade,
For future ages, when on thee they think,
Shall see the perfect image I have made.
   So let Time wreak his will; thou still shalt live
   And have that life which my poor lines can give.

###864###
I have heard music of a wondrous sound
That held in thrall my much-delighted ear,
As though an angel trod on hallowed ground
And sang a hymn to all that heav'n holds dear.
But when thy voice I by some fortune hear,
It doth possess a far more potent skill;
That angel's song, which once I held so clear,
Compared to thine, falls dissonant and still.
No lute nor viol, though a master play,
Can stir my soul from its dull, heavy strings
So much as when I hear thee softly say
Those simple words thy gentle spirit brings.
   Thy voice is music of a higher art,
   That strikes the truest chord within my heart.

###865###
My foolish eye, in gazing upon thee,
Is made a traitor to my sober thought,
For it reports such wonders back to me
As by no truth nor reason can be taught.
It swears that heaven resides within thy face,
And that thy form is of a stuff divine,
Ignoring Time, that runs his weary race
To prove thy substance is the same as mine.
It builds a faith on such a brittle ground,
That at my reason's touch it must descend,
And in this error is my sorrow found,
On which my heart must foolishly depend.
   And so my judgement is entirely betrayed
   By that false idol which my eye hath made.

###866###
When in the court of my own silent breast
I hold a session for the thoughts of you,
All other cares are quickly laid to rest,
And every thought is loyal, fond, and new.
One brings the image of your sun-bright eyes,
Another tells the story of your grace,
A third recounts your voice, to my surprise,
And takes all sadness from this lonely place.
But then a rebel thought, called Jealousy,
Doth poison all the sweetness I had found,
And paints a world of your inconstancy,
Where my devotion is on barren ground.
   Thus in my mind a civil war I find,
   For love of you hath overthrown my mind.

###867###
As weary travellers long for morning's light
When they are lost in darkness and in fear,
So do I yearn for thy most blessed sight
To banish all the shadows I hold dear.
For in thy absence, I am cloaked in night,
A starless sky, a world bereft of sun,
And wander aimlessly, deprived of sight,
My daily race already lost, not run.
But if the memory of thee should appear,
It is a moon that gives a hopeful gleam,
To let me know that morning may be near,
And this long darkness is a passing dream.
   Yet for the sun of thy true face I long,
   To make my world right, which is now all wrong.

###868###
That which is fair must fade, the sages write,
And what is strong, to ruin must submit;
The brightest day must yield itself to night,
And wisest men must lose their sharpest wit.
All things that grow, to their own end must bend,
Consumed by that which did them nourish most;
The sweetest love must find a bitter end,
And turn into a sad and weeping ghost.
So, knowing this, I take my pen in hand
To wage a war against Time's swift decay,
To build a fortress on this shifting sand
Where thy sweet virtues shall not pass away.
   Let all else perish, by grim fate undone,
   My verse shall make thy name and glory one.

###869###
If thou shouldst ask what sustenance I crave,
'Tis not the feast that noble lords provide,
Nor gold, nor gems that worldly princes save,
Nor any honour born of foolish pride.
My famine is not for some earthly bread,
My thirst not for the vintner's rarest wine;
By visions of your beauty I am fed,
And thoughts of you are nourishment divine.
One glance from thee is more than meat and drink,
A single word my spirit's hunger stills,
And on the merest hope of you I think,
Which all my soul with untold riches fills.
   So let me starve, if I am starved of thee,
   For in your grace is all satiety.

###870###
Sometimes I think thy heart is made of stone,
So little pity in thine eyes I see,
And that my love, to thy cold spirit shown,
Is but a foolish, weak infirmity.
Thou sitt'st enthroned in self-sufficient pride,
And tak'st my worship as a simple due,
While my poor heart, with sorrow weapon-eyed,
Doth break itself in homaging of you.
Yet if a flaw in thy perfection gleams,
'Tis but a shadow that the sun doth cast;
Thy coldness is not what it truly seems,
But a defense to make thy beauty last.
   Or so I tell myself, to ease the smart,
   And praise the ice that is encasing thy heart.

###871###
The bitter wind of winter's sharpest breath,
That strips the trees and bids the rivers freeze,
And wraps the world in likeness of its death,
Is but a mirror for my heart's disease.
The sun, a stranger, shows his face no more,
The birds are silent, and the flowers are fled,
The boughs are barren that were green before,
And all the joy of summer's lease is dead.
Such is my soul when you are not in view,
A frozen waste, a landscape of despair,
That finds no warmth, no light, no hopeful hue,
But constant frost and chill within the air.
   So come, my summer, and return to me,
   And thaw the winter that is caused by thee.

###872###
Why dost thou grace me with a gentle look,
If in thy heart thou hast no gentle thought?
Why is my name writ in thy memory's book,
If all my long devotion comes to naught?
Why do thine eyes a promise seem to make
That thy reserved and silent tongue denies?
Is it thy pleasure, for thy beauty's sake,
To live upon the tribute of my sighs?
Oh, be not like the rose, so fair to view,
That hides a thorn to pierce the tender hand,
But let thy kindness be as fair and true
As is the beauty all men understand.
   Either be cruel and have my suit abhorred,
   Or be thou kind, and speak the healing word.

###873###
The world's a stage, and all men players are,
Or so they say, who speak with knowing tongue;
Each plays his part beneath a guiding star,
And speaks the lines that on his fate are hung.
My part is love, a role I did not choose,
But which was thrust upon me by thine eyes;
And in this play, I seem to ever lose,
My only speech a litany of sighs.
You are the queen, for whom the plot is made,
I but the servant kneeling at your feet,
My hopes a script that I have long essayed,
Whose final act is bitter, not the sweet.
   When will the author of this play relent,
   And write a scene where you are kindly bent?

###874###
Love is a fever, burning in the blood,
A sickness that no medicine can cure,
A drowning torrent in a raging flood,
A pain that reason cannot long endure.
It makes the sane man speak a madman's words,
It makes the wise man act a simple fool;
It draws its subjects with invisible cords,
And makes the heart defy the mind's own rule.
And I, dear love, am taken with this ill,
My pulse doth race when you but pass me by;
My judgement's weak, my reason standeth still,
And all my health is sickness, when you're nigh.
   Yet if to love thee is this malady,
   I pray to God he ne'er discovers remedy.

###875###
Thy beauty is a tyrant, cold and great,
That rules my heart with absolute command;
It sets itself within a royal state
And holds my freedom in its conquering hand.
My thoughts are subjects, and they bow to you,
My senses are the spies that serve your throne,
Reporting back on all you say and do,
Until I have no will that is my own.
You levy taxes on my sleep and rest,
You claim a tribute of my every tear,
And host a constant battle in my breast
Between the warring factions, hope and fear.
   And yet, a willing captive I remain,
   And find a strange contentment in my chain.

###876###
As a poor mariner, by tempest tossed,
Doth search the heavens for a single star,
When all his hope and bearing he hath lost,
And angry waves his guide and compass mar;
So in the storm of my own deep despair,
When doubts like clouds obscure my soul's own sky,
I search for thee, who art my only care,
Lest in this endless, churning sea I die.
For you are my fixed point, my northern light,
The constant guide by which my course is set;
The only beacon in my endless night,
A grace and promise I cannot forget.
   So shine thou forth, and let thy light be shown,
   And steer my shipwrecked heart back to thine own.

###877###
What wretched poverty my state reveals,
When all my worth upon your whim depends;
My heart, the treasure that your absence steals,
Finds wealth again when your displeasure ends.
One moment I am rich beyond compare,
If you but grace me with a passing smile;
The next, I'm plunged in pauper's deep despair,
If you should frown on me a little while.
I have no fortune that is truly mine,
No store of joy that I can call my own;
My sun will set if yours should cease to shine,
And I am left in darkness and alone.
   When will you grant me- a more stable state,
   And not so toy with my dependent fate?

###878###
Last night in sleep, my spirit flew to thee,
And broke the bonds of dreary, waking life,
To find a world where I was truly free
From all my daily sorrow and my strife.
There, thou wert kind, and didst my presence greet,
And spoke such words as I do long to hear;
The vision was so potent and so sweet,
I thought my heaven had at last drawn near.
But then the dawn, that traitor to my rest,
Did steal upon me with its hateful light,
And tore the joyful image from my breast,
And left me to the coldness of my plight.
   Thus I prefer the darkness of the night,
   Whose falsehoods bring me more than day's true light.

###879###
The poet's praise of Helen's wondrous face,
Or Cleopatra's power to enthrall,
Are but faint shadows of thy living grace,
Beside which all their storied beauties fall.
They speak of lips, of eyes, of flowing hair,
And build a monument of empty rhyme,
But had they seen thee, they would all despair
And burn their verses to defy sharp Time.
For they did praise what was but merely fair,
A copy of a form they had not seen;
But thou art truth itself, beyond compare,
The perfect model of what they did mean.
   So let them rest, their labour was in vain,
   For all their praise in thee lives fresh again.

###880###
So here I end this sequence of my rhyme,
A feeble testament to all you are;
A poor attempt to conquer cruelest Time,
And hitch my wagon to your beauty's star.
Perhaps these lines a future age will see,
When I am dust and you a memory's ghost,
And wonder at the one who wrote of thee
As what his troubled soul did value most.
Let them not judge my verse, but judge my love,
And how it strove, with words, to make you live,
And place your virtue all things else above,
With all the strength my humble art could give.
   So when all other monuments are gone,
   Read this, and know, your beauty shall live on.

###921###
When envious Time, with his relentless hand,
Doth seek to steal thy beauty from mine eye,
And scatter it like dust across the land,
So that no trace of thy sweet form may lie;
Then shall this verse, a fortress for thy sake,
Defy the siege of winters and of years,
And from thy ruin a new image make,
Preserv'd in ink against all mortal fears.
For what is age but shadow, and what grief
Can long outlive the memory of grace?
Thy summer's day, though transient and brief,
Within these lines shall find a lasting place.
   So let Time rage, and do his worst, for he
   Cannot take what my verse hath sav'd of thee.

###922###
My weary thoughts, that have no other guide,
Do fly to thee when darkness claims the day,
And at the window of thy heart they bide,
To look on that which is so far away.
They speak of sorrows that my soul doth bear,
The heavy burden of thy absence known,
A secret pain no other soul may share,
A winter's chill that freezes to the bone.
But when in dreams thy gentle face I see,
A sudden sun doth banish all my night,
And for a moment sets my spirit free,
To bathe again in thy most blessèd light.
   Yet waking steals this comfort from my head,
   And leaves me with the lonely tears I shed.

###923###
I saw thee stand against the setting sun,
And thought that heav'n had placed an angel there;
The day's last light, its golden labours done,
Did weave a halo in thy wondrous hair.
My foolish heart, a ship without a helm,
Was tossed and turned upon a sudden sea,
And in that light which did me overwhelm,
Surrendered all its sovereignty to thee.
I could not speak, I could not breathe nor move,
My senses bound by beauty's potent spell,
The helpless subject of a newborn love,
Too deep for my poor, stammering tongue to tell.
   And in that silence, all my world was changed,
   By thy sweet form forever rearranged.

###924###
Why dost thou wear a mask upon thy soul,
And speak with words that are not truly thine?
Thy pretty lies, which thou dost think so droll,
Are bitter poison where I looked for wine.
Thy face, a map of honesty and trust,
Doth contradict the purpose of thine heart;
Thou build'st a palace on foundations' dust,
And play'st with skill a false and cunning part.
Didst thou not swear thy faith was strong and deep,
A rock against the shifting tides of doubt?
But in thy promises, what vow didst keep?
Thy summer's fire by autumn is put out.
   So take thy leave, and let thy falsehood go,
   For truth, though stark, is all I wish to know.

###925###
The world's a stage, and all my part is this:
To pine for that which I may not possess;
To dream of heaven in a moment's bliss,
Then wake to long and lasting loneliness.
My lines are learned, the gestures are all known,
I play the lover with a heavy grace,
But when the crowds are gone and I'm alone,
Despair's true shadow falls upon my face.
What foolish playwright gave this role to me,
To love a star I never can embrace?
This tragic play of my nativity,
Hath doomèd me to run a fruitless race.
   And so I act, till the last curtain fall,
   A faithful fool, who gives and loses all.

###926###
O, how my heart doth chide my roving eye,
Which, though it sees a world of fair renown,
Returns to thee, and lets all others by,
As if thy face were its own native town.
For other forms are but as foreign lands,
With curious sights that entertain the brain,
But hold no comfort for the heart's demands,
And cannot soothe its old and constant pain.
Thy presence is the home it longs to find,
The only prospect that can give it peace,
A sanctuary for the troubled mind,
Where all its anxious wanderings may cease.
   So let my eye roam where it will, you see,
   My captive heart shall still abide with thee.

###927###
As winter's frost doth silence the sweet bird,
