And chains the brook that once did freely run,
So hath thy coldness, in a single word,
Eclipsed the light of my affection's sun.
I, who did think my love a summer's rose,
That in thy favour would forever bloom,
Now find it blighted by untimely snows,
And shut within a dark and joyless room.
The passion that did burn so fierce and bright,
Now flickers low, a candle in the wind,
And struggles to withstand the coming night,
Which thy disdain hath cruelly designed.
   Yet in this chill, one ember still doth glow,
   And hopes for spring, which thou alone canst show.

###928###
To me, fair friend, you never can be old,
For as you were when first your eye I eyed,
Such seems your beauty still. The tales are told
Of Time, who doth in seasons' change abide.
His cruel hand may carve the fairest brow,
And fill the fields of youth with wrinkled care,
But holds no sway on my eternal vow,
To find thee still most excellently fair.
Thy beauty is not subject to his rage,
Nor shall his scythe cut down what I hold dear;
Thou art the hero of my spirit's stage,
Exempt from ruin, blight, or mortal fear.
   For in my verse, thy youth shall ever live,
   And mock the years which take, but cannot give.

###929###
When in the mirror I behold my face,
I see the work of pilfering, slow decay;
The lines that steal all youthful, smooth-faced grace,
And turn my summer to a winter's day.
My hair, once dark, with silver threads is fraught,
A warning of the toll that life doth take,
And every battle that my soul hath fought
Is etched thereon for all the world to make.
But when I think of thee, that vision flies,
And I am young, as in my hopeful prime,
Reflected in the kindness of thine eyes,
I find a refuge from the wars of Time.
   Thy love, a fountain, doth my youth restore,
   And makes me feel as I shall age no more.

###930###
Let me not curse the fortune that I have,
Though it be poor, and brings me to my knees;
Nor should I scorn the little that I save,
While others gather riches with more ease.
For what is gold, or what is high estate,
Compared to that one treasure I possess?
In holding thee, I am more fortunate
Than kings who rule in gilded loneliness.
Thy love's a kingdom, and thy heart a throne,
Thy favour is the revenue I claim;
In thy affection, I am not alone,
And bear with pride my unregarded name.
   So let them have their power and their art;
   My greatest wealth is lodged within thy heart.

###931###
Dost thou not know thine eyes are burning spheres,
That set my careful reason all aflame?
They promise joys, and yet they foster fears,
And in their light I quite forget my name.
One glance from thee can build a world anew,
With skies more bright and flowers of richer hue;
Another glance can run that world straight through,
And leave but dust where all that beauty grew.
How shall I navigate this wild terrain,
This push and pull of torment and of bliss?
How can I bear this exquisite, sweet pain,
And find my course by such a star as this?
   I am thy mariner, and thou my sea,
   Lost in the tempest of thy hold on me.

###932###
How oft have I, with melancholy thought,
Considered all the things I cannot change:
The wasted hours, the battles left unfought,
The paths not taken, now forever strange.
I think on words I wish I had not said,
And silences where comfort should have been,
And mourn the ghosts of hope, now long since dead,
The bitter fruit of what I've heard and seen.
This sad account, in sorrow I recount,
And pay old debts with freshly minted tears,
Until my griefs to a great sum do mount,
Compounded by the interest of years.
   But if I think of thee, the score is cleared;
   The debt is paid, the sadness disappeared.

###933###
Thy voice is music, yet it is not sound
That merely strikes upon the listening ear;
It is a force that shakes the very ground
On which my soul doth stand in hope and fear.
It is the theme to which my thoughts are set,
The melody my silent heart doth sing,
A tune so sweet I never can forget,
The herald of a never-ending spring.
Let others praise the lute, the flute, the lyre,
And find their joy in artful, skilled refrains;
Thy speech alone doth feed my soul's desire,
And in its grace, all other music wanes.
   For when thou speak'st, the world itself doth pause,
   Attentive to sweet beauty's living laws.

###934###
The sun, ashamed, doth hide his face away,
When thou dost walk abroad in morning's light,
For thy bright eyes create a brighter day,
And put his golden majesty to flight.
The tender rose, when thou art standing near,
Doth blush with envy at thy gentle cheek,
And sheds its petals in a silent fear,
That its own fame for beauty is too weak.
All nature's pride, which she doth hold so dear,
Seems but a shadow, tribute unto thee;
The fairest things, when thy form doth appear,
Confess their place of lesser quality.
   Then marvel not if I am overthrown,
   By grace that makes the world's best seem unknown.

###935###
I am a miser of each hour with thee,
And count them as a rich man counts his gold,
For in thy presence is my liberty,
From all the sorrows that my heart doth hold.
But when the clock doth strike the parting time,
It is a thief that steals my breath away,
Announcing with its dull and hateful chime
The coming reign of a more wretched day.
Each moment absent is a fortune spent,
A treasure lost which I cannot reclaim;
A punishment for some transgression sent,
To burn my spirit in a sorrowing flame.
   And so I wait, a beggar, poor and grieved,
   Until by thee my fortune is retrieved.

###936###
Should some dark rumour or a sland'rous tongue
Attempt to make our true affection weak,
And say the praises that my heart hath sung
Are hollow words that I but idly speak;
Believe it not. For envious minds will try
To cast a shadow where the sun is bright,
And with the poison of a whispered lie,
Attempt to turn our morning into night.
My love for thee is not a fragile thing,
To be undone by what the jealous say;
It hath deep roots, from which its blossoms spring,
And fears no storm that comes to cloud the day.
   So trust my verse, and not the words of spite;
   My love is true, my faith a burning light.

###937###
This world of ours is but a fevered dream,
A frantic race for that which will not last;
The things that are, are not the things they seem,
And what we build is all too quickly cast.
We chase for honour, which is but a breath,
We dig for gold, a cold and heavy thing,
And in our haste, we quite forget that Death
On all our triumphs his dark cloak will fling.
But in this madness, I have found one truth,
One constant star in this confusing sky,
A lasting joy, a form of endless youth,
The certain comfort that within thine eye
   I see a world more real, more pure, more kind;
   A waking truth to my near-maddened mind.

###938###
Like as the moon doth govern the vast tide,
And pulls the ocean to a distant shore,
So doth thy will o'er my affections ride,
And makes me love thee ever more and more.
I have no power to resist thy sway,
No strength to fight the magic thou dost wield;
My heart, thy subject, willingly obeys,
And to thy sovereign beauty it doth yield.
If thou shouldst frown, my spirit's sea is low,
A barren flat where joy can find no place;
But if thou smil'st, the inward tides do flow,
And all is life, and plenitude, and grace.
   So am I bound to thee by nature's chain,
   To share thy joy and suffer in thy pain.

###939###
My pen is weary and my ink is dry,
From striving still to capture thy sweet grace;
The more I write, the more I find that I
Lack wit to show the glory of thy face.
My finest words are but a clumsy shade,
My richest rhymes are poor and wanting art,
The truest portrait that I've ever made
Falls far below the image in my heart.
Perhaps 'tis folly, then, to try at all,
To paint the sun with charcoal, black and plain,
To answer to a god's immortal call
With human voice, that is so full of pain.
   And yet I must, for silence would betray
   The light in me that comes from thy bright day.

###940###
When all these poems are but dust and age,
And my short time on earth is but a name,
Thy beauty still shall live upon this page,
Preserved forever from Oblivion's flame.
This ink, a vessel, shall transport thy worth
Across the seas of all forthcoming years,
And prove to those who later walk the earth
The cause and subject of my hopeful fears.
Though empires fall and princes turn to clay,
And all the works of mighty hands decline,
Thy sacred essence shall not know decay,
But live immortal in this verse of mine.
   For words, when born of love, can death defy,
   And so, in this, you, love, shall never die.

###841###
Let others praise the sun's most common light,
Whose golden promise is a swift deceit;
It holds no pow'r against my endless night,
Nor makes my bitter, weary journey sweet.
But in thine eyes a truer planet glows,
A constant star to guide my heart's desire,
From which a purer, steadier lustre flows
To set my frozen, hopeless soul on fire.
So when the world in shadow is arrayed,
And I in doubt's dark ocean start to drown,
I look to thee and am no more afraid,
Nor fear the tempests of a tyrant's frown.
Let lesser men by sun or lodestar see;
Thy love's own light is guide enough for me.

###842###
When wasteful Time in his most dreadful rage
Doth seek to turn thy summer to his age,
And casts all living things upon a stage
To read their final lines from his sad page;
Thy beauty shall not break like brittle stone,
Nor see thy sweet perfection turn to bone.
For in my verse thou shalt not be alone,
Though all thy worldly garden be o'ergrown.
Against death's sword and envy's sharpest rust,
My rhyming phalanx shall defend thy dust.
In these black lines place all thy fading trust,
Which shall outlive the kings that say "I must."
For Time may claim thy breath, but not take thee,
Whose truest life lives in my poetry.

###843###
To be away from thee is not to stay
In some far land, nor pass a sunless day,
But 'tis to feel my own true self decay,
And watch my spirit slowly waste away.
For thou art not a person or a place,
But the bestower of all life and grace;
Without thee, there is but an empty space
Where once I saw a world within thy face.
My constant thoughts do make their journey home on tears,
And in my heart take root all baseless fears.
An hour from thee is longer than all years,
And thy dear voice is all my deaf soul hears.
So know, my love, when we are forced to part,
You take the beat and blood out of my heart.

###844###
My heart to thy sweet service I do hold,
A tenement for which no rent is paid in gold,
But by a contract, faithfully enrolled,
My very soul for thy dear love was sold.
Nor can some other claimant break the seal
That on this bond thy virtues did reveal;
No thief of hearts can thy possession steal,
Nor my firm faith be tempted to unfeel.
This sacred cov'nant is a stronger bond
Than any writ by any mortal hand;
It reaches past this life and far beyond,
And thy sole ownership will ever stand.
So take thy due, for all I have is thine,
And in this bondage find I freedom's sign.

###845###
My love is not accounted with the fair,
She hath no suns for eyes, no golden hair;
With goddesses I dare not her compare,
For fear her earthly truth become a snare.
Her skin is not so pure as winter's white,
Her breath not sweeter than a summer's night,
And yet she is for me a wondrous light
That makes my world, in all its darkness, bright.
I do not praise a false and painted cheek,
But eyes that with a living language speak;
She is the only treasure that I seek,
The prize for which my humble words are weak.
Let others love what all the world can see;
I love the faults that make her dear to me.

###846###
Since thou art gone, the world hath lost its hue,
The rose is not so red, the sky so new;
The fairest picture seems no longer true,
And all is sadness to my weary view.
The sun itself doth shine a pallid grey,
The birdsong seems to mock the dying day;
All beauty took its leave when you went away,
And in its place, shadows and sorrows play.
The softest grass feels sharp beneath my ground,
The sweetest bells make but a jarring sound;
No earthly pleasure can for me be found,
For all is barren, all the year around.
My senses fail, and have no life, when gone
Is she who is my blest and only sun.

###8KA###
The blushing rose, which all the world doth praise,
Doth hang its head in shame when thou art nigh;
The lily, queen of all the summer days,
Doth lose its white and for perfection sigh.
For nature, in her plan to make thee well,
Did steal the finest patterns from each flow'r;
The rose's blush upon thy cheek doth dwell,
The lily's white is thy soft skin's own pow'r.
The violet's deep and thoughtful, purple hue
Lives in the secret glances of thine eye;
Thy breath, the sweetest scent that e'er I knew,
Makes jealous blossoms wither up and die.
Thus in thy form all nature's best we see,
And all her garden pays its tax to thee.

###848###
My foolish eyes and my more foolish heart
Are locked in civil war and sore debate;
The eyes would have my constant soul depart
From what the heart doth love and they do hate.
My eyes do swear that they see countless flaws,
And bid my heart to heed what they report;
The heart replies that love knows no such laws,
And in thy very faults finds sweetest sport.
The eyes, defeated, then declare me blind;
The heart contends the eyes have lost their art,
For they see but the shell, and not the mind,
Which is the truest and the noblest part.
And so this endless battle I must prove:
My eyes see not what my true heart doth love.

###849###
My soul is like a kingdom in this land,
Whose government is in a woeful state;
The laws are broken by a rebel hand,
And chaos knocks upon the palace gate.
My reason, once a counsellor of trust,
Hath fled his post, and passion holds the throne,
Reducing all my better thoughts to dust,
While loyal feeling makes a piteous moan.
For thou, the rightful monarch of this place,
Art absent from thy throne and from my sight;
And anarchy doth rule with ugly grace
Where once thy presence made all order bright.
Return, my queen, and end this wretched plight,
And with thy rule, restore my world to right.

###850###
When envious tongues, with poison on their breath,
Do seek to slander thee with bitter wrong,
They sentence their own honesty to death,
And fill my heart with a defiant song.
For I, who know the truth of thy dear name,
Can see their malice for the shame it is;
They seek to profit from a borrowed blame,
And find some joy in telling what's amiss.
But thy great worth, a sun in their dark spite,
Doth shine too strongly to be hid by cloud;
Their feeble words cannot put out thy light,
But merely echo their own sins aloud.
So let them talk; their words are but a lie
That fades to nothing 'neath an honest sky.

###851###
When weary toil doth bid me seek my rest,
And sleep should come to soothe my troubled breast,
My mind, by thoughts of thee, is still possessed,
And by thy shadow is my soul oppressed.
For in the darkness that my slumber brings,
Thine image to my sorrow's eyesight springs,
A ghostly form on swift and silent wings,
That robs my peace and all my comfort wrings.
I see thy face, yet cannot feel thy form;
I hear thy voice, a whisper in a storm.
My bed, which should be safe and soft and warm,
Becomes the stage for sorrow's cruel charm.
And so I wake, finding no comfort in the sight
That haunts my day and persecutes my night.

###852###
If I were bid to tend a garden's grace,
I would not plant the rose within that place,
But rather seek the garden of thy face
And find all flow'rs within that narrow space.
Thy cheeks, two blushing pinks, do sweetly show
More perfect colour than the rose can know;
Thy lips, whereon two red carnations grow,
A sweeter scent on all the air bestow.
The lily's white is in thy noble brow,
The violet's truth is in thine eyes, I vow.
Thy virtues are the soil that doth allow
Such rare perfections on a single bough.
So let all other gardens fade and part;
I'll tend the living garden of thy heart.

###853###
I am accused of foul idolatry,
For making thee the object of my praise;
They say I sin when I do bend my knee
And consecrate to thee all of my days.
My verse, they say, becomes a holy shrine,
Wherein thy image is a god to see;
They call thy beauty heav'nly and divine,
A sacred text for all the world and me.
If this be sin, then I will gladly bear
The damning sentence and the pious thought,
For in my worship, I can only swear
A purer faith was never to be bought.
Let others pray to saints of wood and stone;
My only saint and saviour is thy love alone.

###854###
I bless the cruelty within thy scorn,
And find a ragged comfort in the thorn
That pricks my heart, for of that pain is born
A love that feels perpetually new-torn.
Each sharp rebuke, each cold and careless art,
Doth send a thrilling shiver to my heart,
And though I feel the wound and bear the smart,
I would not have you play a kinder part.
For in your softness I might grow too proud,
Too safe and certain in my happy state;
But in your harshness, 'midst the weeping crowd,
I prize the smallest glance you send my late.
So be thou cruel, and so keep my love ill,
For in that sickness, I adore thy will.

###855###
When in the mirror of my soul I see my worth,
I find a barren and a wretched earth,
A thing of little consequence or mirth,
And curse the lowly station of my birth.
How can so base a creature dare to claim
The sacred honour of thy glorious name?
My verse is poor, my character is shame,
And I am fit for nothing but thy blame.
My store of virtues is a beggar's, poor,
Whilst thy rich bounty daily grows yet more,
As waves of grace do wash upon thy shore,
And leave new treasures unseen there before.
So must I love thee, wanting worth to be
A worthy vessel to encompass thee.

###856###
My love for thee is subject to thy frost,
And by the winter of thy mood is lost;
My hopes, like leaves, are scattered and wind-tossed,
And in that tempest, I must pay the cost.
For when thy brow is shadowed by a frown,
The sun in my small universe goes down;
The world may give to thee a kingly crown,
But thy displeasure is my lost renown.
Yet when a smile doth usher in thy spring,
The frozen rivers of my soul do sing;
Thy sudden warmth doth such a summer bring,
That I forget I am a wretched thing.
Thus, in thy face I mark the changing year,
And know no season save when thou art dear.

###857###
My pen is dipped in sorrow when I write,
For every word is born of endless night;
My ink is black, for thou, who art my light,
Art absent from my paper and my sight.
The lines I draw are rivers that do flow
With all the currents of my silent woe;
And on the page, my wretched feelings show
The barren lands where no delights can grow.
Each letter is a black and bitter stain
That speaks of solitude and ceaseless pain;
To call thy name is effort spent in vain,
A sound absorbed by unrelenting rain.
So when you read what my sad hand doth part,
You read the ink that issues from my heart.

###858###
Thy beauty is a summer that shall not fade,
Nor be confined within a winter's shade;
By Time's cruel scythe thou shalt not be unmade,
Nor in the dust of ages shalt be laid.
For nature gives all things a dated lease,
And bids their fairest glories to decrease;
But thy perfection's reign shall never cease,
Nor find in its long summer a release.
The sun may tire, the very seasons last
For but a span, then into memory's past
Their glories fall; but thou art held so fast
Thy light by no such shadow shall be cast.
For in my verse thy eternal summer shall grow,
And death shall never brag of what I show.

###859###
I am a miser of my thoughts of thee,
And keep them locked within my memory's store,
Fearing that if I set one fancy free,
The world might steal it and leave me the more poor.
I count them over in my mind's dark chest,
Each golden thought a coin that feels more blessed
Than any treasure by a king possessed,
On which my spirit finds its only rest.
I do not wish to share this secret spend,
Nor show my riches to a prying friend,
But keep my wealth until my life shall end,
And on this secret pleasure, I depend.
For though you think I give my love the key,
This treasure's only keeper shall be me.

###860###
If all that I have written is not true,
And my undying passion for you
Is but a fiction, painted fresh and new,
Then beauty hath to truth no payment due.
If your dear eyes, which I so oft deny
Are common glass, and stars that rule the sky,
Then let the world believe my verse a lie
And let all virtuous seeming pass us by.
But if there's truth in this my humble case,
And heav'nly virtue shines within thy grace,
And you are, in this dark and faithless place,
The one perfection of the human race,
Then you must grant my suit, and so forever prove
That truth and beauty are the slaves of love.

###901###
When I upon thy wondrous beauty gaze,
The very sun appears a dimmer sight,
My thoughts are lost within a loving maze,
And thy dear presence makes the darkness bright.
No nightingale doth make so sweet a sound,
No rose displays so delicate a hue,
As thy soft voice which blesses all the ground,
And thy fair cheek, forever fresh and new.
Yet all my verse lacks the sufficient art
To capture half the grace I see above,
It cannot show my captivated heart,
Nor speak the full dimension of my love.
So let these simple lines confess and see,
That thou art all the universe to me.

###902###
When thou art leagues and cruel miles away,
The harmony of all the world feels wrong,
The brightest noon becomes a gloomy day,
And every passing moment seems too long.
I search in others for a single trace
Of that perfection I in thee did find,
But see no substitute for thy dear face,
Whose perfect image occupies my mind.
At night, when I am given o'er to sleep,
Thy likeness comes in dreams, a gentle form,
A sacred vision that my soul doth keep
To be my star in life's uncertain storm.
And so I live upon this precious thought,
Till to my sight thou art again re-brought.

###903###
My humble words are but a clouded glass
That cannot hope thy true effect to show;
The finest praise I fashion must alas
Fall short of all the debt of love I owe.
I steal from stars to say thine eyes do shine,
And rob the rose to praise thy cheek's deep red,
But this weak theft in every paltry line
Proves my poor art is better left unsaid.
For when I look upon thy perfect face,
No simile or gilded phrase holds true;
My wit is bankrout, beggared by thy grace,
Which makes all worldly treasure seem but new.
So I'll be silent, and let gazers see
Thy beauty writes its own best poetry.

###904###
That thief, old Time, with ever-hastening pace,
Doth seek to carve deep furrows in thy brow,
And steal the youthful beauty from thy face
Which holds my heart in adoration now.
His cruel chisel shall wear down hard stone,
And turn to dust the monuments of kings;
No worldly strength can stop his endless moan,
Nor all the wealth that mortal treasure brings.
But thy sweet form, though subject to his blade,
Shall in the fortress of my verse be kept;
Here shall thy summer beauty never fade,
While ages hence by tearful eyes are wept.
So shall my love outlive Time's grim decree,
And keep thee ever young for all to see.

###905###
Let those who will, in courtly fashion speak
Of love as if it were a pleasant game,
And with false sighs a lady's favour seek,
To set a passing lustre on their name.
My heart knows not such calculating art,
Nor can my tongue such hollow vows profess;
It speaks but what is graven on my part,
A truth that time itself cannot make less.
For love to me is not a sunlit stream,
But a great ocean, powerful and vast,
More real than any poet's transient dream,
A bond that shall for evermore outlast.
So let them play their parts with cunning skill,
My constant soul must love thee, and be still.

###906###
My weary eye, when daylight takes its leave,
No comfort from the gentle darkness finds,
But for thy sake, whom I am left to grieve,
It conjures up what waking life unbinds.
It fashions thee from shadows and from air,
And paints thy form upon the blackest night,
A vision so impossibly most fair
That my sad soul doth marvel at the sight.
But when the dawn, that tyrant, doth appear
And chase away the phantoms I hold dear,
I find my tears have proven thou'rt not here,
And all my joy is vanquished by my fear.
Thus light brings dark and darkness brings me light,
In endless torment of my day and night.

###907###
If all my worth were tallied in this age,
And every deed were writ for all to see,
They'd find but one line on life's crowded page:
My only virtue is my love for thee.
My wit is dull, my station is but low,
I have no lands, no titles to my name;
The seeds of greatness in me will not grow,
Nor shall I win the fleeting prize of fame.
But in this love, I am a very king,
And hold a treasure richer than all gold;
The boundless realm from which my praises spring
Is more than any emperor can hold.
So let the world account me poor and small,
In holding thee, I know I have it all.

###908###
Why dost thou use thy beauty as a sword
To wound the heart that offers thee its all?
Why is my constant love by thee ignored,
While at thy feet my truest feelings fall?
The winter frost is kinder than thy scorn,
The harshest wind less biting than thy wit,
Which leaves my hopeful spirit bruised and torn,
And for despair makes my poor soul more fit.
Dost thou not know thy power is so great
That one soft glance could raise me from the dust?
That thy displeasure is a heavy weight
That crushes hope and murders all my trust?
Then use thy beauty kindly, as you should,
And be not only fair, but also good.

###909###
As an unthrifty fool his wealth doth spend
On fleeting joys that vanish in a day,
So have I given, till there is no end,
My store of heart and very soul away.
I made no count of what I freely gave,
Nor sought return for all my loyal trust,
Content to be thy ever-willing slave,
And see my service trampled in the dust.
But now that I am bankrout and undone,
With empty coffers where my love once lay,
I see how foolishly my race was run,
To let one soul command such mighty sway.
Yet were I rich again, I could not choose
But give thee all, and be content to lose.

###910###
The changing seasons, in their constant turn,
Show nature's truth in every leaf and flower;
A lesson that my heart was slow to learn,
Held captive by a moment's fleeting power.
The spring's bright promise to the summer yields,
Which in its turn to autumn's gold must bow,
Then winter reigns o'er barren hills and fields,
With icy breath on every frozen bough.
So did my love, once in its hopeful spring,
Believe thy summer ne'er would pass away;
I could not see the cold that time would bring,
Nor think that such a sun could have its grey.
But now I know that beauty is a lease,
And even love's own season has its cease.

###911###
Some glory in their birth, some in their skill,
Some in their wealth which they have got by might,
Some in their hawks and hounds that hunt the hill,
Some in their garments, beautiful and bright.
Each to his own is some particular joy,
In which his mind a private comfort takes,
But all these things are subject to annoy,
When fortune turns and their foundation shakes.
For me, I find no pleasure in such things,
Nor build my state on what can be undone;
The only pride from which my spirit sings
Is that my love and thy dear self are one.
Wretched in this, that thou mayst take away,
And make my only glory soon decay.

###912###
My tongue is tied when I approach thy grace,
And all the words I've practiced fly from me;
I stand a silent fool before thy face,
A captive dumb for all the world to see.
My mind, that can be eloquent and bold
When other, lesser subjects are the theme,
Becomes a fearful child who can't unfold
The simple story of his waking dream.
And so I fear that thou wilt think me cold,
And deem my love a thing of little worth,
A tale that by another could be told
With greater passion and more joyful mirth.
But silent love is deepest, pray believe,
For what the tongue can't speak, the heart must grieve.

###913###
Against the faithless world and its deceit,
Where painted words conceal a hollow heart,
And every smile upon the street you meet
Is but an actor playing out a part,
Thy honesty is my securest shield,
A steadfast rock in a perfidious sea;
To its pure truth I willingly do yield,
And find therein my soul's integrity.
Let others trust in vows that are but air,
Or build their hopes on promises of sand;
I have one truth beyond all false compare,
And in that knowledge I securely stand.
Thy constant faith is all the truth I know,
And from that faith my truest strengths do grow.

###914###
Let me not say that I have been betrayed
By what I saw, and what I thought was true,
For 'twas my eye, not thee, that surely strayed
And built a false divinity of you.
Mine eye did craft a being from the light,
And gave thee virtues that were mine to give;
It banished shadows, made all darkness bright,
And taught my foolish, loving heart to live.
If now the statue proves to be but stone,
And all the painted colours start to fade,
The fault is mine, and mine to bear alone,
For on myself this trickery was played.
I fashioned gods, and then I bent the knee;
The fault was not in what you are, but me.

###915###
The forward violet, thus did I chide:
"Sweet thief, whence didst thou steal thy sweet that smells,
If not from my love's breath?" The lily, pride
Of all the field, on her white bosom dwells.
The marjoram had stolen thy soft hair,
The roses stand, in fearful, blushing dread,
One of thy bloom, the other of despair,
To be so vanquished by thy white and red.
A third, a thorn, I saw upon the briar,
Which did my finger prick, and make it bleed;
It was thy scorn which set my hand on fire,
A cruel and most unnecessary deed.
Thus all of flowers, in their form and state,
Do either steal thy grace, or mime thy hate.

###916###
So oft have I invoked thee in my rhyme,
That every word now seems to speak thy name,
And this, my verse, becomes a slave to time,
Repeating what has made it known to fame.
My pen, worn out with its one argument,
Can find no other subject to explore,
And on thy praise is so entirely spent,
It knows no art that it has not before.
And yet, when I do try to write anew,
Of wars, or kings, or of some other thing,
My thoughts betray me and return to you,
And of your grace they cannot help but sing.
So all I do, and all that I can write,
Begins with you, and ends with your dear light.

###917###
Was it the moon that I last night did see,
Or thy pale face in contemplation deep?
Was it the wind that whispered through the tree,
Or thy sad voice that lulled my soul to sleep?
The stars that shone with such a constant fire,
Were they but stars, or thine eternal eyes
Which looked on me and kindled my desire,
And filled my heart with wonder and surprise?
All night I questioned what was dream and what
Was truth made real by my adoring mind,
And in this state of doubt, I had forgot
That thou wert cruel, and I to love was blind.
For even in a dream of thy disdain,
To be deceived by thee is sweetest pain.

###918###
If music be the food of love, play on,
But not those tunes of light and happy sport;
Play me a dirge for a devotion gone,
A heavy strain of a much sadder sort.
Let every note be laden with the grief,
That my poor heart does struggle to contain;
Let its lament bring no unkind relief,
But be an echo of my constant pain.
For your dear love, which was my sweetest song,
Is now a silence in an empty hall;
The harmony that made my spirit strong
Has fled, and left me nothing but its fall.
So let the music weep, as I do now,
And with its sorrow consecrate my vow.

###919###
I see thee in the labours of the day,
In every task my weary hands pursue;
I feel thy judgment in the words I say,
As if my every action were for you.
There is no beauty that the world can show
But it reminds my soul of thy fair face,
No loyal friendship that my heart can know
But seems a shadow of thy perfect grace.
So art thou woven in the cloth of life,
The constant pattern that the fates have spun,
The source of all my peace and all my strife,
The rising and the setting of my sun.
And so to thee my every thought is bound,
On this my world's, and my heart's, holy ground.

###920###
When in the chronicle of wasted time
I see descriptions of the fairest wights,
And beauty making beautiful old rhyme,
In praise of ladies dead and lovely knights,
Then, in the blazon of sweet beauty's best,
Of hand, of foot, of lip, of eye, of brow,
I see their antique pen would have express'd
Even such a beauty as you master now.
So all their praises are but prophecies
Of this our time, all you prefiguring;
And, for they look'd but with divining eyes,
They had not skill enough your worth to sing.
For we, which now behold these present days,
Have eyes to wonder, but lack tongues to praise.

###941###
When in the night I cast my gaze on high,
To count the stars that in the heavens burn,
I find no light that with thine eyes can vie,
And from their lesser glory I do turn.
For every distant and unfeeling sphere
Doth shed a cold and solitary beam,
But thy twin suns, to me for ever dear,
Do warm my soul, as in a waking dream.
Let astronomers their curious charts devise,
To map the course of planets far astray,
I need no map but what is in thine eyes,
To guide my heart and light my woeful way.
So let them praise the moon or sun above,
My firmament is compassed by thy love.

###942###
That thief, old Time, with unrelenting hand,
Doth carve deep furrows in the fairest face,
And steals the golden treasures of the land,
Consigning youthful bloom to his disgrace.
He plucks the rose ere it be fully grown,
And silvers o'er the hair of brightest gold,
He makes the strength of mighty kings his own,
And turns the fire of passion into cold.
But 'gainst thy grace his siege shall not prevail,
Nor shall his conquest bring thy beauty low,
For in my verse thy fame shall never fail,
But with each passing season brighter grow.
For what Time takes, my loving pen shall give,
And in these lines, thy truth shall ever live.

###943###
Each weary mile that sets me far from thee
Is but a torment to my longing heart,
A league of sorrow, stretched for misery,
That holds my better self and thine apart.
The sun by day, the moon that rules the night,
Are but reminders of thy distant face,
For thou art gone, who art my only light,
And darkness fills this solitary place.
I teach my thoughts to fly on swiftest wing,
To bridge the space that cruelly doth divide,
That to thy presence they some comfort bring,
And tell thee how for thy return I've cried.
Yet thoughts are wind, and bring me no relief,
Until I see thee, I am one with grief.

###944###
When thou dost speak, all other sounds are naught,
The sweetest lute, the viol's gentle strain,
Compared to music by thy true tongue wrought,
Seem but a discord, wearisome and plain.
The morning lark that sings his tune on high
Doth not so please the newly wakened ear,
As doth thy voice, which makes my spirit fly,
And banishes all doubt and trembling fear.
I am a captive to that silver sound,
And wish for no release from such a chain,
Upon this earth no greater joy is found,
Than hearing thee but speak my name again.
So let me be thy most attentive thrall,
Thy honeyed words my only rise and fall.

###945###
Why dost thou arm thy beauty with such scorn,
To wound the heart that offers thee its all?
Was such a cruel and cutting weapon born
To make thy faithful servant weep and fall?
If nature gave thee riches to command,
A grace that makes the common world seem poor,
Was it her will that thy ungentle hand
Should shut on me affection's open door?
Be not a miser of the wealth thou hast,
For beauty's coin is meant to be well spent;
The time for hoarding treasure is soon past,
And goodness unbestowed is ill content.
Then use thy power kindly, and be wise,
And see my true love mirrored in thine eyes.

###946###
This love I bear is sickness and is cure,
A fever that doth make my senses weak,
A balm that helps my wounded soul endure,
A silent plea when I can hardly speak.
It is a poison, subtle and refined,
That courses through me with a deadly art,
And yet a cordial that restores my mind,
And mends the very breaking of my heart.
My reason tells me I should flee this state,
Wherein such contradictions madly thrive,
And yet I pray it may not soon abate,
For by this malady I feel alive.
So let me ail, if ailing be from thee,
For in thy sickness there is health for me.

###947###
When I behold the richness of a rose,
Or lily's white that shames the winter snow,
My mind at once to thy perfection goes,
From which all earthly forms of beauty flow.
The deepest violet, the sun's bright fire,
The gentle azure of a summer sky,
Do but reflect a portion of my desire,
And of the greater truth that is thine eye.
For thou art Nature's first and truest thought,
And all a-field that she doth since display
Is but a copy, imperfectly wrought,
That fades before thy more resplendent day.
So let all others praise the world's design,
I know its fairest patterns are but thine.

###948###
The world's a stage, and all men players be,
Who speak their lines and fret their little hour;
They play at kings, or bend a subject's knee,
And grasp at fading phantoms of their power.
I watch them strut and hear their hollow sound,
And find their passions but a painted show,
For on this stage no true account is found
Of all the heights and depths a heart can know.
But when thou enter'st, all the scene is changed,
The plot grows real, the shadows take on grace,
My former thoughts are suddenly estranged,
For truth itself is written on thy face.
So let them play their parts and be forgot,
With thee my audience, I need them not.

###949###
When gentle sleep should seal my weary eyes,
And grant my soul a respite from the day,
Thy constant image doth before me rise,
And chase the hope of peaceful rest away.
The darkest curtain of the lonely night
Cannot conceal the vision of thy face,
Which burns within my mind with such a light
That darkness is a too-enlightened place.
My thoughts, like pilgrims, to thy beauty stray,
And keep my heavy eyelids open wide,
While all the world in slumber deep doth lay,
With no sweet comfort standing at my side.
And so by night my mind, for thy sake, wakes,
As by the day my heart for thy sake, aches.

###950###
Thou art a thief, and stand'st of theft accused,
Though not for gold, nor any worldly prize;
My heart is what thy subtle art hath used,
Stolen from me by witchcraft of thine eyes.
It left my breast ere I was well aware,
And to thy keeping did itself commit,
And now resides, a willing captive, there,
And for a new abode finds thine more fit.
I do not ask for its return to me,
For in my vacant chest a pain would grow;
But in its place, I beg thee, let there be
The heart from thine own breast, and I shall know.
A fair exchange is no great robbery,
Give me thy heart, for mine belongs to thee.

###951###
Mine eye and heart are at a mortal war,
And each the other's argument denies;
Mine eye doth claim my love's affections are
For outward grace that in thy person lies.
But my poor heart, which deeper truth can see,
Doth swear thy beauty is thy spirit's cast,
A virtuous soul, a mind from malice free,
A constant faith that will for ever last.
And so this fierce and civil strife holds sway,
As each presents its case with jealous plea;
My lovesick self knows not which to obey,
Thy form, thy spirit, or the whole of thee.
Yet this I judge, to end their bitter fray:
Thy whole is what my whole cannot gainsay.

###952###
Now barren winter with his frosty breath
Doth strip the trembling branches of their green,
And clothes the world in pageant of its death,
A stark and melancholy, joyless scene.
The birds are silent, fled to warmer lands,
The sun hangs low, a weak and watery eye,
The brook is captured in icy bands,
Beneath a dull and unforgiving sky.
Such is my soul, when thou art far away,
A frozen waste where no sweet thing can grow;
Thy absence turns my summer to decay,
And on my heart there falls a chilling snow.
But thy return is all the sun I need,
To thaw my grief and plant a joyful seed.

###953###
My humble words, like beggars, poorly dressed,
Cannot do justice to thy sovereign grace,
And every praise that I have yet expressed
Is but a shadow of thy sun-bright face.
I strive to capture with my mortal pen
The heaven that within thine eyes I see,
But all my skill is overthrown, and then
My verse despairs of ever painting thee.
For how can ink describe the morning's light,
Or syllables contain a symphony?
How can a line show forth what is so bright,
And hold thy pure and living majesty?
My art is bankrupt when it comes to you,
And speech falls silent, for my love is true.

###954###
Since I have known the treasure of thy sight,
The world without thee is a worthless thing;
The sun hath lost its power to be bright,
The birds have quite forgot the way to sing.
The fairest flower seems a common weed,
The richest feast tastes but of bitter gall;
On emptiness my hungry senses feed,
When thy sweet presence answers not my call.
All that was pleasure is now tedious grown,
And company is but a lonely chore;
The sweetest music makes a heavy moan,
For thou, the cause of music, art not here.
Thus all is changed and turned to its reverse,
And without thee, my world is but a curse.

###955###
Let not the thought of death make thee afraid,
Nor fear that all our love shall turn to dust;
For when thy mortal form in earth is laid,
My verse shall keep thy memory in trust.
Though monuments of marble fall to naught,
And gilded tombs by envious time are spoiled,
The living monument my pen has wrought
Shall stand unwasted, by no age defiled.
So long as men can read and eyes can see,
Thy name shall live upon this very page,
And future lovers shall be taught by thee
The definition of a golden age.
So fear not death, fordeath can not kill love,
Which shall outlive the stars that shine above.

###956###
The painter's hand, with long and patient toil,
Hath sought thy perfect likeness to portray,
And with his colours mixed with careful oil,
Hath made a counterfeit of thy bright day.
He hath the colour of thy hair so fine,
The curve of lip, the arching of thy brow,
But where is that which makes thy face divine,
The living spirit that I see there now?
He cannot paint the music of thy speech,
Nor capture how thy gentle laughter sounds;
Such inner grace is not within his reach,
His art is bounded by its earthly grounds.
The frame holds but a shadow, cold and weak;
My heart holds thee, of whom I cannot speak.

###957###
My soul is like a ship upon the sea,
Lost in the storm of sorrow and despair,
Whose sails are torn by my calamity,
Whose course is lost to every anxious prayer.
The waves of doubt do crash upon my deck,
The winds of fortune blow me far astray,
I founder near a miserable wreck,
And see no hope of any coming day.
But then thy memory, a distant star,
Doth pierce the clouds of that unholy night,
And though thy presence be from me so far,
It serves me as a constant, guiding light.
So by that star I set my course anew,
And steer my battered, loving heart to you.

###958###
That beauty which thou hold'st in private trust
Should not be squandered by the hand of Time,
But rendered back from thy triumphant dust
In living copies of thy youthful prime.
For Nature's loan must be with interest paid,
Lest thou a harsh and thriftless steward prove,
And in the grave thy perfect form be laid,
Bereft of issue and the fruits of love.
The world deserves another sun like thee,
A living heir thy likeness to renew,
That men in him thy spring-time yet might see,
And praise the stock from whence such beauty grew.
Then do not let thy summer's flower fade,
But make a glass wherein thyself is made.

###959###
What foolishness this love doth make of me,
Who once did boast a clear and sober mind;
Now all my reason is enthralled by thee,
And to thy smallest error I am blind.
My friends do chide, and say I am undone,
That I have lost the man I used to be;
They see the spots upon my glorious sun,
The very spots that are invisible to me.
They speak of logic and of prudent ways,
And bid me cool this fever in my blood,
But I am deaf to all their cautious praise
Of temperance, lost in passion's flood.
So let them call me fool, I'll not repent;
To be thy fool is to be most content.

###960###
When I have said all that my words can say,
And praised thine eyes, thy spirit, and thy face,
And sworn my love is stronger than decay,
And finds in thee its only resting place;
When I have matched thy worth to summer's day,
And found the summer's day to be less fair,
And sworn thy youth shall never pass away,
But live within the lines of my despair;
What more is there for my poor muse to do,
What final truth is left for me to write?
Naught, save to say that all I say is true,
And thou alone art my whole world's light.
For in the end, all poetry is this:
Thy name, thy love, thy memory, thy kiss.

###881###
What envious shadow dogs me now of late,
That makes me question what I hold most dear?
And turns to bitter what was sweet of state,
And whispers treason to my listening ear?
It is that fiend of base and jealous thought,
Which doth with cunning turn a loving eye
To see a fault where nature has none wrought,
And in each simple gesture, treason spy.
My thoughts conspire to put my soul to shame,
And in a paradise, a serpent find,
On thy perfection laying wrongful blame,
Such is the sickness of a poisoned mind.
  Then with thy truth, this cruel torment part,
  And by a vow, secure my wavering heart.

###882###
I see how Time doth carve the fairest face,
And steal the crimson from the rose's bloom,
And bring all youthful glory to disgrace,
Consigning fairness to a winter's tomb.
I see the raven's black in silvered hair,
And how the straightest back is bent with age,
And how all strength must yield unto despair
As life's brief story turns its final page.
And thinking thus, I gaze upon thy youth,
Which now in fullest splendor doth appear,
And see in it a sad and certain truth,
That this perfection cannot tarry here.
  Yet in my verse I'll fight this cruel decree,
  And keep thee ever young for all to see.

###883###
When I am subject to some pleasing sound,
And hear a lute played with a skillful hand,
No true delight within my heart is found,
For I have heard a music more grand.
For when thou speak'st, such is the melody,
That all the art of minstrels I despise;
Thy voice alone is harmony for me,
And other songs are but discordant cries.
Let others praise the birds of spring that sing,
And laud the notes that from their throats do pour;
Thy softest whisper is a richer thing
That I would listen to for evermore.
  So speak again, and all my senses fill,
  For when thou'rt silent, all the world is still.

###884###
This heavy absence I can scarce sustain,
A tedious journey that my soul must bear,
Where every pleasure turns to bitter pain,
And every hope is brother to despair.
The sun itself to me doth dimly shine,
The sweetest banquet hath a tasteless art,
For I am starved of that true food of mine,
The sight of thee that nourishes my heart.
Each passing hour a new torment brings,
A leaden weight upon my spirit laid;
My muse is silent, and no longer sings,
My very substance seems to waste and fade.
  So come to me, and end this weary strife,
  For in thy presence I find all my life.

###885###
Thou art possessed of such a haughty pride,
That in the fortress of thy self-regard,
My faithful love is cruelly pushed aside,
And all my constant service thou'lt discard.
Doth thy own glass so flatter and deceive,
And tell thee tales of beauty so divine,
That my poor heart, which thou dost make to grieve,
Is counted worthless, though it is all thine?
Take heed, for beauty is a fleeting flower,
Which blooms today and on the morrow dies,
And pride will prove a solitary tower
When from thy cheek the youthful colour flies.
  Then who shall love the ruin of thy face?
  Learn pity now, and dignify thy grace.

###886###
Since from my world thy person was removed,
The very colours have begun to fade;
The vibrant green of fields I once had loved
Is but a tiresome and a weary shade.
The sun's bright gold which I did so admire
Now seems a paltry and a common thing,
And in my breast I feel no warming fire,
No joy that all the songs of summer bring.
For thou art pigment, life, and form, and light,
The substance that gives meaning to the view;
Without thy presence, all is endless night,
And nothing in the world seems fair or new.
  So all my world is painted in despair,
  Until thy sight its colours can repair.

###887###
I have a fever for the which no cure
May be prescribed by any doctor's skill;
It is a sickness I must long endure,
Which holds my reason subject to its will.
It makes me tremble when you are not by,
And burns me with a flush when you are near;
It makes me bold, and yet it makes me shy,
And fills my breast with hope and chilling fear.
Some say that love is health unto the soul,
A balm that mends the spirit and the mind,
But this sweet poison takes a heavy toll,
And leaves all sense and soberness behind.
  The cause and cure are seated in your grace;
  My only medicine is thy dear face.

###888###
The mountain stoops, the river leaves its course,
The straightest pine by winter's wind is bent,
And nature proves her momentary force
Is by her own imperfections rent.
The perfect rose conceals a cruel thorn,
The clearest sky by sudden clouds is veiled,
The fairest day gives way to night forlorn,
And even sun and moon have waxed and failed.
But thou art made of some diviner stuff,
A pattern of a form that cannot change,
Where nature, trying, finds it not enough
To match the wonders that in thee arrange.
  So all in nature shows some flaw to me,
  When I compare its meager worth to thee.

###889###
Last night my soul, in slumber's sweet release,
Did fly to thee on wings of happy dream;
My waking sorrows for a time did cease,
And all the world did full of comfort seem.
I held thy hand and looked into thine eyes,
And heard thee speak in accents soft and low;
I lived a lifetime, free of tears and sighs,
And felt a joy my waking cannot know.
But then the mournful morning did appear,
And with its light my paradise was slain;
I woke to find that thou wert nowhere near,
And all my world was solitude and pain.
  So I must sleep, a shadow's love to find,
  And banish day and leave the world behind.

###890###
Why do they look on my devotion so,
And with their judgment my affection chide?
What of my constant, faithful heart they know,
Or of the torments that I bear inside?
They call it folly that I so adore
A being that to them seems only fair,
But did they see what I have seen, and more,
They would not mock my hope nor my despair.
For in thine eyes a universe I see,
And in thy voice, a reason to exist;
My love is not a thing for them to be
The judge of, with a cold and casual twist.
  So let them talk, their words are idle breath;
  My love for thee is stronger far than death.

###891###
Let others praise the jewels of the earth,
The ruby's fire, the sapphire's holy blue;
I find in them a thing of little worth
When I, my love, compare them unto you.
For in thine eyes two living jewels shine,
More radiant than what the mines can yield,
Whose glorious and ever-constant sign
Doth make the light of other gems concealed.
Their captive glint is but a borrowed fire,
A passive thing that from the sun is caught;
Thine eyes do burn with life and with desire,
From some celestial, inner substance wrought.
  So let men hoard their gems of stone and glass,
  Thy living light all earthly wealth surpass.

###892###
Our love, which once was like a hopeful spring,
When buds of promise opened every day,
And like the lark my spirit learned to sing,
Hath seen its summer greenness fall away.
Now autumn's chill is setting in apace,
The winds of doubt blow colder than before,
And I can see within thy changing face
The look of one who loves me now no more.
The leaves of passion, once so rich and red,
Now lie upon the ground in sorry state;
The trustful words we once so freely said
Are prisoners of a cold and silent fate.
  And so I feel the winter of the heart,
  When two who once were one must live apart.

###893###
I do remember well the very place
Where first my eyes on thy perfection fell;
The sun did seem to shine upon thy face,
And cast my mortal soul into a spell.
I stood as one who is of sense deprived,
And could not speak, nor move, nor scarce could breathe,
As in my heart a sudden spring arrived,
And all my thoughts did a new garland wreathe.
From that one moment all my life was changed,
And every purpose I had held before
Was by that vision newly disarranged;
I was not mine, but thine for evermore.
  And though time pass, that vision shall not cease;
  It is my torment, and my only peace.

###894###
My glass doth show the truth I would deny,
That youth's brief reign in me is at an end;
The lines of care about my weary eye
A message of my coming winter send.
My step is slower than it was of yore,
My hair is flecked with traitorous threads of grey,
And I do fear the one whom I adore
Shall look on me and turn her face away.
For how can summer with December dwell?
How can the fresh rose love the withered leaf?
My constant heart a loving tale can tell,
But who will credit age's mournful grief?
  Yet love me for my soul, which knows no age,
  And let my faith turn life's concluding page.

###895###
Why dost thou arm thy lovely face with scorn,
And bend thy brow into a stormy frown?
My world, which of thy smile is newly born,
Is by thy anger cruelly stricken down.
When thou art pleased, my sun doth brightly shine,
And all my heaven is a perfect blue;
All joy and hope and happiness are mine,
And every sorrow I have known, untrue.
But when that cloud upon thy forehead grows,
A sudden tempest in my heart doth rage;
A chilling wind of desolation blows,
And on my soul a dreadful war doth wage.
  Then smile again, and clear my inner skies,
  And kill the storm, or else thy poet dies.

###896###
Thou hast my heart and I, in turn, have thine,
A fair exchange we made some time ago;
I gave thee that which was most truly mine,
And took thy heart so mine should not know woe.
So now within my breast thy own heart beats,
And gives me life and courage to endure,
And in thy chest my faithful heart repeats
A vow to thee, eternally secure.
Then be thou careful what thy deeds impart,
And guard my heart from sorrow and from pain,
For any wound that thou dost give my heart
Is on thyself inflicted back again.
  So cherish me, as I do cherish thee,
  And in our love, let us one being be.

###897###
They say that love is life's most precious sweet,
A honeyed draught that banishes all care;
And yet I find the bitter and sweet meet,
And joy is ever tangled with despair.
Thy presence is a joy so sharp and deep,
It borders on a most exquisite pain,
And in thine absence, when I cannot sleep,
I call on thee and miss thee, all in vain.
Thy kindness makes my hopeful spirit soar,
Thy coldness casts me to the lowest hell;
Each day I love thee, I desire thee more,
A captive held within thy beauty's spell.
  So is this love a prison or a prize?
  The answer lies within thy fateful eyes.

###898###
Because my verse doth paint you as divine,
And clothes your name in phrases rich and bold,
You think my words are but a flattering sign,
A story by a loving poet told.
You say that ink can make the common, fair,
And wit can fashion praise from things of nought,
That my affection, born of empty air,
Is but a fiction that my pen has wrought.
But, love, my verse is but a shadow poor
Of that true light that in your being lies;
It cannot capture, nor can it secure,
The living wonder that is in thine eyes.
  My words do fail, my humble art is weak;
  I write of less than what I cannot speak.

###899###
Let those who hunger after worldly things
Pursue the favour of a fickle court,
And learn the sorrow that ambition brings,
To find their triumph is both cruel and short.
Let merchants risk their fortunes on the sea
To gain the wealth that moth and rust devour;
Such brittle treasures are not meant for me,
Who seek the comfort of a single hour.
For one soft look from thy beloved eye,
One gentle word from thy most cherished voice,
Is richer far than all the sums that lie
In misers' chests, and makes my soul rejoice.
  I am a king, possessing this great prize,
  And all my kingdom is within thine eyes.

###900###
If all my suffering be but sport to thee,
And thou dost take a pleasure in my pain,
Then let my tears a tragic drama be
For thy delight, again and yet again.
If thy stern heart finds music in my sighs,
Then I will furnish thee with mournful tunes,
And look on thee with sorrow-laden eyes
Beneath the cold and solitary moons.
I ask for pity, but if that's denied,
And cruelty is all that thou canst give,
Then in that humour I will still abide
And for thy strange amusement, I will live.
  But know this truth, when all the play is done:
  The heart that thou hast broken was thine own.

###981###
When Phoebus shows his glorious morning light,
I see no wonder in his golden grace,
Nor praise the moon that governs o'er the night,
For both are shadows to thy beauteous face.
The sweetest music that a lute can sound,
The scent of roses when the dew is sweet,
Is but a noise upon this common ground,
Compared to thee, who mak'st my world complete.
Let misers hoard the treasures they may hold,
And princes boast of their commanding art,
Thy love to me is more than finest gold,
And writes its law upon my captive heart.
So all the world's a bauble without thee,
Whose presence is my sole prosperity.

###982###
Whilst thou, my love, art leagues and miles away,
The heavy hours of day do creep so slow,
What should be brief becomes a year-long day,
And every joy is turn'd to leaden woe.
I search in every crowd thy face to see,
And call thy name in hopeful, yet vain, prayer;
But where I look, there thou canst never be,
Which but renews my solitary care.
My thoughts like frantic birds to thee do fly,
To tell the grief that tears my soul apart,
Yet they return from an unpitying sky,
With no good news to pacify my heart.
For naught can mend this sorrow but thy sight,
Which is my sun, my comfort, and my light.

###983###
When thy dear voice doth bless the common air,
The birds fall silent, envying thy speech,
For such a sound does make all coarse things fair,
And by its music, doth the angels teach.
I count that hour lost I do not hear
That gentle tone which all my senses bound;
All other melodies are not so dear
As this which in thy perfect throat is found.
My ear, thus favour'd, bids my heart be still,
To drink the nectar of thy passing will,
And every phrase, each sweet and casual note,
Is richer than what any poet wrote.
For in thy voice there is a wondrous art,
That both commands and captivates my heart.

###984###
That thievish Time with his relentless hand
Doth steal the bloom from every living thing,
And what seems strong shall not his power withstand,
Nor 'scape the ruin that his hours will bring.
Behold the monuments of kings, how they
Are toppled down and mingled with the dust;
The proudest beauty has its final day,
And iron yields to his unpitying rust.
This law of loss in all things I perceive,
In brazen towers and in the tender leaf,
And know thy youth, which now seems past compare,
Must one day vanish in the common air.
Yet this my verse against his rage shall stand,
And keep thy name immortal in the land.

###985###
My liberty is forfeit to thine eye,
From which no thought of freedom can take root;
I am thy thrall, and cannot pass thee by,
But follow thee as shadow follows foot.
Thine every glance a golden fetter is,
That binds my will and makes it wholly thine;
I have no joy but what is found in this
Sweet servitude, this bondage so divine.
Some men for kingdoms do their freedom sell,
Or for the pride of some great worldly prize,
But in the happy prison of thy gaze I dwell,
And count as naught all other liberties.
My only wish is ne'er to be set free,
For being bound to you is life to me.

###986###
When I in quiet contemplation sit,
And reckon all the virtues thou dost own,
My meager worth doth make me to admit
I am a soil where no such seeds are sown.
What have I done that fortune should be kind,
And grant to me a prize of such account?
There is no merit in my heart or mind,
Which to the height of thy desert could mount.
My state is poor, my reputation weak,
My verse a trifle, and my wit but small;
And yet thy love for me doth kindly speak,
And by its grace doth overlook it all.
So I must think thy love is freely given,
A random blessing from a partial heaven.

###987###
When icicles on winter's beard do cling,
And frosted fields lie silent and forlorn,
The barren trees of no sweet bird do sing,
But stand like ghosts to mock the rising morn.
This chilling scene, this portrait of decay,
Doth put me in the mind of thy remove;
For in thine absence, what is my bright day
But a cold season, emptied of all love?
The sun itself doth seem to lack its heat,
The wind complains with a more bitter sound,
And all the world, which once I found so sweet,
Is but a waste and melancholy ground.
Thou art my summer, and until I see
Thy face again, 'tis winter still for me.

###988###
When summer's friends, like swallows, take their flight
At the first sign of winter's coming storm,
And flattering tongues that prais'd thee in the light
No longer keep thy memory so warm;
When fortune's tide, which bore thee in its swell,
Begins to ebb and leave thee on the shore,
And those who vow'd they lov'd thy soul so well
Do knock upon thy closed gate no more:
Then shalt thou know the substance of my heart,
Which, like a star, in darkness shows its face,
And from thy side shall never wish to part,
But finds in ruin its most proper place.
For what is gold may in the fire be prov'd,
And what is love is best in sorrow lov'd.

###989###
When gentle sleep hath closed my wakeful eyes,
And veil'd the world in darkness from my head,
Thy lovely phantom doth in dreams arise,
And stands in grace beside my lonely bed.
Thou com'st to me in silence of the night,
A blessed vision, perfect and so clear,
That for a moment, sorrow takes its flight,
And all my soul is freed from doubt and fear.
But when the dawn, that cruel messenger,
Doth call me back to day's reality,
I wake to find that thou art not brought near,
But art a ghost of my own memory.
And so I pray for night to come again,
To find in dreams what waking life makes vain.

###990###
Thy two fair eyes are my definitive book,
Wherein I read the whole of my estate;
By their decree, my spirit is mistook,
Or by their light, my soul is made full great.
Should they but cast on me a look of scorn,
My fainting heart a killing frost doth find;
Then do I curse the day that I was born,
And rail against my fortune so unkind.
But if they shine with pity or with grace,
Then am I lifted to the highest sphere,
And think there is no more a blessed place
Than where your heavenly countenance is near.
So hangs my life on what thy looks decree,
They are my stars, and rule my destiny.

###991###
Let not the world's dull censure on me fall
Because my love for thee doth seem too much;
The tongues of men are ever prone to call
That thing a fault which their slow hearts can't touch.
They measure love by reason's careful rule,
And weigh affection in a banker's scale;
They call the constant heart a willing fool,
And at the vows of truest lovers rail.
But we, whose souls a higher knowledge share,
Should not be sway'd by their ungentle sound;
Their bitter words are but as empty air,
On which no stable friendship can be found.
So let them talk, and what they please, construe;
My world's opinion matters less than you.

###992###
When envious Time shall gnaw upon thy grace,
And forty winters shall besiege thy brow,
And dig deep trenches in thy beauty's face,
Making what's smooth and perfect, crooked now;
When that bright fire that in thine eyes doth dwell
Shall burn more dimly with the spent supply,
And youthful blood, which now rebels so well,
Shall in a colder, tamer current lie:
Then in this verse, thy beauty, still in youth,
Shall live anew, beyond Time's cruel blade,
Preserving for the coming age thy truth,
Whose summer's bloom shall not be seen to fade.
So shall you live, when you to dust are gone,
And find your life in these same lines anon.

###993###
I see the praise another's verse doth give,
And watch you read it with a thoughtful smile,
And in that moment, I no longer live,
But am a wretch, tormented by my guile.
For jealous thoughts, those tyrants of the mind,
Do whisper that your favour turns from me;
I search your face, some chilling sign to find,
And in your kindness, read but cruelty.
Each gentle word you speak to someone's name
Is as a dart that finds its mark in me;
My fond affection turns to frantic flame,
And robs my reason of its sovereignty.
Yet this my torment I must keep unseen,
Lest you should know how foolish I have been.

###994###
My love for thee is like a fever's heat,
That burns my veins and will not let me rest;
I have no taste for what I drink or eat,
And by this strange affliction am possess'd.
The learned doctors, with their solemn care,
Can find no physic for my malady;
They bid me take my comfort in the air,
But this advice brings no relief to me.
For thou, the cause of this my burning state,
Art also that which might the cure provide;
One kindly look from thee can change my fate,
And call the raging fever to subside.
So be my doctor, and my sickness quell,
Or by thy coldness, ring my funeral bell.

###995###
Why art thou arm'd with such a cruel heart,
That takes a pleasure in thy lover's pain?
Dost thou not see my sorrow's bleeding smart,
Or hear the loyal voice that doth complain?
Thy words are marble, and thy glance is steel,
That strikes me down, yet will not let me die;
Thou hast no pity for the wounds I feel,
Nor wilt thou answer my lamenting cry.
Is it thy pride to see a true man bow'd,
And beg for mercy that is ne'er in sight?
To be the sun that's hidden by a cloud,
And show a glimpse, then steal away the light?
Then kill me quite, and let my torment cease,
And in the grave, my soul may find its peace.

###996###
I do remember well that blessed day
When first my eyes did look upon thy face;
It was, I think, in the sweet month of May,
When all the world was fill'd with Nature's grace.
The sun shone brighter than it had before,
The birds did seem to sing a sweeter tune,
And in my heart a feeling, new and pure,
Was born beneath the friendly afternoon.
From that one moment all my life was chang'd,
And every thought reordered by thy sight;
My former self became to me estrang'd,
Illuminated by thy sudden light.
And though the years may steal all else away,
My mind shall keep the treasure of that day.

###997###
My glass doth show the progress of the years,
And writes the story of my life's decline;
It shows the truth I harbour in my fears,
That youthful grace no longer can be mine.
For time hath drawn upon my face his map,
With weary lines of sorrow and of thought,
And set me in the winter of life's lap,
Whose cheerless lessons have been dearly bought.
But when I think upon my love for thee,
Which feels no winter and which knows no age,
It seems a thing from time's dominion free,
That scorns the numbers on life's turning page.
And so a youth within my heart I find,
Which keeps me young in spirit and in mind.

###998###
Ask not why beauty doth in thee reside,
Or why thy voice is music to the ear;
One might as well the sun and planets chide,
Or ask why stars at eventide appear.
The rose is not instructed how to grow,
Nor is the lark in his high singing taught;
Some things by nature's law their virtue show,
And are to their perfection simply wrought.
So thou wert made to be a perfect sight,
A living pattern of what is most fair,
A constant source of wonder and delight,
Beyond all reason and beyond compare.
By this truth only is my love defined:
Thou art thyself, the fairest of thy kind.

###999###
My weary pen, which I so often use
To paint the world in colours of my own,
For any other subject doth refuse
To write a word, or make its purpose known.
For thou, my love, art my perpetual theme,
The single thought from which all others grow;
Thou art the substance of my waking dream,
The only knowledge that I seek to know.
If I should try of battles or of kings,
Or of the turning seasons, to endite,
My verse betrays me, and of thee it sings,
Drawn to thy beauty as the moth to light.
And so on every page thy name I find,
The loving tyrant of my captive mind.

###1000###
When this my life hath run its fated course,
And my last breath is mingled with the air,
Let no man say my love hath lost its force,
Or was a passion built on light despair.
For it was not a fancy of the spring,
A fleeting fever of a youthful day,
But the one truth to which my soul could cling,
When all things else were doom'd to swift decay.
Believe it then, when I am turn'd to dust,
My love shall live, beyond the final end,
A testament of an unwavering trust,
Which death itself is powerless to bend.
So think of me as one who, from time's shore,
Sent forth a love that lasts for evermore.

###961###
When worldly cares do cast a dreadful shade,
And all my hopes in disarray I see,
My thoughts to thee, my constant star, are swayed,
And find that darkness hath no power o'er me.
For in the peerless lines of thy sweet face,
A tale of truer worth than pearl or gold,
My weary mind doth find a resting place,
And by that vision makes my spirit bold.
Let envious Time, who steals all youthful art,
With wrinkled hand steal from the rose its hue;
It cannot reach the fortress of my heart,
Wherein thy beauty is forever new.
So what care I for what the world may find,
When thou alone art sun unto my mind?

###962###
When I with this, my humble, inky pen,
Do seek to chronicle thy precious name,
I strive against the memory of men,
To light for thee an everlasting flame.
For monuments of brass are turned to dust,
And tombs of kings are left to stand alone;
The conquering sword is overcome by rust,
And glory's words are worn away from stone.
But in the living measure of this verse,
Thy virtue and thy beauty shall live still,
Which future ages fondly will rehearse,
When envious Time hath worked his cruel will.
My love in these black marks shall ever be,
For all the world in ages hence to see.

###963###
How can a face that seems so passing fair
Be but a mask to hide a fickle heart?
How canst thou breathe sweet nothings on the air,
And of thy former promise play no part?
The words like stars did glitter in thine eyes
And made me think thy protestations real,
But now I see 'twas but a sweet disguise,
Which thy dissembling nature did conceal.
That solemn oath, that deep and sacred vow,
Which thou didst pledge was ever to be true,
Doth sit like shame upon thy smiling brow,
As thou dost turn thy fancy to the new.
So be it, love; find thou some new-won other,
Whilst I in grief my former self will smother.

###964###
Since thou, my comfort, didst depart from here,
The world to my sad eyes hath lost its sight;
The sun provides no warmth, the sky no cheer,
And day itself is shadowed like the night.
I hear no music in the warbling sound
Of birds that once did make the morning ring;
The flowers lie like weeds upon the ground,
And all the joy is gone from everything.
The busy works of men, a frantic race,
Are but a pageant that I cannot see;
I find no solace in this weary place,
Since all my world had its whole worth in thee.
So let this endless winter hold its reign,
Until thy coming makes things new again.

###965###
When in my glass I look, I see with dread
A stranger's face that stares right back at me;
With sad surprise I view that hoary head
And wonder how so old I came to be.
For Time hath carved upon my weary brow
The cruel map of all my anxious fears,
And where youth’s blossom sat upon the bough,
Now sit the sorrows of the weeping years.
Each silver thread that in my hair doth grow
A testament to some past grief appears,
A reckoning of debts that I do owe
To fortune's rage, and to my silent tears.
This thieving age hath stolen all my youth,
And left me naught but this most bitter truth.

###966###
I grant that music hath a pleasing sound
And moves the soul to heights before unknown;
I grant that sweetest harmony is found
When skilful fingers o'er the lute are thrown.
The poet's well-wrought line may catch the ear,
The singer's voice may make the spirit rise,
A chorus sung in unison and clear
May seem a foretaste of some paradise.
But all these pleasures, measured and compared
To the soft cadence of thy gentle voice,
Are but as noise, their artistry ensnared,
Beside the perfect music of my choice.
For when thou speak’st, all other sounds are dim,
As is a candle to the sun's bright rim.

###967###
My love is not like to the fleeting rose
That in a summer's month doth bloom and die,
Nor like the stream that, when the tempest blows,
Doth rage and swell, and then runs foul or dry.
It is no bauble for a wanton's hand,
To be held dear and then be cast aside,
But rather like the firm and solid land,
That doth eternally and fixed abide.
Though you yourself, possessed by some strange art,
Should from our bond and my affection stray,
And play the tyrant with my constant heart,
To wound my trust and turn your face away:
Yet shall my love, though you prove false to it,
True to itself, upon its rock still sit.

###968###
That I am scorned and held in thy disdain
Is plain to see, and felt with every breath;
To sue for grace is labour all in vain,
And every hope is put to certain death.
Thy glances, which were once my sun and shield,
Are now as sharp as any winter frost,
And that dear heart, which I had thought to wield,
Is to my cause and my affection lost.
So must I learn to live with this new pain,
And teach my tongue to speak no more thy praise,
And school my eyes to look on thee again
As one looks on the sun's indifferent rays.
But oh, this lesson is too hard to learn,
For still to thee my foolish passions burn.

###969###
So oft I try to banish thee from mind,
To break the chain that holds my will in thrall,
And in some other, lesser pleasure find
A brief respite from standing at thy call.
I seek the solace of a learned book,
Or walk amongst the busy, common crowd,
Or on the page of patient nature look;
But thee I find in every sun and cloud.
Thy face appears in every stranger's glance,
Thy voice I hear in every passing word,
Each fleeting moment and each twist of chance
Doth bring to mind a memory adored.
So all my efforts are to no avail,
My thoughts of thee are both my ship and sail.

###970###
If ever thou shouldst read these words I write
When I am dust, and this my hand is still,
Know that thy beauty was my only light,
The sovereign ruler of my captive will.
And if some critic with a peevish eye
Should say my lines do lack in subtle art,
Or that my tired metaphors run dry,
Pay thou no mind; 'twas written from the heart.
For I was no great scholar of the schools,
Nor sought the bays of Pindus or of Rome;
I only broke love's sweet and bitter rules,
And found in thee my spirit's final home.
So let them cavil; all I wished to prove,
Was how a mortal heart could truly love.

###971###
Why didst thou make me promise of the sun,
And show me what a golden age might be,
If all thy glorious giving were undone
And thou didst mean to keep it all from me?
Why didst thou lift me to so great a height,
Upon the wings of thine assured grace,
To cast me down into an endless night,
And hide from me the comfort of thy face?
'Twere better ne'er to know the taste of wine
Than sip it once and then for want to pine.
'Twere better ne'er to see the day's clear shine
Than, having seen, to lose what once was mine.
And thus thy gift, which seemed a thing divine,
Is but a curse that makes all sorrow mine.

###972###
The tyrant Time, with his swift, greedy pace,
Doth steal the blush from beauty's softest cheek,
And leave a ruin in that sacred place
Where once the eyes of all a lodging seek.
He bends the back that once stood proud and straight,
He dims the eye that once did brightly burn,
And on a kingdom brings a woeful state,
From whose sad shores no traveller may return.
But thy perfection, which my verse doth hold,
Shall stand against his siege and ruinous rage;
Thou art the story that shall grow not old,
But find new youth upon this humble page.
And so preserved, thou shalt outlive his crime,
And make a mockery of fleeting Time.

###973###
Let me not to the marriage of our minds
Admit the charge of mutability;
For what the world in its rough passage finds,
Should not affect what is 'twixt thee and me.
For our true bond is not of flesh and blood,
Which time and sickness surely will betray,
Nor built on fortunes, which like to a flood,
May raise us high, and then may wash away.
It is a union of the soul's own choice,
A covenant that reason cannot break,
A silent promise that needs not a voice,
Which for no earthly cause will I forsake.
So let the seasons alter as they must,
I'll love thee still, when I am turned to dust.

###974###
When I am dead, my dearest, weep not long,
Nor let my memory be a cause for pain;
But if you will, recall me in a song,
As one who loved, and did not love in vain.
Do not erect for me a marble tomb,
Nor write my name in letters of bright gold,
For in your living heart I shall find room,
A better monument than bought or sold.
Let not the world's performative display
Of formal grief shadow thy spirit's light;
Mourn for a moment, then go on thy way,
And let thy future be both full and bright.
For this my love's last testament shall be:
Thy happiness is dearer than all me.

###975###
I see the way that others watch thine eye,
And linger long upon thy matchless grace,
And wish that they might ever stand so nigh
To know the perfect beauty of thy face.
And when for them thou dost bestow a smile,
A common courtesy, both sweet and kind,
My thoughts are filled with jealousy and guile,
Such wretched fancies poison my poor mind.
To share thy light with others is a blight
That brings my jealous heart a cruel pain,
For I alone would keep thee in my sight
And have thy favour, again and yet again.
Forgive this sin, that I would have thee be
A treasure looked upon by none but me.

###976###
Thou art the book wherein I strive to read
The secret lore of beauty, truth, and grace,
And from thy pages my poor spirit feed,
And all the lines of heaven in thee trace.
The preface is the glory of thy brow,
Thine eyes the text where meaning doth reside,
Thy speech the argument, that shows me how
True virtue can with loveliness abide.
But oh, this volume is too rich for me,
Its subtle sense I cannot comprehend;
I am a scholar of a low degree,
Who fears to read his study to the end.
So I but gaze upon the cover fine,
And wish the wisdom held therein were mine.

###977###
What power is this that in thine eyes doth dwell,
That can at once both wound me and make whole?
What subtle magic and what potent spell
Can thus command the fortress of my soul?
One careless glance can plunge me in despair,
And make me feel the world's most wretched man;
One gentle smile can banish all my care,
And right the wrongs since first the world began.
I am no more the master of my will,
A willing captive to thy sweet decree;
When thou art pleased, my restless heart is still,
When thou art cross, all's out of joint for me.
Thus in the compass of thy moods I live,
And take what joy or sorrow thou dost give.

###978###
This world a stage is, as the poet told,
Wherein we play our parts and fret our hour,
And some are young and some are bent and old,
And some have fame, whilst others live obscure.
My role hath been to be the constant friend,
