The faithful courtier to his monarch's fame,
Who on thy glory solely doth depend,
And seeks no honour but to speak thy name.
My speeches are the praises I rehearse,
My actions but to serve thy least command,
My only costume is this humble verse,
The loyalest servant in this troubled land.
And when the final curtain for me falls,
Thy name shall be the last my tongue recalls.

###979###
My hungry eyes, which can no more behold
The living presence of thy blessed face,
Must feed upon a memory grown cold,
And find thee wanting in this empty place.
My ears, that feasted on thy honeyed words,
Now starve in silence, or hear jarring sound;
No song delights me from the summer birds,
No consolation in my grief is found.
My hands, that once were granted leave to touch
The sacred temple of thy gentle hand,
Now find all other earthly textures much
Like coarsest cloth, or rough and barren sand.
Thus all my senses, starved of their true food,
Languish and die in this sad solitude.

###980###
As some poor ship, by cruel tempest tossed,
Which sees its guiding star obscured by night,
Believes itself to be entirely lost,
And longs in vain for morning's hopeful light;
So is my soul, when you are far from me,
Assailed by doubts and buffeted by fears,
Cast on the waves of a relentless sea,
And sees no landmark through a flood of tears.
But when your image in my mind appears,
A sudden calm upon the waters grows,
The storm recedes, the gloomy darkness clears,
And in my heart a gentle sea-breeze blows.
For you alone are my fixed northern star,
By which I steer, however tossed afar.

###1001###
Why dost thou arm thy beauty with such scorn,
And make thine eyes the captains of my pain?
My heart, by their sharp cruelty thus torn,
Doth plead for peace, but finds its pleading vain.
Why should a face that heaven itself did mould,
Wherein all graces have their dwelling-place,
Be to thy servant's love so stern and cold,
And banish pity from its perfect space?
Oh, think what power thou dost in triumph waste,
A tyrant's power, when mercy were more great;
Lest time, that makes all comely things in haste
To fade, should bring thy sorrows all too late.
   For if no love in thy cold breast doth live,
   How canst thou hope that others love will give?

###1002###
When I behold the work of painters' hands,
That strive to catch the semblance of a face,
Or statues carved in far and foreign lands
Of some dead queen, renowned for her grace,
I find their art a shadow and a lie,
A weak attempt, a forgery of truth;
For next to thy fair form, their skills but die,
And mock the perfect flower of thy youth.
What chisel could thy living spirit trace?
What pigment could thy sun-bright virtue show?
Their craft is bound to form, and time, and place,
But from thy soul doth ceaseless beauty flow.
   So let them boast their works that cannot feel;
   Thy living worth is all that I know real.

###1003###
As some poor merchant, who hath lost his all
To the rough tempest and the greedy sea,
Doth sit and weep, remembering his fall
From fortune's height to barren misery,
So I, bereft of thy sweet company,
Do count my losses in the lonely night,
And find my state is bankrupt, wanting thee,
Who art the sun of all my worldly light.
My thoughts, like ledgers, reckon up the cost
Of every hour I am from thee apart,
And every entry shows me what is lost,
The boundless treasure of a happy heart.
   Thus in my mind thy absence I account,
   And find my grief a sum I cannot count.

###1004###
That I am jealous, do not thou complain,
For where a treasure of such worth is found,
What man would not be vexed with fearful pain,
Lest thieves should break into that sacred ground?
Thou art my Indies, rich with golden store,
My kingdom, and my hope of all renown;
And should I not keep watch upon the shore,
And guard the walls that compass such a town?
Forgive my doubting fear, it is not bred
From any fault I have in thee espied,
But from my own self-love, which is instead
A love of thee, where all my loves abide.
   My fear is born of how thy worth doth shine,
   And my own terror I were not thought thine.

###1005###
When in the chronicle of wasted time
I read of ladies fair and knights of old,
Whose beauty was the subject of all rhyme,
In stories that the ancient bards have told,
I see their virtues were a prophecy
Of the fair pattern that in thee we find;
Their raven hair, their lips, their peerless eye,
Were but a foretaste of thy face and mind.
All that was praised in ages passed away,
The truth, the grace, the honour, and the art,
Were scattered hints of this more perfect day,
And found their single home within thy heart.
   So all old praise doth but describe anew,
   What ancient poets guessed, and I found true.

###1006###
The forward violet, why doth it so haste
To show its purple to the early spring?
Because it knows its beauty cannot last,
And fears the ruin that the suns will bring.
The blushing rose, that opens to the noon,
Believes its brief and glorious hour a prize,
But sees its petals scattered all too soon,
When under evening's breath it droops and dies.
So is it with the flower of thy face,
Which holds its reign in nature's mortal show;
It fears the theft of time, the slow disgrace
That bids the fairest things of all to go.
   But in my verse thy summer shall not end,
   For here thy grace shall with the ages blend.

###1007###
My tongue is sworn to silence, and my pen
Hath made a vow to write of thee no more,
To join the ranks of common, thoughtless men,
And cease the worship that my soul forswore.
For what avails my praise, when every glance
Of thy bright eye doth gain a thousand hearts?
My humble verse can thy state not advance,
Which of itself such wondrous light imparts.
But oh, this vow is traitor to my soul,
Which lives and breathes but to extol thy name;
My mind rebels against this harsh control,
And burns to publish its undying flame.
   So vows are wind, when love the promise makes;
   A heart in love its own firm promise breaks.

###1008###
Oft have I seen a player on a stage,
Who, by his art, doth feign a mighty grief,
And for a painted sorrow, weep and rage,
And move beholders to a sad belief.
He mourns a Hecuba he never knew,
His tears are false, his passion is for hire;
And yet he makes his acted sorrow true,
And sets the very theatre on fire.
If he for nothing can such feeling show,
What, then, should I, who have a truer cause?
Whose heart by thy neglect is brought so low,
And breaks itself against love's cruel laws.
   Yet I stand mute, while players' speech doth flow,
   For my true pain no outward sign can show.

###1009###
Let not the world, with its deceitful grace,
Persuade thy heart that better love can be
Than what is written plainly on my face,
A map of my soul's loyalty to thee.
For others flatter with a silver tongue,
And speak of passions that they do not feel,
A fleeting song, for but a moment sung,
Whose empty notes a hollow heart reveal.
My love is plain, and hath not learned the art
To dress its meaning in a veiled disguise;
It hath no language but a faithful heart,
And finds its truest speech within mine eyes.
   Then trust this book, where my devotion's penned,
   Whose every look cries out, "I am thy friend."

###1010###
Weary with toil, I haste me to my bed,
The dear repose for limbs with travel tired;
But then begins a journey in my head,
To work my mind, when body's work's expired:
For then my thoughts, from far where I abide,
Intend a zealous pilgrimage to thee,
And keep my drooping eyelids open wide,
Looking on darkness which the blind do see:
Save that my soul's imaginary sight
Presents thy shadow to my sightless view,
Which, like a jewel hung in ghastly night,
Makes black night beauteous, and her old face new.
   Lo, thus, by day my limbs, by night my mind,
   For thee, and for myself, no quiet find.

###1011###
If music be the food that lovers eat,
Then thy sweet voice is a celestial feast,
More rich than any harmony complete
That e'er from viol or from lute increased.
When thou dost speak, the birds suspend their song,
As if ashamed of their own meagre sound;
The winds fall silent as you pass along,
And listening stillness holds the air profound.
But when in anger thy stern voice doth rise,
It is a tempest to my sailor's soul,
A dreadful thunder from unkindly skies,
That o'er my hopes with wrecking fury rolls.
   Then speak but gently, lest thy words destroy
   The very heart that lives upon their joy.

###1012###
Against that time, if ever that time come,
When I shall see thy proud and youthful day
To age's tyranny at last succumb,
And all thy morning glories fade away;
When silver threads shall in thy gold appear,
And trenches deep shall in thy beauty's field,
When that straight back which all men hold so dear
Shall to the heavy weight of years then yield;
Against that time, my love shall be prepared,
A fortress strong against decay's assault,
And in my verse thy youth shall be declared,
As free from wrinkle, blemish, or from fault.
   So time may steal the treasure from my sight,
   But not my love, nor memory's clear light.

###1013###
That which is broken can be made anew,
And severed friendships may again unite,
A winter branch may blossom in the dew,
And darkest clouds may yield to sunlit light.
The ship that harsh misfortune runs aground
May be repaired to sail the seas once more;
The deepest wound, in time, may be made sound,
And health return where sickness was before.
But trust, once shattered by a careless word,
Or by the poison of a doubtful thought,
Is like a glass whose pieces, finely stirred,
Can ne'er again to perfect form be brought.
   So guard thy faith, for should that treasure fall,
   No art on earth its wholeness can recall.

###1014###
These humble lines, which I for thee have traced,
Are but poor actors for a theme so grand;
My meagre words are shamefully misplaced,
When of thy boundless virtue they command.
My wit is bankrupt and my muse is dry,
And inspiration fails me at my need;
The more to capture thee my verses try,
The more I find my clumsy efforts bleed.
For how can ink describe the morning's sun?
Or mortal speech a holy thought convey?
The task's too great, and ere it is begun,
My fainting senses falter and give way.
   And yet I write, as if compelled by fate,
   To show my love, if not to show thy state.

###1015###
Shall I compare my love to Fortune's wheel,
That lifts a beggar to a monarch's throne?
Nay, for that turning orb doth oft reveal
How quickly pride may be again o'erthrown.
Or shall I liken it to summer's heat,
That warms the earth and makes the fields to grow?
Nay, for that season's glory is too fleet,
And yields too soon to winter's frost and snow.
My love is not a thing of chance or change,
Nor subject to the seasons' passing whim;
It finds the course of nature passing strange,
And burns more bright as other lights grow dim.
   It is a star, that in its constant sphere,
   Doth feel no turning of the changing year.

###1016###
Thou art my mirror, and in thee I see
The man I was, before the world's despite
Had stamped its lines of sorrow upon me,
And dimmed the lustre of my spirit's light.
When I behold thy pure and hopeful gaze,
Untouched by envy, undismayed by fear,
I do remember my own younger days,
And all I lost seems for a moment dear.
But then the glass doth show my features worn,
A sad reflection of the man I am,
And by the contrast I am made forlorn,
A dying ember to thy perfect flame.
   So in thine eyes a double truth I find:
   The joy I had, and what I left behind.

###1017###
How like a fever is my love for thee,
Which feeds upon the thing that makes it ill,
And in a frenzied quest for remedy,
Doth lose its reason and its guiding will.
One moment I am lifted to the sky
Upon the hope of thy returned affection;
The next, in depths of dark despair I lie,
And curse my heart for its own weak subjection.
This sickness turns my nourishment to gall,
My peace to torment, and my rest to strife;
My thoughts are traitors, holding me in thrall
To one who is the mistress of my life.
   Thus do I live, in this distempered state,
   And love the cause of my most wretched fate.

###1018###
The little bee, that sips from every flower,
And gathers sweetness through the summer's day,
Doth not enjoy a more delightful hour,
Nor from his toil finds richer sweet to pay,
Than I, when I upon thy virtues feed,
And draw my thoughts from thy unending store;
Thy mind's a garden, and thy every deed
A perfumed blossom I can but adore.
One word of thine is honeycomb to me,
A glance, a nectar that my soul doth crave;
In thy fair presence is such rarity,
As from all other, baser foods can save.
   So let me be the bee, and thou the bloom,
   And I shall feast, and fear no winter's gloom.

###1019###
When to the court of my own silent thought
I summon up the ghosts of friendship's past,
And see what ruin time and tide have wrought
On bonds I swore forevermore would last,
I grieve for those whom death hath snatched away,
And weep for others, by false fortune changed,
Who in the sunshine of a brighter day
Have from my poor and humble state estranged.
Then can I mourn for sorrows long since gone,
And feel afresh the pain of old offense,
And heavily from woe to woe tell on
The sad account of my life's great expense.
   But if the while I think on thee, dear friend,
   All losses are restored, and sorrows end.

###1020###
What is this life, if not a debt we owe
To churlish Time, that lends us fleeting breath?
We strut and fret in a most garish show,
And at the end, our wage is only death.
He is a banker who demands his due,
And takes the fairest and the best away;
Nor youth, nor strength, nor titles old or new,
Can bribe that tyrant for a moment's stay.
But love, my love, is not within his power,
It is a coin of some immortal mint,
That doth not lessen with the passing hour,
But brighter shines, and shows a heavenly tint.
   So let Time take my youth, and all my days,
   My love for thee shall mock his vain assays.

###1121###
When I do look upon thy wondrous face,
No painter's skill, no sculptor's patient hand,
Could half so well describe that heavenly grace
That holds the truest beauty in the land.
The sun itself, in its most proud array,
Doth borrow from thy eyes its morning light,
And jealous clouds do steal the gold away
To mend the tattered garments of the night.
Thy voice, a music that the birds might learn,
Doth silence envy in the listening ear,
And in my soul, thy constant virtues burn,
To banish every doubt and every fear.
   So let all other worldly treasures be,
   For I have found my only wealth in thee.

###1122###
The weary clock doth mock my sleepless head,
And chides the hours that pass so very slow,
Since thou art from my longing vision fled,
And left me here companion to my woe.
I call thy name into the vacant air,
And think a phantom echo makes reply;
I shape thy form from shadows of despair,
And with a tearful breath I teach it sigh.
My lonely chamber, now a prison cell,
Doth hold me captive to a sad desire,
Where mournful thoughts in endless number dwell,
And feed upon my heart's consuming fire.
   But when I dream, to me thou dost return,
   And for that peace my waking soul doth yearn.

###1123###
When Fortune's spite hath laid my state so low,
And friends, like summer birds, have taken wing,
Upon the barren ground of all my woe,
Thy constant love, a gentle flower, doth spring.
Where others scorn, thy pity lends a balm,
Where others mock, thy voice doth speak me fair;
Thy presence is for me a holy psalm
That stills the tempest of my own despair.
Should all the world conspire to do me wrong,
And with false tongues my character defame,
Thy faith in me doth make my spirit strong,
And clears the tarnish from my humble name.
   So let them rage, and let their venom fly,
   Thy love's my shield, beneath whose strength I lie.

###1124###
That I am scorned by men of high degree,
Who count my worth by what my coffers hold,
Is but a trifle and means nought to me,
Who trades not love for silver or for gold.
They boast of lineage and of noble birth,
Of titles won by some forgotten deed,
And measure all the souls upon this earth
By what they own, or what their station need.
But I possess a treasure far more rare,
A prize beyond the reach of any king:
The sacred vow of one so true and fair,
Whose praise alone doth make my spirit sing.
   Let them have coin, and lands, and jewelled crown,
   Thy love's my wealth, my fame, and my renown.

###1125###
My tongue is bankrupt when it tries to speak
The sum of all the beauty I behold;
The very words I choose are far too weak,
A story that can never be half told.
I seek a metaphor in sun and moon,
And rob the stars of their celestial fire,
But find my verse is out of step, and soon
Gives up the ghost of its absurd desire.
To say thine eyes are bright, is to say nought,
To call thy voice sweet music, is a lie,
For thou art far beyond what can be taught,
Or captured by the pen of such as I.
   And so I cease, and let my silence prove
   What feeble words can never say of love.

###1126###
Why dost thou spend thy beauty on the proud,
Who see thy face and praise it with their lips,
But in their hearts, a cold and selfish cloud
Doth cause thy sun to suffer an eclipse?
Why lend thy wit to those who cannot hear
The subtle music of thy gentle soul,
Who keep thy body, but not thy spirit, near,
And seek a part when they should have the whole?
Thy worth is wasted in their shallow gaze,
A priceless gem upon a barren shore,
A perfect rose in some forgotten maze,
Whose scent is lost, to be revived no more.
   Oh, come to me, where thou art understood,
   And find a home for all that's fair and good.

###1127###
Time, that old thief, with pilfering, greedy hand,
Doth steal the blush from every maiden's cheek,
And spreads his wrinkles over sea and land,
And makes the strong, in their own season, weak.
He turns the raven's feather into snow,
And carves his triumph on the smoothest brow;
He makes the straightestbacked of poplars bow,
And breaks the lover's ill-considered vow.
But with thy beauty, he is at a loss,
And finds his cruel and usual art defied,
For thy true grace is freed from earthly dross,
With virtue and with constancy allied.
   So Time may rage and bring the world to rust,
   Thy spirit's fairness shall outlive the dust.

###1128###
To me thou art the season of the spring,
When frozen earth awakes from winter's death,
And all the hopeful, new-fledged birds do sing,
And blossoms open with a perfumed breath.
And thou art summer, in its glorious peak,
When long, bright days a golden comfort bring,
And nature's bounty, generous and meek,
Doth make a feast of every living thing.
And thou art autumn, with its ripened store,
A harvest gathered 'gainst the coming chill;
But winter's cold my heart can touch no more,
For thy warm love resides within it still.
   Thus all the seasons in thy person meet,
   And make my world eternally complete.

###1129###
When in the glass I see my features change,
And note the lines that sorrow hath engraved,
I find my own reflection poor and strange,
A shipwreck from the storms I have outbraved.
My youth's bright banner, now all ripped and torn,
Doth speak of battles I was forced to wage,
And makes me wish that I had not been born
To see myself decline in weary age.
But then the thought of thee doth cross my mind,
And thy dear love, so freely given me,
And in that thought, a sweeter youth I find,
That bids my anxious, aging heart be free.
   For in thy love, I am a man newborn,
   And laugh at time, and hold old age in scorn.

###1130###
My eye and heart are at a mortal war,
For how they each may claim a part of thee;
Mine eye thy picture steadfastly would bar
From my heart's vault, where it desires to be.
My heart contends mine eye is but a spy,
That looks on thee but cannot know thy grace,
And argues that thy truest form must lie
Within the soul, and not upon the face.
So to decide this civil, sweet debate,
The factions come before my mind's high court,
And there I do decree their new estate,
And bring their jealous arguments to nought.
   Thine outward form mine eager eye shall own,
   Thy inward love shall have my heart its throne.

###1131###
As some poor actor, frightened on the stage,
Forgets the lines he knew so well before,
So I, in thy grand presence, lose the page
Of all the pretty speeches held in store.
My wit, that serves me well with other men,
Doth turn to folly and deserts me quite;
My steady voice becomes uncertain, then
Is rendered mute by thy commanding light.
I stand before thee like a speechless fool,
Unable to express my own heart's plea,
And break each of my mind's own studied rule,
O'ercome with awe at just the sight of thee.
   Thus gaze I dumbly, letting my eyes say
   What my poor tongue could not speak anyway.

###1132###
I count the sins that do my soul possess,
The pride, the envy, and the dark despair,
And find my spirit a true wilderness
That yields to vice and is devoid of prayer.
I see the good that I have left undone,
The careless word, the selfish, thoughtless deed,
And know that 'neath the cold and judging sun,
My wicked heart plants but a bitter seed.
But when I think upon thy virtuous mind,
Which pardons faults and sees the good within,
A hope for my own cleansing I do find,
And feel the will to cast away my sin.
   Thy grace, a sermon, teaches me to mend,
   My love, my lodestar, my confessor, friend.

###1133###
Thou art the sun whose absence makes my night,
The food my starving, lovesick soul requires;
Thou art the peace that ends my inner fight,
And the sole object of my chaste desires.
Thou art the book wherein I long to read,
The steadfast anchor in a turning tide,
The truth that answers to my spirit's need,
The faithful and uncompromising guide.
Without thee, I am like a ship adrift,
Whose sails are torn, whose rudder is destroyed,
A beggar who hath squandered his last gift,
A gaping space, a miserable void.
   So pity him who on thy grace depends,
   For where thou endest, his whole being ends.

###1134###
Let those who will, chase after worldly fame,
And seek to have their deeds in brass engraved,
That future men might recognize their name,
And know how they in their own time behaved.
Let others hoard up treasure, coin, and plate,
And in their riches, think their worth is found,
Believing that their miserable state
Is much improved by what they see around.
My only fame is that you think me true,
My only treasure is your constant heart;
My world is measured by my thoughts of you,
And from that measure I will ne'er depart.
   Let them have stone and gold to prove their might,
   My love's my world, my riches, and my light.

###1135###
I often wonder what you see in me,
This common man of such an humble kind,
That you should grant so great a gift, and be
So true of heart and so resolved of mind.
I have no lands, no title to my name,
No graceful form to captivate the eye;
My only art is how to speak your fame,
And in your service, how to live and die.
Perhaps you see some virtue hid from view,
Some spark of honour others have not seen,
A steadfast soul that dedicates to you
The barren ground where little else has been.
   Or maybe love, which sees not with the eyes,
   Hath simply made of me a worthy prize.

###1136###
If this be dreaming, let me never wake,
But lie forever in this happy trance,
And from this vision, all my comfort take,
And live within this soul-enchanting dance.
If this be madness, let my mind not heal,
But keep the fever that hath made me so;
The painful truth I have no wish to feel,
The sober world I have no need to know.
For in this dream, you look on me and smile,
And in this madness, you do speak my name;
Let saner men their dreary wits beguile
With what they call life's earnest, bitter game.
   Your love's the world in which I wish to dwell,
   And all outside it is a waking hell.

###1137###
How can I pardon this, my jealous fear,
That rises up when you are not in sight,
And whispers dreadful fancies in mine ear
Throughout the long and solitary night?
It paints your face with smiles for others' eyes,
It lends your voice to praise another's wit,
It conjures rivals out of base surmise,
And on the throne of reason, it doth sit.
This wretched passion, born of my own doubt,
Doth wrong your name and my own soul torment,
And drives all calm and peaceful thinking out,
And on its own dark poison is intent.
   Yet jealousy is but love's fearful twin,
   That dreads to lose the heaven it lives in.

###1138###
The world's a stage of borrowed parts and shows,
Where men do strut in costumes not their own;
Each speaks the line another did compose,
And reaps the harvest that he has not sown.
The flatterer doth praise where he doth hate,
The pious man conceals a heart of sin,
The powerful bemoan their cruel fate,
The loser swears that he shall surely win.
But thou art true in every thought and deed,
Thyself in public as in private known;
Thou dost not plant a flower and call't a weed,
Nor claim another's garden as thine own.
   And for this truth, which shines in thee so bright,
   Thou art my sun, and all the world is night.

###1139###
So long as this my verse has strength to live,
And eyes have power this dark ink to read,
To thee a second being it shall give,
And from the bonds of death thou shalt be freed.
When marble tombs have crumbled into dust,
And monuments of kings are overthrown,
When gilded swords are eaten by the rust,
And all the glories of the past are gone,
Thy name shall flourish in these very lines,
Thy virtue here shall find a lasting place,
Thy beauty, captured in these frail designs,
Shall keep its youth and its eternal grace.
   So fret not that thy summer's day may cease,
   For in my rhyme, thy life shall find new lease.

###1140###
What is this life, if it be lived from thee?
A barren field whereon no rain doth fall,
A silent room where no sweet sounds may be,
A winter's day that lives beneath a pall.
What is this heart, if it hath not thy love?
A broken compass in a trackless wood,
A wingless and abandoned, mourning dove,
An empty vessel, never understood.
I measure not my days by sun or moon,
But by the moments that I hold thee near;
Without thy presence, morning comes too soon,
And every hour is but a home for fear.
   For thou art all that gives my being breath,
   And life without thee is a kind of death.

###1181###
Why dost thou arm thy beauty with such scorn,
To make a weapon of thy perfect face?
Wherefore was such a wondrous fairness born,
If not to grant the world some gentle grace?
Thou art a sun that gives no warming light,
A rose whose petals hide a cruel thorn,
That draws my hopeful gaze into the night,
And leaves my constant heart bereft and torn.
Yet reason cannot bid my soul to flee,
I am a ship that seeks its own shipwrack,
And though I see the ruin made of me,
From thy disastrous shore I turn not back.
So let thy pride my faithful heart consume,
For in thy shadow I accept my doom.

###1182###
When forty winters shall besiege thy brow,
And dig deep trenches in thy beauty's field,
Thy youth's proud livery, so gazed on now,
Will be a tattered weed of little yield.
Then being asked where all thy beauty lies,
Where all the treasure of thy lusty days,
To say within thine own deep-sunken eyes
Were an all-eating shame and thriftless praise.
How much more praise deserved thy beauty's use,
If thou couldst answer, "This fair child of mine
Shall sum my count and make my old excuse,"
Proving his beauty by succession thine.
This were to be new made when thou art old,
And see thy blood warm when thou feel'st it cold.

###1183###
Since I have been from thy dear presence thrust,
The world itself seems but a barren place;
The fairest flower is covered o'er with dust,
And joy is vanished from each smiling face.
The sweetest music to my ear sounds wrong,
The sun itself doth shine with lesser might,
I find no solace in a poet's song,
And walk as one who's lost his only light.
My thoughts, which once did soar on hopeful wings,
Now creep along the ground in heavy grief,
And every object that surrounds me brings
A painful memory, but no relief.
So must I languish in this weary state,
And count each hour that holds me from my fate.

###1184###
My mind a kingdom is, in civil strife,
Where two opposing monarchs claim their reign:
My reason, which doth plead for quiet life,
And my fond heart, which glories in its pain.
My reason shows how thou art false and proud,
And bids me break affection's heavy chain;
My heart replies thy faults are not allowed
To turn my constant service to disdain.
So in my breast a dreadful war is fought,
Where every victory brings new despair;
By reason's logic is my sense distraught,
By love's sweet poison I am held in care.
Thus, I, their subject, suffer all the cost,
And in their conflict am entirely lost.

###1185###
When envious night hath closed the seeing eye,
And all the world is hushed in silent dark,
To thee, my love, my waking thoughts do fly,
And in that shadow, thy bright form I mark.
Thy image, which my faithful mind doth hold,
Is then my sun when other suns be set;
More precious than the miser's hoard of gold,
A debt to love my soul can ne'er forget.
And so I lie, with eyelids open wide,
And gaze upon the darkness for thy sake,
Imagining that thou art by my side,
And with this fancy, my own torment make.
For when the day returns, I find it's true,
My only light was but a dream of you.

###1186###
The sun doth rise and journey through the sky,
And every creature turns to feel its grace,
But if thou pass, all turn away their eye
From heaven's light to gaze upon thy face.
The nightingale that sings her song so sweet,
Falls silent when she hears thy gentle voice,
As if a rival she could not defeat
Hath by its music stolen all her choice.
The fragrant rose, that is the garden's queen,
Doth seem to blush with shame and hang its head,
For in thy cheek a richer red is seen,
By which her own poor colour seems but dead.
All that is fair in this world must confess,
Thy greater beauty makes their beauty less.

###1187###
Let those who will, of worldly treasure speak,
Of gold and gems from some far distant land;
The only fortune that my soul doth seek
Is the soft touch of thy most gentle hand.
Let others boast of titles and of power,
Of armies vanquished and of kingdoms won;
I find more strength in one short, happy hour
Spent in the light of thy most glorious sun.
For what is gold but dross that cannot feel?
And what is power but a heavy crown?
Thy love, a wealth no cunning thief can steal,
A peaceful kingdom that cannot fall down.
So let them have their pride and earthly store,
While I have thee, I am than kings far more.

###1188###
Time's cruel sickle shall not reap thy praise,
Nor his swift passage make thy beauty fade,
For in the garden of my lyric phrase,
A monument to thee shall here be made.
Though marble tombs and statues fall to dust,
And brazen gates are eaten by decay,
And gilded thrones are overcome by rust,
My verse shall keep oblivion at bay.
When all the records of this age are gone,
And none remember emperors or kings,
Thy virtues shall in these black lines live on,
Borne to the future on immortal wings.
So shall you live, where breath can be no more,
In mouths of men who will thy grace adore.

###1189###
My love is like a fever, lingering on,
Which reason cannot cure, nor art appease;
The more I strive to have the sickness gone,
The more I feed upon my own disease.
My sense, the physician to my foolish heart,
Knows that thy presence doth my health impair,
And yet it plays a traitor's wicked part,
And brings me ever back into thy snare.
For thou art both the sickness and the cure,
A poison sweet, a torment I embrace,
The only pain my spirit can endure,
The only heaven is thy fatal grace.
Thus do I burn, and in my fever cry
For that which makes me live, and makes me die.

###1190###
If I should try to write of all thy worth,
My clumsy pen would falter and would fail;
My ink run dry, my wit give sudden birth
To words too weak to tell so grand a tale.
For how can mortal skill presume to paint
A beauty that the heavens did design?
My finest phrases would appear but faint,
My richest rhymes, an offering poor and trine.
To praise thine eyes, I'd need to steal the stars,
To speak thy wisdom, rob the ancient sages,
To show thy grace, break down description's bars,
And fill with miracles a thousand pages.
And so I cease, my tribute to impart,
And write thy name in silence on my heart.

###1191###
I do confess I am not blind to see
The little faults that in thy person dwell;
Sometimes thy mood is harsh and vexes me,
Thy pride, a story that thy brows do tell.
I know thy tongue can be a sharpened blade,
Thy patience, sometimes, is a threadbare thing;
I see the moments when thy virtues fade,
And hear the discord when thy humours sing.
But these small flaws, like shadows in the sun,
Make thy true brightness shine forth all the more;
They are the proof thou art a living one,
And not some cold-wrought idol to adore.
'Tis for these specks that my devotion's such,
For being human, I love thee so much.

###1192###
When in the chronicle of wasted time
I see descriptions of the fairest wights,
And beauty making beautiful old rhyme,
In praise of ladies dead and lovely knights,
Then in the blazon of sweet beauty's best,
Of hand, of foot, of lip, of eye, of brow,
I see their antique pen would have expressed
The very same perfection thou hast now.
So all their praises are but prophecies
Of this our time, all you prefiguring;
And for they looked but with divining eyes,
They had not skill enough thy worth to sing:
For we, which now behold these present days,
Have eyes to wonder, but lack tongues to praise.

###1193###
My heart, a ship upon a stormy sea,
Was tossed and broken by the winds of fate,
Without a star to guide or compass me,
Adrift and lost in my most wretched state.
Despair the captain, sorrow was the crew,
The sails were torn by gales of endless grief,
Until the blessed dawning of thy view
Provided harbour and a sweet relief.
Thy love, the lighthouse, shining in the dark,
Did guide me safely to a peaceful shore;
It saved from ruin my poor, battered bark,
And bid me fear the tempest's rage no more.
So let me in thy tranquil haven rest,
The final port for my long-troubled breast.

###1194###
Oft in my dreams, a council is convened,
Wherein my senses argue and debate,
Upon what grounds thy sovereign form is leaned,
And how my love for thee became so great.
Mine eye doth claim he first was taken in,
By gazing on thy face, so true and fair;
My ear replies, 'twas thy sweet voice did win,
By casting music on the captive air.
My sense of touch doth boast a tender plea,
Of gentle hands that gave my soul its rest;
But all fall silent when the heart, for thee,
Shows forth the wound it carries in my breast.
No single sense thy whole effect can prove;
It takes them all to apprehend my love.

###1195###
Like to the miser, counting o'er his gold,
Who fears a thief in every passing shade,
So is my heart, that jealously doth hold
The sacred image that thy form hath made.
I lock it fast within my mind's strong-box,
And keep the key where none may find the place,
Yet traitor thought my careful cunning mocks,
And steals abroad to gaze upon thy face.
And in the day, I fear each passing eye
That might by chance upon my treasure look,
And seeing its true worth, might wish to try
To steal the prize that I for granted took.
Thus am I rich in thee, and yet so poor,
I live in fear of losing evermore.

###1196###
That time of year thou mayst in me behold
When yellow leaves, or none, or few, do hang
Upon those boughs which shake against the cold,
Bare ruined choirs, where late the sweet birds sang.
In me thou seest the twilight of such day
As after sunset fadeth in the west;
Which by and by black night doth take away,
Death's second self, that seals up all in rest.
In me thou seest the glowing of such fire,
That on the ashes of his youth doth lie,
As the death-bed whereon it must expire,
Consumed with that which it was nourished by.
This thou perceiv'st, which makes thy love more strong,
To love that well which thou must leave ere long.

###1197###
The world's a stage, and all men players be,
Who strut and fret their hour and are forgot;
But thou, my love, art no such player to me,
For thy perfection changes not one jot.
Some play the king, yet have no royal soul,
Some play the fool, who think themselves most wise,
Some play the lover, but have no control
O'er the deceit that flickers in their eyes.
But thou, in whom all honest virtues meet,
Dost play one part, and that part is thine own:
Thy role is truth, thy every line is sweet,
Thy stage, my heart, and thy reward, my throne.
So let the others play their parts for praise,
Thy truth alone shall beautify my days.

###1198###
What is thy substance, whereof art thou made,
That millions of strange shadows on thee tend?
Since every one hath, every one, one shade,
And thou, but one, canst every shadow lend.
Describe Adonis, and the counterfeit
Is poorly imitated after thee;
On Helen's cheek all art of beauty set,
And you in Grecian tires are painted new.
Speak of the spring and foison of the year,
The one doth shadow of thy beauty show,
The other as thy bounty doth appear,
And you in every blessed shape we know.
In all external grace you have some part,
But you like none, none you, for constant heart.

###1199###
How can I then return in happy plight,
That am debarred the benefit of rest?
When day's oppression is not eased by night,
But day by night and night by day oppressed.
And each, though enemies to either's reign,
Do in consent shake hands to torture me,
The one by toil, the other to complain
How far I toil, still further off from thee.
I tell the day, to please him thou art bright,
And dost him grace when clouds do blot the heaven:
So flatter I the swart-complexioned night,
When sparkling stars twire not thou gild'st the even.
But day doth daily draw my sorrows longer,
And night doth nightly make grief's strength seem stronger.

###1200###
Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?
Thou art more lovely and more temperate:
Rough winds do shake the darling buds of May,
And summer's lease hath all too short a date.
Sometime too hot the eye of heaven shines,
And often is his gold complexion dimmed;
And every fair from fair sometime declines,
By chance, or nature's changing course, untrimmed.
But thy eternal summer shall not fade,
Nor lose possession of that fair thou ow'st,
Nor shall death brag thou wander'st in his shade,
When in eternal lines to time thou grow'st.
So long as men can breathe or eyes can see,
So long lives this, and this gives life to thee.

###1021###
When I behold the winter of thy face,
Wherein the lines of sorrow have been writ,
I see no remnant of that summer’s grace,
That once upon thy joyful brow did sit.
The cruel seasons, in their rough employ,
Have stolen all the treasure of thy spring,
And reft thy heart of its once-budding joy,
A hollow where no cheerful birds do sing.
Yet in the quiet autumn of thine eye,
A gentler wisdom doth to me appear,
That sun-scorched youth could never hope to buy,
Nor hold with such a price, so truly dear.
  So grieve not for the rose that time hath slain,
  For in its stead, a richer soul doth reign.

###1022###
My love is like a fever in the blood,
A foolish hope that reason cannot tame,
A torrent rushing in a mighty flood,
That whispers to my senses thy sweet name.
I seek for cure in absence and in time,
And tell myself this madness I must flee,
But every thought commits the selfsame crime,
And brings my wandering spirit back to thee.
The learned doctors shake their heads and sigh,
For such a malady they have no art;
They cannot teach a burning heart to lie,
Nor from its chosen idol to depart.
  And so I’ll let this sickness have its way,
  And in thy love’s delirium I’ll stay.

###1023###
That which the world doth praise as passing fair,
Doth often prove a bauble, bright and vain,
A painted cheek, a lock of borrowed hair,
A pleasing form that hides a deep-set stain.
But thy soul’s beauty, which no eye can see,
Is substance true, where others are but show,
A constant faith, a sweet humility,
From which all virtues in their season grow.
Let others boast of statues, white and cold,
Or jewels that from earthly mines are brought;
Thy inner worth is riches to be told,
A treasure that can never be un-bought.
  For outward grace by sickness is defaced,
  But thy pure spirit cannot be erased.

###1024###
When in the chronicle of wasted days,
I search for beauty that deserved the pen,
I find but shadows of those ancient lays,
That sang the praise of long-forgotten men.
Their ladies were but prophecies of thee,
Their verse a dim foretelling of thine own,
A hint of what perfection was to be,
Before the seed of thy dear self was sown.
All that they praised in colour, form, or height,
Was but a piece of what in thee I find;
They saw the dawn but not the fullest light,
And left the better part of it behind.
  So in thy person all their praise I read,
  And thy perfections prove their words were deed.

###1025###
My heavy heart, a ship upon the sea,
Is tossed and battered by a storm of doubt,
No friendly star doth shine to comfort me,
And winds of thy displeasure rage about.
My course was set to thy love’s gentle shore,
My compass pointed ever to thy grace,
But now I drift, and hear the breakers roar,
A wretched exile from that happy place.
What anchor can hold fast against this tide?
What map can show the way I must now steer?
In this vast ocean, where can I now hide
From the consuming tempest of my fear?
  Unless thy pardon, like a beacon, burn,
  To thy safe harbour I can ne’er return.

###1026###
How oft when I am laid in my lone bed,
And darkness holds the waking world in thrall,
Doth memory raise spectres of the dead,
And make my grieving spirit to recall
Those faces that from my life’s feast are gone,
Those voices that are forfeit to the dust;
Their loss doth make my present state forlorn,
And turns my fondest treasures into rust.
My weeping eyes, which daylight rendered dry,
Renew their lease on sorrow in the night,
And for the sake of friends long past, I cry,
And dim with tears the gloom’s already-light.
  But when I think on thee, all loss is gain,
  And in that thought, dead friends do live again.

###1027###
Take heed, my heart, of that green-eyed disease
That poisons truth and makes a monster seem
A likely guest, who comes the mind to please,
Then turns sweet slumber to a horrid dream.
This jealousy, this canker of the soul,
Doth feed upon the shadow of a slight,
And having fed, it takes a greater toll,
Until it hath consumed all reason’s light.
It makes a prison of a lover’s glance,
A guilty plea of every word unsaid;
It sees betrayal in a friendly dance,
And plants a crown of thorns upon the head.
  Then banish it, or love itself shall fail,
  For in its presence, faith cannot prevail.

###1028###
Thy voice is music, but of such a kind
As puts to shame the lute’s most silver sound;
The sweetest songs that ever man could find
Are but a rasping mimicry, I’ve found.
When thou dost speak, the air itself gives ear,
And churlish winds do cease their blustering,
The birds fall silent in the wood, for fear
Their simple notes should mar the song you bring.
So let not silence be thy stern reply
When I do plead my case with clumsy art;
One word from thee can make my spirits fly,
One syllable can mend my broken heart.
  Speak then, my love, and let my sentence be
  To live within that perfect harmony.

###1029###
When thou art absent, all the world is bare,
A stage stripped down, its actors sent away;
The sun himself doth shine with lesser care,
And casts a mournful pall upon the day.
The rose that yesterday seemed passing sweet,
Today presents a face of common red;
The food I once with appetite would eat
Is tasteless now, like ashes or like lead.
For thou art life and substance to my sight,
The sense and meaning by which I perceive;
Without thee, day is but a paler night,
And all that I behold, I disbelieve.
  Return, my love, and with your presence, bless
  This empty world and cure its nothingness.

###1030###
Let princes build in marble and in stone,
Their proudest works to tell the world their might;
Such monuments shall soon be overthrown,
And all their boasted glory sink from sight.
For envious Time, with his relentless hand,
Doth crack the tow’rs and turn the brass to green,
And what was meant through ages to command
Is made a ruin, as it had not been.
But this my verse, a frail and humble thing,
Built not of quarried rock but breath and thought,
A truer monument to thee shall bring,
And keep thy name from being set at naught.
  For whilst men breathe and have the wit to read,
  Thy worth shall live, immortal in this deed.

###1031###
My glass doth not deceive, but tells me true
That winter’s frost lies now upon my hair,
And that the face that looks at me anew
Is etched with all the characters of care.
Where is the youth that sported in this brow?
Where is the fire that once lit up these eyes?
The glass shows what I am, not then, but now,
And mocks my heart that foolishly denies.
Yet when I look on thee, my best beloved,
Time’s heavy ledger seems a paltry lie,
And from my soul the burden is removed,
Beneath the timeless summer of thine eye.
  So let the mirror show what it must show,
  For in thy love, no age my heart can know.

###1032###
When busy day hath yielded to the night,
And all my cares demand their heavy fee,
I shut my eyes against the fading light
To find a kinder, better world with thee.
For sleep, that is the counterfeit of death,
Doth build for me a stage within my brain,
Where thy dear form, given a truer breath,
Doth act those scenes I long to see again.
No cruel mischance nor distance holds us far,
No bitter word can pass thy perfect lips;
Thou art my sun, my constant, faithful star,
That knows no setting, suffers no eclipse.
  And yet this joy, how swift it is to fade!
  The waking world makes truth a masquerade.

###1033###
Some glory in their birth, some in their gold,
Some in the strength and sinew of their arm,
Some in their garments, splendid to behold,
Some in their lands, a vast and fertile farm.
Each to his humour, let his pride be lent,
And in his chosen treasure take his boast;
For my own part, I am not so content
With that which fortune grants to envy most.
My wealth is not for any man to count,
Nor can it be secured with lock and key;
It is a spring, a never-failing fount
That lives within and flows from thee to me.
  That I am loved by thee is my estate,
  Which makes me richer than the hand of fate.

###1034###
Another pen now labours in thy praise,
And finds new figures for thy wondrous face,
He robs the heavens of their brightest rays
And gives them to thine eyes to lend them grace.
His well-wrought lines, in golden ink set down,
Do entertain thee in a sweeter style,
And by his art, he captures thy renown,
Where my rough verse can only make thee smile.
Am I disgraced that he doth sing so well?
And hath his flattery my love displaced?
Must I, who first thy virtues sought to tell,
Now see my humble offering erased?
  No, for he writes what others think is true;
  I write my heart, which none can know but you.

###1035###
I look upon the lily, pure and white,
And think its fairness is a theft from thee;
I see the rose at morning’s early light
And deem its colour thy sweet property.
The violet’s scent upon the gentle breeze
Is but the memory of thy sweet perfume;
The music of the wind among the trees
Is thy lost voice, speaking from nature’s room.
All that is good, or beautiful, or right,
That mortal sense can touch or comprehend,
Is but a shadow of thy perfect light,
A message that the heavens choose to send.
  For nature, in her best and chiefest part,
  Is but a copy of what lives in thy heart.

###1036###
That thou, my friend, shouldst use me in this wise,
And with a traitor’s kiss my trust betray,
Doth wound me more than all my enemies,
Whose open hate I could at least repay.
For from a foe, I look for bitter deeds,
His sword is drawn, his malice is displayed;
But who can guard against the scattered seeds
Of falsehood in a friendly garden laid?
Thy hand, which I so often held in mine,
Didst guide the knife that struck me in the back;
Thy voice, which I did think was half divine,
Didst praise my worth while planning my attack.
  So farewell, friend, who art no more a friend,
  The truest love hath met the foulest end.

###1037###
This love, a kingdom held by dual kings,
Doth own a nature both of sun and shade,
One moment, with an angel’s voice it sings,
The next, it wields a executioner's blade.
It is a feast of rarest, sweet delight,
Where every dish doth please the appetite;
It is a famine in the blackest night,
That starves the soul and robs the eye of sight.
What magic joins such pleasure and such pain?
What chemistry can mix such fire and ice?
To feel such loss and yet to find such gain,
And sell one’s freedom at so dear a price?
  This riddle love itself cannot explain,
  Why those in bliss still cherish their own chain.

###1038###
Why art thou silent when I beg to know
The state and standing of my poor heart's suit?
Thy quietness doth cause my doubt to grow,
Like bitter ivy from a hidden root.
Speak ill of me, and I can bear the sting;
Deny my love, and I will learn to grieve;
But in this space where all is hovering,
My tortured mind can neither stay nor leave.
Thy silence is a canvas, broad and white,
Whereon my fear doth paint its darkest scene,
And every passing hour of day and night
Doth add new torment, cruel and unforseen.
  One word would serve this misery to kill;
  Without it, thy dear hand doth work me ill.

###1039###
Shall I compare thee to a locked-up chest
Wherein a miser keeps his worldly store?
For thy fair face, by beauty’s seal possessed,
Holds riches that I covet more and more.
But Time, that villain, with his picking key,
Doth plot to break the lock and steal the gold,
To waste the treasure that I find in thee,
And leave the empty coffer, bleak and cold.
But with my pen I'll forge a lock so strong
That Time's own craft shall find no purchase there,
And in the living fortress of my song
I'll guard thy youth from age and spoil and care.
  So let him try; his arts will be in vain,
  For in this verse, thy beauty shall remain.

###1040###
This world’s a stage, as all the wise men write,
And we but players, given a brief part,
Who fret and strut within a fading light,
And hide behind the curtain of our art.
Some play the king, some play the weeping clown,
Some are the heroes, some the villains vile;
Each has his entrance, then his going-down,
And holds the audience for a little while.
But you and I, my love, are cast to be
The constant subject of the central plot,
A tale of love for all the world to see,
That by the other players is forgot.
  And when the final curtain starts to fall,
  Our simple love shall be the sum of all.

###1081###
Why should I praise the morning’s golden ray,
When thy bright glance doth make my darkness part?
The sun itself doth seem to lose its way,
When such a greater sun shines in my heart.
Why should I laud the lark’s melodious tune,
Which greets the dawn from its high, airy sphere?
Thy whispered words beneath the silent moon,
Are sweeter music to my captive ear.
Why should I marvel at the rose’s red,
Or at the lily, for its perfect white?
Such fleeting beauties are too quickly dead,
Compared unto thy soul’s eternal light.
  My praise of them is but a slight excuse,
  To speak of thee, who art my only muse.

###1082###
Each object that my mournful eye doth see
Doth look the poorer, since removed from thee.
The sun itself shines not so warm a grace,
Since I am banished from thy smiling face.
The hours, which once on feathered wings did fly,
Now heavy-footed, creep in sad array,
And every moment utters forth a sigh,
To mark the sorrow of the passing day.
Yea, all that once brought pleasure to my sight,
Doth now but serve my sorrow to increase;
The brightest day is but a starless night,
In which my pining heart can find no peace.
  Thus in this lonely state I must remain,
  'Til thy dear sight shall make me whole again.

###1083###
O cruel Time, that with thy silent pace,
Dost steal the blush from beauty's tender cheek,
And with thy ploughshare furroweth the face,
Leaving the strong, in their own winter, weak;
Thou art a thief that taketh without sound,
The golden treasure of a youthful head,
Until no trace of summer can be found,
And all the glories of the spring are dead.
Thy cruel conquest spareth none at all,
The kingly tower and the bloom must bend;
At thy command the greatest state must fall,
And all that lives must hasten to its end.
  Yet fear thou not, my love, this tyrant's rage,
  Thy beauty lives immortal on my page.

###1084###
When I in thought consider what shall last,
And what shall be to dusty ruin brought,
I see proud monuments to ages past
By winds and rains of centuries set at naught.
The carven stone, the gilded idol's shrine,
The conqueror's arch, the poet's hopeful bust,
All bow to nature's terrible design,
And mix their substance with the common dust.
How then can thy fair form hope to evade
The wasteful war that all the world must lose?
What fortress can be for thy beauty made,
Against the siege of Time's most dreadful news?
  Only this verse, which scorns Time's slow decay,
  And keeps thy name alive beyond its day.

###1085###
I look upon thy face and see a sky
Wherein no cloud of treachery can live,
And from thy lips I hear a sweet reply
That bids my soul its every doubt forgive.
I see such truth within thy constant gaze,
I feel such virtue in thy gentle hand,
That I am lost in a believing maze,
And think no falsehood in this world can stand.
But when I turn and see what men can be,
How fair a show can hide the foulest deed,
A sudden tremor taketh hold of me,
And plants in my fond heart a fearful seed.
  For if that face should ever prove untrue,
  No truth is left for man to measure to.

###1086###
Last night thou cam'st to me within a dream,
So like thyself in every perfect grace,
That though I slept, it did most truly seem
I felt the living sunlight of thy face.
Thou spok'st such words as banish all despair,
And putt'st thy hand in mine; the world grew bright.
No waking joy could ever yet compare
With the deep solace of that blissful sight.
But then the dawn, that envious, pale-faced spy,
Did banish sleep and steal my joy away,
And I awoke to hear my own heart cry
Against the coming of the lonely day.
  So is my night made richer than my day,
  By dreams that truth itself cannot outweigh.

###1087###
Mine eye and heart are at a mortal war,
And each the other's argument denies;
Mine eye doth claim the sight of thee its star,
My heart contests where thy true image lies.
My heart doth say it holds thee in its core,
A perfect treasure, hidden from all view;
Mine eye replies that it should triumph more,
Which looks upon thy form, forever new.
The one doth feast on thy most outward part,
The other on thy spirit from within;
The one would claim possession of thy art,
The other where all virtues must begin.
  Yet this long strife they may at last decide,
  When heart has sight, and sight has heart to guide.

###1088###
The world's a stage, as all the wise men write,
Where we but play the parts we are assigned,
And in the space between the day and night
We speak the lines residing in the mind.
Some play the king, in robes of purple hue,
And some the fool, in motley, jesting guise;
Some play the lover, faithful, fond and true,
And some the villain, with deceiving eyes.
But when I look on thee, my dearest friend,
The metaphor itself appears too weak;
No part seems worthy for thee to attend,
Nor can the stage of thy perfection speak.
  For thou art not a player in the show,
  But the true light by which the actors glow.

###1089###
Behold the trees, now stripped of summer’s green,
That stand like skeletons against the grey,
Where lately sweet-voiced choirs of birds were seen,
But now cold silence holds its solemn sway.
This is the year’s own twilight, bleak and brief,
The after-image of a brighter age,
When every branch was lord of its own leaf,
Before grim Winter took the world for stage.
So in my soul, a like cold season reigns,
Where joys that blossomed once, now barren lie,
And in my heart a frozen grief remains,
Beneath the darkness of a tear-filled sky.
  Yet thinking on thee, summer I retrieve,
  And in thy memory, refuse to grieve.

###1090###
A fever holds me, running through my veins,
For which my reason hath no power to kill,
It is a sickness made of pleasant pains,
Which overthrows my judgment and my will.
My mind, the doctor that should find a cure,
Doth look on thee, and straight forgets his art;
There is no torment that I can’t endure,
So long as I am prisoned in thy heart.
And so this malady that makes me weak,
Is yet the source of all my strength and might;
'Tis only of thy wondrous self I speak,
Who art my fever's dark and burning light.
  Let reason go, I'll not be rendered free,
  But live and die in this sweet malady.

###1091###
Some other pen, more skillful than my own,
May praise your beauty with a greater art,
And find such words that heaven has not known,
To paint the perfect picture of your heart.
He may compare your eyes to summer skies,
Your voice to music from an angel's throat,
And in the grace of his sweet flattery rise,
To be the age's most admired note.
But let his verse, with all its golden phrase,
Be but the herald of my truer thought;
I, who in silence number all your days,
Have with my soul your truest substance bought.
  He writes on paper what will fade and bend,
  I write on heart, and that shall have no end.

###1092###
When in the glass I see my features plain,
I trust it not, for it is prone to lie,
Reflecting back what sun and wearying rain
Have etched on me as long days have gone by.
It shows a brow where care hath set its mark,
And eyes grown dim from staring at the past,
A face that travels onward to the dark,
A fleeting shape not ever meant to last.
This flattering fraud, this surface-showing friend,
Knows nothing of the soul that dwells inside,
The constant heart on which you may depend,
Which age cannot corrupt, nor sorrow hide.
  So trust not what my outward form shall be,
  But read my love, and you shall truly see me.

###1093###
What tyrant taught thee such a cruel art,
To wound the one who offers thee his all?
To take the fortress of a willing heart,
And make its chambers echo with thy call,
Only to leave it, desolate and bare,
A conquered province, plundered of its joy,
And smile to see the ruin and despair
Which thy capricious humors thus employ.
Is it thy pride that takes so much delight
In proving what a power thou dost hold?
Or art thou blind to my most sorry plight,
A tale of worship that remains untold?
  Be not so proud of that which you possess,
  Lest beauty lose its worth through heartlessness.

###1094###
When to the sessions of my silent mind
I summon up the ghosts of vanished days,
A thousand joys and sorrows I do find,
That walk again in memory’s strange maze.
I see a face, I hear a word once spoken,
I feel a loss as if it were brand new;
I mend again a heart that was long broken,
And pay old debts with tears that are still due.
But in this court of sorrow and of shade,
Where every phantom claims its heavy cost,
If I but think on thee, all sorrows fade,
And all the treasure that I thought was lost.
  For thy dear memory can so restore,
  I count my gains and grieve for loss no more.

###1095###
Look how the moon, that mistress of the night,
Doth change her face from one hour to the next,
Now full and round, a globe of perfect light,
Now but a sliver, leaving me perplexed.
She is the queen of all inconstancy,
Who pulls the tides and governs frantic minds,
And with a cold and pale divinity,
She with our fleeting human nature binds.
And so art thou, who with a changing grace,
Dost show me now a heaven, now a hell;
A perfect smile may lighten on thy face,
Then sudden frowns a different story tell.
  Yet unlike her, thy darkness and thy light,
  Both make my world more beautiful and bright.

###1096###
When sickness holds me in its clammy hand,
And bids my strength and lively spirits fail,
My body seems a foreign, conquered land,
Beset by foes that everywhere assail.
The world of health is but a distant shore,
A memory of some forgotten bliss,
And every breath I take but asks for more
Than this poor, weakened frame can grant in this.
But if a thought of thee should cross my brain,
Or if thy name be whispered in the room,
My flagging heart finds courage in its pain,
And sees a candle glowing in the gloom.
  For thou art health, and in thy name I find,
  A sovereign cure for body and for mind.

###1097###
My love is great, but my own worth so small,
I wonder that you notice me at all,
I, who possess no grace, no wealth, no might,
To justify your presence in my sight.
My verse is poor, my wit is often lame,
I have no titles to adorn my name,
My only fortune is the love I bear,
Which often feels more like a weight of care.
For when I count the sum of your deserts,
And all the graces that in you reside,
My spirit fails, and my poor reason hurts
To think my lowly self should be your guide.
  But then I think, your choice makes it not so,
  For loving me, you make my value grow.

###1098###
Why art thou silent, when a single word
From thy sweet lips could banish all my doubt?
Have my poor sonnets not as yet been heard?
Or dost thou mean to shut my true love out?
The greatest kings will listen to a plea,
And grant a pardon to a kneeling man;
Shall I who serve, and only worship thee,
Be met with silence since my love began?
Speak ill of me, if that be thy desire,
Or mock the verse that I for thee have made;
'Twere better to be scorned with open fire,
Than be ignored and in this stillness fade.
  For any sound thy gentle voice doth make,
  Is kinder than the silence I must take.

###1099###
The world may chide, and say I am to blame,
That I should spend my days in praise of you,
And for my pains get nothing but ill-fame,
For loving where I am not granted due.
Let them account the hours I have lost
In idle worship of a distant star,
And let them measure what my love has cost,
In terms of what my public fortunes are.
These worldly men, who measure loss and gain,
See not the wealth that I in you possess;
Their chiding words are but a winter's rain,
That cannot touch my summer's happiness.
  For having you, my fortune is so great,
  I would not trade it for a king's estate.

###1100###
Should this my final verse be, let it tell
The self-same story that I told at first,
That in your heart all beauty's virtues dwell,
At which font my poor poetry is nursed.
From first to last, my subject is the same,
Though I have tried a thousand ways to write;
I start and end by speaking thy dear name,
Who art my only argument and light.
I have no other topic for my art,
No other world that I desire to know,
No other passion living in my heart,
From which a line of worthy verse could grow.
  So all my songs, both what are past and new,
  Are but of you, and you, and only you.

###1161###
When thou art absent, comfort takes its flight,
And o'er my spirit spreads a grey despair,
The sun itself doth seem to lose its light,
And heavy sorrow seasons all the air.
I look upon the rose in its full bloom,
And find its boasted beauty is not true,
It serves but to remind me of my gloom,
For all its fairness is not fair as you.
Yet if by chance thy image I embrace,
Within the crowded theatre of my mind,
A sudden joy illuminates that place,
And leaves all trace of sorrow far behind.
   For in my thoughts thy constant light I see,
   And that alone is world enough for me.

###1162###
Those eyes, two suns in thy face's firmament,
Do govern all the motions of my soul,
And by their glance my destiny is spent,
Or rais'd to heights beyond my own control.
When they do shine with favour and with grace,
My world is warm, and all my fears take leave;
But if a cloud should darken that sweet place,
My heart hath no more solace but to grieve.
So let thy gaze a gentle aspect wear,
And have no commerce with my sad despair,
Lest I, poor mariner, should shipwrecked be,
Upon the waves of a tempestuous sea.
   Look kindly, then, and be my northern star,
   Whose steady beam is seen and loved from far.

###1163###
I see how Time with his relentless hand
Doth steal the blush from beauty's tender cheek,
And how the proudest works in all the land
Are made by his unconquerable power weak.
The tow'r that stands so loftily to-day,
Tomorrow is but dust upon the ground,
And kings who hold an undisputed sway
Must in the silence of the tomb be found.
But thy fair form, which nature did so make,
Shall not be yielded up to foul decay;
From Time's sharp scythe my living verse shall take
Thy grace, and bear it from his wrath away.
   So ages hence, when all these things are past,
   Within this line thy beauty still shall last.

###1164###
Another pen now labours for thy sake,
And fashions words of such a golden art,
That my poor muse her humble leave must take,
Lest she should fail to truly show thy heart.
He speaks of thee in such a lofty strain,
As if some angel had inspir'd his tongue,
While my plain speech can only speak my pain,
And leaves thy highest virtues all unsung.
But his sweet phrases are a painted show,
An empty casket, beautiful and bright,
The jewel of my love he cannot know,
Which burns for thee with ever-constant light.
   Then judge not by the skill that words impart,
   But by the simple truth within the heart.

###1165###
Let those who judge by outward semblance say
That thy fair youth will not for ever last,
That raven hair in time shall turn to grey,
And beauty's season will be quickly past.
Let them declare a wrinkle must appear
Upon the smooth perfection of thy brow,
And that the grace which all men hold so dear
Shall to the crooked scythe of ages bow.
They see not with the spirit's truer eye,
Which looks beyond the perishable shell,
The constant soul they cannot there descry,
Wherein all truth and lasting virtues dwell.
   For that which makes thee lovely unto me,
   Is not a thing that Time can make to flee.

###1166###
When all the world to silent rest has gone,
And darkest night her shadowy curtain draws,
I lie awake, in my sad bed alone,
And find in thee of my despair the cause.
For then thy image, like a waking dream,
Doth banish sleep and occupy my mind,
And thy dear face, lit by an inward gleam,
Leaves all the comfort of the day behind.
I chide the hours that they so slowly creep,
And wish for morn to break upon my sight,
But day no more can give my sorrows sleep,
For thou art absent both by day and night.
   Thus neither sun nor moon can bring me rest,
   While this deep torment lodges in my breast.

###1167###
This world's a stage, and all men have their part;
Some seek for honour, some for worldly gain,
Some play the fool, and some the villain's art,
And some but seek an antidote to pain.
Each follows on his pre-appointed way,
And struts and frets his hour for all to see,
Then quits the scene at the closing of the day,
To be forgot in dark obscurity.
But I, who have no care for fortune's prize,
Have spurned the quest for glory or for might,
And find my only purpose in thine eyes,
Which are my sun, my story, and my light.
   To be thy truest love is all my plea,
   No other role has any worth for me.

###1168###
As some poor subject, bound by loyal grace,
Doth lift his eyes unto his monarch's throne,
So do I gaze upon thy sovereign face,
And make my heart's devoted service known.
Thou art too high for my unworthy hand
To ever hope thy precious self to hold,
And by the strictures of this weary land,
My silent love may never be made bold.
Yet from that distance, I can still admire
The light of virtue that in thee doth shine,
And feed my soul upon this sacred fire,
Though I may never hope to call thee mine.
   To love thee is a fate I cannot choose,
   And 'tis a grace I would not bear to lose.

###1169###
Why dost thou hoard thy beauty for thyself,
And keep such treasure under lock and key?
It is a sin to store such boundless wealth,
When giving it would make thee richer be.
For nature gave that fairness not in vain,
But as a gift that thou should'st give again;
Make of thy sweetness a more lasting strain,
And let a child thy legacy maintain.
Thy summer's lease will all too soon expire,
And age will come to claim its legal due;
Then let thy likeness, born of sweet desire,
Renew in youth what Time takes from thy view.
   So make a copy of thy perfect form,
   To keep thy beauty safe from winter's storm.

###1170###
My tongue is bankrupt when it speaks of thee,
And can but stammer where it ought to praise;
There is no word in all the world for me
To truly capture thy most perfect ways.
I call thee fair; 'tis true, but does not tell
The deeper grace that in thy spirit lies;
I say thou'rt kind, but cannot justly spell
The boundless pity seated in thine eyes.
I liken thee to summer's brightest day,
But thou art more constant and more temperate,
Thy beauty shines when summer fades away,
And makes the bleakest winter fortunate.
   So all my words must fail, and stand ashamed,
   For thou art more than ever could be named.

###1171###
Why dost thou use such cruelty on one
Whose only crime is that he loves thee so?
What satisfaction or what triumph's won
By making my true heart a stage for woe?
I have been constant as the northern star,
And built my world upon thy gentle grace;
Why dost thou now my hopeful progress bar,
And look on me with an offended face?
If this be but a test to prove my worth,
Then thou dost tax thy servant overmuch,
And make a hell of what was heaven on earth,
With one unkindness, or a careless touch.
   Have mercy, lest when you for mercy plead,
   You find that none will answer to your need.

###1172###
The biting frost now holds the world in thrall,
And winter's breath makes all the branches bare,
The birds are silent at his chilling call,
And barren sadness hangs upon the air.
The fields that once with summer's pride were drest
Are now a waste of unrelenting white,
The sun itself, low-sinking in the west,
Doth grant the world a pale and grudging light.
But in my breast no season's change I find,
For there the thought of thee a spring doth keep;
The memory of thy face within my mind
Awakes a joy that will not fall asleep.
   So let the winter rage, I take no chill,
   For in my heart 'tis warm and summer still.

###1173###
I have heard music that could charm the soul,
And lull the troubled senses into peace,
Whose sweet accord could make a spirit whole,
And grant the weary heart a brief release.
I have heard songs that soaring larks have made,


###1101###
When I upon the midnight heaven gaze,
I find no star that holds so true a light
As that which in thine eyes doth me amaze,
And banishes the shadows of the night.
The firmament shows but a distant fire,
A lonely spark in stories ages old,
But from thy look is born a warm desire,
That saves my frozen spirit from the cold.
What need have I of constellations, see,
When thy twin suns make all their glory vain?
So let the heavens in their courses be,
Nor shall I seek their guidance once again.
For in thy sight my whole of world thou art,
And of that world I am a willing part.

###1102###
That churlish Time, a sly and subtle thief,
Doth carve new lines upon thy perfect brow,
And plants the seeds of winter and of grief,
Where summer's blush was resident but now.
He etches sorrows on thy lovely face,
With the slow cruelty of his aged hand,
And seeks to steal each remnant of thy grace,
As waves consume the castles made of sand.
Yet in the treasured fortress of my mind,
Thy beauty's form he cannot take away;
There, safe from ruin, shall the ages find
The light that shone upon a fairer day.
So let him rage, thy bloom shall never fade,
For in this verse, a second spring is made.

###1103###
Since thou, my sun, art absent and away,
The very colours of the world do seem
A faded masque, a pageant of dull gray,
The waking echo of a troubled dream.
The morning sun, who once appeared so bright,
Doth lend no warmth, but shows that all is wrong;
The birds, methinks, mistake the day for night,
And have forgot the purpose of their song.
Each passing hour is but a heavy thought,
A tedious guest that brings no friendly cheer,
My daily labour comes to less than nought,
For all my life depends on thee being here.
So come again and make my spirit whole,
For thou art the subsistence of my soul.

###1104###
I am with such a strange affliction ill,
That reason runs distempered in my brain,
And though I strive, my thoughts are never still,
But turn and burn in cycles of sweet pain.
No potion can a remedy procure,
To cool the frantic beating of my heart;
This fever I am fated to endure,
Unless you will consent to play a part.
'Tis only by the comfort of thy sight,
That this consuming torment can be healed,
Which robs my weary senses of the night,
The deepest hurt that ever was concealed.
So be my physic, and give health to me,
For without thee, I cannot hope to be.

###1105###
What poison works so foully on the mind
As jealous thoughts of what I cannot see?
They make me in my clearest judgment blind,
And set my own invention against me.
Each smile you give to others seems less fair,
Each whispered word becomes a devil's thought,
It hangs like venom in the summer air,
A subtle net by my own fancy wrought.
This monster feeds on what is but a lie,
And turns my truest faith to bitter doubt,
It makes my certain knowledge go awry,
And leaves me lost, in torment, and without.
Only thy constant voice can this beast kill,
So speak me true, and bend it to my will.

###1106###
Methinks the world is but a painted stage,
Wherein we play our parts out for a day;
We fret and strut from infancy to age,
Then bow, and let the curtain end the play.
We speak the lines assigned to us by heart,
And hide our truer selves in a disguise,
And though we act a brave or foolish part,
The truth is oft reflected in the eyes.
But when thou dost appear, I cannot speak,
And I forget my lines, my part, my role;
My well-rehearsed composure becomes weak,
Exposing all the secrets of my soul.
For in this play, thou my whole audience art,
And thy neglect could break this actor's heart.

###1107###
No instrument that mortal hands have made,
Nor silver lute, nor viol's sweet complaint,
Hath ever such a perfect music played
As thy dear voice, which makes all others faint.
When thou dost speak, the nightingale is shamed,
And holds her tongue, lest she should sound amiss;
The very air, before untamed,
Is taught a grace and melody by this.
I hang upon each word as on a thread,
A silken cord that pulls me to thy will;
By that sweet sound my hungry soul is fed,
And when thou'rt silent, all the world is still.
Then speak again, and let thy music flow,
For it is heaven's tune, on earth below.

###1108###
Last night in sleep, my soul did find its way
To that sweet court where thou dost hold thy reign;
And for a time, my sorrows fell away,
And I was freed from all my waking pain.
I saw thee smile, and heard thee speak my name,
A vision brighter than the truest thing;
My heart, rekindled, burst into a flame,
As doth a forest at the touch of spring.
But then the dawn, that cruel and jealous light,
Did tear the fabric of this perfect scene,
And banished thee, and brought the endless night
Of knowing what is, from what might have been.
So must I sleep, if I would see thy face,
And find in dreams what day cannot embrace.

###1109###
A civil war is fought within this frame,
Between my heart, which vows that thou art kind,
And my two eyes, which cruelly proclaim
The cold disdain that they do ever find.
My heart says thou art good, and true, and fair,
The pattern of all virtue, grace, and wit;
My eyes reply, and bid my heart beware
The scornful brow where all thy humours sit.
The heart, being blind, builds castles in the air,
And calls thy frown the shadow of the sun;
The eyes do see the truth of my despair,
And know my courtship never can be won.
To which side shall I give my loyalty?
My heart's false hope, or what my eyes do see?

###1110###
To be a beggar, suing for a crust,
Is not so poor a state as that I know,
For I, who in thy mercy put my trust,
Am starved by looks that naught but winter show.
The meanest wretch may hope for some relief,
A coin, a word, to ease his heavy lot;
But I, who nurse a far more noble grief,
Am by thy charity entirely forgot.
Why dost thou spend thy smiles on lesser men,
Who cannot value them at half their worth,
And leave me here to languish in my den,
The most forsaken creature on this earth?
One gentle glance from thee would make me rich,
But thou art deaf to the pleas which I beseech.

###1111###
My clumsy verse is but a crooked glass,
That cannot hope thy beauty to contain;
The finest words I fashion come to pass
As halting prose, inadequate and plain.
I strive to capture all that I behold,
The perfect arch of thy commanding brow,
A treasure far more precious than pure gold,
To which all other earthly things must bow.
But ink is black, and cannot paint the light
That from thine eyes in radiant splendour streams;
My poet's craft is conquered by the sight,
And lives but in the shadow of my dreams.
So blame my pen, and not my heart's true fire,
Whose art falls short of its immense desire.

###1112###
When barren winter holds the land in thrall,
And strips the trees of all their summer dress,
My heavy spirit doth in sorrow fall,
And mirrors all the season's emptiness.
But when the spring returns with gentle rain,
And wakes the buds that slumbered in the cold,
My hope, like them, begins to live again,
A story that can never grow too old.
The summer's heat is like my love's full blaze,
And autumn's gold, my wealth when you are near;
You are the sun that governs all my days,
The changing reason for each passing year.
So let my mood by nature's turn be known,
For my soul's weather is thy love alone.

###1113###
From the red rose, you stole its richest hue,
And from the lily, whiteness for your hand;
From heaven's vault, you took the deepest blue,
And placed it in your eyes at your command.
The morning sun did lend you threads of gold,
To weave a crown of light upon your head;
The nightingale, a story to be told,
Gave up its song, to grace the words you said.
You are a thief, who pilfered all the best
From Nature's store, to make yourself complete;
You took her treasures and you left the rest,
To make a whole impossibly more sweet.
Yet this one theft I gladly will forgive,
If in the world you stole, you let me live.

###1114###
When all the world is hushed in silent sleep,
And darkness holds its undisputed reign,
My wakeful eyes their lonely vigil keep,
And summon up your image to my pain.
I count the hours by my own heart's beat,
A heavy drum that marks the passing time,
And think of you, a torment bitter-sweet,
For which my love is both the cause and crime.
The moon, a witness, climbs the lonely sky,
And casts long shadows on my chamber floor,
It seems to ask my weary spirit why
I crave the thing that makes me suffer more.
But ere the dawn can bring its grey relief,
Thy memory comes, and magnifies my grief.

###1115###
I have become a true idolater,
And made thy beauty my religion's creed;
I kneel to thee and offer up my prayer,
And in thy service do my spirit bleed.
Thy face, the altar where I make my vow,
Thine eyes, the saints to whom I plead my case;
There is no other god I will allow
To hold dominion in this holy place.
Some say this worship is a mortal sin,
To give a human what is God's by right,
But they see not the heaven that's within
The sacred temple of thy mortal light.
So let them preach; my faith I'll not deny,
