And at thy shrine, I'll either live or die.

###1116###
When brass and stone are by the years consumed,
And monuments to kings are turned to dust,
How shall thy beauty, which all life perfumed,
Escape the rage of time's destructive lust?
The strongest fortress and the highest wall
Cannot defend against decay's slow might;
The fairest flower is destined first to fall,
And sink forgotten into endless night.
But fear thou not the tyrant's cruel decree,
Nor wasteful war, nor envy's biting sting,
For in the living record of my plea,
A second life unto thy form I bring.
So shall you live, in these black lines enshrined,
More fresh and fair than those you leave behind.

###1117###
So oft have I declared my love in rhyme,
And sworn my faith with every breath I take,
That my poor words, worn out by constant time,
No new impression on your heart can make.
You hear my pleas as one might hear the sea,
A constant murmur that is never new;
The boundless passion that resides in me
Is but a common, tiresome sound to you.
My greatest art, my verse, my soul's true plea,
Have failed to move your spirit from its stone;
There is no hope, as far as I can see,
But I must love, and I must love alone.
And so I'll weep, since words have lost their power,
And water this dead love, flower by flower.

###1118###
My heart, it seems, no longer beats for me,
But has become a traitor to my breast;
It fled my frame, and took its leave to be
A willing captive, and your constant guest.
In fair exchange, I prayed you send me thine,
To fill the hollow chamber it had left;
A worthy trade, your loyal heart for mine,
To mend the sorrow of this aching theft.
But you, so rich in hearts, keep mine in chains,
And send me none to recompense my loss;
And so this empty shell alone remains,
To bear a double, and a lonely cross.
Then give me back my heart to ease my pain,
Or take me with it, to erase the stain.

###1119###
Be not so proud of that which heaven lent,
The fleeting gift of your unmatched face;
For such perfection is but

###1061###
Whence comes this torment that afflicts mine eye,
To find in beauty such a world of pain?
For when thy perfect form I chance to spy,
My heart is parch'd like fields that beg for rain.
The peerless art that nature did impart,
To grant thy cheek a blush of living rose,
Hath made thy face a weapon and a dart,
Which gives my soul no comfort nor repose.
Why must my sight upon such fairness feed,
If in the feast my spirit is undone?
Thy beauty plants a strange and bitter seed,
That grows to sorrow when the day is done.
   So is thy presence but a cruel art,
   To show me heav’n, and then to break my heart.

###1062###
That Time shall steal the treasure of thy face,
And with his wrinkles plough thy smoothest brow,
I know too well, and see the coming space
Where all that is thy glory is not now.
He shall with cruel fingers dim thine eyes,
And turn to silver all thy threads of gold;
His theft shall be what all the world doth prize,
To make thee common, wither'd, poor, and old.
But ere that day shall turn thy youth to night,
And in his ledger write thy beauty's sum,
My verse shall build for thee a truer light,
To stand against the ages yet to come.
   So in this ink thy fairness shall not fade,
   But have a summer that can know no shade.

###1063###
Since thou, my sun, art from my presence gone,
A second winter falls upon the year;
The birds are silent at the lonely dawn,
And every pleasing object seems severe.
The fairest flower, in its proudest dress,
Appears to me as if it wore a shroud;
The world is clothed in common sulleness,
And thy sad absence is a constant cloud.
I seek thy voice in every passing wind,
Thy bright reflection in the clearest stream,
But empty longing is the all I find,
The waking echo of a sleeping dream.
   For naught hath beauty, worth, or life for me,
   Unless my world be decorated with thee.

###1064###
What civil war is in my loving breast,
That finds a pleasure in the pain you give?
My heart, by your own sweet disdain oppressed,
Yet by that same disdain desires to live.
Your frown, a cloud upon a summer's day,
Doth fright the birds of comfort from their song;
And yet I would not have it go away,
For in its strangeness I have gaz'd too long.
So is my love a creature taught to feed
On bitter herbs instead of wholesome fare,
And in your coldest and most cruel deed,
It finds a reason to subdue despair.
   Thus am I captive, yet I hold no key,
   For my own jailer is a part of me.

###1065###
O lonely moon, that climb'st the vault of night,
Dost thou look down upon my wretched state?
Thou art a witness to this candle's light,
By which I mourn my solitary fate.
Thou seest how my sleepless eyes do burn,
And how my quill doth scratch upon the page,
To mark the lessons that my grief must learn,
And with sad verses try my war to wage.
Perhaps thou see'st my love in some far place,
Who sleeps, nor dreams of this my heavy woe;
Perhaps thy beams do fall upon that face
Which is the only heaven I can know.
   Shine gently on her, though she thinks not on
   The one who watches till thy light is gone.

###1066###
Let not the scholar praise the distant stars,
Nor tell me of the fire in their spheres;
For I have seen, beyond these earthly bars,
A light that banishes all doubts and fears.
I have no need of what the heavens lend,
Nor that cold beauty which they do profess;
For in thy two dear eyes, my sweetest friend,
I find a far more perfect loveliness.
Let others chart the course of planets bright,
And in their motions read a destiny;
I am content with thy more certain light,
And find my future and my world in thee.
   So let them have their skies, both far and wide,
   Whilst I have thee, and naught I need beside.

###1067###
The world's a playhouse, and we mortals bear
Our comic masks, or tragic faces show,
To strut and fret and fill the common air
With all our passions, for an hour or so.
Each man believes his part the first of all,
And speaks his lines as if they were divine;
He hears no prompter waiting for his fall,
Nor sees the limits of the play's design.
But when thou tread'st the boards, the scene is chang'd,
The other players fade to shades and dust;
By thy perfection is the plot arrang'd,
And in thy truth all other parts are rust.
   For thou art both the substance and the art,
   And I, the audience, have learn'd my part.

###1068###
My pen is dipp'd in sorrow, and the ink
Which stains this page is black as my despair;
It writes the heavy thoughts my mind must think,
And lends a sad voice to my silent prayer.
Each stroke a tear, each letter is a sigh,
Each word a fragment of a heart that breaks;
This poem is the question asking why
My constant love such little difference makes.
It cannot paint the red upon thy lip,
Nor catch the living sparkle in thine eye;
From truth's fair garden it can only snip
A wilted flower that was born to die.
   And so my verse, which strives to give thee praise,
   But chronicles the sorrow of my days.

###1069###
When anxious silence holds the world in thrall,
And weary thoughts possess my waking mind,
Upon the memory of thy voice I call,
A sweeter music than I e'er could find.
It falls upon my soul like gentle rain,
And bids the flowers of my spirit grow;
It soothes the sharpest and most bitter pain,
And makes a quiet solace from my woe.
Though envious distance may our persons part,
And I not hear that sound which I hold dear,
I keep its echo locked within my heart,
And in my fancy I can make it near.
   For in the court of memory, thy voice
   Doth make my sad and drooping soul rejoice.

###1070###
That thieving Time, with his relentless hand,
Will steal the rose that on thy soft cheek lies,
And take from thee what none can countermand,
The youthful fire that sparkles in thine eyes;
That he will change thy hair of golden hue,
And with his age make all thy beauties fade,
Is truth as certain as the sky is blue,
A heavy price for being mortal made.
But let him plunder, let him do his worst,
And with his spoils enrich his endless store;
The love which in my constant heart was nursed,
He cannot touch, nor make it less or more.
   For though thy form to his decree must bend,
   My love is changeless and shall have no end.

###1071###
What wretched poison hath my fancy wrought,
That turns a glance into a cause for dread?
This jealousy, from idle nothings caught,
Doth plant a thorny crown upon my head.
It whispers tales into my listening ear,
Of faithless smiles you give when I'm not there;
It bids me take my every hope and fear
And build of them a castle of despair.
O green-ey'd monster, that dost make the meat
Thou feed'st upon, how hast thou conquer'd me?
Thou mak'st the bitter seem almost as sweet,
And truth itself a form of treachery.
   Thus in my mind a tyrant's war is won,
   And by my own self is my self undone.

###1072###
The fields are white with winter's biting frost,
The wretched branches shiver in the breeze;
The summer's lease of beauty hath been lost,
And frozen silence doth possess the trees.
This barren scene a mirror is, for me,
Of that cold country where my heart doth dwell,
Where thy disdain, for all the world to see,
Hath cast a grim and inescapable spell.
No sun may melt the ice within thy soul,
No hopeful spring may bid thy pity grow;
Thy winter's anger holds complete control,
And buries kindness under drifts of snow.
   So must I in this chilly season find
   The perfect image of thy heart and mind.

###1073###
Why dost thou let a discontented frown
Become a cloud to hide thy face from me?
It casts a shadow on thy beauty's crown,
And robs my world of its felicity.
One scornful look, a word in anger spoken,
Is all it takes to make my daylight gray,
And by that sign, my spirit's peace is broken,
And all the joys of life are stol'n away.
O, know'st thou not the power thou dost wield,
That with a smile my fortune can be made?
Thy kindness is my comfort and my shield,
And in thy anger, I am sore afraid.
   Then banish from thy brow that stormy part,
   And let thy sun shine full upon my heart.

###1074###
Behold me, love, a bankrupt in my state,
Who spent his fortune ere it was possess'd;
For all the riches of my heart, of late,
I wager'd on thee, and have been suppress'd.
I gave thee sighs, which had the price of gold,
And offer'd tears as if they were a prize;
My store of faith, a wealth none can behold,
I laid before the judgment of thine eyes.
But you, like one who will not see my need,
Have taken all, and given naught again;
And by your cold and avaricious deed,
Have left me with the poverty of pain.
   So I am ruin'd, having spent my all
   For but a hope that never came to call.

###1075###
When restful sleep doth capture my worn sense,
And from this world of sorrow sets me free,
I fly to thee, my soul's sole recompense,
And in my dreams I am at peace with thee.
There, thy dear hand in mine I gently press,
And gaze upon thy face without a tear;
There, I receive a look of tenderness,
And speak the love that waking I do fear.
But when the dawn, that cruel and jealous light,
Doth break the spell that fantasy has spun,
It steals away the treasure of my night,
And all my comfort is again undone.
   Thus I prefer my dreams and their sweet lie,
   To this true world, where I but live to die.

###1076###
How can a thing so wicked be so fair,
Or such a cruel heart so sweetly speak?
Thy beauty is a well-disguised snare,
That makes the strongest resolution weak.
Thy words are music, but they tell a lie,
Thy smile a sun that shines on barren ground;
The heaven that is painted in thine eye
Is where my spirit's greatest loss is found.
Thou art a flower with a poison'd root,
A golden cup that holds a bitter draught,
A tree that bears a fair but fatal fruit,
A ship of hope by hidden treachery raft.
   So is thy form a beautiful disguise,
   For the cold torment that within thee lies.

###1077###
My humble pen cannot thy beauty praise,
Nor can my words do justice to thy worth;
They are but pilgrims walking common ways,
Who speak of heavens they have not seen on earth.
I strive to capture with my faulty art
The living light that in thy being dwells,
But my poor verse can only show a part,
And not the magic that my soul compels.
For who can paint a fragrance, or make fast
The fleeting music of a gentle sigh?
Thy excellence is too profound and vast
For such a weak and mortal tool as I.
   And so I fail, and in my failure, show
   A greater wonder than the skilled could know.

###1078###
Though many leagues of weary land and sea
Divide my body from thy body's grace,
Thou canst not take my constant mind from me,
Nor from my heart thy well-beloved face.
For in the chambers of my memory,
I hold thy picture, faithfully enshrined;
And there I turn, for sweetest company,
When all the waking world has been unkind.
There is no distance that my soul can know,
For in a thought, I am again with thee;
And by this art, my faithful love doth grow,
And mocks the bounds of what can truly be.
   So though we're parted, we are not astray,
   My love can travel what obstructs the way.

###1079###
I look'd upon thy kind and honest face,
And saw therein a haven for my trust;
I heard thee speak with such a sober grace,
I thought thy soul incapable of rust.
I took thy hand and swore myself thy friend,
And built my fortress on that steady ground,
Believing our true bond would have no end,
That in thy faith, my safety could be found.
But I have seen the serpent in the flower,
The subtle turning of a look or word;
And in the space of one deceiving hour,
The sweet song of our friendship is unheard.
   So farewell, friend, whose friendship was a show;
   The deepest wound is from a secret foe.

###1080###
If all the verse wherein my love is penn'd
Should fail to move thee, or to win regard,
And if my sorrow's tale should have no end,
But meet a judgment that is cold and hard;
Then grant me this one mercy in thy pride,
A single grace before my hope is slain:
Do not with laughter turn my plea aside,
Nor with a public triumph show my pain.
Let but a silent pity in thine eye
Acknowledge that you see my wretched case;
A fleeting glance, a soft and quiet sigh,
Is all I ask thee from that powerful place.
   For such a crumb might save me from despair,
   And show that some small part of thee doth care.

###1041###
When in thy presence all my senses fail,
And reason's voice can not be heard or found,
My captive heart, held fast within thy jail,
Doth find its freedom on that holy ground.
For there my thoughts, that were so wild before,
Are tamed and humbled by thy sovereign grace,
And all the worldly strife I did deplore
Doth vanish when I look upon thy face.
And shouldst thou frown, a winter chills my soul,
And all my world is cast in shades of night;
Thy smile alone can make my spirit whole
And turn my deepest darkness into light.
So by thy moods my very life is swayed,
For in thy being, all my own is made.

###1042###
When I do count the ways that thou art fair,
My wits do fail, my tongue is stricken dumb;
I cannot match with words thy golden hair,
From which all my poetic fancies come.
To praise thine eyes, I steal from stars their light,
To paint thy cheek, I borrow from the rose,
But all my verse, for all its striving might,
A faithless and a poor reflection shows.
For how can ink describe the sun's warm rays,
Or mortal pen portray a thing divine?
My art is beggared by my ceaseless praise,
And cannot capture what is truly thine.
So I'll be mute, and let my silence prove
What speech cannot: the measure of my love.



###1141###
When I upon thy wondrous beauty gaze,
Which doth outshine the sun's most glorious light,
My soul is captured in a happy maze
That banishes the darkness of the night.
Thy voice is music of a sweeter sound
Than any lute that e'er was played with grace;
No greater treasure can on earth be found,
Nor heaven's likeness in another's face.
The fairest rose must wither and must fade,
The sharpest winter makes the rivers slow,
But thy perfection was divinely made
And time's decay thy form shall never know.
So let me ever in thy presence be,
For all the world is nothing without thee.

###1142###
Now that my sun from my sad world is gone,
A heavy sorrow occupies my heart,
And on my soul great shadows fall upon,
To feel the sting that keeps our lives apart.
The weary minutes like to snails do creep,
And bring no comfort, only bitter woe;
I cannot find sweet solace in my sleep,
For thoughts of thee do make my sorrows grow.
Thy face in memory I strive to find,
A fleeting vision that is held so dear,
Yet this same image tortures my poor mind,
And from mine eye compels a silent tear.
Come back to me, and end this cruel pain,
And with thy light, make my world whole again.

###1143###
Though cruel Time, with his destructive hand,
Doth

###1261###
When cruelest Fortune doth my works assail,
And worldly cares upon my spirit feed,
My tired muse begins to droop and fail,
And finds no succour for its present need.
I look upon my verse with heavy heart,
And think my labours are but spent in vain,
That I have played a fool's unskilful part
To get from poesy but barren pain.
Yet when the thought of thee doth cross my mind,
A sudden sun a brighter scene doth make,
And in that light, new argument I find,
And write again, for thy most precious sake.
   So is thy love the font of all my art,
   And thy dear name, the comfort of my heart.

###1262###
Mine eye, a thief, hath pilfered thy sweet face,
And in my heart's dark chamber holds it fast,
Wherein no other image may find place,
For of all treasures, thine is first and last.
My thoughts, like guards, do keep a zealous watch,
Lest any rival semblance should draw near,
And at my memory's gate they lift the latch
For none but thee, who art esteemed so dear.
But oh, this prison where thy likeness dwells
Doth but torment the keeper with his prize,
For so thy painted shadow love compels,
It makes me starve for what the sight denies.
   So am I rich in show, and poor in truth,
   To hold thy picture, yet to lose thy youth.

###1263###
When winter lays its hand on field and wood,
And all the cheerful birds have ceased to sing,
The frozen stream, where once the willow stood,
Forgets the promise of the coming spring.
So feels my soul when thou art far away,
A barren landscape, clad in frosty white,
That knows no comfort from the sun's pale ray,
And finds a deeper darkness in the night.
No inward warmth can this my climate change,
No thought of summer can the chill remove,
My joys take wing, my hopes themselves estrange,
When I am severed from the one I love.
   But at thy sight, the suns of June appear,
   And in a moment, banish all the year.

###1264###
Why dost thou cloak thy thoughts in such disguise,
And speak in riddles that I cannot read?
The honest love that in my bosom lies
Deserves a truer and a plainer creed.
I search thy words to know what they confess,
But find them veils that thy true mind conceals;
Thy speech is but a richly patterned dress
That hides the very form which it reveals.
Ah, do not use such cunning with a friend
Who offers thee a heart that is so plain,
Lest in these shadows, love should find its end,
And trust, once broken, cannot heal again.
   For if thy meaning match not what I see,
   Then I love falsehood when I worship thee.

###1265###
My love is not proclaimed with outward show,
Nor gilded phrases that the tongue can speak;
It is a silent stream, whose currents flow
Too deep for words, which are by nature weak.
For what is speech but air, a fleeting sound,
That may with equal ease bless or betray?
The truest vows on truer faith are found,
And live in deeds that shall not pass away.
Let others praise with well-rehearsed art,
And count the measure of their flattery;
I have no skill to play so false a part,
My quiet heart must my sole witness be.
   Then read my love not in the words I say,
   But in the silence where my feelings stay.

###1266###
As some poor merchant, whose last ship is lost,
Doth stand and gaze upon the hungry sea,
And reckons up the dire and heavy cost
Of all that fortune hath now snatched from me;
So I, made bankrupt of thy company,
Do count my sorrows on the lonely shore,
Lamenting that which was my treasury,
A wealth that being gone, returns no more.
No other venture can my state repair,
No tide can bring such precious cargo home;
My enterprise is turned to blank despair,
My only port, the darkness of the tomb.
   For what is all the world, its gain or grace,
   If I am banished from thy blessed face?

###1267###
Each flower that springs from the forgotten earth,
Each blade of grass that drinks the morning dew,
Is by thy presence given a second birth,
And borrows all its beauty fresh from you.
The jealous rose, that sees thy cheek so bright,
Doth blush for shame that it is less than fair;
The lily, seeing thee, grows pale with fright,
And cannot with thy purer white compare.
The very sun, that gives the world its heat,
Seems but a candle when thy glories shine,
And envious clouds in solemn council meet
To hide a radiance that is more divine.
   If senseless things are by thy beauty moved,
   How must I be, who have thy beauty loved?

###1268###
Time's cruel hand shall carve upon thy brow,
And steal the treasure that thy youth doth hold;
The rosy lips that are so tempting now
Shall fade and wither, and the story told.
The raven hair shall turn to silver thread,
Those star-like eyes lose their commanding fire;
All that is fair must join the silent dead,
And unto dust and nothingness retire.
But in my verse, thy beauty shall not cease,
But live immortal, proof against decay,
And find from Time's own ravages, release,
When monuments of stone have passed away.
   So shall you live when you are naught but dust,
   Held in the keeping of this poem's trust.

###1269###
When in the sleepless watches of the night,
My thoughts, like restless pilgrims, turn to thee,
Thy sacred image, beautiful and bright,
Doth rise and shine in darkest memory.
I close mine eyes, the better to behold
The perfect form that daytime doth deny,
A richer vision than all gems or gold,
That only my adoring soul can spy.
But then the dawn, that envious, waking foe,
Doth steal this blessed phantom from my sight,
And I am left to wonder at my woe,
That day should bring a sorrow, and not light.
   Thus day my eyes, and night my mind doth keep,
   For thoughts of thee, from thee deny me sleep.

###1270###
I am a barren and unfertile land,
Wherein no plant of any worth can grow,
A stretch of worthless and forgotten sand,
Where only winds of discontent do blow.
My wit is dull, my spirit is brought low,
My name obscured by some unhappy fate,
The seeds of joy that others seem to sow
Find in my breast a soil to generate but hate.
Yet if thy love, a sunbeam from the sky,
Should chance to fall upon this wretched ground,
Then virtues from my very faults would fly,
And in my desert, paradise be found.
   So by thy grace, I am transformed anew,
   And all I am or may be, comes from you.

###1271###
The poet's pen, the painter's skilful hand,
Do strive in vain to capture thy sweet look;
For nature's work, they cannot understand,
Nor write thy graces in their feeblest book.
The painter mixes colours, red and white,
To make a cheek that might with thine compare,
But can he paint a soul that is so bright,
Or catch the living spirit dwelling there?
The poet searches for a worthy rhyme,
To tell the world how fair and good thou art,
But finds his words are conquered by old Time,
And cannot show the treasure of thy heart.
   Thus art is shamed, and craft is overthrown,
   For thy perfection can by love alone be known.

###1272###
Why should I weep that youth must pass away,
Or that the lines of age will mark my face?
Why should I fear the coming of the day
When time shall rob me of my former grace?
I have a shield against such idle dread,
A potent charm to hold despair at bay;
For in the memory of a word you said,
My heart finds summer in the winter's day.
That single kindness, like a fixed bright star,
Shines ever constant in my changing sky,
And proves that true affection, near or far,
Is but one thing that cannot fade or die.
   So let time do its worst, I shall not care,
   For in my heart, you are forever fair.

###1273###
My tongue is made a traitor by my heart,
And cannot speak the thing it feels so well;
It plays a stammering and enfeebled part,
And but a fraction of the truth can tell.
When I would praise thee for thy boundless worth,
My words grow faint and falter in their quest,
As if the grandest phrases of the earth
Were all too poor for what is in my breast.
I bid my mind to find some newer way,
Some fresher image, or some sweeter sound,
But all my thoughts thy beauty doth dismay,
And in thy presence, no fit words are found.
   So must I stand, in silent awe of thee,
   And let my worship in my looking be.

###1274###
Oft in my dreams, a spectre of your face
Appears to me from out the misty past;
A fleeting vision, full of mournful grace,
Too sad to welcome, and too dear to last.
It speaks no word, but in its hollow eye,
I read the story of a love undone,
And hear the echo of a last goodbye,
Beneath a cold and disapproving sun.
This nightly ghost doth haunt my weary bed,
And brings again the sorrow and the pain,
A living torment, risen from the dead,
To make my wounded spirit bleed again.
   So even sleep, which should give all men rest,
   Brings but your shadow to a hopeless breast.

###1275###
Thou art a music that I cannot hear,
A beauty that my poor eyes cannot see,
Yet is thy essence to my soul more dear
Than all the world that is revealed to me.
For what I see is subject to decay,
And what I hear is but a passing breath,
But thy true self shall never fade away,
And knows no conquest by the hand of death.
This inner truth, this spirit pure and bright,
My thoughts embrace when senses are denied,
A constant source of comfort and of light,
Wherein my hopes and all my faith abide.
   So though my earthly eye see not thy face,
   My spirit holds thee in a surer place.

###1276###
As a poor actor on a humble stage
Forgets the lines he had resolved to say,
And by the fear that doth his mind engage,
Doth mar the meaning of the author's play;
So I, in presence of your majesty,
Forget the well-composed and loving speech
That I had planned to offer unto thee,
And find all eloquence beyond my reach.
My part is love, and yet I speak it not,
My cue is passion, yet I stand stone-still,
The plot of all my being is forgot,
And my own silence works against my will.
   Blame not my tongue, which fears to be so bold,
   But read the story my two eyes have told.

###1277###
What is this life, but for a constant war
Between the spirit and the baser clay?
The soul desires to be a shining star,
The flesh to dally and to go astray.
One part of me doth seek a higher plane,
Aspiring to a thing that is divine;
The other part, rebellious and vain,
Doth pull me down to be no more than thine.
For in thy love, both heaven and earth I find,
The sacred passion and the sweet delight,
The peace of soul and turbulence of mind,
The calm of daytime and the storm of night.
   So in this conflict, I am pleased to be,
   A soul in heaven, while a slave to thee.

###1278###
No jewelled crown, nor sceptre made of gold,
No kingly throne whereon the mighty sit,
Can such a wealth of true dominion hold
As is contained within your subtle wit.
For kings rule men through power and through fear,
And force a subject homage from the knee;
But thy sharp mind, so brilliant and so clear,
Makes all who know it serve thee willingly.
Thy words are laws that reason must approve,
Thy judgements are decrees that must be right;
Thou rul'st a kingdom by the power of love,
And conquer'st hearts by wisdom's gentle light.
   So am I proud to be thy loyal thrall,
   Whose sweetest bondage is to give thee all.

###1279###
The sun at morning, glorious to the view,
Doth climb the heavens with a golden face,
But oft a cloud will hide its royal hue
And stain its journey with a dark disgrace.
The sweetest rose that in the garden grows,
Though fair its colour and though rich its scent,
Is food for cankers, as the gardener knows,
And by a worm its beauty may be rent.
There is no earthly thing so strong or pure,
That is not subject to some foul defect,
No state so perfect that it can endure,
No grace that envy will not disrespect.
   Only my love for thee remains without a stain,
   And in its constancy, finds sun in spite of rain.

###1280###
Let this my verse be as a looking-glass,
Wherein thy beauty may reflected be,
So that when fleeting years of youth shall pass,
Thy younger self thou mayst forever see.
For here thy lips their redness shall retain,
Thine eyes their light which time can not make dim;
Here shall thy grace defy the sun and rain,
And live beyond life's short and bitter interim.
This rhyming portrait, drawn with faithful art,
Shall be a witness for a future age,
And show the world the treasure of thy heart,
Forever captured on this timeless page.
   So when thy mortal form to dust is gone,
   Thy truest essence in my love lives on.

###1201###
When Cynthia's lamp doth govern all the night,
And o'er the silent world her silver reign,
I find no comfort in her borrowed light,
And think her cold and regal beauty vain.
For 'tis another planet I adore,
Whose living lustre is a waking gleam,
That makes my hungry soul but long for more,
And turns my truth into a fevered dream.
Thy beauty's grace is of a kind so true,
It puts to shame each faint and distant star,
It doth the very face of heaven imbue
With wonder at what mortal creatures are.
So let the moon her lonely vigil see;
Her reign is naught when I do gaze on thee.

###1202###
That thievish Time, which plunders youthful prime,
Hath drawn his map of wrinkles on thy face,
Commits to memory his cruelest crime,
And robs thy summer of its sunlit grace.
The lines that winter carves upon thy brow,
Where once the silken threads of youth were gold,
Do but declare what all must suffer now,
As every story by the years is told.
Yet what that tyrant from the eye doth steal,
Is but the treasure that the world hath lost;
My constant heart shall secretly reveal
And know thy fairness, whatsoe'er the cost.
For in the faithful ledger of my mind,
Thy perfect, ageless beauty I shall find.

###1203###
Let princes build their monuments of stone,
Which shall with war and bitter envy dust;
Their gilded thrones shall soon be overthrown,
And all their swords and sceptres turn to rust.
Their brief renown in ages shall decay,
And none shall look on them with loving eyes;
Their fleeting triumphs quickly pass away,
Their memory the future will despise.
But I shall raise for thee a truer art,
A living shrine where thy sweet virtues live,
Engraved forever on my constant heart,
And in these lines a second breath I give.
So when their tombs in ruin men shall see,
The world shall still read all the best of thee.

###1204###
How can I call this weary time a day,
When thou, my sun, dost shed on me no light?
For all my joy with thee is gone away,
And noon itself is blacker than the night.
The world for me hath lost its cheerful hue,
My heavy thoughts are garments dark and sad,
Nor can the morn my spirit's strength renew,
Remembering the happiness I had.
My reason chides my soul as most unkind,
To hang such clouds before my outward sight;
But reason is a fool, and love is blind,
And cannot give a grieving soul its right.
For what is day, when we are kept apart?
'Tis but a shadow in my broken heart.

###1205###
That jealous madness which offends the eye,
Doth plant a bitter seed within my breast,
Which makes my fearful, watching mind to spy,
And from my spirit steals all peaceful rest.
A casual glance, a word I deem mis-thought,
A smile bestowed where I would have it wrought
Upon my face alone, a kindness ill,
Can overthrow my weak and lovesick will.
O, teach my heart a surer way to cure
This green-eyed sickness that I must endure,
Or else my thoughts, where dark suspicions lie,
Shall cause this poor and foolish love to die.
For if in thee my faith hath no true art,
Then jealousy must tear my soul apart.

###1206###
That time of year in me thou mayst now hold,
When summer's lease hath met its final year,
And chilling winds of autumn whisper cold,
And all the forest's trembling leaves are sere.
The joyous birds that did frequent the bough
Have flown to warmer climes, and silent now
Is all the choir that made such pleasant song,
Where days grow short and lonely nights are long.
Yet in this bare and ruin'd state dost take
My soul, and from my final embers make
A warmth that doth defy the coming brief,
And finds a solace in my winter's grief.
And thus thou see'st a late and fading fire,
Made strong by nearness to its own desire.

###1207###
My love for thee contains all seasons' spring,
When thy sweet humour makes the flowers sing;
And passion's summer, with its burning heat,
Is in thy touch, so wonderfully sweet.
And autumn's gentle richness I can see,
When quiet contemplation dwells in thee,
Whose bounty all my hungry spirit shows,
As from thy fertile mind my wisdom grows.
But when a frown thy perfect brow doth chill,
My world is winter, and my heart stands still;
Thy cold displeasure makes the landscape white,
And robs my senses of all warm delight.
Thus in thy moods my seasons have their state;
'Tis thou alone my weather canst create.

###1208###
When I am lock'd within the arms of sleep,
My soul doth hasten to a world more deep,
Where thy dear image is so brightly shown,
A truth my waking eyes have never known.
And in that place, I to thy shade arise,
Uncheck'd by scornful looks from others' eyes;
Thy spirit's form, so gentle, warm, and true,
Doth make my world of shadows feel brand new.
But oh, how cruel the light that makes things seem
To break the bond of my most perfect dream;
And as the truth of morning starts to break,
My greater sorrow I must feel awake.
For day to me a lonely prison block,
Announced by ringing of the hateful clock.

###1209###
When thou dost turn on me that lovely face,
I feel a joy that time cannot disgrace;
But should a cloud of anger cause a frown,
The sun itself from heaven tumbles down.
One harsh word spoken makes a stormy sky,
As nature's gentle breezes cease and fly;
One look of scorn becomes a tempest storm
That robs my world of everything that's warm.
So do not let thy perfect lips thus speak,
Nor draw such dreadful lines upon thy cheek,
For in thy aspect all my hope and fear
Is writ as plainly as a falling tear.
Let not thy mood a tragedy now be;
Be kind in aspect, when thou look'st on me.

###1210###
My tongue is bankrupt when it comes to praise,
And cannot match the glory of thy days;
The store of words I have is held unsung,
For thy perfection ties my feeble tongue.
My poet's wit, which others may find skill,
Is but a servant to my greater will,
Which longs to show how thou art truly poor
In faults, and rich in virtues evermore.
But all my verse, an insufficient art,
Can not the fullness of thy soul impart;
Thy worth's a summit, so immensely high,
My lowly rhyme cannot but pass it by.
And so I fail, and can but mutely write,
A shadow falling from a perfect light.

###1211###
Were all the world's musicians to be found
And from their instruments to draw a sound
So rich 'twould make an angel bend an ear,
It would not be as sweet, nor half so dear
As when you speak one word. That simple note
Upon the very air itself doth float;
No lute nor viol hath so fine a string
As those sweet vocal cords that make you sing.
And so let others with their music play
And think to hold the raptured soul in sway;
Your quiet voice can make a passion rise
That shames the loudest chorus in the skies.
Let them have music; I need no such art,
Your speaking voice is music to my heart.

###1212###
I thought our bond was truer than a friend,
But now I see that all good things must end,
For you have stolen what was held in trust,
And turn'd my faithful friendship into dust.
He was my other self, a wicked deed
To plant in him betrayal's cursed seed;
And she, my love, whose faith she did profess
And swear, now clothes me in this heaviness.
Was it not sin enough she stray'd from me?
But must she find her sinful joy with thee?
The double wound, the loss of love and name,
Hath brought my spirit to the brink of shame.
A threefold cord is broken now in two,
And I am left with neither her nor you.

###1213###
When wasteful time shall grind this present age
To naught but dust upon a history's page,
And all the beauty that we now see fade
To some forgotten and forsaken shade;
When brass and marble, with their boastful might,
Are swallowed by oblivion and night,
And all the glories of this world at last
Are but a whisper of a forgotten past;
Then shall these humble lines of ink still see
A life that looks on all eternity.
For here thy virtue in this verse shall shine,
And make thy memory a holy shrine.
My blackened ink will make thy name to prove
Far stronger than the tomb of any love.

###1214###
The world's a theatre, and all we take a stage,
Where each man plays his part from youth to age,
And I have watch'd full many act their heart
In roles of sorrow, playing a fool's part.
I've seen the king strut through his passing scene,
And heard the lover tell what might have been;
The poor philosopher, with all his grief,
Recites a wisdom that is all too brief.
But when you enter, all the foolish play
Is cast aside, and heralds a new day;
Your every motion doth a story tell
Where truth and beauty have agreed to dwell.
So let them have their acts; my play I write
To frame the glory of your coming light.

###1215###
My heart within thy bosom finds its rest,
And I do treasure thine within my breast;
A fair exchange we made, and so I hold
A prize more precious than the finest gold.
And for thy heart, which thou to me dost give,
I vow to cherish it for as I live,
To keep it safe from sorrow and from harm,
And guard it with mine own, to keep it warm.
Thus being two, we are in spirit one,
Our separate lives into a single run;
For what can injure me must injure thine,
And thy concerns are now forever mine.
This bond we make, no power can remove;
So let our hearts together live and prove.

###1216###
Let astronomers gaze upon the skies
And map the course of stars with searching eyes;
I need not look to that far, distant bright,
To find a compass in the darkest night.
For in thy face two brighter stars are fair,
That shame the constellations in the air;
No heavenly body can with them compare,
Nor teach the lost soul rescued from despair.
Let them praise Venus, or the distant Mars,
I find my guidance in these mortal stars,
Whose gentle glance, a true and loving light,
Can make a morning of my saddest sight.
Thy eyes, two suns, shine down on me above,
The fixed and constant pole-stars of my love.

###1217###
I cannot flatter thee, as some would say,
Whose silver tongues can make the truth give way,
And build a shrine of words to please thy heart,
But hide a hollow and a faithless art.
I cannot claim thy lips like roses sung
By every poet's false and eager tongue,
Nor say thy cheeks could make the lilies false,
Erecting praise on praise, like prison walls.
My love is plainer, and my tongue speaks truth,
It sees the passing of thy perfect youth;
I do not feign, nor make a gilded lie
That with the turning of the years will die.
My compliment is simple, stark, and plain:
I love thee truly. All the rest is vain.

###1218###
When I am lost upon a sea of doubt,
And all my hopes and certitudes are tossed about,
When stormy passions my poor judgment guide,
And in the crushing waves my sorrows ride,
There is a constant star that shines so dark
Cannot obscure its light from my poor bark.
It is thy love, a beacon shining high
Within the chaos of my mental sky,
Whose steady beam, whose worth is never known,
Though by its guiding grace my way is shown.
So let the tempests of my fortune prove
How firm the anchor of thy constant love.
My wandering ship to its safe harbour I'll remove,
Held ever true and steadfast by thy love.

###1219###
Thy beauty is a source of my despair,
For it is so intolerably fair
That other sights which once gave me my grace
Now seem but shadows when I see thy face.
The sweetest music which mine ears could see
Now sounds but noise when I do think on thee;
Thy virtues are a torment and a harm,
For they enslave me with their perfect charm.
Each grace a wound, each smile a piercing sound,
My heart with lovely injuries is bound,
And yet so sweet a pain I find it, yet
I would not for the world be from it set.
Thou art my sickness, and my only cure,
A pleasing pain that I must still endure.

###1220###
This solemn promise that I make thee now,
Shall not grow weary as the seasons bow;
My heart shall not, with frosted age, grow cold,
Nor shall my story of affection be told
As some light fancy that would change or fade,
A summer's pastime, curiously made.
No, like the northern star that does not run
Its changing course as doth the faithless sun,
My love for thee shall faithfully remain,
And over all my other thoughts shall reign.
My vow of constancy shall bind me fast,
And from this day shall to the very last.
So know this truth, which time cannot undo:
My final, first, and only love is you.

###1281###
When envious morning shows its golden sight,
And robs the world of night's more gentle hue,
I find thy face a far more wondrous light,
And deem its beauty ever fresh and new.
No precious jewel from the earth is stone
So rich as that which in thine eyes I find,
For nature's fairest treasures, when alone,
Are nought compared to what thou hast enshrined.
So let the poet praise the sun's warm grace,
Or sing of flowers that in the meadows throng,
He doth but err, for in thy perfect face,
All earthly beauty finds its truest song.
   And so all praise that to the world is due,
   Is but a shadow and a dream of you.

###1282###
How can the world proceed in cheerful day,
When from my senses thou art torn away?
How can my heart find reason still to see,
When all its light did draw its life from me?
My every thought is but a constant pain,
A wish to have thee in my sight again,
For in thy absence, sorrow holds my heart,
And makes each joyful thing seem torn apart.
I look upon the sun, but feel no heat,
I hear sweet music, yet it is not wrought
With any comfort, for our souls did meet,
And parting this sad quietude has taught.
   So must I count the hours till I may be,
   Delivered from this grief, and joined with thee.

###1283###
Let cruel Time with his swift-footed crime
Assault the young and make their beauty fade;
My love for thee shall live in this my rhyme,
A monument that cannot be unmade.
Though rosy cheeks must lose their vibrant youth,
And raven locks must turn themselves to snow,
Thy memory is bound within this truth,
And in these lines, forever young shall grow.
For what is fleeting beauty but a breath,
A fragile bloom that winter must make bare?
My verse shall be thy triumph over death,
And guard thy likeness from time's hungry snare.
   So, ages hence, when all else is forgot,
   Thy grace in these eternal lines shall not.

###1284###
When thoughtful silence captures the long night,
And lesser lamps do litter up the skies,
I have no need of their convenient light,
But find my truest north within thine eyes.
Those twin-born spheres, my soul's unerring guide,
More constant than the stars that wax and wane,
Within whose deep and certain truth abide
The maps that make my crooked pathways plain.
And though my ship upon a sea of dream
Be sorely tried by doubt's tempestuous cost,
I fix my gaze upon their steady gleam,
And in that vision, never can be lost.
   So let the world be dark, I shall not mind,
   Whilst in thine eyes my firmament I find.

###1285###
I see the world with all its painted show,
And hear the words that men do not believe;
'Tis but a stage where false affections grow,
And cunning hearts the simple folk deceive.
They fashion praise from insincere an art,
And hide their envy with a smiling face,
But thou, my love, dost have a truthful heart,
Wherein deceit can find no dwelling place.
For in thy plain and honest look I read
A testament that puts all doubt to flight,
And from the prison of their words I'm freed,
Finding in thee a pure and honest light.
   So let them play their parts with hollow pride,
   Whilst I in thy sweet honesty abide.

###1286###
When weary night invites the world to sleep,
And draws a cloak across the toil of day,
My wakeful eyes their lonely vigil keep,
And chase all hope of peaceful rest away.
For in the dark, thy phantom I do long
To hold, but find my reaching arms are wrong,
Thy form, a torment in my troubled mind,
A precious thing I search for but can't find.
And then I think upon thy gentle face,
And all the store of thy most lovely grace,
Which but renews the sorrow of my sight,
And makes an endless torment of the night.
   Thus in thy absence I can find no rest,
   Until by thy true presence I am blest.

###1287###
The sweetest music that was ever found
To soothe the soul or make a king rejoice,
Is but a harsh and melancholy sound
Compared, my love, unto thy gentle voice.
When thou dost speak, the birds forget to sing,
As if ashamed their chorus to impart,
And hang their heads, for thou alone dost bring
A truer music to the listening heart.
Each whispered word is like a perfect note
That on the stillness of the air doth float,
A melody that doth my senses bind,
And leaves all other, lesser sounds behind.
   For in that sound, a magic I can hear,
   That makes thee ever present, ever dear.

###1288###
A creeping doubt, the poison of my fear,
Doth ask if I alone possess thy heart,
And questions if some other may be dear,
Or play in thy affections some small part.
This jealous thought, a most unwelcome guest,
Doth drive all comfort from my weary thought,
And puts my steadfast loyalty to test,
With wretched fancies that have come from nought.
It whispers when another's friendly eye
Doth linger on thy beauty overlong,
And makes my soul in silent anguish sigh,
Though reason tells me that my fear is wrong.
   Yet reason fails when love's own doubt I see,
   And praise from others steals thy gaze from me.

###1289###
I see the seasons of the passing year,
How summer's pride to autumn's gold must bow,
How winter makes the cheerful world look drear,
And springtime's green forgets its solemn vow.
The rose that doth in June so sweetly sing
Of perfect form, by August is made old;
The very hope that buds in early spring
Is slain at last by winter's biting cold.
But in the constant season of thy brow,
No change is wrought by time's most cruel hand;
The love I saw is still the love I now
Perceive, a rock in time's uncertain sand.
   The world may alter, and its glories wane,
   But thy fair truth shall constantly remain.

###1290###
The world's a theatre where, upon a stage,
We strut and fret our hour in borrowed light,
And play the parts assigned to us by age,
Until we fade into eternal night.
Each man learns lines to speak a hollow part,
And paints a smile to hide a heart of woe,
His passions learned by rote, a feigned art,
A fleeting fame the only prize to know.
But thou art not a player in this scene,
Nor dost thou act a role for others' eyes;
Thou art the truth, the constant, and the queen,
In whom no shade of false performance lies.
   So let them play their games of make-believe,
   For in thy soul, the only truth's to grieve.

###1291###
Thy heart is made of some unpitying steel,
To look upon my pain and show no sign,
For all the wounding sorrow that I feel
Doth seem to draw no sympathy from thine.
Thy words of scorn are arrows to my soul,
That leave me bleeding, wretched, and worn,
And though I give to thee my spirit whole,
Thou payest me with cruelty and scorn.
What pleasure canst thou in this torment find,
To see a loyal heart so sadly break?
Is it the triumph of a cruel mind
To watch the ruin that thy coldness makes?
   If this be love, to cause such bitter strife,
   Then take at once my miserable life.

###1292###
Sometimes I call to mind a cherished past,
When first I saw thee in that sunlit hour;
The die of my whole fortune then was cast,
And my poor heart submitted to thy power.
I mark the court, the garden, and the place,
The very words of thy first speech I find
Are writ upon my soul; thy perfect grace
Is now the foremost treasure of my mind.
Though many years have passed since that sweet look,
And time hath stolen many things since then,
The memory of the moment that you took
My heart, can make me young and new again.
   That single glance, a gift I still possess,
   To solace me in all my loneliness.

###1293###
Let this my verse forever make a vow,
Which neither time nor distance can make less,
That all my love, which I present thee now,
Shall never know fatigue or faithlessness.
Though other hearts may wander and may stray,
And break the bonds they swore would ever bind,
My own shall be as constant as the day,
And hold thy image ever in my mind.
This sacred oath shall bear the cruellest test,
And stand unbent though all the world may crime;
Of all affections, mine shall be most blest,
For it shall not be overthrown by time.
   Thus by this ink my faith I mean to prove,
   And seal to thee my everlasting love.

###1294###
When melancholy holds me in its gloom,
And shadows fall where sunshine ought to be,
I shut the door upon my sorry room,
And in my thoughts, I turn myself to thee.
For thou art comfort and a gentle soul,
A certain cure for all my inward pain;
Thy memory alone can make me whole,
And bring the light into my world again.
I think upon thy voice, thy walk, thy hair,
And all the darkness that my heart possessed
Doth flee before a vision so fair,
And lead my troubled spirit into rest.
   So when despair my heavy thoughts doth steal,
   The thought of thee is all that can me heal.

###1295###
What paradox is this strange tyrant, Love,
That brings such pleasure and such bitter pain?
It is a thing all other things above,
And yet it falls like cold and chilling rain.
It is a fire that warms a frozen heart,
Yet in its flames a soul can be consumed;
It is the joy from which we cannot part,
And yet a curse to which we all are doomed.
I find in thee my greatest earthly prize,
And yet from thee my deepest sorrows grow;
I seek my heaven in thy loving eyes,
Yet find a hell in fearing they will go.
   Thus bound to thee in pleasure and in strife,
   Thou art my peace, and yet torment my life.

###1296###
When winter lays its hand upon the land,
And clothes the barren fields in bitter white,
The frosted trees in silent sorrow stand,
And sunless days give way to endless night.
The very air seems frozen by the frost,
And all the songs of summer now are lost,
The world itself seems weary, old, and bleak,
And of a coming spring it dare not speak.
But when the thought of thee doth cross my mind,
A sudden summer in my soul I find,
A warmth that bids the winter chill to run,
More potent than the true and living sun.
   So let the seasons bring their frost and fire,
   Thy love's my sun, and all that I require.

###1297###
My clumsy words do thee a great disservice,
When they attempt thy beauty to make plain;
They are but beggars at a palace, nervous,
And fail to capture aught but my own pain.
For how can this my ink and humble art
Convey the light that in thine eyes doth live?
Or show the truth that dwells within thy heart,
And all the boundless comfort thou dost give?
My failing pen cannot describe thy grace,
Nor can my tongue thy virtues rightly tell,
The perfect form of thy most lovely face
Is 'yond the reach of any poet's spell.
   And so I cease, for words cannot impart
   The perfect image printed on my heart.

###1298###
As when a sailor on a starless sea,
Doth lose his way and fears he is undone,
So in this world I oft despair to be,
And find my course is difficult to run.
The waves of fortune, cruel and unkind,
Do toss my little bark with dreadful might,
And in the storm, no solace I can find,
But am consumed by the surrounding night.
But then the thought of thee, a constant star,
Doth break the clouds and show a hopeful gleam,
A certain beacon shining from afar,
The single truth in life's confusing dream.
   So though the seas of this dark world may rage,
   Thy love's the light that doth my fears assuage.

###1299###
Let others seek the glory of a crown,
Or spend their days in quest of worldly prize;
Let them seek praise and fear a rival's frown,
And build their hope on what the world calls wise.
Let them pursue the warrior's bloody fame,
Or hoard up gold with a corrupted hand,
And think the world's applause for their own name
The greatest treasure in this mortal land.
But I, who have been blessed to know thine eyes,
And see the kingdom that within them lies,
Do count such empty triumphs as but dross,
And all their victories a bitter loss.
   For what is power, riches, or renown,
   When thy sweet love is my eternal crown?

###1300###
Whatever worth is found in this my line,
Whatever grace my humble verse can claim,
Is but a gift and portion that is thine,
And serves for nought but to exalt thy name.
My every thought is but thy servant's breath,
My art a mirror where thy form is shown,
To build for thee a house defying death,
Upon a surer ground than marble stone.
And so these poems, children of my art,
I send to thee as tribute from my end,
That they a portion of thy worth impart,
To serve and honour thee, my dearest friend.
   For all that I may write or hope to be,
   Begins, and ends, and finds its all in thee.

###1221###
When I do look upon thy perfect face,
My hopeful spirit doth begin to fail,
Wherein no fault or blemish I can trace,
And o'er my reason passions do prevail.
But from thine eyes, so clear and full of light,
No warming ray of pity can I find,
There shines a winter's day, though seeming bright,
A cruel disdain to my devoted mind.
Why didst thou, Nature, frame a thing so fair,
To hold a heart that knows not how to feel,
And fill her form with beauty past compare,
Then arm her soul with temper made of steel?
So am I bound to what my soul doth slay,
And in thy light, I lose the light of day.

###1222###
That thief, old Time, with his relentless hand,
Doth steal the rose from thy sweet cheek away,
And casts his shadows o'er this pleasant land,
To turn thy golden morning into grey.
He'll dig deep trenches in thy beauty's field,
And dim the lustre of thy sun-like eyes;
The youthful pride thy present form doth wield
Shall be a spoil for his triumphant prize.
Yet fear thou not his ruinous attack,
For in these lines thy grace shall ever live;
This verse shall hold his cruel forces back,
And to thy charms eternal summer give.
So when by Time thy sweetness is defaced,
Within this verse thy youth is ever placed.

###1223###
When gentle night doth wrap the world in peace,
And weary creatures to their slumber go,
My thoughts of thee do find no sweet release,
But in my mind a constant torrent flow.
My heavy eyelids I in vain do close,
For thou, an image, dost before them stand,
Denying me the gift of soft repose,
And hold'st my waking soul in thy command.
My spirit makes a pilgrimage to thee,
Though my poor body on its couch doth lie,
And with the mind's eye doth thy beauty see,
Beneath the darkness of a starless sky.
So is my day a night of weary thought,
And my dark night with sunlike vision fraught.

###1224###
Since thou art gone, the sun gives forth no heat,
The fairest flowers have no scent to give,
The songs of birds no longer sound so sweet,
And in this joyless world I scarce can live.
Each lovely thing that doth my senses greet,
Doth but remind me of a greater grace,
And serves to make my sorrow more complete,
Since I can find thee not in any place.
The moon at night, a lonely silver coin,
Doth light a path that leads me to despair,
It bids my lonely, weeping soul to join
The empty silence of the midnight air.
For thou art all the world's delight to me,
And where thou art not, nothing good can be.

###1225###
Mine own two eyes have turn'd to traitors now,
And forged a league with every passing face;
They break the faith of my most solemn vow,
And seek thy rival in a stranger's place.
My heart, that fortress once so strong and sure,
Is now besieged by thoughts of foul deceit;
How can my love this constant war endure,
Fed on the poison of a false conceit?
For every smile that thou dost grant elsewhere
Doth plant a thorn of anguish in my soul;
Each gentle word thou speak'st fills me with care,
And o'er my mind doth jealous madness roll.
Yet this my torment, I must bear alone,
Lest I should lose thee by my making moan.

###1226###
How can my verse thy truest worth e'er see,
When thou thyself so far from me must be?
This cruel distance robs me of my art,
And writes a sadder story on my heart.
The lonely hours like ages seem to pass;
I count the moments in Time's running glass,
And curse the miles that keep my world in two,
And chide the heavy sky for being blue.
For naught seems right when we are kept apart,
A walking shadow with a broken heart.
I see thy face in every shifting cloud,
And speak thy name, though never out aloud.
Let this poor ink a faithful message send:
My love for thee shall never have an end.

###1227###
My thoughts are sick, and I am in a fever,
A burning malady that will not leave here.
This strange affliction finds no common cure,
But in the cause of it, must still endure.
For thou, dear doctor, art the source of ill,
Whose absence makes my pulse grow faint and still,
Whose presence makes it race beyond control,
A frantic rhythm that consumes my soul.
Physicians bring me potions, all in vain,
For mortal medicine cannot ease my pain;
Their clumsy arts can never make me whole,
It is thy shadow that infects my soul.
So let me die, or grant the only grace:
The healing balm of thy beloved face.

###1228###
Thy two bright eyes are not like earthly things,
But rival stars that rule my darkest night;
No heavenly orb such holy comfort brings,
Nor sheds upon the world so pure a light.
When lesser suns their daily course have run,
And drawn the veil of evening o'er the land,
My day of true devotion is begun,
Held in the gaze that I can scarce withstand.
Let astronomers their foolish charts devise,
And seek their fates in planets far above;
I find my fortune in thy constant eyes,
That teach the truest astrology of love.
So whilst they burn to grant my soul their grace,
No other light I need, in any place.

###1229###
It is not for thy face that I do love thee,
Though fairer creature Nature never made;
Nor for thine eyes that sit like stars above me,
Whose light alone can put the sun to shade.
It is the beauty of thy constant mind,
Wherein all virtues in their triumph sit,
A treasure that a man might seek and find
His better self ennobled by thy wit.
Thy gentle heart, thy judgment true and strong,
Thy grace that turns all common things to gold;
'Tis for these gifts I praise thee in my song,
A story that can never be full told.
So outward form may with the seasons fade,
But thy fair mind a conquest hath been made.

###1230###
I make my sorrow my most chosen friend,
And in its company find strange delight;
The pains of love that seem to have no end
Are now the comfort of my lonely night.
What other men would flee, I do embrace,
And find a sweetness in my bitter tears;
I see the ghost of joy in sorrow's face,
And nourish grief to quiet all my fears.
For if this sadness were to pass away,
What would be left of my long love for thee?
'Twould be a dawn without a coming day,
A silent ship upon a soundless sea.
So let me cherish this most loyal grief,
Since in my pain my love finds all relief.

###1231###
Forgive the error of my foolish tongue,
And pardon what my witless heart hath done;
My reason by a sudden passion stung,
A foolish battle with its king hath won.
This rash transgression, born of idle thought,
Hath left a stain where all was clean before;
Oh, let a lesson by my shame be taught,
That I may guard my lips forevermore.
Let not my fault in thy displeasure live,
Nor let this cloud obscure thy friendly light;
'Tis the divinest of all things to give
Forgiveness, and to make the darkness bright.
Then let thy grace my penitence outrun,
And I'll not rest until thy pardon's won.

###1232###
Sometimes I think thee as a blessed saint,
To whom my soul in reverence must pray;
Thy virtue chastens every foul complaint,
And leads my better thoughts from sin astray.
Thy speech is scripture, and thy face a shrine,
Before whose altar I do kneel in awe;
I seek in thee a goodness so divine,
It seems to be Creation's highest law.
But then a look, a gesture, or a word,
Doth kindle in my blood a profane flame;
A whispered promise in the silence heard,
Makes me forget thy consecrated name.
So am I torn between a prayer and plea:
To save my soul, or lose my soul in thee.

###1233###
My love for thee is like the changing year,
Which with the hopeful green of spring began,
And saw thy beauty in its pride appear,
Fulfilling all of Nature's perfect plan.
Then came the summer of our high content,
With long, warm days of sun and sweet delight,
When all our time in golden ease was spent,
And love's hot sun burned ever strong and bright.
But now, I feel the autumn chill draw near,
The leaves of passion lose their vibrant hue;
A creeping doubt, a melancholy fear,
Doth ask if such a love can be held true.
So must we face the winter of our days,
Or find new fire to light these shadowed ways?

###1234###
I search my mind for all the words I know,
To build a monument of fitting praise,
But all my craft doth but my weakness show,
And in a maze of speech my judgment strays.
Should I compare thee to a summer's day?
'Tis but a shadow of thy truer light.
Or to the fairest flower of the May?
It withers, where thy beauty stays so bright.
My finest verse, my most inventive rhyme,
Doth fail to capture what mine eyes can see;
Thy grace exceeds the boundaries of time,
And mocks the feeble artistry in me.
So I must cease, and let the silence speak,
For where thou art, all mortal praise is weak.

###1235###
In that dark theatre of the sleeping mind,
Where hidden truths in silent plays appear,
Last night a vision, wondrously kind,
Didst banish all my wakeful doubt and fear.
I dreamt that thou wert seated by my side,
Thy gentle hand was laid upon my own,
And in thine eyes, where pity used to hide,
A look of constant, true affection shone.
Thou spok'st no word, but in that peaceful state,
My soul did understand what thine confessed;
No need for sound to pass that sacred gate,
To know my love was welcome and was blessed.
But then I woke, and found it but a dream,
And all the world more cold and grey did seem.

###1236###
When to the sessions of my silent thought
I summon up the ghosts of things long past,
Thy dear-loved image to my mind is brought,
On which my fond and mournful eyes are cast.
I see again the hour we first did meet,
The sunlit garden where our friendship grew,
I hear the echo of thy voice so sweet,
And feel the burning of that love anew.
These memories, a bittersweet perfume,
Do both delight and wound my present state,
Reminding me in my now empty room
Of all I had, and lost by cruelest fate.
Yet is it better to have known such bliss,
Than live a life that knows not what I miss.

###1237###
My foolish brain, which thinks itself so wise,
Doth build a prison for my captive heart;
It tells me that my loving thee defies
All logic, and I play a senseless part.
It argues that the pain I do receive
Is greater than the joy I hope to find,
And that in time thy beauty will deceive
The very vows that have my soul entwined.
But when my heart, that scoffs at Reason's plea,
Doth feel the simple presence of thy grace,
All argument and cold debate must flee,
And lose its cause before thy lovely face.
So let my mind its losing battle fight;
My heart knows well its darkness from its light.

###1238###
When in a crowd I hear a stranger speak
Thy name, though not in reference to thee,
A sudden flush doth burn upon my cheek,
And all the world seems to go still for me.
That single sound, a mere and common word,
Becomes a bell that tolls within my soul;
A secret chord within my heart is stirred,
And passions rise which I can scarce control.
For in those letters all thy being's held,
The memory of thy voice, thy form, thy grace;
By that one syllable I am compelled
To see in my mind's eye thy perfect face.
If but thy name can cause such sweet, sharp pain,
What hope have I to see thee whole again?

###1239###
My love's eyes are not sapphires, clear and bright,
Her hair is not like threads of spun moonlight;
Her skin is not as pure as winter snow,
Nor from her cheeks do scarlet roses grow.
I will not say her voice is music's sound,
For earthly words on her soft tongue are found;
Nor that a goddess' tread is in her walk,
But like a woman she doth move and talk.
And yet, by heaven, I do think my love
As rare as any she who's praised above
The reach of truth with some false, strained compare,
That makes of beauty a celestial snare.
For she is real, and in her truth I find
The perfect love that captivates my mind.

###1240###
When in my glass I see the coming years,
And count the furrows of Time's cruel design,
My youth's departure summons up my fears,
That this decaying form can ne'er be thine.
My hair, once dark, admits a thread of grey,
A solemn herald of my winter season;
The strength I had begins to steal away,
And makes my loving thee a kind of treason.
For how can autumn with the spring reside?
Or weary age deserve a morning rose?
My fading light must shame me from thy side,
Where all the grace of living summer shows.
And yet, this love makes my old heart feel new,
And in

###1241###
When instruments do make a pleasing sound,
And teach the very air a cunning art,
Their sweetest notes fall dull upon the ground
Compared to words that issue from thy heart.
Though unseen choirs of heaven softly sing
And lend their chorus to the listening ear,
'Tis but a faint and insubstantial thing
Next to the voice I hold so truly dear.
Thy speech contains all music's truest pleasure,
Whose every phrase a story sweet doth tell,
And every word is of a perfect measure
To cast upon my soul a blissful spell.
    So let all other earthly concerts cease,
    For in thy voice my spirit finds its peace.

###1242###
Let cruel Time conspire to wrong thy face,
And carve his map of sorrows with his years,
To steal away thy youth's delightful grace,
And mock the world with undeserved tears.
His wicked work assaults my grieving sight,
But cannot reach the fortress of my mind,
Wherein thy beauty shines, a constant light,
Whose perfect copy he shall never find.
For what he ruins is but common clay,
A fragile vessel for a work of art;
And though he wastes that vessel day by day,
He cannot touch the image in my heart.
    So let old Time wreak havoc where he may,
    My mind preserves you from his foul decay.

###1243###
When in a crowd I see thee turn thine eyes
To grace another with a passing glance,
My heart doth wear a sorrowful disguise,
And falls into a dark and jealous trance.
For that small moment, I am quite forgot,
A courtier banished from his monarch's grace,
And all thy vows of love dissolve to naught,
Whilst happy sunshine settles on that place.
My reason chides this foolish, weak despair,
And tells my soul it is a petty thing
To covet every motion, word, and air,
Or be the captive that thy beauties bring.
    But reason is a fool, and cannot see
    That all my world begins and ends with thee.

###1244###
The boastful prince may build his tomb of stone,
And with bright gold his final chamber dress,
But soon by war his state is overthrown,
And all his pride is turned to nothingness.
The mightiest captain, whose all-conquering hand
Did make the trembling nations bend the knee,
Is but a whisper in a barren land,
A faded name for history to see.
But thou, my love, a truer fame shalt find,
Engraved within the compass of my rhyme,
To be reborn in every thoughtful mind
That reads these words in some far-distant time.
    For stone shall crumble, gold shall lose its gleam,
    But thou shalt live, the poet's breathing theme.

###1245###
Behold the landscape of a winter's day,
When frozen silence holds the barren ground,
The cheerful birds have fled and flown away,
And in the wood is made no living sound.
The trees, like skeletons against the grey,
Lift up their naked arms as if to mourn
The sun, who shows his face then makes no stay,
Leaving the world abandoned and forlorn.
Such is my state, my dear, when thou art gone,
A frozen country, void of all delight,
Wherein my heavy heart must sit alone
And find no comfort in the day or night.
    For thou art summer to my seasons' strife;
    Without thee, I have but the frost of life.

###1246###
My foolish eyes and my more foolish heart
Engage in civil war and endless strife,
For each one claims to know the truer part
Of that which gives my weary spirit life.
My eyes do swear they see a fault in thee,
A human blemish common to us all;
My heart replies that it can nothing see
But perfect grace, on which it waits in thrall.
The eyes present their proof for me to find,
A list of flaws that reason must confess;
The heart, in scorn of reason, stays quite blind,
And loves thee not in spite, but nonetheless.
    And in this war, I find no victory,
    For sight is pain, and blindness misery.

###1247###
In heavy sleep, when all the world is still,
My dreams conspire to bring thy form to me,
And with thy phantom presence they do fill
The lonely darkness, for my soul to see.
Thou comest then with such a living grace,
I reach my hand to touch thy lovely brow,
To gaze upon thy well-remembered face,
And speak to thee my constant, loving vow.
But at my touch, the vision starts to fade,
A ghost of smoke that vanishes in air,
And I, by my own hopeful sense betrayed,
Awake to find a greater, deep despair.
    Thus is my night more bright than weary day,
    Which shows me light, but takes thy form away.

###1248###
As some lost sailor, on a sunless sea,
Doth search the clouds for one familiar star
To ease his fear of what is yet to be
And guide him home from unknown lands afar;
So I, upon this life's bewildering stage,
Where fortune's storms do threaten and disarm,
Look only to thy light, and turn the page
Of my sad fate, protected from all harm.
Thou art the only compass I require,
A fixed point in a world of shifting sand,
The single flame of my soul's true desire,
The guiding beacon in this troubled land.
    Let others trust what maps and charts decree,
    I have no need of them, while I have thee.

###1249###
The world's a 'scutcheon, painted to deceive,
Where every virtue makes a hollow sound,
And men do act a part they don't believe,
On which false stage no honest man is found.
For loyalty is but a mask for gain,
And whispered praise a hook for catching fools,
And seeming friendship but a gilded chain
That crafty minds employ as selfish tools.
Amidst this show of counterfeit and art,
Where all is seeming, nothing truly is,
Thou stand'st alone, with an unblemished heart,
A truth that shames all other promises.
    Let others play their parts for all to see;
    My only truth, and all the world, is thee.

###1250###
Why dost thou use the power of thy face
To levy such a tax of tears and sighs,
And in thy pride, afford me no more grace
Than that which in a tyrant's cold heart lies?
Thou know'st thy beauty holds my soul in fee,
A willing captive to thy conquering eyes,
Yet show'st no pity for my misery,
But tak'st a cruel pleasure in my cries.
Is it not triumph-enough that thou art fair,
And that my world upon thy whim depends?
Must thou compound my loving, deep despair
By treating me as less than common friends?
    Take heed, my love, for beauty cannot last,
    And Karma's shadow is already cast.

###1251###
My love for thee is like the fickle year,
That turns its humour with the changing sky,
And feels the summer's warmth when thou art near,
But winter's chill when thou dost pass me by.
Thy smile is April, when the buds appear
And all the world is painted fresh and green;
Thy frown, a bleak November, cold and drear,
When not a single cheerful sight is seen.
And if thy mood be changeable and brief,
'Tis like an autumn day of sun and rain,
At once bestowing solace and then grief,
A moment's pleasure and an age of pain.
    So must my heart attend thy monthly state,
    For in thy face, it reads its own true fate.

###1252###
From every lovely thing that I do see,
My mind doth steal some portion of its grace
To build a rich and varied praise for thee,
And find all virtues mirrored in thy face.
The rose's blush is stolen for thy cheek,
The star's high fire is borrowed for thine eye,
The lily's white thy flawless hand doth seek,
The morning's gold doth in thy tresses lie.
From kindness I have learned thy truest heart;
From music, learned the measure of thy speech;
From truth itself, I learned thy honest part,
A lesson that no other thing can teach.
    Yet this is theft, and makes a poor design,
    For all their grace is but a shade of thine.

###1253###
When in my glass I see my sorry state,
And count the lines that chronicle my years,
I curse the harsh injustice of my fate,
Which gave me little cause for aught but tears.
I see my name writ in no book of worth,
My deeds as common as the roadside dust,
A wretched player on this mortal earth,
Whose briefest triumphs have betrayed his trust.
And in this mood of utter self-disdain,
When I am all but lost in my own shame,
A single thought of thee erases pain,
And I in silence speak thy holy name.
    And for thy love, which thou dost give to me,
    I would not change my state to be a king.

###1254###
The weary miles that keep me far from you
Are tyrants that hold back my heart's desire,
And every league that separates us two
Doth feed my longing with a hotter fire.
My swiftest thoughts can cross the space between,
And in a moment, conjure up thy face,
To see what my poor eyes have not yet seen,
And feel thy presence in this lonely place.
But then that thought, a traitor to my mind,
Doth leave me with a sorrow more intense,
For in its wake, I am but left behind,
A cheated soul of mocked and empty sense.
    Thus, what should comfort me but makes me weep,
    This waking dream I cannot truly keep.

###1255###
My troubled mind is like a storm-tossed sea,
When raging winds do make the heavens dark,
And every thought, a wave that crashes, be
A threat to drown my spirit's fragile bark.
My hope's a sail, torn by the gales of doubt,
My reason is a rudder, lost and broke,
As fears like monstrous leviathans shout
And wrap my soul in terror's choking cloak.
No peaceful harbour can I hope to find,
No shore of calm, no anchor for my soul,
For in the tempest of my loving mind,
The waves of passion have assumed control.
    And in this storm, one truth alone shines bright:
    Thou art the only star that gives me light.

###1256###
My muse, of late, has been a barren thing,
My pen grown dull, my verses stiff and slow;
I have no joyful melody to sing,
Nor can I make a pleasing number flow.
My wit is bankrupt, and my fancies die,
My store of words is empty and grown poor,
And when to write a verse of praise I try,
The ink itself seems borrowed from before.
But when I think of thee, the spring returns,
A sudden torrent breaks the frozen ground,
My spirit with a holy fever burns,
And in my heart a thousand songs are found.
    So praise me not for what these lines impart,
    The poet is but thee, who owns my heart.

###1257###
Since you did part, the world hath lost its hue,
And all that was once vibrant seems now grey;
The summer sky presents a paler blue,
The brightest rose a spectre of decay.
The food I eat hath not its former taste,
The wine I drink, a bitter, flat despair;
The world's a 'wildering and a barren waste
Where shadows move and whisper in the air.
My eye, which once found beauty everywhere,
Now looks on all with dull and heavy sight,
Perceiving only that thou art not there,
And so mistakes the morning for the night.
    For thou art substance, and the world is show;
    Without thee, what is real I do not know.

###1258###
Within the locked and secret treasury
Of my devoted heart, thy image lies,
A prize more dear than all the gems that be
Beneath the earth or watched by dragons' eyes.
No thief may enter where it is enshrined,
Nor rust of time corrupt its perfect gold;
It is the wealth and comfort of my mind,
A thing of beauty wondrous to behold.
And though my outer state be poor and mean,
And Fortune scorn me with her fickle grace,
I am a king when I survey unseen
The perfect lines of thy remembered face.
    So let the world its fleeting riches boast,
    I have a hoard that I do value most.

###1259###
Some men may say that I am not my own,
That in thy love I am a willing thrall,
And that my heart, upon thy beauty thrown,
Hath forfeited its freedom at thy call.
They say these bonds of love, they do but bind,
A gilded cage that keeps my soul confined,
But in this sweet imprisonment I find
A liberty they cannot understand.
For being thine, I am at last set free
From all the idle chase of lesser things,
From vanity, and from my own self, me,
And borne aloft on contemplation's wings.
    So call it prison, I will not complain,
    For in thy service is my truest reign.

###1260###
Let me not make a fickle, changing vow,
Which alters when the circumstance doth change,
Or bend my heart because the world, somehow,
Finds in thy form a feature new or strange.
No, it is fixed, and ever shall endure,
A faith that Time itself can not betray,
A star whose light is constant and is pure,
Though all the other heavens fall away.
It is the rock on which my soul doth stand,
The final truth when all else is a lie,
A sacred oath held in my willing hand,
That will not falter, lessen, or else die.
    This is my creed, and all I have to prove:
    My world is dust, but what is real is love.

###1301###
Let me not praise the lily or the rose,
Nor yet the sun in his most gold array,
For in thy face a fairer garden grows,
Which turns to dusk the brightness of the day.
The sweetest music that the lark can make,
Cannot compare unto thy gentle voice,
And for a lesser, hollow echo'll ache
When I am given so divine a choice.
Yea, all the world's delights which men hold dear
Are but a shade, a poorly fashioned cast
Of that true substance which in thee is clear,
A fleeting beauty, by thine own surpassed.
So let them boast of treasures they possess;
I find my world in thy soul's loveliness.

###1302###
When cruel distance keeps me far away,
My life becomes a play without a heart,
And tedious seems the passing of the day,
Devoid of substance, meaning, and of art.
For thou art banish'd from my hungry sight,
And in that space, what sorrows I do find!
My sun is hidden, and I greet the night,
Which brings thine image only to my mind.
But this same image, which should comfort give,
Doth make me weep for what I cannot hold,
And in this waking dream I scarce can live,
A story by a grieving madman told.
My only peace is when I cease to be,
And find my rest in thinking thoughts of thee.

###1303###
When ruthless Time shall steal thy youthful grace,
And carve deep lines upon thy lovely brow,
This verse shall stand and vow to keep thy place
Against the ruin that he brings forth now.
For what is beauty but a flower's lease,
That for a moment blossoms and is bright?
My ink shall grant it title to a peace
That lives beyond death's long and final night.
So though the ages turn thy gold to grey,
And though thy form to common dust descend,
Thy truest essence shall not know decay,
But in this rhyme shall find an endless friend.
So fear not winter's cold, nor summer's end,
For on this page, thy beauty I defend.

###1304###
So base am I, I marvel at thy grace
Which would descend to look upon my flaw,
