For next to the perfection of thy face,
My own seems but a thing of mud and straw.
How canst thou love what is so far below?
A simple weed that gazes at a star,
And in my halting verse I cannot show
The half of all the virtues that you are.
My tongue is tied, my wit is rendered weak,
My finest phrases but a paltry sound;
The very praise I desperately would speak
Falls to a whisper, lost upon the ground.
And yet you smile, and in that smile I see
A worth in me that is a gift from thee.

###1305###
Why dost thou boast, O Time, with conquering hand
To crumble castles and to humble kings?
Thy patient siege devours the sea and land,
And silence all the vibrant songbird sings.
Thou art a thief that pilfers precious breath,
And turns the cheek of youth to ashen grey;
Thy final servant is the tyrant Death,
Who claims all things as his eventual prey.
But there is one domain thou canst not rule,
A fortress built beyond thy creeping hour;
My love for him, which makes thee seem a fool,
Stands fast against thy melancholy power.
So let thy seasons rage and do their worst;
My constant heart shall see thy kingdom burst.

###1306###
Thine eyes are not the sun, for they are spheres
Of such a dark and captivating night,
That in their depths a richer world appears
Than e'er was shown by day's most common light.
They hold the wisdom of some ancient lore,
A silent language only I may read,
And when they look on me, I ask no more,
For in their gaze is planted all my seed
Of hope, of joy, of sorrow, and of pain;
They are my twin astrologers that tell
If I shall live in sun or die in rain,
If I am bound for heaven or for hell.
And yet I would not trade their shadowed art
For all the light that shines upon my heart.

###1307###
My reason chides my heart for loving thee,
And calls my passion but a foolish game;
It shows with logic how I am not free,
And how this love will only bring me shame.
"She is but mortal," doth my reason say,
"Her beauty leased from Time, her breath from Fate."
It builds a case against thee all the day,
And proves with proof that I should learn to hate.
But then my heart, that most rebellious thing,
Mounts its own stand and pleads its own sweet cause,
And of thy virtue it begins to sing,
Ignoring reason's cold and sterile laws.
So let them war, this mind and this fond soul,
For thy dear love hath taken all control.

###1308###
When I am lock'd within the arms of sleep,
A kinder world doth often visit me,
Wherein thy precious phantom I may keep
More close than waking hours will let me be.
No cruel mischance, no bitter word is spoken,
We walk through fields where all is fair and green,
And that dear bond between us is not broken
By any jealous eye or force unseen.
But then the dawn doth rudely interfere,
And with its light, it stealeth thee away;
I wake to find that thou art nowhere near,
And face the barren landscape of the day.
Thus sleep's sweet lie is my most treasured prize,
Until the truth returns to my sad eyes.

###1309###
Since last I saw thee, all the world seems wrong,
The sun shines with a dull and borrowed fire,
The birds have quite forgot their cheerful song,
And all my thoughts are plagued by sharp desire.
The sweetest rose that in the garden grows
Doth seem to mock me with its scentless face,
The gentle wind that through the meadow blows
Is but a sigh in this forsaken place.
All that was fair is now a weary sight,
All that was music, now a jarring sound;
The world is but a canvas, black and white,
Where no true colour can by me be found.
For thou art all the beauty that I see,
And when thou art gone, it goes away with thee.

###1310###
Your beauty gives you leave to be unkind,
And so you wield your power with some pride,
Too conscious of the spell by which you bind
The humble hearts that in your orbit ride.
You wear a look of such indifferent grace,
As if the praise men give were but your due,
And in the perfect lines of your dear face,
A touch of royal scorn is showing through.
And yet, forgive me if I speak too bold,
This very pride becomes you more than well,
It is the setting for a gem of gold,
A haughty guard before love's citadel.
My captive soul doth not desire release,
For in this bondage I have found my peace.

###1311###
If ever pity in thy heart held sway,
Then let it look upon my wretched state;
Behold a wretch who kneels to thee to pray,
And begs for alms before thy guarded gate.
My words are but the sighs of one in need,
My tears the rain that proves my sorrow's truth,
So let thy gentle spirit plant a seed
Of tender mercy for my aching youth.
For thou art like a queen upon a throne,
And I a subject, pleading for his life,
Who in thy judgment finds his fate alone,
And ends in joy or in eternal strife.
So with one look, one word, one gentle sign,
Tell me that hope may one day yet be mine.

###1312###
I saw the spring in all its hopeful green,
When tender buds did first begin to show;
A mirror of my love when first 'twas seen,
Which in your warming presence 'gan to grow.
Then came the summer, bold and full and proud,
When all the world in richest colour dressed;
So was my love, which spoke its name aloud,
And found a home within your loving breast.
But now the autumn wind begins to bite,
And golden leaves in sorrow tumble down;
My heart is filled with shadow and with night,
Reflecting all the coldness of your frown.
Yet winter's snow holds spring within its keep,
And so my hope is only but asleep.

###1313###
Thou art the fever that consumes my blood,
And mak'st my mind to rave with heated thought;
Thou art the storm, the tempest, and the flood
That leaves my careful reason set at naught.
Thou art the wound that bleeds my life away,
The constant ache, the source of all my ill;
And yet within thy presence I must stay,
For want of thee doth leave me colder still.
For thou art also my most certain cure,
The only balm that can my torment cease,
The gentle hand, the promise sweet and pure,
That brings my wild and warring soul to peace.
So must I have thee, be it for my good,
Or for the very poison in my blood.

###1314###
When I am weary of the world's harsh ways,
And fortune's spite hath left me poor and low,
I do not curse the darkness of my days,
But to a secret, inner comfort go.
For in the quiet chamber of my mind,
I have a treasury of thoughts of thee,
A perfect image, beautifully designed,
That from all worldly trouble sets me free.
Your memory is a feast that I may keep
When hunger makes the outer world seem grim,
A light that shines when I am in the deep,
A cup of solace filled up to the brim.
And so I live, sustained by this sweet art,
Possessing all, whilst holding but a part.

###1315###
Some poets praise a beauty like the sun,
With hair of gold and cheeks of rose and white;
My love's complexion is a duskier one,
And holds the richer mystery of night.
Her eyes are not the blue of summer skies,
But deep, dark pools where hidden truths reside;
No common pearl nor ruby can comprise
The wealth of wonders that within them hide.
Her voice is not a lark's, but low and clear,
It speaks a music reason understands,
And banishes all doubt and foolish fear
With the soft "amen" that my soul demands.
So let them praise the light they think so fair;
My world is built of her dark eyes and hair.

###1316###
My pen is dull, my wit is poor and weak,
And all my words are beggars in the street,
Until the blessed subject I would speak
Doth make my humble line of verse complete.
For when I turn my labour to thy name,
The ink flows freely and the rhymes are found;
My barren thoughts, which once were wild and tame,
Now blossom forth on consecrated ground.
It is not I who fashions this good verse,
But thy perfection working through my hand;
Thou art the muse, the blessing, and the curse
That makes me write what I scarce understand.
And if these lines should find some future praise,
The credit's thine, which brightened all my days.

###1317###
Let those who will, take notice of my state,
And with their idle judgment cast their stone;
Let them speak ill of me and curse my fate,
And for my choice of love, let them bemoan.
Their blood is cold, their reason is a book
Of narrow rules, where passion is a crime;
They cannot comprehend the single look
That lifts my soul beyond the reach of Time.
They see a fault, where I a virtue find;
They see a weakness, where I see my strength;
They call me lost, because they are so blind,
And measure love by its poor width and length.
But what care I what tedious fools believe?
Thy love is all the truth I shall receive.

###1318###
My heart, which once was mine, now lives with thee,
And in thy breast hath found a better home;
And thy heart, by a sweet exchange with me,
Hath in my body chosen now to roam.
So I am thee, and thou in turn art I,
A single soul that in two bodies lies,
And when men look at me they may descry
A shadow of the soul within thine eyes.
This bargain, sealed without a word or deed,
Doth make us richer than we were apart;
For in our union, we have planted seed
Of one new life that springs from a shared heart.
So let us guard this treasure we possess,
And live as one in twinned lovingness.

###1319###
When all the world is still, and night is deep,
And weary men have found their due repose,
My thoughts of thee their faithful vigil keep,
And banish from my eyes the peace of those.
I see thy face upon the darkened air,
I hear thy voice within the silent room,
And argue with the shadows of despair
That haunt the corners of my lonely gloom.
My bed becomes a rack whereon I turn,
In torment for the thing I cannot have;
My very pillow seems to mock and burn,
A cold reminder of the warmth I crave.
Thus all my night is spent in thy pursuit,
Which bears a bitter and a sleepless fruit.

###1320###
Though worms shall try this body, and the grave
Shall claim this flesh as its triumphant prize,
The deathless love for thee which I do have
Shall scorn the dust in which my fortune lies.
For it is not a thing of blood and bone,
Nor is it bound to breath, nor sight, nor sound,
But is a spirit seated on a throne
That in my truest, inmost soul is found.
So when my final hour hath come and passed,
And all that's mortal of me is undone,
Think not that love itself can fail so fast,
Or that its long and faithful race is run.
For what we were, we shall for ever be,
A love made perfect for eternity.

###1321###
When I consider how the painter’s art
Doth fail to capture what in thee I see,
It cannot sketch the beating of thy heart,
Nor show the soul that makes thee dear to me.
The summer rose, though lovely in its grace,
And sweeter still when kissed by morning dew,
Is but a shadow of thy beauteous face,
And lacks the constancy of what is true.
The fairest stars that in the heavens turn
Are but cold lamps to thy more gentle light;
For in thine eyes eternal lessons burn,
To make a day out of the darkest night.
   Thus art and nature both defeated be,
   For naught but love can truly measure thee.

###1322###
My weary eyes, to welcome sleep in vain,
Do search the darkness for a peaceful rest,
But find thine image figured in my brain,
A constant guest that robs me of my best.
This tyrant thought, that holds me in its thrall,
Doth make the silent hours of night a stage,
Wherein thy virtues I do oft recall,
And read thy memory like a sacred page.
And so the gloom, which others hold in dread,
Becomes a gallery where thou art shown;
My lonely pillow, and my empty bed,
Are peopled by thy presence, though alone.
   So is the darkness that about me lies
   Made bright by visions for my waking eyes.

###1323###
Hath not the world enough of sorrow known,
That thou shouldst add thy coldness to the store,
And leave my constant heart to sigh alone,
Which for thy love hath all it had before?
I gave thee trust, a coin of purest gold,
And all my hopes I laid before thy feet;
A truer tale by lover was ne'er told,
Nor any love with passion so complete.
But thou, a miser of thine own affection,
Dost hoard the grace that freely should be spent,
And in the glass dost study thy perfection,
On cruel triumphs of the heart intent.
   Yet when that glass thy winter doth proclaim,
   Thou'lt find no comfort in a frozen name.

###1324###
Why dost thou make a virtue of thy pride,
And in thy scorn a kind of greatness find?
Know’st not that beauty, if it be denied
To gentle thoughts, corrupts the heart and mind?
For what is fairness but a fragile flower
That lives a day and withers in the sun,
If from its heart there grows a will to power
And cruelties are by its beauty done?
Thy lovely face a mask for coldness is,
A painted heaven o'er a frozen sea;
Thy words like honey promise fleeting bliss,
But leave the taste of bitterest misery.
   So let thy kindness, not thy beauty, plead,
   For beauty dies, but lives in a good deed.

###1325###
When in the chronicles of ages gone,
I read of ladies praised for face and form,
Whose glorious deeds and golden virtues shone,
A shelter from the world’s most bitter storm;
I think those poets of a bygone day
Were prophets who, with keen and seeing eye,
Foretold thy coming, and prepared the way
For the perfection that could never die.
All their sweet praise, their metaphors, and art,
Were but a shadow of the thing to be;
They dimly felt within each hopeful heart
A future grace they could not truly see.
   For in their verse they saw thee as a dream,
   But I in truth do live within thy beam.

###1326###
Oft have I cursed this ink that stains my hand,
This heavy labour of the rhyming art,
That seeks to make the world to understand
The silent language of a captive heart.
For every word a universe betrays,
And every line a poorer version seems
Of that perfection which my thought surveys,
The sunlike substance of my waking dreams.
My clumsy verse, a web of failing sound,
Doth try to catch the music of thy grace,
But thou, the source, art nowhere to be found
Within this cramped and artificial space.
   And yet I write, for what else can I do,
   When every thought begins and ends with you?

###1327###
As some poor actor, thrust upon the stage,
Forgets the lines he knew so well by rote,
And in his fear doth miss his finest age,
A strangled whisper issuing from his throat;
So I, in presence of thy majesty,
Find all my well-rehearsed composure fall,
And all my wit and practiced poetry
Can find no words to answer to thy call.
My tongue, so bold in matters less divine,
Grows mute, and cannot speak what love hath taught,
And these poor eyes, which long for thee to shine,
Are blinded by the prize that they have sought.
   So read my silence, let my looks impart
   The fearful homage of a speechless heart.

###1328###
That time of year thou mayst in me behold
When summer’s lease hath been forsworn by fall,
And shivering trees, their stories having told,
Await the winter that will take their all.
Or see in me the twilight of a day,
That after sunset fadeth in the west,
Which by and by black night doth take away,
To seal all things in death’s eternal rest.
So is the fire that once did burn so bright,
Now glowing embers on a dying hearth,
Consuming that which gave it strength and light,
And turning all its former joy to dearth.
   This thou perceiv'st, which makes thy love more strong,
   To love that well which thou must leave ere long.

###1329###
If music be the nourishment of love,
As wise men say, then is thy voice the same;
It is a sound all other sounds above,
That sets my very senses all aflame.
Let lutes be silent and let singers cease,
For in thy speech a sweeter tune is found,
That brings the warring spirit endless peace,
And makes the earth seem consecrated ground.
For when thou speak'st, the birds attend thy words,
And hold their breath for fear to miss a note,
And envious winds that pass in fleeting herds
Do stop to wonder at thy perfect throat.
   Thus all the world in listening is engrossed,
   But I, thy captive, hear and love it most.

###1330###
Let me not be a beggar for thy grace,
Who craves a crumb from thy o'erflowing store,
But let me find in thy beloved face
A welcome that doth bid me ask no more.
For love that's given out of charity,
Or as a duty which the heart denies,
Hath but the form of true sincerity,
And in its giving, it condemns and dies.
I seek a love that's given full and free,
A river flowing from a boundless spring,
Not measured drops of pity granted me,
A sorry and a melancholy thing.
   So love me for no reason but to love,
   And in that choice, our equal fortune prove.

###1331###
Thine eyes are books where I do love to read
The fairest stories that were ever writ;
But one dark chapter makes my spirit bleed,
And casts a shadow on my struggling wit.
It tells a tale of distance and of doubt,
Of glances turned to where I cannot be,
And makes me fear my love will be shut out,
A lonely vessel on a shoreless sea.
So turn that page, and let me not peruse
The painful script of what might come to pass,
Nor let my heart the bitter lesson choose
That beauty is as transient as the grass.
   Let me but read the part that speaks of me,
   And in that chapter find eternity.

###1332###
The forward violet, that dares to show
Its purple head before the winter’s gone,
Is by the late and lingering frosts laid low,
And rues the pride that it had put upon.
So did my heart, too eager for the spring,
Unfurl its love before the time was right,
And taught my hopeful tongue too soon to sing
Of a new dawn, before the end of night.
Now thy cold front, a blast of arctic air,
Hath scourged the bloom of my presumptuous suit,
And left my soul in desolate despair,
To bear a winter’s crop of bitter fruit.
   Yet in the root, a constant faith doth lie,
   To wait for sun, or in the waiting die.

###1333###
Thou art the map by which my journey’s planned,
The single star that guides my wandering bark;
Without thy light, in this forsaken land,
My every path is treacherous and dark.
For other men may seek for gold or fame,
Or build them kingdoms on the shifting sand;
My world is circumscribed within thy name,
My only treasure is to touch thy hand.
Let merchants cross the sea for newfound spice,
And soldiers fight for some contested ground;
I find my Indies in thy loving eyes,
And all my triumph in thy gentle sound.
   So let the world its own ambitions chase;
   My universe is in this single place.

###1334###
So oft I've sworn my love in labored verse,
And dressed my passion in a poet's guise,
That now I fear my blessings are a curse,
And truth is hidden by a web of lies.
For words are cheap, and any man may write
Of sun-like eyes and breath of sweet perfume,
And turn the darkness of his soul to light
With borrowed phrases that he can assume.
How canst thou know my sonnets are not feigned,
My vows not false, my purpose not to cheat,
When every fawning fool that would be gained
Can lay a similar offering at thy feet?
   My only proof is that my words are plain,
   And love that's true speaks not, but speaks again.

###1335###
They say that Time’s a thief with nimble hand,
That steals the bloom from every youthful cheek,
And with a power that none can withstand,
Doth make the strong and mighty to be weak.
He carves his wrinkles on the smoothest brow,
And silvers o’er the finest gold-spun hair,
And makes the straightest back to bend and bow,
And fills the heart with sorrow and with care.
But on thy beauty he hath no such power,
For what he takes, thy virtue doth restore;
Thy spirit blossoms like a deathless flower,
And makes thee lovelier than thou wast before.
   So let old Time his cruelest work begin;
   He alters but the shell, not what's within.

###1336###
As a poor fool who, having lost his way
In some dark wood, where every path's a maze,
Sees in the distance a deceiving ray,
And runs toward it through the gathering haze;
So I, being lost in sorrow's wilderness,
Did see the false light of your passing smile,
And thought it promised hope and happiness,
And followed it for many a weary mile.
But it was only moonlight on the frost,
A cold and barren promise of a fire,
And in pursuing it, my soul is lost,
Consumed by unfulfilled and vain desire.
   Thus I have learned what every fool must learn:
   The lights that guide us can as easily burn.

###1337###
This world's a stage, and we are players all,
Each with his entrance and his exit planned,
And I have played the lover, to my fall,
Whose fondest speeches you misunderstand.
I learn my lines of heartfelt adoration,
I speak them with a passion true and deep,
But you mistake them for mere affectation,
And while I praise you, you are fast asleep.
You think my sighs are but a player’s art,
My tears but glycerin, my vows a script;
You cannot see you’ve truly broken my heart,
And from its core the honest words are ripped.
   This play is done, the tragedy is here;
   You gave no comfort, but you cost me dear.

###1338###
Like to the sun, which having run his race,
Doth sink in glory in the western sky,
Yet leaves a memory of his golden face,
That even in the dark doth never die;
So shall thy beauty, when its day is done,
And age and envious time have had their will,
Live on in verses that my love hath spun,
And in the memory of the heart be still.
For though the rose must wither on the vine,
And raven locks must turn to winter's snow,
The spirit of thy loveliness shall shine,
And in these lines, to endless ages grow.
   So death may claim thy body as its prize,
   But not the love that lives within my eyes.

###1339###
Why should I weep that you are less than kind,
Or mourn the loss of what was never mine?
I should have known a free and noble mind
Would not be caught in such a paltry line.
You owe me nothing; all the love I gave
Was given freely, with no thought of gain,
And if my heart is now a willing slave,
'Tis I, not you, who must endure the pain.
A king is not indebted to the man
Who gazes at him with an admiring eye;
So why should you, by some celestial plan,
Be bound to love me, even though I die?
   It is my fault, and I confess the crime
   Of loving far above my humble clime.

###1340###
When all these poems are but dust and age,
And my poor name forgotten in the earth,
When some new scholar turns the yellowed page,
And wonders what this ancient verse was worth;
He shall not know the colour of thine hair,
Nor the sweet music that thy voice did make,
Nor how thy presence filled the common air
With a strange grace, for sheer perfection’s sake.
But this he’ll know: that once a poet strove
To grasp a beauty that his words could not,
That he was thrall to an consuming love,
By which all other worldly things forgot.
   And in that thought my love shall live anew,
   For he shall see that I saw only you.

###1341###
When I behold the morning's gentle grace,
That bids the rose awake from sunless day,
I see a mirror of thy lovely face,
And all the world must fall beneath its sway.
But soon the sun doth lose his golden light,
And nature's fleeting beauty proves untrue,
As shadows lengthen into solemn night,
To steal away the freshness of the dew.
Yet thy fair form, a work of higher art,
Defies the course of seasons flying past;
Thy summer lives eternal in my heart,
A beauty made of substance that will last.
So let Time's scythe cut down what it may see,
It hath no power to take thy worth from thee.

###1342###
My love, I fear that cruel and envious Fate,
Which gives to beauty such a fragile date,
Doth with a wrinkled hand now desecrate
The perfect form of thy most high estate.
I see in every glass and judging eye
The truth that all things fair must one day lie
Beneath the dust, while seasons passing by
Command each living thing to fade and die.
But with this ink a war I'll wage in verse,
And all thy virtues to the world rehearse;
That Time's assault will make thee none the worse,
Nor cause thy memory ever to disperse.
For in these lines thy being I shall raise,
To live in fame beyond our mortal days.

###1343###
The weary world its silent watch doth keep,
Whilst I, a lonely vassal, cannot sleep,
For thoughts of thee, from chasms dark and deep,
Arise within my soul and make me weep.
All through the night thy image is my thought,
A painful bliss, a treasure dearly brought;
In waking dreams thy constant form is sought,
And all my blessed rest is turned to naught.
For when I close mine eyes, my busy mind
Refuses peace or solace there to find,
Portraying thee, both cruel and yet so kind,
A vision bright that makes my senses blind.
So is thy shadow which makes darkness light,
And my sad day seems darker than my night.

###1344###
Love came to me adorned with features fair,
A gentle sprite that danced upon the air;
But in his hand he held a hidden snare,
And in his heart, the seeds of my despair.
He hath a face that kindness seems to show,
A tongue whose words like sweetest honey flow,
But what dark thoughts reside and thrive below,
My trusting heart was not prepared to know.
Why must a thing so beautiful to trust,
Whose vows did seem so true, now turn to dust?
My soul now tells me that his form unjust
Doth hide a spirit cankered with self-lust.
His truth is but a falsehood he implies,
A devil's soul behind an angel's eyes.

###1345###
What is this poison eating at my heart,
That teaches me to doubt thy every art?
I see thee smile, and wish thou wouldst depart,
Lest that same smile should cause another's smart.
I watch thee grant another's eye thy gaze,
And hear thee give what I desire, thy praise;
This monster Jealousy corrupts my ways,
And turns to torment all my sunlit days.
It fills my nights with waking, awful dread,
A hungry beast on its own sorrow fed;
It questions every gentle word once said,
And plants a thorny crown upon my head.
So in thy presence, I can only spy
A thousand reasons for my love to die.

###1346###
I pray thee, do not let thy soft lips speak,
Lest all my reason grow confused and weak,
Nor let the perfect blush upon thy cheek
Be rivalled by the tender words you seek.
For when thy voice doth fall upon my ear,
The world's own music seems no longer sweet;
I hold no other earthly comfort dear,
But fall a silent captive at thy feet.
The lutenist who plays his song is wrong,
The poet's line is tedious and long,
The sweetest choir seems a noisy throng,
If I may but attend thy spoken song.
So let all other earthly senses cease to be,
And leave my soul to listen but to thee.

###1347###
The lines of sorrow mark my heavy brow,
Wherein a map of former grief I pass;
My youth's forgotten kingdom is not now
Reflected truly in this faithful glass.
For I have watched my summer turn to grey,
And seen the thief of moments, cruel Time,
Steal all my hopeful colours to decay,
And rob my vigour of its former prime.
Yet in this ruin, worn by passing years,
One constancy my weary thoughts bestow;
My love for thee, which conquers all my fears,
Doth not consent to be a thing made slow.
So when my youth and beauty are all gone,
My love remains a light to shine upon.

###1348###
This winter's breath, that turns the world so cold,
Doth make of me a mirror for its state;
A barren landscape, sorrowful and old,
Upon whose hopes the frost lies thick and late.
The sun, a pale and distant memory,
Hath left the sky a canvas dull and grey;
The branch, bereft of leaf, on every tree,
Doth mourn the loss of a much brighter day.
So am I, love, when thou art far from me,
A frozen stream, a world of silent white;
My inward sun can find no warmth to be,
And my soul's compass cannot find its light.
But think of thy return, and spring is in my mind,
And winter's cruelest grip I leave behind.

###1349###
The world's a stage, as wise men have professed,
Where each man plays his part and exits hence;
I have played many, and have been distressed
By scripts that gave my suffering no sense.
I have been king, and beggar, fool, and sage,
And felt the hollow echo of applause;
But found no meaning on this shifting stage,
Nor felt the truth behind its flimsy cause.
But since I knew thee, my true role began,
A simple one, and free from all disguise:
To be thy servant, thy devoted man,
Reflected only in thy knowing eyes.
So let the world its pageant scenes remove,
My only part is this: to give thee all my love.

###1350###
Mine eye and heart are at a bitter war,
And each doth claim a greater right to thee;
Mine eye would keep thee as its guiding star,
My heart would hold thee where no eye can see.
My heart objects that in its private thought
Thou art enshrined, a treasure set apart;
Mine eye replies thy image has been caught
And is a painting hung upon the heart.
To end this strife, a pact is thus decreed:
My faithful eye shall feast upon thy look,
My loving heart on thy sweet love shall feed,
As if it were a lesson in a book.
So art thou mine, by outward part or inward sign,
Through my fond heart, and through these eyes of mine.

###1351###
Thy cruelty is fashioned by some art
That makes thy scorn more treasured than a prize;
For when thou wound'st my ever-loving heart,
A greater beauty shines within thine eyes.
Thou art a rose whose thorns do prick me sore,
And draw my blood, yet I must praise the sting;
The more thou dost deny, I crave the more,
And to thy tyrant's throne my tributes bring.
Methinks that nature made thy face so fair
That thou might'st practise tyranny with grace,
And with a sweet smile foster my despair,
And hold my soul a captive in this place.
So frown on me, yet do not send me from thy sight,
For in thy shadow I still find my only light.

###1352###
My soul's a barque upon a stormy sea,
Tossed by the winds of doubt and dark despair;
It seeks a peaceful harbour found in thee,
Beyond the reach of worldly toil and care.
But when my hopes have almost reached the shore,
A wave of thy disdain doth cast me back,
And I am lost amid the ocean's roar,
My compass broken and my rigging slack.
What distant star can guide my weary plight,
When thou, my only constellation, fade?
What anchor holds against the endless night,
If thy firm ground cannot my ship persuade?
Yet I will sail this tempest, and not cease to try,
Until in thy safe harbour I may live or die.

###1353###
I see a ghost when I do look on you,
Not of a spirit from the grave returned,
But of a love I once believed was true,
A fiery passion that too brightly burned.
For in the kindness of your present gaze,
I see the echo of a former grace,
A memory of those sun-drenched, happy days,
Before the mask was set upon your face.
I hear a voice, much like the one you own,
That swore its vows would never know decay,
Yet all those promises aside were thrown,
And like a dream, they paled and fled away.
So pardon me if in my love some doubt you see,
A ghost of what you were still stands 'twixt you and me.

###1354###
Let others seek the glory of a crown,
Or heap up gold to guard against their age,
And chase the fleeting phantom of renown,
To write their name on history's great page.
Let them build monuments of stone and brass,
And boast of conquests in some foreign land,
Not knowing that their triumph soon shall pass,
Like words that have been written in the sand.
My sole ambition is a state more small,
Confined within the compass of thy heart;
To answer when I hear thy gentle call,
And from thy company to never part.
For what is empire, set against a lover's kiss?
All other kingdoms are but poor, compared to this.

###1355###
My love for thee is both a sin and grace,
A holy shrine where sacrilege is done;
For I do worship at thy human face,
And bow to thee as to the morning sun.
My conscience warns me of my soul's damnation,
And tells me heavens can be won no more,
If I should seek my spiritual salvation
In a false idol whom my senses adore.
But then my heart, a cunning advocate,
Argues thy virtue is a thing divine,
That such perfection cannot be a state
Where any fault or worldly sin can shine.
So I am torn, and know not which my soul should choose:
The heaven I am promised, or the one I fear to lose.

###1356###
What treasure can the wealthy merchant boast,
Or what bright jewel can the monarch wear,
From India's mine or Afric's sun-scorched coast,
That with thy sum of beauty could compare?
They count their fortunes up in gold and ships,
And store their gems inside a guarded room,
But I find richer treasure on thy lips,
And greater splendour in thy beauty's bloom.
Their worldly goods are slaves to rust and thieves,
A heavy burden causing anxious strife,
A brittle glory that at last deceives
The foolish owner at the end of life.
But thou, my wealth, art locked within my memory,
A prize that death itself cannot take thence from me.

###1357###
In sleep's dark theatre, I did dream of late
That thou wert mine by some celestial right,
Delivered to me by a gentle fate
To be the constant sun of my dark night.
I felt the comfort of thy hand in mine,
And heard thy voice speak words both soft and low;
Thy presence seemed a true and certain sign
That all my lonely grief to joy would grow.
But then the cock, that herald of the day,
Did crow his note and break the fragile spell;
I woke to find the shadows fled away,
And in their place, a truth I know too well.
For with the light, my waking eyes could only see
That all my joy was but a dream, and not for me.

###1358###
As seasons turn the greens of June to gold,
Then strip the branch to face the winter's chill,
So do my humours, manifold and bold,
Respond entirely to thy sovereign will.
For with thy smile, a summer's day is born,
My heart puts forth its buds in hopeful green;
But with thy frown, I am a thing forlorn,
A barren landscape, pitiful and mean.
If thou art kind, the birds of spring do sing,
And all the world seems filled with pleasant rhyme;
If thou art cold, a bitter frost you bring,
That puts a sudden winter on my prime.
So must I be, a creature of a changing state,
Whose inner weather on thy outward mood must wait.

###1359###
When all my days are done, and my short name
Is quite forgot by those who follow on,
I shall not leave a monument to fame,
Nor mark of greatness on the cold grey stone.
My deeds shall vanish as the dew-drops dry,
My spoken words be lost upon the air;
No trace of what I was shall meet the eye,
Nor show the world I wrestled with despair.
And yet, one part of me shall still endure,
Preserved against oblivion's dark decree;
This verse, which makes my love for thee secure,
Shall speak my truth for centuries to see.
So let my life be naught, my substance turn to dust,
These lines shall live, and in them, my true heart you'll trust.

###1360###
